Stress and Fatigue 
Stress and Fatigue 
Aircraft are subject to stress whether they are flying, taxiing or just parked on the ground. In this chapter we will briefly consider the types of stresses involved, the design specifications laid down by the authorities to mitigate risk. Finally,.we consider strategies used by designers and maintenance engineers to meet the design specifications. 
The forces of lift, weight, thrust and drag acting on an aircraft create stresses in the aircraft structure. Stress is the internal force per unit area inside a structural part as a result of external loads, mathematically expressed as the force divided by the cross­sectional area to which it is applied. When stress is applied it causes strain which we witness when the shape of an object changes when being bent, stretched or squashed. Strain is defined as the amount of deformation in the direction of the applied force divided by the initial length ofthe material. Put simply, strain is the deformation resulting from stress. 
Stresses can either be twisting or torsion stresses, tension, compression or shear. These stresses can act individually or together. Bending a structure, for instance, creates tension on the outside and compression on the inside. 
High compression loads can cause buckling of a structure. 

ft.ihi-l o 
---,--­
i 

---t +--­


Stress and Fatigue 
Stress and Fatigue continued 
When a sufficient load is applied to a metal or other structural material it will change shape. This change in shape is called deformation. A temporary shape change that is self-reversing after the force is removed is called elastic deformation. When the stress is sufficient to permanently deform the metal it is called plastic deformation. 
Stress may be applied as a single long-term load, say when the aircraft is parked. For example, the weight of the aeroplane is taken by the undercarriage, and the weight of the fuselage is borne by the wing root. However, for aircraft in use, most loads are dynamic or cyclical. Dynamic loads are those that are applied and released, for example the occasional application of high 'g'. Cyclical loads are also dynamic in nature but occur in frequently repeated patterns such as the loads exerted on a fuselage when pressurised, loads on the undercarriage on landing or loads experienced by the whole aircraft during turbulence. The examples given are low frequency loads higher frequency but lower amplitude loads result from propeller or engine vibration.

Stress and System Failure 
The stress at which the structure fails is called the ultimate stress. This is the fail point for a single application of a static load. In flight the structure is loaded and unloaded many times at levels below the ultimate stress. In metals, this causes cumulative damage which in turn allows the structure to fail catastrophically at a stress level well below ultimate stress. This cumulative damage and weakening of the structure due to cyclical loading is called metal fatigue. 
In metal structures failures usually occur under tensile stress, for example on the bottom surface of a wing that is being bent upwards. The failures usually start as cracks at the points of concentrated stress, such as rivet holes, machining marks, sharp corners and screw threads. 
Composite structures also suffer from fatigue damage, but react in a different way. Composites "soft fail" as the fibres break. 
Visual detection of fatigue in composites is limited, therefore more sophisticated non-destructive testing is normally necessary.

Structural Mass Limitations 
There are several definitions that are important when considering structural mass limitations on aircraft. You will meet them in several other areas of your studies, but it is worth repeating four of them here, as they do appear in the EASA learning objectives for this subject. 
¦ 
Maximum ramp mass -the structural limitation of the mass of the aeroplane at commencement of taxi. 

¦ 
Maximum take-off mass -the maximum permissible total aeroplane mass at the start of the take-off run. 

¦ 
Maximum zero fuel mass -the maximum permissible mass of an aeroplane with no usable fuel. 

¦ 
Maximum landing mass-the maximum permissible total aeroplane mass on landing in normal circumstances. 



Stress and Fatigue 
The S/N Curve 
The relationship between repeated applications of stress and the gradual degradation of the safe stress level is given by the S/N Curve. This is a plot of the number oftimes and degrees of stress applied and shows what percentage of the original ultimate stress will cause catastrophic failure for a given fatigue history. 
The designers now assess the stress and the S/N forecast for the aircraft -taken from the forecast flight profiles, weights and loading -and design a structure that should be safe for the life of the aircraft. If the aircraft is operated in an entirely different manner from the original design, for instance short haul instead of long haul, then this will affect the life of the aircraft and the planned servicing and spares holdings.

St ress and Fatigue 
Stress Concentration Factor 
Stresses need to be spread evenly rather than concentrated in one point. If they are concentrated, the effects can be catastrophic. To consider the problem, imagine trying to split a log for firewood. A hammer, no matter how large, will never split the wood as effectively as an axe or a splitting wedge, as the stresses are not concentrated with the hammer, but they certainly are with the axe. 
An often-cited example of the stress concentration factor is the 'square' windows on the de Havilland Comet, a British built airliner. Shortly after entering service in the early 1950s three Comets suffered three catastrophic structural failures in flight, resulting in the whole fleet being grounded. This image shows the ADF antenna apertures, which also had square corners, from the upper surface of Comet G-AL YP, which crashed in January 1954. 
The problem was that the stresses were concentrated in the corners of the square windows and other apertures, though this was not sufficiently understood at the time. Wreckage painstakingly pieced together by investigators eventually pointed to the corners as the points of failure. It was later calculated that 70% of the aircraft's ultimate stress was concentrated on the corners of the windows. 

ftiiii-lo 

After extensive re-design with oval windows, the Comet was returned to airworthiness some four years later, but by then Boeing had cornered the airliner market with the 707, which could carry twice as many passengers as the Comet. 
Stress and Fatigue 
Other Factors Affecting Fatigue 
The manner is which the aircraft is operated has a marked influence on its fatigue life. Type of flight manoeuvres, excessive use of 'g', flight in turbulence, low level flight, frequent changes in engine power, high zero fuel weights, all serve to reduce fatigue life. Where the aircraft is based or operated also has effects, for example, coastal air is not only humid but contains corrosive salts. Temperature too plays a part as warm air encourages corrosion, so we find aircraft based in warm coastal areas such as Florida are more prone to corrosion fatigue than perhaps an aircraft based in Central Europe. 
Reducing Fatigue 
It should be clear from all this that an aircraft's fatigue life can be greatly extended if stress levels are kept low. This means smooth flying, avoiding high g, avoiding turbulence and going easy on the power. Weight is a critical factor. Increasing aircraft all-up weight by 1 % can increase fatigue life consumption by 5%. 
If operating in contaminated areas regular aircraft washes should be scheduled. For example, the RAF Nimrod which flew long sorties low over the sea frequently taxied through a wash bay on landing. Furthermore, components known to be prone to corrosion are inspected more regularly and it goes without saying, the quality of the maintenance is paramount. 




ft+Hi-lo 
Corrosive Materials 
Corrosive materials are a part of the aircraft. Battery fluids, particularly sulphuric acid is highly corrosive as well as being a hazard to humans. Hydraulic fluids damage protective surfaces, leaving them prone to corrosion. Fuel and hydraulic contaminated surfaces damage tyres. Mercury, still around on some older aircraft but no longer used on modern aircraft, readily forms an amalgam with aluminium, weakening the structure within minutes. When these items are carried as freight they are classified as Dangerous Goods.

The Design Specifications cover a host of aspects such as design, material quality and build quality and behavioural characteristics

Stress and Fatigue 
Design Strategies 
Aircraft design is one of compromise: how to make the aircraft meet the Certification Specifications yet be as light as possible. Many strategies are used but we only need to know of a few of them here. 
Fail Safe 
Fail safe design is one in which a component can fail, and another is able to take its load. Clearly to achieve this there needs to be at least one other path. Critical components, say flight or engine controls, may have triple paths. An electrical failure would cause major problems on modern computer driven aircraft so there are multiple ways in which electrical power can be restored. 
Another term used to describe this is redundancy. 


Safe Life 
For some components it's not practical to employ a fail-safe strategy. Instead a safe life philosophy is used. Undercarriages are a good example they are heavy items not required for flight, so they need to be kept to a minimum. Undercarriages are normally 'lifed' on number of cycles i.e. number of landings. The idea of safe-life being that the item will not fail within its safe life, and once the life is used it becomes life expired and the component is replaced with a new or reconditioned item. Depending on the function of a component it may be 'lifed' according to flight hours, running hours, landings, or by calendar. 
Damage Tolerance 
Some parts of the aircraft are prone to damage either in flight or during ground handling leading edges, undercarriages, tyres and wing tips are examples. To protect against this a principle of damage tolerance must be included in structural design based on the capability to withstand a certain amount of damage to structural parts until the damage is detected during inspection. 
St ress and Fatigue 

ftiiiifilo 
Maintenance Strategies 
Maintenance is really an ongoing part of the design strategy to ensure the safety of the aircraft. Here we mention a couple of techniques. 
Hard Time/Fixed Time 
Hard time maintenance equates to safe-life components that are replaced after a prescribed number of cycles or hours. Hard time is sometimes referred to as Fixed time. 
On Condition/Condition Monitoring 
Aircraft have scheduled maintenance when components are inspected. If an item fails to meet the critical parameters, it is replaced. Scheduled maintenance varies from daily inspection such as pre-flight, to major servicing when the aircraft is out of service for a long period. 
A deviation of On Condition occurs when a defect is found but a decision is made to delay the repair to align it with a scheduled servicing, in this case the component is monitored at regular intervals this becomes Condition Monitoring. 
Some items are subject to more than one strategy. An example might be tyres they become worn but continue in use until either life expired (hard time) or worn to limits (on condition). 


St ress and Fatigue 
Pilot's Walk Around 
The pilot's walk around is a vital part of the maintenance inspection programme. It is the last chance to save your life and you might be surprised or even shocked at what is occasionally found during this inspection. 
As well as a general look around, the inspection targets specific threat areas. Without specialist equipment you are limited to basic visual inspection. In terms of structure mentioned in this chapter, you are looking for damage which might show as dents or scratched paintwork and wear and tear of tyres. 
You might be surprised that rivets can wear, particularly when subject to vibration. Such wear is indicated by 'fretting', meaning that the panel is becoming loose. It is most easily detected by oily streaks coming from the rivets. 
You are also looking for corrosion. The signs on ferrous base materials are reddish stains, aluminium produces a greyish powder. Paintwork can hide corrosion but there are still signs the paint may have blisters or thin wormlike trails highlighting filiform corrosion. Unpainted surfaces may be pitted. 


As well as corrosion, you are looking for cracks. You might be surprised that prior to the Aloha incident a passenger noticed a crack but didn't say anything. Why had no one else noticed? 
Finally, from a structural perspective you are looking for faulty attachment, loose panels being one the more frequent issues. The author once had an engine not properly bolted so it was loose!

The ideal material for aircraft structures would have the following properties: 
• 
Low density . 

• 
High strength . 

• 
High stiffness. 

• 
Good corrosion resistance . 

• 
Good fatigue performance . 

• 
High operating temperature . 

• 
Ease of fabrication . 

• 
Low cost 


No one material has all these properties. In the construction of an aircraft a variety of materials may be used to meet the requirements of a particular structure. The designer must consider whether, for example, he wants high rigidity (stiffness or plasticity), or flexibility (elasticity) in a component. Or whether the material offered has the correct strength-to-density ratio to achieve the desired outcome. 
Early aircraft used wood with fabric covering for the main structure with metal fittings at critical points. In the 1920s and '30s steel and aluminium replaced the wooden frame but the fabric covering remained. 



I 
Advances in metallurgy eventually led to aluminium alloys that were light yet had similar properties to steel. Engine design advanced in parallel and, as more thrust became available, all­metal aircraft were eventually built. 
Stress and Fatigue 
Aluminium and Aluminium Alloys 
Aluminium is less dense than steel, has good corrosion resistance but is relatively weak. Aluminium alloys are stronger but have worse corrosion resistance. 
Duralumin is a common aluminium alloy in aircraft structures which was invented in the USA in 1909. It may contain about 3% or 4% copper, 0.5% to 1 % manganese, 0.5% to 1 ˝% magnesium, and, in some formulations, a little silicon. 
Duralumin alloys are relatively soft and workable in the normal state. They may be rolled, forged, extruded, or drawn into a variety of shapes and products. After heat treatment and ageing these alloys are comparable to soft steel in strength and hardness. Once manufactured, duralumin is sensitive to temperature and should not be heated above 120°C. 
This makes duralumin unsuitable for welding and prevents its use for aircraft operating above the speed of sound where surface temperatures can rise above this level. Although restricted by operating temperatures, duralumin has good thermal conductivity, and being metal also conducts electricity. 
Duralumin has poor resistance to corrosion. To overcome this, a thin layer of pure aluminium or a corrosion-resistant aluminium alloy is used to cover the duralumin core. These special laminated sheets are called Alclad and it is in this form that duralumin is most used for aircraft construction. 
In summary, the advantages of duralumin are: 
• 
low density 

• 
high strength 

• 
high stiffness 

• 
fatigue tolerant 

• 
ease of fabrication 

• 
good thermal conductivity and 

• 
low cost. The disadvantages are: 

• 
poor corrosion resistance and 

• 
low operating temperature. 


Stress and Fatigue 
Magnesium Alloys 
Magnesium alloys are less dense than aluminium but have very low operating temperatures and a high susceptibility to corrosion. Magnesium alloys should only be used where they can be easily inspected. 
Titanium Alloys 
Titanium alloys are expensive and difficult to work but are extremely strong and will sustain operating temperatures up to 400°C. Titanium is used for engine firewalls and other critical components such as helicopter rotor heads. Titanium can be welded with electron beams. 
Monel 
Monel is an alloy of copper and nickel with small amounts of iron and manganese. Monel alloy's high resistance to corrosion, its low coefficient of expansion and its high strength make it useful for certain applications like the exhaust system for aircraft engines. 
Honeycomb Materials 
The conventional method of fixing metal structures is riveting or bolting. In the 1940s epoxy adhesive metal-to-metal bonding came into use, and is still widely used in aircraft construction. A spin-off from this was the development of"honeycomb" materials, where a cellular fill is bonded between two sheets of metal to give a light but stiff structure.

Tapered Core 
The core itself is weak but stabilises the outer skins and produces a light yet strong torsional structure. The main function of the core material is to stabilise the covering sheets. A honeycomb or sandwich structure is unsuitable for absorbing concentrated 
Core 
loads, and extra protection is required if the structure is to be 
Skin 
subjected to such loads. 
Honeycomb panels are often used for floor surfaces in an aircraft, with the seat mounting rails bonded to the sandwich to help spread the loads. Cargo floors need load-spreaders to avoid 
Skin 
damaging the sandwich panels, as they do not cope well with concentrated stresses. Control surfaces are frequently constructed of honeycomb. 

Stress and Fatigue 
Composites 
Composites are generally used because they have desirable properties which cannot be achieved by the individual components alone. The most common example is a fibrous composite consisting of reinforcing fibres embedded in a binder or matrix material. The idea is not new: the Old Testament book of Genesis refers to clay bricks reinforced with straw. 
Modern composites uses a polymer matrix (also known as resin or filler) reinforced by fibres. The fibres can be glass or aramid (Kevlar), but the most commonly used are carbon fibres, which have the best tensile strength to weight ratio. Aircraft manufacturers are rapidly turning away from metal structures because of the advantages of composites, particularly with regards to strength to weight ratio. 
The fibres can be laid in a random pattern, which gives a material with the same bending strength in any direction. Alternatively, fibres can be laid in one direction to give great bending strength along the fibre run but a much lower strength across the run. In this way, the bending response ofthe material can be tailored exactly to the designer's needs, a fact which becomes important when we deal with the wing bending response of swept wing aircraft. 


Composites may need conductive elements embedded in them to achieve the correct path through for lightning strikes. They may also have reflective elements embedded in them behind mounts for radio antennas. 
Another advantage of composites is their multiple path loads, making them inherently more resistant to fatigue. They are also considered to be 'soft fail' as any failures in the fibres should be detectable long before they become critical. Naturally, being non­metallic they offer excellent corrosion resistance and are very cost effective. 
One disadvantage of composites is that they are very susceptible to damage which is difficult to detect. Metals dent when impacted, giving a visual clue without suffering much loss of strength. It is not the same for composites because the internal fibres can be damaged, causing considerable weakening while no surface damage is visible. The best way to detect damage is by non­destructive testing (NOT) however, this is expensive and time consuming. An in-service technique frequently employed is the tap test which can detect the presence of many defects in composite materials. The person conducting the tap test simply taps the surface with a coin or small hammer and notes any change in acoustic response suggesting an area of damage. Obviously, this must be conducted in a quiet zone. 
Stress and Fatigue 


Attachment and Joining 
Aircraft materials are attached and joined in much the same way as materials in other industries. Bolts are strong, but heavy, and are not normally used in forming structures. However, they are commonly used to attach items to structures, or indeed to attach one piece of structure to another. 
Riveting is very common, where relatively soft metal rivets are 
0  I I I o : I I o : I o : I I I  I I : o : o : o : o : o I I I  'I I I I I I I I I I I I I I  

deformed to hold two pieces of skin together. Solid rivets as 
shown in the image have the domed surface already formed, and the flat surface deformed by direct pressure from a rivet hammer to close the joint. Blind rivets are used where the interior of the structure is not accessible -commonly called 'pop' rivets. 

One Three Five Continuous Bolt Solid Blind Bond Bonding is very common in modern aircraft construction, where an Rivets Rivets adhesive substance is applied to one or both surfaces to be joined. Bonding is very reliable nowadays with advanced adhesive technology and has the advantage of not leading to stress concentration. 
Screwing may be used on light items such as panelling. 
Pinning (like nailing) may be used for non-load bearing items such as cabin trim, but not for any critical structures. 



Stress and Fatigue 
Corrosion results from the fact that most metals will try to revert to their natural and more stable state. Although there are a number of reactions that can take place between metals and their environment, they may broadly be divided into two categories: oxidation and electrolytical. 
Oxidation, or dry corrosion, is the reaction between a metal and its environment without the intervention of an electrolyte. 
Electrolytical, or wet corrosion, requires an electrolyte which conducts electricity, such as impure water. A potential difference exists between dissimilar metals of two surfaces, or two areas of the same surface, the electrolyte provides the current path. One of the areas becomes anodic(+) and the other area becomes cathodic(-). The anodic area usually corrodes while the cathodic are has material added to it. 
If a structure is subjected to corrosion and fatigue then stress corrosion, or stress corrosion cracking can occur. In 1988 the Aloha Airlines Flight 243, a Boeing 737 suffered a failure in the fuselage due to metal fatigue exacerbated by crevice corrosion in an overlap joint. Sadly, a flight attendant was killed in the resulting explosive decompression, and her body was never found. Amazingly, there were no other fatalities in this accident. Another remarkable fact is that an embarking passenger noticed cracking near the forward doorway, but did not think to mention it to anyone.

Aircraft Configuration 
In this lesson we consider the structure of aircraft fuselages, wings, empennages, doors and windows. 
The structure of the conventional aeroplane can be broken down into three major sub-sections. The fuselage carries the crew, cargo and passengers the wings provide lift and the empennage (the fin and the tailplane) provide stabilisation. 
As aircraft design has advanced, these distinctions have become blurred in the search for efficiency. Most passenger aircraft rely on the fuselage shape to provide some of the lift, stabilising surfaces have been combined into V tails, and extreme designs such as the American 82 combine all the features into a flying wing. 
Where the functions of control surfaces are combined so are the names. Elevons are combination ailerons and elevators fitted to the outer wing, tailerons are the same things but fitted to the tail. Flaperons are a combination flap and aileron used mostly on light aircraft and fighters. 
Fuel is usually carried in the wing but is often carried in fuselage tanks, particularly on large aircraft and, occasionally, even in the fin.

Airframe Design 

ft+iifto 
Fuselage Design 
Early aircraft were built of a fabric covered wooden frame. Three or four long wooden struts called longerons ran the length of the fuselage. The longerons were held apart by compression struts or bracing struts forming individual 'bays' which were in turn cross braced with tie-bars or wire. This is also known as truss construction. The frame of a structure like this carries the entire structural load. The fabric skin is purely for aerodynamic efficiency. 
Later versions of the braced fuselage used steel rather than wood. The structure was bolted together in the early years rather than welded as welding reduced the strength of the joint. As lighter and more easily welded steel became available in the late 1930s welded structures became the norm. 

Airframe Design 
Fuselages may incorporate any or all of the following structural components, dependent upon the design. 
Frames, as the name implies, are components that form a framework for the attachment offurther components. In a fuselage, a frame is a cross sectional component, giving the shape and form. 
Square or rectangular cross sections are ideal for accommodating passenger and freight, unfortunately it would need to be very heavy to deal with the stresses of pressurisation. A circular cross section would be ideal for pressurised aircraft but it's not so good at accommodating passengers and freight, so most aircraft are a compromise and deviate from circular oval being the most common. The B747 incorporates the double bubble which relies on two circles, which may be of different radii, intersecting at a common point usually at a floor or deck. The floor takes tension holding the two bubbles together. The A380 features a triple bubble. The shape of the fuselage will be determined by the task for which the aircraft is being designed. In increasing order of suitability for pressurised cabins the shapes are rectangular, flat bottomed circle, double bubble, oval and circular, as shown opposite. 
A bulkhead (a term borrowed from shipbuilding) implies a cross sectional form like the frame, but usually solid. We shall meet forward and aft pressure bulkheads shortly when we discuss 




ft+it+lo 
11 
L_J ...................... . 
08······················ 0 0 
Stiffeners are components that provide extra rigidity or reduced flexibility wherever that is required. Doublers are like stiffeners. They act to carry loads at critical points by 'doubling' and sometimes 'trebling' the skin thickness at such points. Doubler and trebler plates reduce in size as their load-carrying function is no longer required, thus blending the load path and reducing unnecessary weight. 
Airframe Design 
Fuselage Structural Components continued 
Doubler plates can be seen clearly at the tail boom and fin junction on the helicopter in the image. 
Stringers and longerons are longitudinal components. They connect frames, ribs and bulkheads to form the shape ofthe fuselage. The terms have become almost interchangeable, though strictly a longeron runs the whole length of the fuselage. 
Skin usually refers to the outside covering of the aircraft, formed upon the framework described above. Stressed skin will be discussed shortly. 
Crossbeams fitted laterally across the fuselage carry floor loads. The floor panels may be sandwich panels, as already discussed. 
The last general component to be discussed before we look at various types of fuselage is the firewall. As the name implies, firewalls are designed to resist intense heat. They may take the form of a bulkhead between an engine and the rest of the aircraft, or they may take the form of a tunnel or channel to carry critical services such as flight control runs, fuel and hydraulic lines past areas of high fire risk, for example engine bays. 
Airframe Design 
ft.ii+lo 
Monocoque Fuselages 
Monocoque
A true monocoque structure relies on the shape of the outer skin ofthe aircraft for strength and can be recognised by the absence Skin 
of a load carrying internal structure. Some early wooden aircraft utilising plywood were described as being monocoque even though they had some internal frames. Later aircraft either use alloys of aluminium, magnesium and titanium or composite materials. 
The ideal cross section is circular and the shape is maintained by circular formers and longitudinal stringers. This stressed skin structure can only be built from light, strong and easily worked materials. 
The disadvantage of pure monocoque structures is that they 

depend too heavily on their shape for strength. Any damage or deformation weakens the structure and can eventually lead to failure. 
Airframe Design 

ft+H+lo 
Semi-Monocoque Fuselages 
Semimonocoque 
Semi-monocoque fuselages include some of the features of the frame structure with some of the features of a pure monocoque Skin 
structure. This results in a design where the skin only takes part of the load and allows fuselages to be other than circular in cross section. 
A semi-monocoque structure uses bulkheads and formers to support the load bearing skin and the longitudinal stringers are more robust to take some of the tensile and compression load. Frame structures like the cabins of light aircraft can be included in the design. 


Airframe Design 


ft+H+lo 
Machined Skins 
As the skin carries more of the load in monocoque and semi-monocoque structures it becomes necessary to move away from a simple uniform sheet skin, early semi-monocoque aircraft like the JU52 used corrugated skins for extra strength. In more modern aircraft large areas of skin are machined on the underside in complex patterns to carry the varying loads. For some very convoluted patterns chemical etching has replaced milling to form the internal skin profile. 
Machined inner skin 
Airframe Design 
Reinforced Shell Fuselages 
The final development of semi-monocoque structures is called a reinforced shell. The basic structure is stressed skin with skin shape being defined by frames, bulkheads and stringers but now reinforced with longerons. Where openings are made for windows and doors a frame is fitted around the hole which is bolted, riveted or welded to the frame. 

Bonded doubler 
Frame 



ft++++lo 

Where weak points such as window openings occur the structure around them is strengthened with additional straps called doublers. Doublers can be either riveted or bonded to the main structure. 
The floor panels are normally suspended on cross beams. 
Airframe Design 
Pressurisation Loads 
Modern transport aircraft have pressurised cabins to allow passengers and crew to breathe normally at high altitudes without having to wear breathing apparatus. The pressure inside the cabin is maintained at a higher pressure than outside. Doors and windows are designed to open inwards, so when the aircraft is pressurised they are forced into their apertures forming airtight seals. 
The loads caused by pressurisation can be split into axial or longitudinal loads and hoop or radial stresses. The fuselage ends are sealed by the forward and aft pressure bulkheads leaving the nose in front ofthe pilots and the tail cone unpressurised. The pressurisation loads are applied once each flight and it makes little difference whether the aircraft stays at height for one hour or ten. Because of this, the number of pressurisations, called the pressurisation cycles, are recorded along with the flight time. 
If an aircraft suffers a tail strike on take-off or landing apart from the obvious possibility of fuselage damage, the aft pressure bulkhead may also be damaged. 
ft+W+lo 


Pressurisation loads are one reason why it is not always straightforward to convert a long haul aircraft to short haul operations -the aircraft may end up with a significantly shorter life. 

Airframe Design 
The Wings 
The wings of a modern aircraft are of a cantilever design, that is to say they are self-supporting and do not require external bracing or wires. The structure is stressed skin with a rigid beam called a spar running the full span ofthe wing, many aircraft have two or three spars in the wing. The spar may be designed to run the full length of the wing, full span, or may be assembled in half span sections and bolted together. Wing spars can be girders or box section. 
One type of spar is called an 'I beam spar'. This has two horizontal members, called 'girders', joined by a vertical component, called 'the web'. Together, they form an I shape in cross section. 
The aerodynamic shape ofthe wing is maintained by ribs in the classic aerofoil shape which run fore and aft and stringers which run along the wing. Ribs correspond to the frames used to define the shape of a stressed skin fuselage. Cavities in the wing are designed to carry fuel, either in flexible tanks or sealed into the structure itself. 
The forces on the skin are transmitted by the ribs and stringers to the spars which ultimately take the load. This diagram shows a wing with three spars, ribs and stringers. 

Wing construction Rear spar

Front spar 

The skin between the spars has been corrugated to increase its strength and would have another layer of thin metal over the top. The spars and the stressed skin now form a box section called a torsion box that is very rigid and resistant to twisting. 
Main / centre spar 
Airframe Design 
Wing Loads 
The main loads on a wing are bending loads, incorporating tension and compression, both in flight and on the ground. In flight the weight of the aircraft is supported by the lift of the wings, on the ground by the landing gear. Although the normal loads tend to bend the wing upwards, on landing and in turbulence the wing flexes downwards so the spar has to take bending loads in both directions. The load is at a maximum at the wing root. 
The primary bending and shear loads are controlled by observing 'g' and loading limits, in particular the Maximum Zero Fuel Mass (MZFM). This manufacturer's limit ignores the effect of fuel load in the wings. It ensures that the value of maximum bending of the wing at the wing root is not exceeded at the designed maximum load factor ('g') of the aircraft. Although it is mainly concerned with the weight of the fuselage the MZFM is defined for convenience as the maximum permissible aircraft weight disregarding fuel. 
The forces on the spar are, however, affected by the amount of fuel in the wing. Some aircraft have special fuel management procedures which keep fuel in the outboard tanks to balance the lift and reduce the fatigue loads on the wing root. Wing loading may also be reduced by uprigging the ailerons, by causing them to produce less lift in their 'at rest' position.

Weight 

Twisting or torsional loads caused by shifts in the centre of gravity and centre of pressure and by control surface deflection are also present as are shear loads on the centre section of the main spar, particularly if half span spars are bolted to a centre box section. 
Airframe Design 
Aeroelastic Flutter 
Aeroelastic flutter is defined as 'an unstable, self-excited structural oscillation at a definite frequency where energy is extracted from the airstream by the motion of the structure'. Flutter results from the interaction between 3 forces 1. inertial forces, 2. elastic forces and 3. aerodynamic forces. Ifthe combination of these forces is wrong, then an oscillation occurs at the natural resonant frequency of the structure which is divergent in nature i.e. it gets stronger and stronger until the structure breaks. Flutter normally affects the wings or empennage but sometimes it affects the whole aircraft. 
Clearly, flutter must be designed out of aircraft and a part of the flight-testing process is to check that the aircraft is not prone to flutter when operated within limits. How can it be designed out? Answer: change one of the interacting forces. 

c-~(;-------~ 


Airframe Design 
Aeroelastic Flutter continued 
Inertial forces can be changed by altering either the mass of the structure or the distribution of mass within the structure: a heavy structure resonates at a lower frequency, conversely a light wing is susceptible to higher frequency oscillations. In aircraft, the positioning of wing mounted engines forward of the centre of pressure is key moving the mass forward along the chord line is a chord-wise countermeasure. Additionally, positioning the mass along the span i.e. how far outboard are the engines mounted is a span-wise countermeasure. Fuel distribution can als be used to position the mass. 
Fuel may therefore be retained in the outboard tanks not only to reduce the stress on the spar but also to reduce the onset of flutter. Note that on swept wing aircraft, moving fuel outboard also moves it backwards, in this case fuel is moved both spanwise and chordwise directions. In older aircraft fuel distribution was managed by the flight engineer in modern aircraft it tends to be automatic. 
In aircraft with either manual or power assisted flight controls an oscillation of the control surface can occur leading to wing flutter. Positioning the C of G of the control surface forward of the hinge prevents flutter mass balances are used for this purpose. The previously mentioned forward mounting of engine pods is, in effect, using the engine as a mass balance for the wing. 

Airframe Design 

Aeroelastic Flutter continued

Airframe Design 
When operating a multi-engine aircraft an engine failure imposes a new combination of loads on the aircraft. The thrust from the remaining engine, now offset from the centreline of the aircraft, creates a moment arm which yaws the aircraft towards the dead engine. Not only is the thrust offset but the failed engine produces drag which is offset from the centreline. The secondary effect of yaw is to roll the aircraft towards the dead engine. On propeller driven aircraft the failed engine results in lower airflow over that wing which means a loss of lift on that side which adds to the roll. If these forces are not handled correctly the aircraft will rapidly spiral out of control. 
The pilot counteracts the yaw by applying opposite rudder which adds to the drag. If the pilot applies aileron to combat the roll that too adds to the drag. The aircraft now sideslips which further adds to the drag. You might understand that with the increase in drag available thrust becomes an issue: for most light twins it becomes a serious issue. 
If the aircraft is flying slowly the rudder may not create enough force to overcome the yaw so you will find you have a minimum speed at which you can maintain directional control. In this situation it would be easy for the pilot to apply too much rudder leading to fin stall which is likely to be catastrophic. 
A more detailed description of asymmetric flight can be found in the Principles of Flight Manual. 


Wings may be mounted under the fuselage (low), through the fuselage (mid) or above the fuselage (high). 
Airliners generally employ a low wing mounting. This works well for jet engine aircraft and keeps the fuselage fairly close to the ground meaning the undercarriage legs are short saving weight. The whole fuselage can be used for passengers or freight. 
High wing mounting is more frequently associated with propeller driven aircraft as it provides ground clearance for long propeller blades. It is also favoured by cargo carrying aircraft as it allows the fuselage to be very close to the ground allowing vehicles to be driven into the aircraft via a ramp at the back of the aircraft. 
Mid mounted wings are normally associated with fast jets where the fuselage is not needed for passengers or freight. This structurally stronger method helps with high 'g' manoeuvres. 
Airframe Design 
Empennages 
An empennage serves two functions. It provides directional stability and control in yaw, and longitudinal stability and control in pitch. Directional stability is provided by the fin, and pitch stability is provided by the horizontal stabiliser. 
Traditionally an empennage is split into a vertical surface, the fin, with the rudder attached and a horizontal stabilizer or tailplane with the elevators attached. 
Empennages are constructed in the same way and carry the same type of loads as the wing structure. 
Tailplane position is affected by the stall characteristics of the aircraft and the desire to keep the tailplane away from the wing vortices at low speeds. A low set tailplane has the best stall characteristics because the tailplane moves into undisturbed air as the aircraft pitch increases in the stall. It is most commonly found on fighter aircraft with fuselage mounted engines: however, it is rarely seen on commercial aircraft where wing mounted engines would leave the tailplane in the jet efflux. For this reason, commercial aircraft generally employ mid mounted tailplanes. The disadvantage of mid mounted tailplanes is that they are under the influence of downwash from the wings cruciform mounting, placing the tailplane a little higher, can overcome the downwash issue. 

High mounted tail plane is kept clear of turbulence of the wing 

a 

'T' tail reduces vortices at tip of fin, increasing efficiency of the fin 



Airframe Design 

a 
Empennages continued 
T-tails have the advantage of presenting undisturbed air to the tailplane they also avoid the efflux of rear fuselage mounted engines. However, when coupled with a wing that has a pitch-up stall characteristic it can lead to a phenomenon known as the deep or super stall from which there is no recovery. AT-tail requires a stronger fin to hold the tailplane so it is structurally heavy however, the plate effect of the tailplane renders the fin more efficient so it can be smaller. 
V tails combine the functions the fin and horizontal stabiliser.

Airframe Design 

Doors 
Aircraft doors and hatches form a vital part of the fuselage construction. In pressurised aircraft they often take the form of a wedge-shaped 'plug' door, where the door opens internally. The advantage of the plug door is that it will then be largely held in the closed position by the pressurisation in the cabin, therefore requiring less robust and heavy locking mechanisms. 
Complex hinge geometry means that many aircraft, most notably Boeing, have plug doors that open inwards initially, and then swing back through the aperture to open outwards, making more space available internally on the aircraft, and facilitating quicker evacuation than a wholly inward-opening door. 
Non-plug type doors rely upon strong locking mechanisms to keep them in place. Airbus cabin doors are of the non-plug type. Non­plug doors are also used where there is no space to open them internally, such as cargo holds. Once closed, they are secured by either sliding pins on the sides, 'dog' lugs on the opposite side to the hinges, or indeed a combination of pins and dogs. Six dog lugs are visible on the lower surface of the cargo door in the image. 
Some aircraft have doors that slide upwards on rails. They may operate electrically or the weight of the door may be taken by a combination of springs and counterbalances. 


Airf rame Design 
General Aviation Aircraft Doors 
Smaller regional or business aircraft often incorporate the boarding steps or 'air stairs' into the door. Whilst there is a weight penalty to this method, the benefits of being independent of ground support equipment make this type of door very popular. The type shown in this image is a typical design. 
Non-pressurised aircraft generally have doors of much lighter construction, and are usually secured by mechanical latches. They are most often hinged on the forward edge, so that an insecure door in flight is a much lesser hazard than it otherwise might be. 


Airframe Design 
fuselage construction. They need to withstand not only aerodynamic forces, but can also survive the impact of a 4 lb (1.8 kg) birdstrike at cruise speed. To achieve the strength and resilience required the front windows are of laminar construction. In pressurised transport aircraft the flight deck windows are automatically heated, typically to around 35°C. This is done not only for anti-icing and anti-misting purposes, but to ensure that the windows remain malleable rather than brittle. 
Heating elements are built up in the laminations of the flight deck window. Typically, the sandwich would consist of a tough glass outer layer, a film oftransparent heating element, then further laminations of glass or polycarbonate with vinyl layers between them. The front windows are usually well over an inch (25 mm) thick. 
There is a requirement laid down in the Certification Specifications (CS 25.773) for a 'direct vision' window. Specifically, it states that there must be 'a clear portion ofthe windshield during precipitation conditions, sufficient for both pilots to have a sufficiently extensive view along the flight path in normal flight attitudes'. It goes on to specify that 'the first pilot must have a window that is openable ... when the cabin is not pressurised, provides the view specified (above), and gives sufficient protection from the elements against impairment ofthe pilot's vision'.

These specifications would assist in taxiing if the windshield were to be obscured, for example from a birdstrike, or indeed from hailstorm damage. The pilots of the Delta Airlines Airbus A320 shown here would certainly have been grateful for an opening side window after landing at Denver with this severe hail damage in August 2015. 
Cabin windows are normally made from acrylic or polycarbonate material, and in two or more layers for pressurised aircraft.

Lesson 3 
Principles of Hydraulics 

AGK 03 
Principles of Hyd raul ics 
ftiikf-lo 
Introduction 
Hydraulic systems use fluid in a pipe to transmit and multiply force or to transmit power. In aircraft, hydraulic systems are used as servos so the pilot can operate controls and systems remotely and at loads far beyond their bare physical capacity. These will include operation of the flying controls, raising and lowering the undercarriage and operation of the wheel brakes. 
Except in the braking systems of light aircraft the hydraulic system will be a powered system. A pump will provide the operating power and the pilot's function will be to control the system. A powered system is correctly called an active system, and those simple hydraulic systems without a pump are called passive systems. 
In a modern transport aircraft hydraulic power is essential for safe efficient operation, so aircraft are fitted with multiple systems to provide system redundancy. 


Hydraulic systems are used to transmit and increase forces 
Principles of Hydraulics 
Static or hydrostatic pressure is a form of potential energy. Water in a reservoir on a hill has potential energy because of its height. 
The shape of the container does not matter a column of fluid will exert a static pressure at its base. This pressure will be the same regardless of the shape of the container, providing the height of the top of the fluid remains the same. If the outlet at the bottom is opened, this energy can be released in a fluid flow, which has dynamic energy. The hydraulic power generated depends on the pressure and the volume of the flow. 
Hydraulic pressures in aircraft systems range from low pressure at 1000 pounds per square inch (psi), to very high pressure at 5000 psi. Standard high-pressure systems operate at 3000 psi, and standard low pressure systems operate at 2000 psi. More recent aircraft such as the Airbus A380 and Boeing 787 are much larger and heavier than their predecessors, so logically need more hydraulic services. Both the A380 and the B787 operate at around 5000 psi. 
High pressure high systems can transmit more power without the need for larger components thereby saving weight. Disadvantages are that leaks are more prone to spraying, and the rate of leak is faster. Both represent a greater fire risk. Additionally, a high pressure leak presents a greater hazard to personnel. 
ft.iiflo 

The shape of the container does not matter

Pascal's Law 

Pascal's law states that, in an enclosed container, any change in pressure is experienced throughout the fluid and pressure acts at l right angles to the container walls. 

Principles of Hydraulics 

Force, Area and Pressure 
Force acts either as an input or an output. Pressure is the energy 
raised in the fluid, and by acting on an area produces a force. This is normally expressed as: F=AxP but by transposing the formula: A=F+P we can find each part P=F + A 
While S.I. units are used on occasions, the use of the Imperial system is more common. So: 
Force is expressed in pounds (lb) 
Area in square inches (sq in or in2) 


Pressure is pounds per square inch (psi) 
This means an input force of 100 lb applied to an area of 10 sq in will raise a pressure of 10 psi. 
Obviously ifwe use two pistons of equal size interconnected by a pipe, an input force at one end will produce an equal output force. To gain the mechanical advantage that levers and pulley blocks 
Force is increased by using a small input piston and a large output 
would give in hydraulics we use a small input piston and a larger

Principles of Hydraulics 
Force, Area and Pressure continued 
In the example an input force of 20 lb acting on a piston of 2 sq in area will raise a pressure of 10 psi. This will be felt equally throughout the fluid in the enclosing container and acts at right angles to the container walls, so as the fluid acts upon the larger piston of 20 sq in area this will give an output force of 200 lb. Ten times the area means ten times the force. 
There will, however, be no piston movement unless there is a flow of fluid. In this case the small piston being pushed in will displace a volume equal to its area multiplied by the distance it moved -its stroke. The fluid is transferred into the larger cylinder where it will spread out, so the larger piston will not move the same distance. 
Although imperial units have been used in the examples above, use what ever units of pressure, distance, area and force are provided in the questions. 
You could be asked to perform calculations using, area, stroke, force or pressure. Which formula you use will depend on what information you are given and what you are asked to find.

This concept only works if the hydraulic fluid is a perfect fluid, and incompressible. In reality, there is no such thing as a perfect fluid and we have to accept a little compressibility. For our purposes a fluid is considered incompressible so long as it will only compress by 10% of its volume when a force of 32 tons per square inch is applied. This is about 70 000 psi, well above the maximum operating pressure of aircraft systems. 

Principles of Hydraulics 
Hydraulic Fluids 
At this point we should look at the requirements for the fluids in use. Hydraulic fluids are subject to extremes of temperature and pressure and must meet stringent specifications. Ideally a hydraulic fluid should have these properties: 
Thermal Stability 
Hydraulic fluid must be able to cope with a wide temperature range say from -50°C to close to 100°C. In other words it must have a low freezing point and high boiling point. 
Resistance to Corrosion 
Hydraulic oil is in constant contact with metal parts manufactured to very high tolerances any pitting caused by corrosion would render the components unserviceable. 
High Flashpoint and Low Flammability 
The concern here is fire hazard in the case of a hydraulic leak. For example, a leak in the engine cowling or onto hot brakes could lead to a fire. 

ftjf=f 

Low Volatility 
Hydraulic oil is subject to rapidly varying pressures, it is vital that it remains in liquid form and does not show tendencies to vaporise. 
Low Viscosity 
Hydraulic fluid needs to flow easily and rapidly through narrow pipes and orifices therefore it must be of low viscosity throughout its temperature range. 
Incompressible 
It is vital that hydraulic fluid is incompressible, as any sponginess would lead to inaccurate responses particularly so of the flight controls. At the pressures used in aircraft the oil is considered incompressible. 
The two types offluid in common use are: 
¦ Mineral based 
• Synthetic based 
Principles of Hydraulics 
Mineral Based Fluid -DTD 585 
Mineral based fluid is made from kerosene and is coloured red for identification. This fluid must be used with synthetic rubber seals and hoses only. It is suitable for use in high-pressure systems. Because it has a kerosene base it is flammable, but has high boiling and flash point temperatures, it is chemically stable to a high temperature and has good resistance to foaming 
DTD 585 is red and can only be used with synthetic seals 


Synthetic Base -Skydrol 
Modern transport aircraft use synthetic based fluid. The base is phosphate ester and the fluid is generically known as Skydrol. It comes in three grades the common grade is 500A. Skydrol 500A is a high quality fluid which is coloured purple. These fluids are fire resistant and do not support combustion. The lower viscosity of Skydrol means that there is a lower probability of cavitation. These fluids must be used with butyl rubber, ethylene propylene or Teflon seals and hoses. Skydrol meets all the requirements for aircraft hydraulic fluids used in modern aircraft. 
ft.Mflo 

The different types of hydraulic fluid must not be used together as mineral and phosphate based fluids will not mix. Seals may also be damaged if the wrong fluid is used. It should also be appreciated that the colours specified for the fluids are for when the fluid is new. In use the colour ofthe fluids darken and may eventually all appear the same colour. Very old hydraulic systems using leather seals work satisfactorily with all the fluids listed above. 
Skydrol 500A is a purple high quality fluid used in modern aircraft 


Health and Handling of Hydraulic Fluids 
Hydraulic fluids used as recommended do not present any particular health hazards but are an irritant if they come in contact with the skin and eyes. First aid treatment is to wash off with large volumes ofwater. 
Principles of Hydraulics 
Advantages of hydraulic systems 
Force multiplication -the pilot's physical inputs are a small force on the toe brakes but the force applied to the brakes can be large. 
Power -the pilot's power input is small, say for the controls but the power output to the controls is large. The operation of undercarriage, large and heavy, only requires the operation of a switch/lever. 
Accuracy -the pilot's input can be replicated accurately. 
Efficiency -hydraulics are very efficient they lose very little energy. 
Easier to install than a mechanical system of rods, levers and pulleys. 
Power can be attained through multiple sources, say a pump on each engine. 


Disadvantages 
Can leak, in which case you have no system, so redundancy is essential. 
Leaking oil can damage some materials. For example, synthetic oils are good with butyl and ethylene but damage vinyl, rubber and some paint finishes.

Principles of Hydraulics 

Components of an Active (Powered) Hydraulic System 
The simplest of active hydraulic systems will, by definition, need a pump as a source of power. It will need something to control where the power goes, a selector valve, non return valves to ensure flow in the correct direction and something to convert the 
fluid energy into rotary or linear motion, an actuator or jack. 
More complex systems will need a reservoir offluid, and an accumulator to act as a store of energy for emergencies and to smooth out the power delivery. These components must be fitted in the correct order for the hydraulic system to operate correctly. This animation shows the layout of a simple system. 


The simplest system has a pump, a selector and an actuator

A modern aircraft hydraulic system is designed to produce high pressure and large flow rates. Hydraulic power can be used for operation of:

These components will now be introduced as we work through typical hydraulic systems. 


Principles of Hydraulics 
Pumps 
The main hydraulic pumps are usually driven from the accessory gearbox of the engine and therefore known as Engine Driven Pumps (EDPs). To protect the engine or prime mover that drives the pump, a weak link is provided that is designed to shear in the event of mechanical failure. If the pump should fail mechanically, there should be no damage to the engine -though you will of course lose that pump. The weak link is variously known as a 'shear neck' or a 'quill drive'. 
The pumps supply the flow to hydraulic fluid, and resistance to that flow produces pressure in the system. Spur gear and rotor type pumps are generally used in lower pressure systems of light aircraft, piston pumps are used in larger aircraft. As there are two types of piston pumps they need to be considered in context and we will look at them later. This animation shows a spur gear pump. 
Electrically driven pumps are used as backup pumps on big aircraft and, sometimes, as the main pumps on smaller aircraft. Air Driven Pumps (ADPs) feature as backup pumps on many large aircraft including the B747. Hand operated pumps are often fitted for use in ground operation, for maintenance and for emergency use when airborne. 



Pumps are usually engine driven 
Spur gear pumps force fluid between the gear teeth and the housing

Double Acting Balanced Actuators 
A double acting actuator uses hydraulic pressure to move it in both directions. A double acting balanced or compensated actuator has ram rods of equal area on both sides of the piston, so for a given pressure the actuator will give equal force for extension and retraction. These actuators are commonly used for flight controls where the same response is desirable in both directions.

Principles of Hydraulics 
Rotary Actuators (Hydraulic Motors) 
If rotary rather than linear motion is required for a system it can be achieved by directing hydraulic pressure to a hydraulic motor. The speed of the motor is dependant on the rate of flow of the fluid. A selector valve can be used to change the direction of the flow and so change the direction of the motor. 
In addition to driving flap and undercarriage systems hydraulic motors are sometimes also used to drive back up electrical generators. 
Principles of Hydraulics 
Hydraulic Seals 
Hydraulic components have to be sealed to prevent both internal and external leakage. Seals that are fitted between moving surfaces are termed dynamic, those between two fixed parts are termed static, and as shown below, seals get their names from their cross section. 
Friction in dynamic seals can result in the seal 'rolling out'. To prevent this happening backing rings are fitted for support. 
Leaks 
An external leak will result in a loss of system pressure, loss of fluid, a rise in the temperature of the remaining fluid and cause a possible fire risk. An internal leak, from high pressure to low, causes a drop in system pressure, more activity of pressure regulating or cut-out valves and a rise in system temperature.

Principles of Hydraulics 

Selector Valves 
Selector valves direct the hydraulic fluid to and from the actuators to control the direction of their movement. The valves can be either be of the rotary or spool type. They can be operated 
ReNrYOII" 
mechanically or electrically. 
Rotary Selectors 
A four port rotary selector has two fluid paths and is used with a double acting actuator. A four port selector allows one path for fluid flow into the actuator and the other path simultaneously makes the return flow to the reservoir. 
A two port rotary selector has one fluid path and is used with a single acting actuator. 


A four port selector is used with a double acting actuator 
Principles of Hydraulics 
Spool Valves 

Spool valves are also known as pilot valves. The valve is cylindrical in shape and forms a sliding but leak-tight fit in its housing. The spool valve consists of 'lands' and 'grooves'. The Spool 
valve --~ 
lands block oil flow through the valve body. The grooves allow oil or gas to flow around the spool and through the valve body. Movement of the valve allows fluid to flow or blocks it off by the lands of the valve. 
Principles of Hydraulics 
Hydraulic Lock 
If the selector is placed so no fluid can flow, neither in nor out of the actuator, then the actuator piston will be locked in position, unable to move. This is called a hydraulic lock. Hydraulic locks are used deliberately to lock actuators in position. 
Principles of Hydraulics 

Open Centred Systems 
An open centred system is a low pressure system fitted on older light aircraft. It is used to operate flaps and undercarriage. Only one actuator can be operated at a time. 
RuefVOir 
The system uses a spur gear type of engine driven pump (EDP). The fluid is pumped continuously around the system through the centre ofthe open centred selectors. The pressure will be low until a selection is made. This reduces the load on the pump, which reduces the engine power required, while the system is not operating. 
Selecting the service will divert all the pump's output into the selected actuator. When the pressure reaches a set level the selector automatically returns to the open position, hydraulically locking the actuator. To protect the system should the selector fail to return to the open position a Pressure Relief Valve ("PRV" -see 


the next lesson) is fitted. 
Only one actuator can be operated at a time When off load the fluid passes through the centre of the selectors 




Lesson 4 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 

AGK04 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
Valves 
Pressure and Thermal Relief Valve 
Pressure relief valves (PRV) are used to protect hydraulic systems from over pressurisation in the event of failure of the normal pressure control of the pumps. A common design, consisting of of a ball valve held onto a valve seat by spring pressure, is shown here. The area of the ball exposed to the fluid is very small, thus the spring's force is relatively low. 
At normal working pressure the valve remains firmly seated. A rise in pressure will unseat the ball, allowing the fluid to return to the reservoir. The pressure at which the ball first lifts is called the cracking pressure. Any further increase in pressure will lift the ball higher, allowing more fluid to return. If the valve can relieve the total output of the pump it is called a full flow relief valve (FFRV). 
ft+IF·Ho 


Pump limits pressure (3000 PSI) 
Relief pressure reached (3300 PSI) 
Pressure 
The valve opens at the cracking pressure 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
ft.ifid o 

Pressure and Thermal Relief Valve 
As the pressure drops the valve will start to close. The pressure at which the valve seats is called the re-seating pressure, this is always below the cracking pressure. 
The pressure relief valve can also act as a thermal relief valve (TRV), tapping off system pressure caused by thermal expansion. This condition may occur when the aircraft is parked in a warm place. A hydraulic lock is formed and there is very little fluid flow. When it is used as a TRV it typically operates at higher pressures than it would when used as a PRV. 
Pressure 
The valve closes at a lower pressure, the re-seating pressure 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
ft+itido 


Non Return Valves (NRVs) 
Non return valves only allow fluid flow in one direction they are also known as check valves. 
The common design for an NRV is a ball valve held onto a seat by the force of a spring. Fluid pressure in one direction 'unseats' the ball, allowing flow, when the pressure falls and flow stops the ball will reseat under spring pressure. Pressure in the opposite direction will seal the ball more firmly onto its seating. To guard against incorrect installation of an NRV, an arrow is stamped or moulded into it showing the direction of flow. 
Non-return valve 
Non return valves only allow fluid flow in one direction 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 

Shuttle Valves  
A shuttle valve is used to allow one service to be operated by two  
independent supplies at different times for instance a normal and  
an emergency supply. If the primary supply fails, the secondary  
supply will push the shuttle across automatically, both connecting  
the service to the most appropriate pressure and isolating the  
primary port.  
Shuttle valves are fitted in the supply lines of critical services like  
primary flying controls or wheel brakes. A sticking shuttle valve  
can prevent a service operating from the emergency supply.  
Shuttle valves allow an actuator to be supplied by a secondary  
system  

Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
ft.ifid o 
Restrictor Valves or Chokes 
Restrictors valves or chokes are used to reduce the flow offluid in order to reduce the speed of operation of a services that would otherwise move too quickly. 
A one way restrictor opens to allow full flow in one direction but closes to reduce the flow in the return direction. To show the direction of full flow a bold arrow is stamped on the case, with an 
Full 
arrow made of dots showing the restricted flow. 

Flow 

One way restrictors slow services that would otherwise move too quickly 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 

n +e+•o 
Two Way Restrictor Valves 
A two way restrictor slows the flow of fluid in both directions. This would be used, for example, to reduce the flow to a nose gear that requires less force to operate than the main gear. 

Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
ft.HU o 
Throttling Valves 
Throttling valves are a more sophisticated form of restrictors. 
They ensure that the fluid flow rate to and from a component, and therefore the speed of operation, is correct. As the supply flow rate increases the valve closes down throttling, or reducing, the output. 


Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
ft++·+•­
Flow Control Valves 
Flow control valves are used upstream of hydraulic motors to ensure an even flow rate to maintain constant speed. They react to fluid flow, increased flow closes the valve down. A damper slows the closing action and prevents damage.

Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
Sequence Valves 
Sequence valves are used to ensure that one operation is completed before another one starts, for example ensuring that the landing gear doors open before the landing gear extends. 
Hydraulic sequence valves work by directing system fluid into one service, ensuring its complete operation before the fluid can enter the second service. 
In the animation the door actuator operates first then, when it reaches the end of its stroke, the pressure builds up and moves the sequence valve across allowing fluid into the gear down line. Mechanical sequence valves may also be used. On retraction this will prevent the door from closing before the gear is up. The reverse is true for gear extension. The animation shows this very clearly, and in both directions. 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
ft.iiiiWo 
Hydraulic Fuses 
Hydraulic fuses are fitted upstream of components to shut off flow and prevent total loss of system fluid if there is a leak. 
The fuse is designed to sense flow rate if it increases above the normal rate the piston will move across to stop the flow completely. The service will be inoperative but the rest of the system will function as normal. 


Hydraulic fuses are fitted upstream of components to shut off flow if there is a leak 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
Modulators 
This component is only used in the older hydro-mechanical anti­skid systems (Maxarets). It is fitted between the brake control valve and the single acting pistons of the brake unit and provides fine control of brake pressure in the maxarets. 
With the brakes off, the spring holds the piston across to the left. When the brakes are applied the piston moves to the right pushing the fluid into the cylinders, applying the brakes. The swept volume of the modulator is equal to the brake cylinders' swept volume. 
If the Maxaret detects a skid it operates an anti-skid valve which releases a small amount of fluid upstream of the modulator. This results in a pressure drop in the brake supply line. To equalise the pressure both sides of the modulator piston a small amount of fluid passes through the orifice, which in turn reduces the force applied to the brake. As the pressure is re-applied only a small amount of fluid passes back through the orifice. This prevents the sub system using a large amount of fluid yet it is still able to control the force applied to the brakes. On releasing the brakes completely, the spring returns the piston to the original position. 
Bra • To 
prusure bra 
0 Cl 
Modulators provide fine control of brake pressure in maxarets 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
Priority Valve or Pressure Maintaining Valve 
The priority of a hydraulic system is to supply pressure (flow) to the primary flying controls and wheel brakes. The Priority Valve (PMV) achieves this by closing and isolating non-essential services. If the pressure drops below a set limit, any remaining pressure will then go to these primary services. 
....------Primary StlVlc.s 
The priority valve isolates non-essential services if pressure falls 
Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
Fluid Temperature 
During operation ofthe hydraulic system the fluid temperature increases. To keep the fluid at its correct operating temperature, hot fluid returning to the reservoir is passed through a heat exchanger. This can be a ram air unit however it is more normal to mount the heat exchanger in the fuel tank under the unusable fuel. This cools the fluid and at the same time heats the fuel preventing waxing and freezing. 
The system's fluid temperature is displayed on the flight deck overheat indicators are installed at the pumps. Over heated fluid will discolour by darkening and becoming more viscous. 
Filtration and Hydraulic Circuit Protection 
Hydraulic systems need to be kept free of any contamination. The hydraulic fluid is filtered to remove any debris greater than 25 microns that could cause damage to the system. As the pump is a likely source of contamination it is standard procedure to fit a filter downstream of the pump, which is referred to as a full flow micronic pressure filter (FFMPF). Some systems fit a filter unit into the return line before the reservoir as well as or instead ofthe FFMPF. 

-Bypass relief valve In Out 
--Filter element Filter bowl 

A micronic filter is fitted downstream of the pump The filter light indicates the filter is becoming blocked 

Hydraulic Valves and Actuators 
Filtration and Hydraulic Circuit Protection continued 
The filter consists of a bowl, head and element. Fluid enters the 
In
head and flows into the bowl around the outside of the element. As it passes through the element any particles of more than 25 microns are deposited on the filter, the fluid then flows up and out through the head. As the debris builds up on the element (known as clogging) a differential pressure will occur across the element. This actuates a filter light on the flight deck, warning that the element is in the process of becoming blocked.

On some filters a red button will show when the bypass has activated, this is referred to as 'filter popping'. It should be noted that not all filters are of the full flow type operation ofthe indicator button warns of impending operation of the by-pass on some filters.

Lesson 5 Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

AGKOS 

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
ft+IHhlo 
Fixed Volume or Constant Displacement Pumps 
Fixed volume pumps are high-pressure engine driven pumps (EDP) and move a constant volume of fluid into the system for each revolution output is therefore a function of rpm. To regulate the system pressure and reduce wear on the pump an automatic cut out valve (ACOV), a non-return valve, and its associated accumulator must be used. 
Fixed volume pumps are sometimes referred to as constant displacement or axial piston pumps and are driven by the engine via the ancillary gearbox and the Constant Speed Unit at a fixed speed. The reciprocating action of the pistons is achieved by angling the body of the pump. There are normally 7 or 9 pistons in the pump, each piston moves in its own cylinder in the rotating cylinder block. The output from the pump does not vary. 


The output from the pump does not vary 
Constant displacement pumps must be used with an ACOV, a non­return valve, and an accumulator 

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
ft.ii+lo 
Fixed Volume or Constant Displacement Pumps continued 
Fluid is normally supplied to the pump under a slight pressure to avoid cavitation. It passes in and out through two kidney shaped ports in the valve plate. The pistons move down their cylinders as they pass the inlet valve, drawing in fluid, and move back up their cylinders to expel the fluid as they pass the outlet valve. This process ensures a constant output volume. 
To cool and lubricate the moving parts in the pump a leakage of system fluid is allowed. This fluid exits through the case drain and returns to the reservoir via a case drain filter or the low-pressure filter. An overheat sensor is fitted at the pump and the pump outlet pressure is monitored and displayed to the pilot. 


Cooling and lubrication is by fluid leakage 

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
ft+it+lo 
Automatic Cut-Out Valve 
The automatic cut-out valve (ACOV) is used to regulate the pressure in the system between a high setting and a low setting when a constant volume pump is used to power the hydraulic system. When system pressure builds up it is felt on the piston and the poppet valve. As the piston is larger than the poppet valve the force produced in the piston will overcome the combined forces of the spring and the poppet valve when the upper pressure is achieved. The opening of the poppet valve allows the fluid to flow to the reservoir. The resulting drop in pressure causes the NRV to close, locking the pressure in the system and the poppet valve to open fully. This now provides an idling circuit for the pumps total output and the ACOV is said to be 'kicked out'. 
Operation of any service will cause a drop in the system pressure, when it falls to the value of the lower setting the spring snaps the poppet valve closed and the pump output is directed back into the system. This is referred to as the 'kick in' pressure. 
If this form of pressure regulation fails then a FFRV will control the system at a higher setting. To prevent the ACOV cutting in and out rapidly, which can be referred to as ACOV or hydraulic hammering, an accumulator and a non-return valve are always used in conjunction with the ACOV. 
Idling flow--• 


The poppet valve opens to create an idling circuit and the ACOV is 'kicked out' The 'kick in' pressure is lower than the 'kick out' pressure A leak can cause hydraulic hammering

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
ft.ii+lo 
Accumulators continued 
With the hydraulic system de-pressurised, or slack, a pre-charge of nitrogen is put into the gas chamber ofthe accumulator. This gas pressure must be equal to the minimum working pressure of the system: usually approximately half the maximum working pressure. As hydraulic fluid is pumped into the system raising pressure, the accumulator will charge with fluid, compressing the gas until the fluid pressure and gas pressure are equal. Any demand on the system will result in a drop in the system pressure, so the gas expels the fluid into the system and maintains the system at a slightly lower operating pressure -this ensures smooth operation of components and services. 
Accumulators are fitted into hydraulic sub systems, like the brake system. The accumulator acts as a limited pressure reservoir in emergency and dampens pressure fluctuations. 
If the EDP fails then the NRV upstream of the accumulator will close and the accumulator will provide a limited number of brake operations before exhausting. 
The gas in the accumulator, which is compressible where the hydraulic fluid is not, will dampen out pressure fluctuations in the system. 

The accumulator will cope with small internal leakages by maintaining the system pressure and will absorb any increase in fluid pressure created by an increase of fluid temperature downstream of a NRV. 
An accumulator must be used in conjunction with an ACOV. If the gas charge of an accumulator is incorrect, either too high or too low, this will cause the ACOV to operate more frequently. This can lead to hydraulic hammering, which must be investigated as soon as possible. Another effect is a rise in the fluid temperature. 
Most modern aircraft have a pressure gauge fitted in the gas charging line this gauge will show the pre-charge pressure when the system is slack and the fluid pressure when the system is operating. 
The accumulator acts as a limited pressure reservoir and dampens pressure fluctuations 
Too high or too low charge pressure will cause hydraulic hammering 

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Variable Volume or Constant Pressure Pumps 
In systems using a variable volume pump the ACOV and its associated accumulator are not required, although system accumulators may be installed for other reasons. 
A variable volume pump operates in a similar way to a fixed stroke pump except its output is not dependant on pump speed as the volume of the pistons can be altered. This means the pump self regulates and only supplies the fluid needed to maintain the constant working pressure within the system. 
Variable volume pumps are driven by the engine via the ancillary gearbox. Unlike fixed volume pumps, the drive and cylinder block are directly in line.
