Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 



Variable Volume or Constant Pressure Pumps continued Cylinder 
A movable 'swash plate' controls the piston travel. Altering the swash plate's datum will alter the distance of the piston pumping action. At maximum deflection or maximum stroke the pistons will move the most fluid, at neutral the pistons will not pump any fluid as they do not move in or out. This provides an idle condition for the pump. 
The swash plate is connected to a control piston or hanger piston. This has two forces acting on it, spring pressure to move towards maximum deflection or 'on stroke' and system pressure to move towards neutral or 'off stroke'. 
On start up of the engine the pump will be at maximum stroke, this 
Pressure Case drain 
Control 

will cause the pump to try and pressurise the whole system on output 
piston 
starter motor power. To offload the starter motor a blocking valve, described below, is used. 

The pump is lubricated and cooled again by internal fluid leakage The pump delivers most fluid at maximum stroke to the case drain. A overheat sensor is fitted at the pump and the On start up the pump will be at maximum stroke pump outlet pressure is monitored and displayed to the pilot. 
Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
ft++++fo 
Blocking Valves 
A blocking valve is used to keep starting loads on the engine to a minimum by setting a variable output EDP to zero output. The blocking valve is closed by a spring and opened by system pressure. 
To offload the pump on start, the pilot selects "depressurise" on the hydraulics panel. This energises the depressurising or blocking solenoid, moving the valve down so the pump's output acts on both sides of the blocking valve and it remains shut. At the same time the output acts on the compensator which boosts the pressure to the control piston, moving the swash plate to the neutral position and offloading the pump. 
After the engine has reached idling RPM, the pilot selects "pressurise" which de-energises the solenoid and allows the depressurising valve to return to its original position. The fluid under the blocking valve drains away so the blocking valve opens and the pump goes to maximum deflection. 
In an engine fire situation, actuation of the fire handle operates the de-pressurising solenoid and the fluid supply valves are operated to isolate the EDPs. Note that both valves use 28 volts DC. 


Fire switch EDP control switch 
The blocking valve off loads the pump during engine start

Back Up Hydraulic Power Hydraulic Power Transfer 
Power transfer
On a modern transport aircraft with more than one hydraulic 
shaft 
system, back up power to a system in the event of failure ofthe main pump can be obtained by use of hydraulic motor in one system to operate a hydraulic pump in another system. These linked units are referred to as power transfer units or PTUs. A flow of fluid enters the motor, which acts in reverse to a pump, pushing the pistons down and creating rotary motion. This in turn drives the pump. Fluid does not transfer from one system to the other, only power. PTUs are not designed to be used when the hydraulic 
Hydraulic
system has a fault, such as overheat, or leak. On some aircraft, 
motor 
A320 for example, the PTU is reversible, it is known as a bi­directional PTU or reversible motor pump. 
PTUs are common on large aircraft. They transfer power not fluid

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
Back Up Hydraulic Power continued  
Air Turbine Motors  
Pneumatic (air) systems can be used to operate a back up hydraulic pump. Air is bled from the compressors of a gas turbine engine and directed onto a Air Turbine Motor (ATMs) which is then  Power transfer shaft  
in turn drives a hydraulic pump. These are used as a backup  
source of hydraulic power on older aircraft, notably the L 1011 Tri Star and the 8747.  
On the 8747, the air driven pump is called a demand pump.  
When switched to AUTO it activates to support the engine driven pump in times of high demand, say when operating the landing gear, flap and flight controls at the same time. If it failed to activate there is a risk of sluggish response from the flight controls.  Hydraulic motor  Hydraulic pump  

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
AC Pumps  
Back up hydraulic pumps can also be driven by electric motors.  
The output from these pumps is considerably less than would be  
obtained from the main Engine Driven Pumps. As with the ATM  
pumps, AC pumps may also be used as demand pumps.  
Ram Air Turbines  
If all the normal sources of hydraulic power fail, some aircraft will  
be fitted with a Ram Air Turbine (RAT). The RAT provides  
emergency hydraulic power for flight controls in the event of loss  
of engine driven hydraulic power.  
When the main system pressure falls below a pre-set value, the  
RAT is deployed into the air stream, using pressure from its own  
accumulator. The blades will windmill in the slipstream and drive a  
hydraulic pump, a speed sensor will control the pitch of the blades  
to prevent over speeding. Although the RAT will operate at full  
system pressure, the flow rate is much smaller that the EDP.  Hand Pumps  
Therefore, it's use is usually limited to the primary flying controls.  Some aircraft are fitted with hand operated pumps. These pumps  
are generally fitted to provide hydraulic operations on the ground  
during maintenance, they can also be used in emergency.  

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Pressure Gauges 
Direct Reading Gauges 
In older aircraft the system pressure is displayed using direct reading gauges. These have a curved "Bourdon" tube inside them, which is filled with hydraulic fluid. Any increase in the system pressure will also increase the pressure in the tube, causing it to try and straighten this movement is translated into a reading. To prevent a leakage from the gauge at high-pressure a pressure relay is fitted. This isolates the system fluid from the gauge fluid. It is the outlet pressure from the pump that is sensed and displayed. 

Pressure Transmitters 
Modern aircraft make use of pressure transducers, which sense pressure and convert the signal into an electrical output. The electrical signal is then transmitted to remote gauges in the cockpit. The overall system is called a pressure transmitter. As well as deriving a signal of pump output pressure the transmitters can also be used to actuate overpressure or low pressure warnings of pump output and operate warning lamps or annunciators. 
Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Reservoirs 
Reservoirs provide: 
¦ 
A reserve of system fluid that can cater for small external leakage 

¦ 
An air space to cater for jack ram displacement 

¦ 
An air space that allows for fluid expansion 

¦ 
The normal supply offluid to the EDP 

¦ 
A limited emergency reserve of fluid 

¦ 
A positive head of pressurised fluid into the inlet of the EDP 

¦ 
A means to bleed air and dissolved gases from the fluid 

¦ 
A means of checking the system fluid quantity. 


Reservoirs are designed to provide storage for the system fluid. A sight glass allows for the visual reading of the fluid level and normally has an upper horizontal line marked max and a lower line marked min. These are accepted levels for normal operation. In modern aircraft a quantity gauge or low level warning is incorporated on the flight deck. The filler cap is fitted with a finger screen filter to strain incoming fluid. Remember only the correct hydraulic fluid can be used, mixing of fluid is not allowed. The level of the fluid alters during operation as jacks are displaced and the fluid expands with temperature.

Reservoirs hold a reserve of fluid and allow it to be de-aerated.

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Reservoirs continued 
The main system supply is taken via a stack or standpipe. The purpose of this is to ensure an emergency supply of hydraulic fluid. If a leak occurs in the reservoir or in the return line the reservoir will only empty to the top of the standpipe, allowing a reserve of fluid within the reservoir for emergency use. 
The emergency (hand pump) supply is taken from the bottom of the reservoir. To keep fluid at the mouth of the stack pipe, fins and baffles are fitted to prevent swirling due to turbulence. 
The low pressure fluid returning to the reservoir flows over a de­aerator tray, which can be either a series of steps or a flat tray with small holes through it. The de-aerator tray allows any air bubbles and dissolved gas to leave the fluid ensuring that only liquid collects around the mouth of the stack pipe. 
Reservoirs can be vented to atmosphere or pressurised. If they are vented to atmosphere there is a danger that the fluid in the system could boil at high altitude leading to pump cavitation and even the possibility of gas trapped in the lines and actuators. For this reason pressurised reservoirs are now the norm.

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Pressurised Reservoirs 
Pressurised systems prevent fluid boiling at altitude and provide a positive pressure at the inlet to the EDPs to prevent cavitation. Engine bleed air is used to pressurise the reservoir. It also allows for design of systems where the reservoir is below the level of the pump or components. If the air pressure is lost then there will be a rise in the reservoir quantity. However, it is a design requirement that ifthis should happen then the EDP must be able to draw the fluid and supply more than 70% of its normal pressure. 
Other methods of achieving a positive pressure in the inlet of the high-pressure engine driven pump are the use of: 
¦ 
A boot strapped reservoir. 

¦ 
Electric booster pumps. 


To prevent leakage and to isolate the system from the reservoir an isolation or on/off supply valve is fitted. This is electrically activated and operation ofthe fire handle will close the valve.

Light Aircraft Power Packs 

A modern system for light aircraft with retractable undercarriages is the self contained hydraulic power pack. This consists ofa reservoir, a reversible DC electric motor and flow and pressure control valves. The significant difference between power packs and standard hydraulic systems is that they are only used on demand to raise landing gear and maybe flaps. Once raised, the system is switched off until preparations are made for landing. The typical power pack system are: 
¦ 
The up or down movement is initiated by starting the pump motor in one direction or the other. 

¦ 
The fluid is only filtered on the up selection. 

¦ 
Fluid pressure opens the gear up check valve on "up" selection. 

¦ 
The spring moves the shuttle back on up selection, opening the down line to the reservoir. 

¦ 
When the gear is up a pressure switch removes the electrical power and the gear up check valve closes, forming a hydraulic lock to keep the gear fully retracted. 

¦ 
If the pressure drops in the up line, the pump automatically restarts to restore the pressure. 

¦ 
A higher pressure is required to raise the undercarriage than lower it, so the up line has a higher relief valve setting than the down line.

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Large Aircraft Systems 
Large aircraft place greater requirements on a hydraulic system, these are: 
¦ 
The need to operate multiple services at the same time 

¦ 
Instant response 

¦ 
Duplicated or triplicated primary systems 

¦ 
Emergency back-up systems 

¦ 
Priority to the flying controls and wheel brakes 

¦ 
Storage of a limited reserve of hydraulic energy for emergency use 

¦ 
Greater output force, to overcome heavier operating loads. 


These requirements are achieved using closed centred systems where the working pressure is available all the time and there is the instant response needed for the operation of the flying controls. 
The system shown has three independent hydraulic systems, called the blue, green and yellow systems. These names are not universal, other systems use names or letters like A, Band C to identify the systems. 


Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Large Aircraft Systems continued 
In this example, there are two engines: N°1 drives EDPs in the blue and green system and N°2 drives EDPs in the green and yellow system. The green system has two EDPs and two electric pumps, so it could be regarded as the 'master' system. 
There are two PTUs. If pressure is lost in the blue or yellow systems they can be pressurised from the green system through PTUs. 
There are three priority valves isolating the landing gear and the high lift devices. The landing gear will have an emergency lowering system independent of hydraulic power, and high lift devices are not absolutely essential for a safe emergency landing. 
The RAT powers the yellow system so that, at the very least, the flying controls will keep working. 


Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Large Aircraft Systems continued 
High system pressures allow smaller actuators to be used. As these will have a smaller volume to fill, the pressure supply pipes can be reduced in diameter. 
The small bore piping has four benefits: 
• 
The pipes are easier to route around the aircraft's structure. 

• 
There is less internal surface area for the pressure to act on. 

• 
Reduced fluid quantity is required. 

• 
System weight is reduced. 


In closed centred systems there must be strict regulation ofthe system pressure to prevent over-pressurisation and damage. This pressure regulation can be achieved by one of two methods. 
• 
Use of a fixed volume pump with an idling circuit, an automatic cut-out valve and its associated accumulator. 

• 
Use of a variable volume pump. 


If there is a high demand on the system, for example when operating flaps, undercarriage and flying controls at the same time, the large drop in pressure could result in a sluggish flight control response. 


Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators en • 
Pipes and Hoses 
All of the components described above are of little use unless they are connected by reliable pipework. These fall into two broad categories, pipes and hoses. As the name implies, pipes are solid, rigid components built to a very high specification in steel or stainless steel to withstand the tremendous pressures -typically 3000 psi on a large aircraft system. On all but the smallest of hydraulic systems the pressure line will be of a smaller diameter than the return line, as the hydraulic fluid will have expanded with heat while producing work for us in the actuators. 
Flexible hoses are essential to allow movement in the components that they supply, such as flight control actuators or landing gear. Not only do they have to withstand the same pressure as pipes, but they must be built to cope with many thousands of movements over their life cycle. They consist of layers of alternating rubber or polymers for flexibility and sealing, and steel braiding for strength. Radius of operation is also critical, as the effect of a flexible hose kinking and failing under pressure could be catastrophic. 
You can see examples of both solid pipes and flexible hoses in the braking lines in the photograph. 


Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
Hydraulic Controls and Indicators 

Most modern aircraft only permit limited pilot control ofthe hydraulic system with functions such as PTU operation being automatically controlled. The diagram opposite, shows the controls which operate the hydraulic pumps on a B737 overhead panel and the low pump pressure and pump overheat warning lights. Although the B737 has three hydraulic systems, A, Band standby, only systems A and B are powered from engine driven pumps. The standby system is powered by an electric pump. 


Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 
Hydraulic Controls and Indicators continued 
The pilots also have an indication of hydraulic brake pressure, in this case on the First Officer's panel. 
Older aircraft require much more extensive crew operation of the hydraulics and will have indications of system contents and pressure, over temperature conditions and low pump output pressure. Selections of hydraulic power source are manual, rather than automatic. 

Hydraulic Pumps and Accumulators 

Hydraulic Controls and Indicators continued 
In the Airbus A320 the hydraulic systems are largely automatic. Failures are brought to the pilots' attention by the cautions & warning system, accompanied by an audible warning depending on severity. On the issuance of a caution, the basic supply system is presented on the lower ECAM. In the case shown, both the upper and lower ECAMs are displayed. The green system has lost fluid resulting in a LOW LEVEL and LOW PRESSURE warnings which are displayed on left hand side of the upper ECAM. The actions required by the pilots are shown in cyan. 
On the lower ECAM, functioning parts are shown in green and non-functioning parts in amber. Because there is no fluid it is not possible to transfer power from the yellow system via the PTU. 
The loss of the green system has secondary failures which are shown on the right hand side of the upper ECAM. In this case WHEEL & F/CTL are illuminated meaning landing gear will need free fall extension, nose wheel steering and normal braking is not available but most of the flight controls can be operated via the other systems.

Lesson 6 
Flying Controls 

AGK 06 
Flying Controls 
ftiihi-lo 
Introduction 
The primary flight controls of an aircraft manoeuvre the aircraft around the three axes comprising elevators, ailerons, roll spoilers, flaperons and rudder. Secondary flight controls are intended to improve the aircraft performance characteristics or to relieve excessive control loading they include lift augmentation devices such as flaps and slats, speed brakes, flight and ground spoilers, trimming devices such as trim tabs, and trimmable horizontal stabiliser. You should note that in the examinations it is assumed that a conventional jet transport aircraft has a swept wing and a trimable stabiliser (variable incidence trimming tailplane). 
Control surfaces can be actuated by three means of control, manual (reversible) in which the pilot gains no assistance, power­assisted (reversible) in which the pilot has some hydraulic help to move the control and, fully powered (irreversible), in which the pilot makes only a selection and the hydraulics provide all of the power. 


Flying Controls 
Manual Flight Controls 
Primary flight controls in light aircraft are operated directly by cables, pulleys and chains. The clear advantage of such a system is its simplicity, the disadvantages are that the control runs are rarely duplicated, and therefore a jammed control can cause a crash, and that aerodynamic forces which increase rapidly with forward speed can make the controls increasingly difficult to operate. This diagram shows a rare manual control run from a large aircraft, the aileron control on a BAe146. 
Most larger modern and high speed aircraft use hydraulically powered flight control systems. Powered flight controls can be either power assisted or fully powered. Both systems will have an input from the pilot to move a hydraulic control valve. This will direct pressure to an actuator which moves the flight control. The system is designed so that there is a follow up action to reposition the control valve. This ensures control surface movement is proportional to control input. 
ft+i.i-lo 


Flying Controls 
Power Assisted Flight Controls 
In a power assisted flight control system the pilot still has a physical input to the flight controls. 
As the name of the system suggests, hydraulic power will assist the pilot in moving the controls. The pilot will still get feed back of the air loads imposed on the control. This provides the pilot with 'feel'. In the event of hydraulic failure the pilot can still operate the control, although stick forces would be very high. 
Manual and power assisted flying controls are known as reversible flying controls. 
ft.lhi-lo 

Aircraft with reversible flight controls should be fitted with a flying control lock system called a gust lock, so that controls can be locked for parking. They are usually operated by a handle fitted in the flight deck and connected to moveable locking pins by a cable system. When operated the pins are pushed through holes in pulleys/quadrants which lock the control system and prevent movement due to wind loads. 
The system must be designed so that take-off with the gust lock still engaged is impossible. 
Flying Controls 
Powered Flying Controls 
In a fully powered system there is no physical connection between the pilot's controls and the control surfaces and therefore there is no feedback of control forces to the pilot. This type of powered control system is known as an irreversible control and requires some form of artificial feel. If the hydraulic system were to fail, the pilot would not be able to move the controls manually. 
Artificial Feel 
The simplest artificial feel system is spring feel, in which the pilot simply deflects the control column against a spring. These may incorporate a trim system by centring the spring, but there is no relation between the aerodynamic loads and the feedback to the pilot. 

More sophisticated systems insert a synthetic feedback that is proportional to EAS2, sometimes referred to as 'Q', the dynamic force element. An independent pitot static system provides the input of dynamic pressure. Artificial feel systems are often known as 'Q' feel systems. 
Some systems modify this further to give lower control forces at high speed by making the feel proportional only to EAS or, by making the control forces proportional to EAS3, produce artificially high control forces at high speed. 
In the pitch control system 'g' feel is sometimes used which increases control forces as the load factor increases. 
All artificial feel systems operate in parallel to the flying controls, they do not form part of the direct linkage between the control column and the actuator. Artificial feel may also be referred to as a 'force gradient unit'. 
Flying Controls 
Gear Change and Datum Shift 
Gear changing, or ratio changing in a control system is an attempt to match the need for large control deflections at low speed with the need to safeguard the aircraft from overstress at high speed. At a set speed the mechanical advantage of the system is changed, so that full cockpit control deflection gives full control deflection at low speed, but restricted control deflection at high speed. 
This is most often seen in the rudder system, for the rudder, designed to give the yaw necessary to balance thrust asymmetry at low speed is too powerful for normal flight. So a rudder ratio changer system reduces the rudder deflection for a given rudder pedal deflection as the IAS increases. A variable stop system limits both rudder and rudder pedal travel as the IAS increases. 
Another method of rudder limiting, as used on the Boeing 737, is blowback, also called blowdown. With this system the pressure applied through the control system actuators is constant, but the movement of the rudder reduces because of the increase in dynamic pressure on the control surface. Typically this changes from +/-15° on the ground to around +/-8° in the cruise. Blowback is also used in flap systems as a protection against damage from excessive aerodynamic forces. 
ft+iffi-lo 

Gear Change and Datum Shift continued 
Datum shift is an adjustment of the relationship between the control column range of movement and the control range of movement to make the maximum deflection available in the desired sense. At least one modern aircraft has a datum shift in the elevator system that operates at the stall to ensure that full nose down control will be available for recovery. 
Flying Controls 
Power Control Units 
A servo control unit or power control unit (PCU) is a closed loop, or follow-up, servo controller used to actuate powered flying controls. 
PCUs consist of a balanced double acting actuator where the body of the actuator is attached to the flying control surface input and the ram is attached to the structure of the aircraft. The pressure delivered to the PCU comes from the main hydraulic system. 
The pilot's selector is linked to a spool type valve. Moving the selector will pull the spool valve across, opening one side of the actuator to pressure, the other to return. As the fluid acts on the body and piston equally, and the piston is locked to the aircraft structure, the body will move in the same direction as the spool valve. This will move the control surface and at the same time centre the spool valve, holding the control surface in its new position. Ifthere is a hydraulic failure the interconnecting valve drops to break the actuator hydraulic lock, allowing the control to be moved manually. There will be some small dead movement, as the manual linkage has to work through the spool valve. 
ft+EH-lo 


PCUs use main system pressure If pressure is lost the control can be moved manually

Autopilot Control 
The aileron autopilot actuator acts on the PCU through the feel control unit, this causes the control wheels to move in the correct sense as the autopilot controls the aircraft. 
Safety Features 
Redundancy is achieved by having duplicated control runs. If one ofthe duplicated control runs jams the control wheel on that side ofthe cockpit will also be jammed in roll. The other pilot will be able to override the linkage with sufficient force and then control the aircraft in roll through the second control run. Although, on the B737, this feature only requires force to operate it, on many other aircraft there are specific roll and pitch disconnect switches which are used in the event of a control jam.

Flying Controls 
Fly-By-Wire Systems 
Fly-By-Wire Systems remove the direct linkage from the control wheel to the PCU. As there is no direct linkage between the control surface and the pilot's control, this is an irreversible control system. 
The advantages of a fly-by-wire systems over conventional systems are that they: 
¦ 
are lighter 

¦ 
require less maintenance 


• 
are self testing 

• 
are more precise (no play or backlash) 

• 
are easy to interface with other electrical equipment (autopilot) 


¦ 
are more responsive 

¦ 
are safer (for example, a computerised system can have built in flight envelope protection) and 

¦ 
reduce pilot workload. 


Although they are fly-by-wire systems, some aircraft such as the 8777 retain the mechanical linkages as shown opposite. 
ft+EH-lo 

Flying Controls 
Control Runs 
As the control wheel is turned to give a roll input, mechanical linkages of pulleys and cables move the second control wheel. Short duplicated control runs take the inputs back to a point, just under the pilots' seats, where the force of the roll application and the displacement ofthe control wheel are sensed by transducers, which convert these inputs into analouge electrical signals. 
In Boeing aircraft the analouge signals are sent to the actuator control electronics (ACE). The ACE takes these inputs, and inputs from other aircraft systems, Primary Flight Control system (PFC) computes a required control deflection and sends digital electronic signals to the second ACE. 
The second ACE converts the signal into an analogue signal which is fed into the PCUs. The feedback is therefore also analogue. 
Actuator 
r 
W--IACEI Digital•IACE rnalogu:1!!!11 CE3====>lliiil Control 
Hand surtace 
control Digital 
IPFCI 


The PCUs operate hydraulically to move the control surface and simultaneously send a position feedback signal to the ACE. When the position feedback signal equals the commanded position the ACE stops the PCU command. 
Flying Controls 
Feel and Trim 
Aileron trim is provided by a mechanical feel and centering mechanism located at the back of and in parallel with the control runs. Trim inputs adjust the central position of the feel and centering mechanism. As the controls are deflected from the neutral trim position springs are compressed to provide artificial feel. 
Autopilot Control 
The autopilot acts with the Primary Flight Computer (PFC) to send signals directly to the ACE. The ACE computes the commands, as before, for the PCUs. The autopilot and PFC commands are also transmitted to backdrive actuators, DC electric motors, which move the control wheels to follow up the autopilot commands. 
Generic Safety Features 
Control jams are still possible in the mechanical linkages near the control wheels, so a breakout mechanism is provided, as before, to allow the other pilot to control the aircraft if a control wheel jams. To avoid errors multiple computers check each other. Not only multiple computers but computers of different types and programming check against each other. 
ft.iiifilo 

Information between computers and other electronic components is via data cable. For safety reasons redundancy is achieved by using multiple data cables. 
Digital systems require feedback as mentioned earlier. The control surface position feedback to the computer is also available to the pilots on a page displaying the position of all the flight controls. To avoid erroneous position reporting multiple control position feedback sensors are used. The idea of multiple sensors is used wherever there are critical components or parameters. 
You might have heard of 'Fred the wheel tapper' stories. His job was to tap train wheels at the end of the day using a special long handled hammer. When struck, the wheel reverberated creating an audible tone like a bell. If the wheel was cracked then the tone was different enabling Fred to identify and report faulty wheels. One day Fred went about his business as usual, except that every wheel created the 'cracked' tone. Fred was no fool, he understood it was very unlikely that every wheel was cracked, so he checked his hammer and found that it was cracked. The message here is: never rely on a single source of information. 
Flying Controls 
Control laws 
As already mentioned, modern aircraft rely on several sophisticated computers to control and protect the aircraft in flight. As computers fail occasionally the system has to be designed to manage the failures safely. Manufacturers create a system of degraded service known as control laws. Airbus use the terms, Normal, Alternate, Direct and Mechanical back up. 
In Normal mode full service is provided, meaning there is full control operation and full protection. 
In Alternate mode, depending on which computers have failed some ofthe protection is lost, e.g. Pitch attitude protection, or Bank angle protection or High AOA protection. 
In Direct Mode, most computer protection is lost and the controls respond to control input in a similar way to manual flight controls. 
Mechanical Back-up Mode, mandated in case of total electrical failure can be achieved in different ways. On Airbus models, the pilots have direct 'old fashioned' control of the rudder and mechanical horizontal stabiliser trimmer by using the trim wheel. There is no aileron or elevator control. On the B777 there are cable controls to the stabiliser and some spoilers. There is no aileron or rudder control. 

Clearly pilot workload will increase further with each mode. Additionally, without flight protection the flight envelope may shrink to avoid flight dangerously close to limits. 
For safety reasons, modern aircraft have multiple control surfaces, e.g. the A320 has one aileron and four spoilers per wing, two pairs of elevators and three rudders, each under control of different computers and different hydraulic systems to reduce the possibility of total control failure. 
Side Sticks 
Airbus aeroplanes use side stick controls with no mechanical linkage between them. The sticks are spring loaded to neutral. Normally only one pilot is in control however, if both pilots make an input then the signals are added algebraically. This function could be useful during pilot training for example. A pilot can choose to take control by pressing a takeover button which de-activates the other stick. 
Flying Controls 
Secondary Flight Control Systems 
Secondary flight controls are flaps and slats, speedbrakes, spoilers and trimming devices. 
Light Aircraft 
Light aircraft may have flaps, possibly have slats and will certainly have some form of trimming device. They will not have spoilers or speed brakes. 
Flaps and Slats 
The operation of leading edge slats can be automatic. As the angle of attack increases, the centre of pressure moves forward on the wing. The pressure differential between the top and bottom of the leading edge is used to extend the slat. At lower angles of attack the pressure on the leading edge closes the slats. 
Flap operation on light aircraft is sometimes unpowered, relying on the pilot for the operating force. More usually the selection is powered either electrically or hydraulically. 
If the selection is powered and the power supply fails, flaps will tend to be slowly blown back in by the aerodynamic forces. 
The speed for flap extension VFE is marked on the air speed indicator as the top of the white arc. 


Flying Controls 
Trim 
On early aircraft trimming was accomplished by fixed tabs on the control surface. 
On later light aircraft trim tabs can be operated from the cockpit, either directly or electrically. Where electrical elevator trim is provided dual switches prevent a single switch failure leading to trim runaway. 



NOTE: The use of 2 switches prevents trim runaway 
Flying Controls 
Large Aircraft 
Secondary flight controls on larger aircraft are always powered, usually by hydraulics but also occasionally by pneumatics or even electrical motors. 
Secondary control usage differs from primary controls in that they are usually selected to a prescribed position and remain there for some time. For example, flaps set to take-off position. If hydraulically powered either by jacks or hydraulic motors they are held in position by hydraulic lock when the selector closes. In the case of electrical actuators a mechanical brake within the actuator is used. 
Flaps and Slats 
Most transport aircraft have both leading edge flaps or slats and trailing edge flaps. The leading edge flaps are either deployed or retracted, but slats can have intermediate positions and trailing edge flaps certainly will. The initial selection may only partly extend the leading edge devices, the next selection may fully deploy them and provide a small amount of trailing edge flap, subsequent selections will increase only the trailing edge flap angle. On retraction the leading edge devices will be retracted last. 


On a large transport aeroplane, the auto-slat system extends (part of) the slats automatically when a certain value of angle of attack is exceeded to improve the stall characteristics. 
Flying Controls 

Flaps and Slats 
This diagram shows flap/slat indicators from a BAe146 on the left and from an A320 on the right. 
Load Limiting Devices 
To prevent a high speed selection overstressing the control surface most aircraft with powered systems are fitted with load limiting protection devices. These devices will limit the pressures to pneumatic and hydraulic systems to prevent extension above the limiting speeds or will auto-retract the flaps and slats at speeds above the limit. This method where aerodynamic forces overrule hydraulic forces is known as a blowback system. 
More sophisticated systems will retract the flaps automatically if the limiting speed is exceeded -though with no change in the flap selection lever -and then automatically extend the flaps again once the speed is back in the safe range for the selected stage of flaps. 
Alpha / Speed Locks 
On the Airbus and other aircraft retraction of the leading edge slats is inhibited if the aircraft is at high alpha or below a minimum airspeed. This system is known as Alpha or Speed Lock. Angle of attack information is fed from the alpha probe and speed data comes from the air data computers.

Outboard aileron Speedbrakes and Spoilers 
Speed brakes on large aircraft may be tail-mounted, as on the BAe 146 or, more usually double as wing spoilers. The tail mounting 
Inboard aileron 
on the BAe 146 has the advantages that the speed brakes act 
along the axis of the aircraft and therefore cause no pitching moment and that their operation only increases drag, but does not reduce lift. 
Spoilers have an additional function assisting the ailerons in roll control. Spoilers can also be used as rate of descent controllers to give a constant attitude approach. Speed brakes and spoilers will be hydraulically powered. 
Flying Controls 
A 8747-400 landing 
Look at the adjacent movie of a B747-400 landing on a damp day. Before landing the leading edge devices are deployed, in this case Kreuger flaps. On touchdown, the spoilers deploy to destroy lift and create drag. Shortly after, the reverse doors open. Notice spray from the inner engine going into the intake of the outer engine, showing how debris can be blown into the engine during reverse thrust. Notice also that the pilot stops reverse thrust while the aircraft is still going quite fast, this is because below 50 kt or thereabouts, reverse thrust is not very effective. A quirk of this aeroplane is that when reverse is selected the inner and midsection Kreuger flaps are withdrawn leaving only the outer section deployed. 
Flying Controls 
Trim 
Aileron and rudder trim devices on large aircraft with fully powered flight control systems, are usually the centering devices we saw earlier, where the zero force position changes. The result is the same as for manual flight controls, if say 5 degrees of right trim were applied then the zero force position of the control column would be 5 degrees off the centre position. 
While rudder and aileron trim are unlikely to change during flight, there are significant changes to elevator trim with operation of flaps, undercarriage and with fuel burn. Such large changes would leave very little elevator movement around the trimmed position as shown opposite. 
A variable incidence tailplane overcomes this problem by trimming the position of the whole horizontal stabiliser. This is a very powerful trimming mechanism as one degree of stabiliser trim represents several degrees of elevator trim. It creates much less disturbance to the airflow thereby reducing drag. Note that once trimmed, the elevator is back in the central position this means that the elevator control in the cockpit would also be centralised even when a lot of trim is applied, unlike the rudder and aileron controls. To put it in more technical terms, when using a variable incidence tailplane the zero-force stick position does not change with application of trim.

Lesson 7 
Landing Gear 

AGK 07 
Land ing Gear 

Landing Gear Types 
There are two designs of undercarriage layout, the tricycle and the tail wheel, also known as the tail dragger. The tricycle gear has the stabilising auxiliary wheel at the front, the tail dragger at the back 
The tail wheel layout is simple and better for rough field operations but gives poor forward visibility and, with the CG behind the main wheels, is dynamically unstable on the ground. Harsh braking can cause tail wheel aircraft to nose over as can strong tailwinds, particularly when the aircraft is parked. 
Modern aircraft have tricycle undercarriages. The normal arrangement is for a nose auxiliary wheel and two main gears. Larger air transport aircraft such as the B747 can also have extra 


gear mounted beneath the fuselage these are referred to as body gear. The landing gear is sometimes referred to as the alighting gear. 
The tail wheel layout is simple but gives poor forward visibility 
Landing Gear 
Function of Landing Gear 
The function of landing gear is: 
¦ 
To absorb the landing loads and dampen vibrations. 

¦ 
To support the aircraft on the ground. 

¦ 
To allow ground manoeuvring. 

¦ 
To withstand side loads. 

¦ 
To withstand aerodynamic loads/ flight air loads. 

¦ 
To give the minimum friction between the ground and the aircraft. 


The landing gear is, of course, only required when the aircraft is on the ground. In flight it adds unnecessary weight to the aircraft and, iffixed, increases the parasite drag. Fixed undercarriages are simple to make but the reduction in flight performance even with drag reducing fairings or spats fitted restricts its use to low performance aircraft. 
If spats are fitted they can become so caked with mud, slush or snow that they have to be removed to be cleaned. Although a pilot can remove them the spats must be refitted by an engineer. 
ft+ii1-lo 

For larger and high performance aircraft the undercarriages are retracted into the wings or fuselage during flight. Hydraulic power is normally used for retraction and extension of the undercarriage, some smaller aircraft do use electrical power. 
In the event of a failure of the normal power source an alternative means of lowering the gear is provided. 
Retractable gear reduces the drag 

While the tricycle layout is tried and tested, and favoured on most modern aircraft, there is an inherent problem with them. In the event of touching down in an incorrect attitude, with the nose gear landing first, then both the nose leg and the fuselage could exceed their design limitations. The usual landing attitude will prevent that from occurring, as the normal touch down sequence will be main (and/or body) gear, followed by the nose gear. 
Land ing Gear 
ftiii1-lo

Fixed undercarriages for light aircraft can be of a variety of types. The following are some of the more common used:

¦

Retractable Landing Gear 
Landing gear types
Larger aircraft with retractable undercarriage are usually fitted with oleo pneumatic undercarriage struts. The oleo pneumatic shock absorber can only absorb vertical loads. 
Oleo Pneumatic Strut Configuration 
There are four patterns of landing gear configuration, shown in here. The strongest, as you might expect, is the dual wheel layout. 

Dual wheel 
Landing Gear 
ft+ii1-lo 
Bogies 
On very large aircraft multiple wheels are placed on the same gear unit they are attached together on a structural device called a bogie. A good example is the Boeing 747. The 747 is equipped with four main gear units, each with four-wheel bogies, allowing multiple wheels on a bogie beam on the same undercarriage leg, plus twin nose wheels so that the plane's weight is spread across a total of 18 wheels. A typical bogie is shown in figure 4 with the wheel removed. The bogie beam connects the front and rear axles, to which the wheels are fitted. 

Landing Gear 
fti.H-lo 
Undercarriage Doors 
Undercarriage doors serve one purpose to reduce drag by providing a smooth aerodynamic surface when the undercarriage is retracted. Doors can be simply attached to the undercarriage legs -a cheap option -and others employ complicated mechanisms adding weight and expense. The B737 is an interesting compromise as it has a small door but the wheel remains exposed. 


Landing Gear 

Shock Absorption  
Oleo Pneumatic Shock Absorber Strut  
Oleo pneumatic struts consist of a piston rod that moves up and  
down within a cylinder. The cylinder is fixed to the aircraft's  
structure and an axle is connected to the lower end of the piston  
rod. The cylinder is charged with gas -nitrogen is preferred as it is  
inert -and oil. Mineral hydraulic oil DTD 585 is standard.  
The function of the gas is to support the weight of the aircraft at  
rest and acts as a spring to absorb the landing and taxiing loads.  
The oil is used to control the speed of compression and  
expansion, referred to as dampening of landing load and recoil  
action.  
Nitrogen and DTD 585 is used  
The oil damps the landing load and recoil action  

Landing Gear 
Oleo Pneumatic Shock Absorber without Separator 
Older light aircraft normally use a metering valve and orifice in an un-separated shock absorber. 
As the oleo strut is compressed on landing the gas pressure increases as the length ofthe strut is reduced and the oil is forced to flow up through the orifice in the fixed piston as the volume reduces. The metering rod that is connected to the bottom of the piston and passes though the orifice controls the rate at which the strut compresses. 
During landing load and recoil action the rod and orifice act as a variable restrictor, reducing the flow of oil from one side of the fixed piston to the other. The control ofthe oil flowing back during the recoil action helps to prevent the aircraft from being sprung back into the air.

Landing Gear 
ft+W+lo 
Oleo Pneumatic Shock Absorber with Separator 
In large aircraft the oleo uses a free-floating separator to ensure that the gas and fluid cannot mix. The speed of compression and recoil is controlled via fixed orifices. During recoil some of the orifices are closed by valves to slow the recoil action. 
When an aircraft is stationary the pressure in the gas equals the weight of the aircraft. Any additional load will cause the oleo to compress further. This must be taken into account when equipment is placed around or underneath the aircraft. 
A heavy landing can result in the oleo strut bottoming out. This occurs when the piston rod travel exceeds the distance available, and can cause damage as the castings meet. 


Land ing Gear 
Oleo setup 
Each oleo will have a correct extension at a given weight. The extension is adjusted by adjusting the gas pressure in the oleo strut. Oil levels must also be correct. If the oil has been leaking the gas pressure can be topped up to restore the extension but there will be more gas than normal and the oil contents will be lower so the strut will be less damped, too bouncy. Conversely, but less likely, a strut overfilled with oil but at the correct extension will have too little gas in it and will be excessively rigid. 


The oleo pneumatic shock absorber can only absorb vertical loads 

Landing Gear 
ft+ii&fto 
Torque Link (Scissor link) 
To prevent the inner piston rod rotating inside cylinder and to ensure that the wheels align with the centre line of the aircraft, a torque link also called a scissor link is fitted. This is attached to the cylinder at one end and the piston rod at the other. A joint in the middle of the torque link allows the oleo to move up and down. If the torque link is worn it can cause rotary movement of the piston within the cylinder this is called nose wheel shimmy. 
Torque links 
Landing Gear 
Shimmy Damper 
Wheel shimmy is described as a sinusoidal motion of the nose wheel when the aircraft is taxiing on the ground. A shimmy damper is either a spring or a hydraulic damper which acts like an accumulator and is used to prevent or dampen these oscillations with an opposing force. Spring systems are used on light aircraft and hydraulic systems for larger aircraft. They can be incorporated as part of the nose wheel steering system.

Landing Gear 
Retraction and Extension 
Bracing of Retractable Landing Gear 
As just seen the vertical loads on landing are taken by the shock absorber strut however, there are other loads to consider. On touchdown, particularly on a crosswind landing, the side loads on the undercarriage can be large, and then moments later when the brakes are applied a strong fore-aft force is exerted. Once slowed down, taxiing manoeuvres result in torsional forces from the tyres scrubbing being transmitted through the bogies to the struts. Very tight turns should be avoided to limit the torsional stresses on the undercarriage. To deal with such forces bracing struts are required, otherwise the legs are at risk of being torn off. 
Main Gear 
The hydraulically operated retractable landing gears have to be braced to prevent collapse. Main gears that retract sideways have side stays to withstand the side loads and prevent collapse. They also require forestays or drag stays which brace the lower leg and stop it collapsing rearwards.

Side stays prevent the u/c collapsing sideways. Fore and aft stays prevent the u/c collapsing backwards and forwards. 
Landing Gear 
Nose Gear 
Nose gear and main gears that retract fore or aft require only fore and aft bracing. Drag stays brace forward retracting legs and push stays brace rearward retracting legs. These stays have to be articulated to allow the gear to retract, and be stowed in the smallest space possible.

Steering actuators 
Landing Gear 
Undercarriage Locks 

Hook lock
Up Locks and Down Locks 
A retractable undercarriage has to be locked when in the up (retracted) and down (extended) positions. The locks prevent the undercarriage from un-commanded extension or collapse. 
Types of Lock Geometric lock 
The geometric or over centre lock is only used as a down lock. It requires mechanical force to unlock it these locks can be formed by using the stays bracing the struts. 
Hook lock 
Hook locks engage without hydraulic pressure but require 


hydraulic pressure to release. Hook locks are normally used as up-locks, with a geometric lock for the down lock, but they can, rarely, also be used as down locks. If hydraulic pressure fails to properly release the lock, hook locks can sometimes be persuaded to let go by manoeuvring the aircraft sharply, obviously within limits, to use the weight of the gear to assist. 
Some light aircraft use a hydraulic lock to lock the landing gear both up and down. 
A geometric lock is only used as a down lock 
Landing Gear 
Landing Gear Position Indications 
Micro switches or proximity switches are fitted to the undercarriage to relay an indication of the gear position. These are linked to indicator lights on the flight deck. 
¦ 
Green lights indicate that the gear is locked down. 

¦ 
Red lights indicate that the gear is unlocked or the gear position disagrees with the lever position. 

¦ 
No lights indicate that the gear is locked up. 


This diagram shows B737 landing gear indicators. 
Land ing Gear 
Landing Gear Position Indications 
On some aircraft both green and red lights illuminate together when the gear has been lowered by emergency means. 
For large aircraft with main wheel bay doors -known as D doors ­a dedicated red light will indicate that they are unlocked. These doors close when the gear is locked down after normal selection to reduce drag and prevent them being damaged on touch down. Hydraulic sequencing valves ensure that the doors open and close at the appropriate times. 
For large aircraft using bogies on the main gears and body gears, an amber truck light indicates if a truck (bogie) is in the wrong position for retraction. When this light illuminates, the gear selector lever is physically locked preventing an up selection being made. The panel shown here is from the Lockheed L1011 TriStar. 
Mechanical indicators are used as a back up should the electrical indicating system fail to indicate gear down and sometimes if it fails to indicate up. These are viewed through panels in the pressure hull or out of the cabin windows. 
The truck light indicates the bogie is in the wrong position for retraction 
Landing Gear 
Most aircraft with retractable undercarriage have a warning system to prevent inadvertent landing with gear up. In older aircraft, low throttle position without flaps would sound a warning horn that could be cancelled provided the flaps were not extended beyond a defined point. In modern aircraft in addition to the throttle position and flap setting, there is usually an input from the ground proximity warning system to give an audible warning "TOO LOW-GEAR". 
Ground Locks 
Ground locks are fitted to the door and leg actuators or linkages to prevent them from moving and allowing the undercarriage to collapse. For ease of spotting them, as some are only small pins, they are painted red and have a red or red and white flag attached to them. Ground locks are always fitted before an aircraft is towed or moved without its main engines running. In the past, pilots have managed to get airborne with one or more of the landing gear locks still fitted. In such a situation it is unlikely the aircraft would be able to fly to its intended destination as both gear down and the associated speed restriction add to the drag. The options would be to land back on departure airfield once a landing mass is achieved or continue en-route, landing early to pick up fuel: either way incurs a cost penalty! To avoid such errors, each aircraft has a set of ground locks, carried in the aircraft in a clear or open fronted container where the Captain can check that they are stowed prior to take off. 


A number of systems such as thrust reverse, lift dump and gear safety locks must only function when the aircraft is on the ground. There are systems that must only function in flight. 
To ensure that these systems operate at the correct stage, an air/ground logic system is used. This system can use micro switches or pressure switches to determine whether the landing gear is in contact with the ground and bearing the aircraft's weight. The micro switches note physical compression of the gear, the pressure switches note increase in pressure in the oleos. These are referred to as Weight on Wheels (WoW), Weight on Ground (WoG) or squat switches. 
Landing Gear 
Gear Selector 
The gear selector has a wheel shaped knob for tactile recognition and must be mounted forward of the throttle or thrust levers. The diagram shows the B737 selector and indicators. 
To prevent an inadvertent wheels up landing, a warning horn is operated if a throttle lever or power lever is retarded past a set position without the gear having been lowered. This will continue to sound until the throttle or power lever is advanced, the gear lowered or the warning horn cutout switch is pressed. The GPWS mode 4A alert provides an additional warning in larger aircraft. 
To prevent inadvertent up selection of the landing gear when on the ground, some aircraft are fitted with a safety latch to physically prevent the gear selector lever from being moved. As a back up, the air/ground logic system prevents the gear from operating when on the ground and, ifthe down lock is of geometric over centre type, this will also prevent retraction. These safety devices are not fitted to all aircraft, especially some of the older types. 
In an emergency, the pilot may be able to override a safety lock and collapse the gear. Should an up selection be made on the ground when the aircraft is stationary, the warning horn will sound and the red gear unsafe light will illuminate. 
The gear selector is shaped like a wheel A squat switch prevents the gear lever being moved on the ground The gear cannot be retracted on the ground if the down lock is 
geometric 
Landing Gear 
Retraction and Extension Sequence 
In light aircraft the selector has two positions -up for gear retraction and down for gear extension. Large transport aircraft often have a third selector position, OFF. This locks the gear in the last selected position but it is normally only selected after the gear is up. 
On retraction and extension the main gears move before the nose gear, this is to ensure that the aircraft does not become unbalanced, as the drag alters. The nose wheel must be straight or the nose gear will not retract this is to prevent the nose wheel damaging the narrow bay. A self-centring mechanism can be used to achieve this. One way restrictor valves are fitted in the return line on extension to prevent the gear lowering too quickly, under both gravity and hydraulic pressure. On some aircraft, automatic braking occurs during gear retraction. 

Landing Gear Operating Speeds 
The landing gear, in particular the doors are not designed to take the same aerodynamic loads as the main airframe. Speed limits are therefore imposed on the operation of the gear and flight with the landing gear extended. 
VLO The maximum speed at which the land gear can be operated. It is possible that the retraction and extension speeds may be different. In this case the speed will be identified as VLo (RET) and 
VLO (EXT)· 
VLE The maximum speed at which the aircraft can be flown with the landing gear extended. VLE is generally a higher speed than VLO. This is because once the gear is extended the undercarriage doors close and the undercarriage legs can withstand higher aerodynamic loads. 
Unlike the flap operating speed, VFE, the landing gear speeds are not marked on light aircraftASls. You do often find the speeds placarded near the selector. 
The nose gear must be straight to be retracted 


Landing Gear 
The aircraft's main hydraulic system is the normal source of power for raising and lowering the gear. In the event of loss of main hydraulic power an emergency method of extending the landing gear must be provided which follows the normal sequence of: unlocking, operating and down-locking. Steering and Braking Systems 

AGK08 
Steering and Braking Systems 
ft+IHhlo 
Steering 
Steering of aircraft for ground manoeuvring is achieved in various ways. Older tail dragging aircraft with a castoring tailwheel use differential braking and aerodynamic forces from the rudder. Rudder forces can be increased by increasing propeller rpm. On light aircraft with tricycle landing gear steering is normally achieved by mechanically connecting a steerable nose wheel to the rudder bar. This, of course, will limit the range of movement to that of the rudder. Tighter turns can sometimes be achieved by the use of differential braking in this case, the nose gear is moved to the limit of the rudder bar but is free to castor to a greater angle under the influence of differential braking. 
Some aircraft do not have any steering connection to the rudder but have fully castoring nose wheels. These aircraft are steered by the use of differential braking, power and rudder only. To reduce shimmy this system makes use of a twin contact tyre called a Marstrand tyre in addition to a shimmy damper. 
Shimmy, defined as a sinusoidal movement of the nosewheel in the longitudinal axis, must be controlled or damped, otherwise the shimmy could damage the nose gear and would certainly make steering difficult. 


Steering and Braking Systems 
Steering continued 
Damping can be by the use of small hydraulic shock absorbers, or by using a silicon fluid that changes its viscosity in reaction to the speed of movement between fixed and rotating components. 
Shimmy dampers reduce the severity of shimmy but do not eliminate it. Although shimmy is generally associated with nose wheels it can affect mainwheels, particularly when bearings are worn. 
Nose gear needs to be centred on retraction, as already mentioned. Most designs allow for two cams that mate together as the leg extends off load. As the two cams meet the fixed cam forces the moveable cam to follow it, moving the gear to the centreline position before retraction. 
Failures in some centering mechanisms have attracted adverse publicity, such as the JetBlue A320 which landed at LAX in September 2005 with the gear stuck 90° out of alignment. 
ft.ii+lo 


Steering and Braking Systems 
Large Aircraft Nose Wheel Steering 
Larger transport aircraft have a dedicated hydraulically powered nose wheel steering system. At low speeds the pilot uses a tiller or control wheel to pivot the nose gear through 75°. On very large air transport aircraft the body gear is automatically activated beyond 20° of nosewheel deflection and will steer in the opposite direction to the nose wheel to reduce the turning radius. 
The rudder pedals will still turn the nose wheel up to 7° either side of the neutral. This 'rudder fine nose wheel steering' allows limited control when the pilots' hands are off the tiller, during take-off and landing, and permits the autopilot rudder channel to steer the aircraft on the ground during an autoland. 
For large aircraft the smallest radius turn can be achieved by using differential braking, nose wheel and body gear steering all together. In general, though, tight radius turns should be avoided whenever possible and never carried out at anything other than a very slow speed in order to prevent damage to tyres and reduce the sideways stresses on the undercarriage. During tight turns the loads are particularly high on the torque links of bogie type gear. 
Pilots of swept wing aircraft need to be particularly careful in ground manoeuvring as the radius of turn of the wingtip and tailplane is greater than that of the pilot's eye position.

Steering and Braking Systems 
Wheel Brakes 
Introduction 
Reverse thrust helps to slow a landing aircraft but the greatest retardation comes from the brakes. 
On light aircraft passive hydraulically operated single disc brakes are normally used to convert the kinetic energy of the aircraft into heat energy through friction. On larger aircraft hydraulic powered multi-plate disc brakes are used. 
A single disc brake consists of a disc rotating with the main wheel and a caliper unit attached to the main gear leg. Each brake unit is connected to a master cylinder attached to the toe brake pedal on the pilot's rudder bar. The pilot pressing down on the toe brake pedal creates hydraulic pressure this is transmitted to the caliper unit where a piston squeezes the disc between two friction pads. On releasing the brakes a small spring returns the piston to its former position. 
For light aircraft the rate of braking is controlled totally by the pilot. The ideal condition is where the wheels are turning slightly slower than the aircraft's forward speed. Ifthe pilot slows the wheels to a point where they are turning too slowly then a skid can develop. This may lead to loss of braking efficiency, loss of directional control, a flat spot in the affected tyre and ultimately a burst tyre. 

Single disc brake Brake unit housing Operating pad Fixed pad Torque plate 
Operating piston 
Disc 

Braking effectiveness reduces if the aircraft skids 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Brake Wear 
Any use of the brakes will result in wear of both the friction material ofthe pads and the disc this wear must be checked after every flight with the brakes applied. A measurement is taken between the disc and the brake housing, using a rule, a gauge or special tool. As the disc wears down in thickness the amount of heat energy that it is able to absorb is reduced, thus reducing the ability of the brakes to stop the aircraft. 
Single disc brake wear is measured between the disc and the housing when the brakes are on. 
If the brake disc becomes so hot that it is unable to absorb further heat the braking action ceases. This is called brake fade. 
Overheated brakes make chattering and squealing noises. Should the brake disc and pads fail to separate after the brakes are released, the brake is said to be dragging or binding. Dragging will increase brake temperatures. 
ft.ii+lo 

Measuring brake wear 
Disc Fixed pad 

Brake wear is checked with the brakes applied 
Brakes with steel discs work best when cold, carbon fibre brakes work best when hot Overheated brakes fade 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Steel and Carbon brakes 
Traditionally brake discs were made from steel but they are being replaced by carbon brakes which have several advantages. The main disadvantages with steel brakes are weight and brake fade at high temperatures. The main disadvantage with carbon brakes was price so use of carbon brakes was limited to long haul aircraft where the weight saving reduced fuel costs. For example, the weight saving on a 8767 is around 800 lbs. However, carbon brake prices have reduced and fuel prices risen so many modern aircraft now use carbon brakes. 
What are the other advantages of carbon brakes? First, they don't suffer from brake fade, in fact the reverse is true, as braking friction increases with an increase in temperature. Second, they can operate at higher temperature than steel brakes without suffering from distortion or structural damage. Third, they can absorb more energy than steel brakes. Fourth, they can dissipate heat energy more quickly than steel brakes so they cool more quickly. The combination of these four things means they are better at dealing with a large braking effort such as an aborted take-off. Regardless of brake type, heavy braking converts huge amounts of kinetic energy into heat and even carbon brakes have an upper temperature limit. 

After such braking there is normally a waiting period to allow the brakes to cool prior to another take-off. Brake temperature gauges help the pilots to estimate the waiting period and check that the brakes are cool enough to attempt a take-off. 
Other than cost what are the disadvantages of carbon brakes? In short, they don't work so well when cold and they wear quickly when cold. This influences how they might be used during taxi. 
Steering and Braking Syst ems 
ftiH+lo 
Taxi Braking Recommendations 
Carbon and steel brakes wear differently, so different taxi techniques are recommended for each type of brake. 
Steel brake wear is directly proportional to the kinetic energy absorbed by the brakes. Maximum brake life can be achieved by using a large number of small applications. High aircraft masses and speeds tend to reduce brake life because of the large amounts of kinetic energy absorbed. 
On the other hand, carbon brakes are less sensitive to aircraft mass, instead wear depends of the number of brake applications -one firm application results in less wear than several lighter applications. Therefore, it is recommended that the aircraft be permitted to accelerate to a higher speed during taxi followed by a single firm application to reduce speed rather than trying to maintain a constant taxi speed as with steel brakes. 
These are guidelines only, safety and passenger comfort remain the primary considerations. 


Steering and Braking Systems 
Multi Disc Brakes 
Large air transport aircraft use multi disc and multi piston brakes as these are able to absorb more kinetic energy. Traditionally, brakes have been hydraulically operated but recently designers are moving towards electric brakes as they are lighter, and cabling is easier to install and more reliable than piping. However, the current syllabus refers to hydraulic brakes which use the aircraft main hydraulic system for normal power supply on occasions the main system pressure is reduced by means of a pressure reducing valve downstream of the brake accumulator. 
Under manual braking the pilot operates the brake master cylinders with the toe pedals. The low hydraulic pressures created by the brake master cylinders operate a brake control valve that determines how much system pressure should be applied to the wheel brakes.

Steering and Braking Systems 
Multi Disc Brake Construction 
The body of a multi-disc unit is made up of a torque plate, a torque tube and a thrust ring. The torque plate is attached to the main gear leg and, as its name suggests, is designed to withstand the torque reaction of braking. The thrust ring is attached to the torque plate via the torque tube. The torque plate houses a series of interlinked single acting actuators and wear adjusters. The actuator pistons act on a pressure plate. The disc pack is between the pressure plate and the thrust ring this consists of alternate brake discs and friction plates. 
The discs have external lugs known as tenons, and these locate into drive blocks located on the wheel. The friction plates have lugs located on the inner circumference these locate over the castellation of the torque tube. The discs are referred to as rotors, the friction plates as stators. 
In ideal conditions, with a dry surface, it would be possible to put in more braking energy than the landing gear and attachment points are designed to cope with. This is because the brake pistons are over-sized so that they will provide the necessary braking action with wet discs and pads in adverse weather. To overcome this problem brake torque limiters are fitted that dump some of the brake pressure back to the hydraulic system as the design brake torque limits are approaching.

Rotor segment assembly 
Multi-disc brake 

In operation the pressure plate pushes the whole pack across onto the thrust ring. Because the heat energy absorbed by the multi-disc brake is so great, the discs are often made up of segments to allow for expansion and prevent warping. These are linked together the jigsaw plate is a common system of linking. 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Automatic Brake Wear Adjuster 
Automatic brake wear adjusters are used to make sure that there is adequate clearance between the rotors and the stators when the brakes are off. These consist of a housing, a guide, a spring, a retraction pin and a friction bush. The housing is attached to the torque plate, located inside the housing is the guide which incorporates the friction bush and the spring. The head of the retraction pin is held by the pressure plate, the pin passes through the torque plate and friction bush to protrude out of the end of the housing. 
The distance between the torque plate and the pressure plate is indicated by how much the retraction pin sticks out. When the brakes are applied the pressure plate pulls the pin with it, the friction in the bush holds the guide onto the pin, so the guide moves with the pin which in turn compresses the spring. When the brakes are released the spring's force pulls the pressure plate back, allowing the disc pack to separate.

Steering and Braking Systems 
Automatic Brake Wear Adjuster continued 
As the pack wears, the guide will bottom before the pressure plate stops moving, so the pin is pulled through the friction bush. On release the guide returns to its original position so the pressure plate will be pulled back the same distance. 
The brake wear can be measured using the distance between the retraction pin and the housing. Some pins have a mark around them, when this is proud of the housing the brake wear is acceptable. Whatever method is used, special tool, gauge, rule or indication, the actual measurement is being taken between the torque plate and the pressure plate.

Steering and Braking Systems 
Anti-Skid 
Maximum braking is achieved when the maximum torque is applied to the wheel in opposition to rotation. If too much torque is applied the wheel locks and a skid develops with reduced friction between the wheel and surface reducing braking efficiency. Anti ­skid systems are fitted to prevent the brakes 'locking up' the wheels by releasing brake pressure before the wheel stops turning thereby achieving optimum braking performance. Anti­skid systems operate: 
¦ 
In normal conditions 

¦ 
In icy conditions 

¦ 
In wet conditions 


It is normal for anti-skid to be selected 'on' for every landing and take off. 
There are two methods of skid control, the Maxaret (mechanical), and the electronic. The electronic system is more modern and more effective. 
Both systems function by controlling the hydraulic pressure that is felt in the brake unit. By releasing some ofthe pressure the wheel will be allowed to speed up again before full system pressure is reapplied. 

This keeps each brake unit braking and at the optimum which is a point just before the skid. 
If a wheel locks when the aircraft is rolling the system will fully release the brakes on that wheel until it spins back up, this is called locked wheel skid control. At taxi speeds, typically below a speed of 20 KT, the anti-skid system is inoperative so the aircraft can be brought to a halt. The system is also inoperative when the parking brakes are applied. 
If fitted and serviceable anti-skid will be on for take-off and landing 
Not all the brake pressure is released. Anti-skid is inoperative below 20 KT 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Maxaret System 
The Maxaret system is an older hydro-mechanical method of anti­skid not found on modern aircraft. It mechanically senses the speed of the wheel, a skid causes this to drop to a point where a valve opens, allowing some of the pressure in the brake to bleed to return. This system uses the modulating valve described in the hydraulics section of these notes.

Steering and Braking Systems 
Electronic Anti-Skid 
Electronic anti-skid units use a tachogenerator that is driven by the wheels. The tachogenerator produces an electric signal that varies with speed. This signal is sent to the anti-skid controller where wheel speed is compared with the aeroplane reference speed. If a wheel is turning too slowly the anti-skid controller will electrically signal an anti-skid valve to release sufficient pressure from the brake unit so that the wheel can speed up. Conversely, if the pilot is using manual braking and applying maximum force to the brake pedals, the anti-skid system will maintain a specified slip ratio to achieve the highest retardation possible in the conditions. 
In the case of hydroplaning the system will detect a high slip rate and reduce the pressure to restore wheel rotation. 
The inputs to a modern anti-skid computer are: 
¦ 
Idle Wheel Speed (measured). 

¦ 
Braked Wheel speed. (measured). 

¦ 
The desired wheel slip rate.

Steering and Braking Systems 
Touchdown and Bounce Protection 
To stop the wheel brakes being fully applied and locking the wheels up before landing a protection circuit is sometimes fitted in the control unit to prevent the brakes being applied. This means a pilot can, in theory, land with anti-skid on and his feet hard on the toe brakes without damaging the tyres. Needless to say, this is not the recommended operational practice. 
If a wheel bounces after touchdown there is a possibility that the brakes will lock up the wheel before it touches down again. Bounce protection senses the wheel's lift off and releases the brake pressure from the affected unit. It then delays the re­application of the brake until the wheel has touched down and started to rotate. 

Failure Indications 
If the anti-skid system fails an amber warning light alerts the crew, the same light will be illuminated if the system is selected off. The braking system reverts to manual operation without anti-skid the event offailure. Failure of the anti-skid system has serious effects on both take-off and landing performance, leading to a 50% increase in landing distance. 
Failure of the anti-skid system can lead to a 50% increase in landing distance 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Automatic Braking 
Auto Brake is a modern system that can bring the aircraft to a complete stop using constant g deceleration. This reduces stress on the airframe and greatly enhances passenger comfort during the landing. 
The auto brake system uses anti-skid therefore auto brake can only function with a serviceable anti-skid. The pilot selects the desired rate of deceleration on an auto brake control panel before take-off and landing. 
Prior to landing the pilot will normally select a rate of deceleration. The selections vary between aircraft. The diagram opposite shows a Boeing 737 with 1,2,3 and MAX. 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Automatic Braking continued 
The diagram opposite shows an Airbus A380 panel with LO, 2 3 and HI. The MAX selection is confusing because it is not maximum braking effort but the highest value of a fixed deceleration rate. To avoid confusion, Airbus changed the name from MAX to HI. To reiterate, each setting is a specified deceleration rate. 
Both panels, shown opposite and on the previous page, also have a rejected take-off selection, mentioned later in this lesson this does give the maximum braking effort. 
The A380 has an interesting switch position called BTV meaning Brake To Vacate. The pilots can insert the landing runway into the system and select a turn off. The braking will be to a value such that the aircraft will be at 10 KT when it reaches the turn off. It is claimed to save around 20% wear on brakes and tyres and minimise runway occupancy time. The aircraft will also inform the pilots if the runway is too short in both wet and dry conditions. 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Automatic Braking continued 
When the aircraft touches down the brakes are applied once the thrust levers have been retarded to idle and the wheels have spun up to speed. Brake system hydraulic pressure is directed to the brakes and the auto brake controller compares the aircraft's rate of deceleration to the selected rate and varies the pressure accordingly. 
The selection of reverse thrust, and spoilers will reduce braking force to maintain the selected deceleration rate. The system can be used to bring the aircraft to a complete stop in an auto land situation. The anti-skid system and the runway conditions (slippery runway) will determine the absolute maximum braking available for the landing. The pilot can alter the demanded rate of deceleration while the aircraft is braking. 
If the pilot presses the toe brakes when auto brake is selected, the system automatically cancels, and the braking reverts to manual with anti-skid. Modern aircraft will usually both land and take-off with auto braking selected, older aircraft only use it for take-off.
