Steering and Braking Syst ems 
Rejected Take-Off (RTO) 
Maximum Braking is applied when RTO is selected. If the take-off is abandoned before lift-off as the thrust levers are retarded the auto brake system will apply the brakes at the maximum deceleration possible. This is not a set deceleration rate it is maximum braking effort. 
Again, if the pilot touches the toe brakes when auto brake is selected, the system automatically cancels, and the braking reverts to manual with anti-skid. After a heavy weight RTO, the tyres on the main wheels are likely to need to be changed. 
Maximum auto brake is a set deceleration rate and will not slow the aircraft down as quickly as RTO. 
Should the auto-brake system fail the controller will revert to normal anti-skid and a fail light will illuminate. 
In-flight braking. 
On most aircraft the wheel brakes are automatically applied when the gear is selected to 'UP'. The idea is to protect components in the wheel well from loose tread on a spinning wheel. Nose wheels don't have brakes so a snubber (brake pad) fitted in the wheel well retards the wheels on retraction. 
A rejected take-off trashes the tyres 
Steering and Braking Syst ems 
The three modes of operation 
The auto brake system has three modes of operation: OFF, ARMED and Activated/Deactivated. 
OFF -The auto brake system will not function. The pilot will use manual braking with anti-skid if it is available. 
ARMED prior to taxi -During taxi prior to take off the pilot selects Rejected Take Off (RTO), Normal braking for taxi can be used but RTO mode is now armed but not activated. To activate, the aeroplane must exceed a target speed, typically around 70 kts and the pilot must have selected idle power. On the B737, the spoilers will deploy and full pressure is applied to the brakes. 
ARMED during flight for landing. -Prior to landing the pilot selects the required braking. The system is now armed. It activates when landing by wheel spin up and power levers to idle. 
The system is normally deactivated by the pilot depressing the brake pedals to take control of the brakes. 


Steering and Braking Systems 

Brake Temperatures 


Excessive brake temperatures reduce braking effectiveness and create a fire risk. To reduce the heat in the brake units electrically controlled cooling fans may be fitted, switching on and off as required. 
An indication of brake temperature is given to the pilot either on an analogue gauge or via numbers in boxes on the ECAM or EICAS screen. 
A mechanical gauge is shown here. When the switch lights for individual brakes are selected the gauge will show individual brake temperatures, only one can be selected at any one time. When no brakes are selected the gauge will show the highest brake temperature. Brake overheats are indicated by the switch light showing OVHT. 
Brake overheat indications can be amber, red or white according to type. 
Brake overheat indications can be amber, red or white according to type 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Brake Temperatures 
This is an electronic brake temperature indicating system where temperature of each brake is shown as a number in a box, the numbers and outline of the box change colour as the temperature increases, on this system 3 and white are the hottest. 
Any brake overheats must be reported to the Fire section via ATC and the aircraft must not be refuelled until the brakes have been checked. Operations Manuals would also prevent the aircraft being moved until the brakes cool down. This can take up to an hour and can prove embarrassing some airports have giant mobile air cooling units which can assist. 
A brake overheat will immobilise the aircraft for some time. 
Steering and Braking Systems 
Parking Brake 
All aircraft have a parking brake system that locks hydraulic pressure into the wheel brakes. Where the parking brake uses a separate power source it can be used in an emergency, as a means to stop the aircraft. The parking brake disengages anti­skid and there is no differential control of the brakes. 
Emergency Braking Systems 
It is a certification requirement that if there is any system or component failure the aircraft must be brought to a controlled stop within 1.5 times the normal braking distance. If the hydraulics completely fail an accumulator in the brake circuit will provide a limited number of braking applications (about 6) 
An emergency braking power source may be provided and can be supplied by compressed gas or from dedicated electrical pumps that pressurise the wheel brake accumulators. This system also allows operation of brakes when the aircraft is towed on the ground without having to start the main engines. 
When an emergency brake selection is made the anti-skid system is made inoperative. 

Stopping in the Shortest Possible Distance 
As we have seen aircraft braking systems, at least on large aircraft, are fitted with anti-skid units. This means the most effective braking occurs with the brakes fully applied and the anti­skid units fully operating. On a jet most of the retardation on the landing run or rejected take-off comes from the brakes rather than the thrust reversers. 
The shortest possible landing distance will be achieved by crossing the threshold at the correct height and speed, applying maximum foot pedal braking with anti-skid on touch down, and by applying reverse thrust and extending the speed brakes or lift dumping devices as soon as possible. 
If the hydraulics completely fail the brake accumulator will give limited braking 
The shortest stopping distance is obtained by applying maximum foot pedal braking with anti-skid and thrust reversers or for a rejected take-off RTO setting and thrust reversers

Wheels and Tyres 
Aircraft wheels and tyres are designed to withstand high speeds and impact loads. As aircraft landing speeds and weights have increased, large low-pressure tyres have given way to smaller 
SPLIT HUB
high-pressure tyres. This has allowed them to be retracted into smaller wheel bays and the use of bogies allows four or more wheels on one leg to share the load.

The detachable flange wheel, as its name suggests, has a flange that can be removed. When a tyre has been fitted the flange is held in place by a locking ring. The pressure in the tyre forces the flange onto the locking ring making it secure.

spot
The tyres provide the necessary friction between the aircraft and the surface for braking and steering and can be designed to make electrical contact between the aircraft and the ground and dissipate any static charge of the aircraft. 
Two types of tyre are used. They can be either tubed or tubeless. Tubeless tyres have many advantages and are used for most commercial airliners. 
Awl marks 
As with motor vehicles, aircraft tyres have a maximum speed rating, often 225 MPH. The speed rating is based on practical testing to ensure the tyre remains safe. At high aircraft weights and high-density altitudes the tyre speed limit may restrict the aircraft Maximum Take-Off Mass. 
Size 
Wheels and Tyres 
Tyre Construction 
Tyres are not fabricated just from rubber they would be too weak and flexible. Within the rubber are a series of plies of cord and steel that acts as reinforcement. 
The tyre construction starts with the bead, which is formed around a series of steel wire rings laid side by side, around which strands of nylon or rayon are layered each strand is coated in rubber. These are built up in layers, collectively referred to as the carcass these give the tyre its strength. 
The tyre's strength is indicated by its ply rating, an 8 ply tyre being stronger than a 6 ply, the higher the number the stronger the tyre. If the layers of the carcass are laid across each other at an angle the tyre is called a cross ply (or bias ply, an older term). 
In a radial ply tyre the carcass plies run from bead to bead ­radially. To add further strength additional belts of steel or Kevlar are added to the radial tyres with the ply running circumferentially, in the direction of travel. This outer belt also helps the tyre to resist punctures. 
Aircraft tyres can be remoulded (retreaded) several times when they are worn by bonding new rubber to the carcasses. 
Steel wire core 
The ply rating indicates the strength of the tyre

Wheels and Tyres 

n +e+•o 
Tubed Tyres  
Tubed tyres use an inner tube of gas resistant rubber as a bladder  
to hold the inflation medium, usually nitrogen for transport aircraft.  
The inner tube has a thin inflation tube and valve that protrudes  
through a hole in the wheel.  
When a new tyre is fitted to a wheel it takes up to five landings for  
the bead to bed in against the knurled wheel flange properly.  
During this time the friction on landing can cause circumferential  
movement of the tyre in relation to the wheel flange, called tyre  
"creep".  
To monitor the creep, a bar is painted across the wheel flange and  
tyre called a creep mark. It is 1" (25 mm) wide for tyres of 24" (60  
cm) or less in diameter and 1.5" (37 mm) for wheels over 24" (60  
cm) diameter. The maximum permissible creep is indicated when  
the edge ofthe tyre mark aligns with the opposite edge of the  
wheel mark. This range limits the stress on the inflation valve of  
the inner tube.  
Aircraft tyres are inflated with nitrogen  
Maximum creep is 1" or 1.5" for wheels over 24"  

Wheels and Tyres 
Tubeless Tyres 
In the construction of tubeless tyres, a layer of gas resistant rubber like butyl is applied to the inner surface of the tyre. The side wall has a radial construction which makes the tyre more flexible and a chamfered bead provides a better fit to the wheel. This design has many advantages over the tube tyre. They are: 
¦ The tyre is lighter (no tube) 
• 
The tyre runs cooler (no friction between tube and tyre) 

• 
Lower risk of bursting (when punctured) 


¦ There is no inflation valve to rip out 
• 
Better adjustment to the wheels (no knurled flange required) 

• 
The tyre is more flexible 


It must be remembered that tubeless tyres need to be fitted to wheels designed for their use.

• 
Reached after 5 landings 

• 
1.5 inches for tyres of 24 inches or less in diameter and 1 inch for wheels over 24 inches diameter 


• Indicated when the edge of the tyre mark aligns with the opposite edge of the wheel mark

Around the bead of a tubeless tyre are a series of small green or grey paint marks, called awl marks. Awl marks indicate where small holes were made during manufacture to let entrapped gasses out. A tyre must have these marks. 
There will be a red spot painted on the sidewall: this indicates the light spot of the tyre which will be placed against the inflation adapter in the wheel, which is the wheel's heavy spot unless otherwise marked with a B. There will of course be creep marks on the tyre as well, if it is a tubed tyre.

Wheels and Tyres  
Marstrand Tyre and Chined Tyres  
The Marstrand tyre is a single tyre used on castoring nose wheels.  
It has two contact areas, this double contact, helps to eliminate  
shimmy as each area will resist the oscillation. These tyres can be  
used as the contact areas wear away until the centre section  
starts to contact the ground.  
A chined tyre has a strip of rubber that is moulded onto one or both  
sides of the tyre. These chine's deflect the water displaced by the  
tread back onto the runway. They can be used on either the nose  
or the main wheels and are often used to prevent water ingestion  
by engines mounted behind the wheels.

Wheels and Tyres  
Tyre Damage  
Aircraft tyres are designed for the short duration, high-speed loads  
of normal take off and landing. Tyre damage can be caused by  
high taxy speed, low tyre pressure, hard landings, excessive load,  
runway surface faults, contamination and ageing. The  
recommended maximum taxy speed is 22 mph, often given as 20  
KT.  
When the carcass of the tyre is damaged a blister on a sidewall  
indicates that the plies of the carcass are delaminating. The tyre  
must be replaced.  
Too low a tyre pressure will cause excessive wear on the  
shoulders. Too high a tyre pressure will cause excessive wear on  
the crown. This diagram illustrates excessive shoulder wear  
caused by low inflation pressure.  
Tyre pressures that are too high cause wear on the crown,  
pressures that are too low cause wear on the shoulder  

Wheels and Tyres 
Tyre Damage continued 
Rough runway surfaces will score and cut the tyres. If these are deep enough to sever the plies the tyre is said to be cut to cords and must be changed. If these cuts are not as deep as the cords the cut can be filled with tyre putty. Heavy braking on a cross­grooved runway when it is dry causes a series of chevron shaped cuts. Any object that penetrates the tyre should be left in situ and expert advice taken. 
Oil or solvents spilt on a tyre can damage the rubber causing it to become soft and spongy the contaminants must be removed immediately and the affected area washed. 
Tyres age due to exposure to sunlight and ozone this causes small radial cracks in the surface of the sidewalls, which are also referred to as weathering cracks, and are acceptable unless the cords are exposed. Another indication of ageing is black carbon dust coming from the tyre. 
If a tyre bursts from a puncture, the load of that tyre is then carried by the remaining tyres on that axle or bogey. That increases the stresses on the remaining tyres, increasing the risk of them failing. 
One of the more spectacular failings on a tyre is a tread separation. As the name suggests, the thick pad of rubber, which was bonded on to the casing in manufacture by the process of vulcanisation, fails and comes away from the carcass. 


The flying debris from the separation can damage not only other tyres but also other parts of the airframe, especially if the failure is at high speed, when a failure is more likely anyway. A pilot will inspect the tyres for the above damage during the pre-flight walkaround 
This photograph shows a Boeing 737 tyre that failed due to sidewall fatigue which was not detected when it was retreaded. 
Wheels and Tyres  
Tyre Pressures  
The tyre pressures used on aircraft can be divided into two  
groups. High-pressure tyres have pressures between 200 and  
315 PSI. Low-pressure tyres have pressures up to 200 PSI.  
Modern transport aircraft use high pressure tyres.  
The manufacturer's tyre pressure that is marked on the side of the  
tyre is the unloaded bench pressure for the tyre. On fitment to the  
aircraft this pressure must be increased by 4% to allow for tyre  
deflection. Tyre pressures should be checked and adjusted only  
when the tyres are at ambient temperature.  
As the tyres are not completely gas tight a loss of pressure of up to  
5% can be expected over a 24 hour period.  
If, on a pre-flight check, the tyres are found to have lost between  
0% and 5% they are topped up to the correct pressure. If they are  
down between 5% and 10% then they are topped up and a record  
is made in the tech log for a further check when the tyres are cold. Should the same tyre be found to be down again it is rejected.  High pressure tyres allow faster landing speeds  
If a tyre is found deflated it is replaced, if it was on a bogie then its  If a tyre on a bogie is replaced its axle partner is replaced as well  
axle partner is replaced as well. If two or more tyres on a bogie  
are deflated then all the tyres are replaced.  

Wheels and Tyres 
Aquaplaning or Hydroplaning 
Either of these terms can be used to refer to a condition where the tyre's tread is unable to clear all the surface water from under the wheel. Water is then pushed ahead of the tyre and forms a wedge that lifts the tyre off the runway. This results in a loss of braking and directional control. The tyre can become scalded and blister as friction causes the water to heat up and eventually boil as it is pushed along the runway.The speed at which aquaplaning occurs on a rotating wheel can be calculated using the formula 
9. (tyre pressure in PSI)= The speed of aquaplaning in KT 
The aquaplaning speed on a non-rotating wheel is less 
7 .7. (tyre pressure in PSI)= The speed of aquaplaning in KT 
From these formulae it can be seen that the higher the tyre pressure the higher the speed for the onset of aquaplaning. This can become a limiting factor when working out take off and landing speeds. Beware that these formula only apply to one form of aquaplaning, and there are several. Reverted rubber aquaplaning can occur at very low speeds.The use of the anti-skid system will help to prevent the wheel from aquaplaning by releasing brake pressure and allowing the wheel to speed up and clear the water from under the tyre. 


The aquaplaning speed on a rotating wheel is 9.p in PSI 
Wheels and Tyres 
Fusible Plugs 
Heavy braking of the aircraft can generate excessive heat that could cause the pressure in a tyre to increase to such a level it could explode. In a tubeless tyre a fusible or thermal plug is used as a means of deflating an overheated and therefore over pressurised tyre before this can happen. 
The plugs are fitted into the wheel rims and have an insert that is designed to melt at a certain temperature. When these melt they allow the tyre to deflate relatively quickly but not explosively. The tyre and wheel would then have to be replaced but additional damage from an exploding tyre would be avoided. 
A further safety enhancement is that the nitrogen venting from the plug will normally eject on the same side as the brake disc, where it will help prevent a brake fire. 


Blown thermal plugs will deflate the tyre within seconds

Pneumatic Systems 
Pneumatic Systems 
Air driven or pneumatic systems are all systems driven by air or compressed gas. Pneumatic systems operate much like hydraulic systems and will employ controlling valves, filters and selector valves. They fall into four main categories. The first category contains systems that are driven by a constant supply of compressed air, usually derived from engine driven compressor pumps or from gas turbine engine compressor bleeds. Into this category fall: 
• 
Air conditioning 

• 
Pressurisation 

• 
Instruments 

• 
Hot air anti-icing 

• 
De-icing boots 

• 
Operation of secondary flight controls 

• 
Control ofjet pipe nozzles 

• 
Control of thrust reverser systems 

• 
Door seals 

• 
Air starting systems 

• 
Pressurised hydraulic reservoir 

• 
Air driven hydraulic pumps 


ft.ihtrl o 

On some installations the constant supply of air is used to replenish storage tanks which can then be used for emergency back up of a system. 
The second category contains systems that use bottled nitrogen or some other relatively inert gas as their main operating mechanism. 
Into this category fall: 
¦ 
Fire extinguishers 

¦ 
Life jackets 

¦ 
Slide rafts 



The third category contains systems that use bottled nitrogen or similar to provide a 'one-shot' back up when all other systems have failed. Systems that use pneumatics as a last resort are: 
¦ 
Undercarriage 'down' selection 

¦ 
Wheel brakes 



The final category is vacuum pumps. Some older piston engine aircraft use small vacuum pumps to provide a vacuum for gyroscopic instruments. 
Certain pneumatic systems use the air once, then dump it overboard. These are called total loss systems . 
Pneumatic Systems 
The Advantages of Pneumatic Systems 
The principle advantage of pneumatic systems is that air is readily available. Piston engines can be used to drive air compressors and gas turbine engines always have a ready supply of clean pressurised air for use. One subsidiary advantage is that, although air supports combustion, an air-driven system presents much less of a fire risk than a hydraulic system and can be used in high temperature areas. Another is that, because gas is compressible, pressurised gas can be used as a shock absorber. A third is that compressed gas occupies a relatively small volume at minimal mass and yet can expand to fill a large area. 
ft+i.+l o 

The Disadvantages of Pneumatic Systems 
Pneumatic systems have three main disadvantages: 
¦ 
Because gas is compressible it is not usable in systems that require precise and rapid responses like flying controls. 

¦ 
Because gas is much less viscous than fluid it is very difficult to seal high pressure pneumatic components. 

¦ 
Because gas is colourless and odourless, leaks in pneumatic systems are difficult and time consuming to trace. 


Pneumatic Systems 
Gas Turbine Engine Bleed Air Supplies 
The most common source of a constant air supply is engine bleed air from the compressor of gas turbine engine, either a turbojet or a turboprop. Engine bleed air is regarded as being at a comparatively low pressure and being available in high volume. 
The control valves, called engine bleed isolation/shut off valves, can be selected by the crew in normal operation and are closed automatically when the appropriate engine fire handle is pulled. Taking bleed air from the engines reduces mass flow through the engine, and therefore the thrust, so engine bleeds are often selected off for take-off. 
Pressure regulating valves, along with temperature and pressure sensors, protect both the aircraft systems and the persons on board from over-temperature or over-pressure events. 
On modern large gas turbines air may be bled from the IP and HP compressors. On leaving the engine bleed valves the IP and HP air may be mixed to regulate pressure by a pressure regulating valve. It may also pass through a heat exchanger where it is cooled by fan air. From there it is ducted into a gallery of pipes known as the bleed air manifold where there are temperature and pressure sensors to provide feedback to the control system. From there on, further isolation/control valves control its delivery to the various services. 
ft+IH+lo 
Bleed air supply from high pressure compressor 


Pneumatic Systems 
Gas Turbine Engine Bleed Air Supplies continued 
Bleed air is not only available from the main engines but may also be supplied by the Auxiliary Power Unit (APU), either in the air or on the ground. It is not unusual to use an electrical starter motor to start the APU then use engine bleed air from the APU to start the other engines. 
While they are on the ground, aircraft can be supplied from ground sources via ground pneumatic connections.

Pneumatic Systems 
Compressor Air Supplies 
Some older turbine engine aircraft did not have sufficient bleed air available for the pneumatics, and in that case a dedicated compressor, usually centrifugal, was driven from the auxiliary gearbox on the engine. 
Piston engine aircraft need air for instrumentation, cabin heating and de-icing. Air may be supplied by a single cylinder air cooled compressor driven by the engine, either by belts or from the gearbox or use pressurised air from a turbocharger system. If the air is supplied by the supercharger, engine power will be reduced. A vacuum pump may be used to supply some older gyroscopic instruments.

Which of the following statements is UNTRUE? 
• Bleed air may be supplied by an APU 
• Bleed air may be taken from the HP compressor of a gas turbine engine 
• Engine bleed air is considered to be high presssure 
• Use of engine bleed air reduces thrust 
Pneumatic Systems 
The most common applications of pneumatics are in pressurisation and air-conditioning. These will be considered in detail in the next section. The following systems may also use pneumatics: 
Emergency Brakes 
Compressed air is normally fed to the system via a shuttle valve and a control valve, which allows proportional pressure into the system, depending on the amount an emergency brake handle is turned. 
Fixed Fire Extinguisher Bottles 
On all modern transport aircraft, the engine and APU fire extinguisher bottles are pressurised by nitrogen to deliver the extinguishant where it is required. The portable fire extinguishers are pressurised with various mediums depending on their use. 
Emergency Undercarriage Blow Down. 
Some aircraft use pneumatics for the emergency backup for undercarriage extension in case of total hydraulic failure. To reduce the risk of fire the system is normally pressurised with nitrogen, as it is an inert gas which will not react with hydraulic fluid. The pressures in these systems are normally high, in the vicinity of 3000 PSI, as the air is required to do the work normally done by powerful hydraulic pumps.

Pneumatic Systems 
Carbon Dioxide 
Life vests and life rafts, are normally inflated by individual, self-contained, CO2 bottles. 
Anti-icing and De-icing Systems 
Hot air is used in jet engine intakes and to provide ice protection in the leading edges of the wings and empennage ofjet aircraft. Bleed air is also used in turboprops to inflate de-icing boots on the leading edges. 
Hydraulic Systems 
Hydraulic systems usually contain a pneumatic accumulator containing nitrogen charged to approximately half of system pressure. The accumulator damps out fluid pressure fluctuations in the system and maintains system pressure so that it is instantly available when a service is selected. 
ft+H+lo 

Oleo Legs 
Pneumatics are used, together with oil, to absorb shocks in undercarriage systems on landing. 
Door Seals 
Low pressure air can be used to inflate rubber door and hatch seals to make the cabin airtight for pressurisation. This is rare on modern aircraft. 
Air Starters 
Whilst small turbine engines can use electrical starter motors, most large gas turbine engines are started by directing bleed air, which is obtained from the APU, a ground air supply or, from an engine that is already running, on to the turbine air driven starter motor. 
This starter motor is similar to the motor illustrated on the next page, the air turbine motor. 
Pneumatic Systems 
ft.f:h+lo 
Air Turbine Motors 
Drive gearElectrical output
Air Turbine Motors (ATMs) are small turbines driven by engine bleed air. 
On some older aircraft they are used to drive hydraulic pumps that can back up the main system. On modern aircraftATMs sometimes drive constant frequency AC generators. 

Pneumatic Systems 
ft+M+lo 
Jet Pipe Nozzle Control 
Pneumatics are used in military aircraft to control the position of after-burner nozzles. The use of air in this area removes any fire risk that would be present if hydraulic fluid, or oil, were to be used. 
Thrust Reverser Control 
Thrust reversers that operate in hot stream air can use pneumatics as a control mechanism. The advantage is, as before, the reduced fire risk. Pneumatic rams or air driven motors drive the thrust reversal doors to their selected positions.

It is interesting that in this system only the HP air is regulated, the LP air is free to move through the NRV provided it is at a higher pressure than the downstream air. 
Note also how the fan air through the heat exchanger is regulated to control the temperature.

Pneumatic Systems 
Cockpit Controls, Indications and System Failures 
The pneumatic system is remotely controlled from the cockpit by the operation of shut off and selector valves to the various services. System indications may include pressure and temperature gauges. 
High pressures and temperatures can cause failure of the ducting and possible damage to the surrounding airframe and engine structures, whereas low pressure could be an indication of a supply malfunction, or a leak in the system. Overheat sensors are normally located by the bleed air take off points and within the ducting, providing alerting indications to the flight deck. 
Depending on the fault and its location the impact on the flight may be small or serious. For example, isolating one wing because of a leak could prohibit flight through icing because wing leading edge anti-icing is lost. The X-bleed valve protects pressurisation to some extent, but it may reduce the altitude you can attain and therefore restrict flight range. 



Lesson 77 
Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 

AGK 77 

Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft.ihil o 
Introduction 
This section looks at how cabin temperature is regulated. 'Conditioned air' is defined as air that has been controlled in respect to ventilation, temperature, and pressure. Humidity is generally limited but not controlled. In this lesson we'll examine temperature control. 
The Need for Temperature Control 
The outside air temperature at height can be very low, often below -60°C, whereas the temperature on the ground can be very high, as much as +45° in some parts of the world. The aircraft temperature control systems need to be able to cope with such large extremes and still maintain a comfortable cabin temperature. 
Light Aircraft Temperature Control 
Light aircraft temperature control systems are essentially heaters. There is no air conditioning in high temperatures you just open the window. 

The Need for Pressure Regulation 
The atmosphere is made up of 21 % Oxygen, 78% Nitrogen and 1 % other gases. The percentages of these gases remain constant throughout the lower atmosphere but, as altitude increases, their density, pressure and temperature decrease. 
At altitudes above approximately 10 000 ft there is insufficient air pressure to allow the gaseous exchange across the alveoli in the lungs. This can lead to hypoxia and its associated problems of loss of mental ability, loss of consciousness and possible death. Pressurising the cabin of the aircraft overcomes these problems and allows us to breathe normally at altitude without having to wear oxygen masks. 
Pressurisation is achieved by ducting engine bleed air into the cabin and controlling the rate at which it is let out. 
Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ftiihi·lo 
Large Aircraft Temperature Control 
Temperature control on large aircraft, particularly jets and turboprops, is achieved by taking hot bleed air from the engines and splitting the supply into two. One part is cooled and is then re­mixed with the hot air to obtain the correct temperature. The hot air can be added at different locations on the aircraft to give varying temperatures. This is called hot trim air. The temperature-controlled air is also used to pressurise the aircraft, if pressurisation is required. 
The engine bleed air is called 'charge air'. 
The devices that deliver conditioned air are called air conditioning packs. There will be two or three packs on a transport aircraft depending on its size. 


This is the air conditioning system from an Airbus A340 showing the heat exchangers on the left, air cycle machine, condenser, re­heater and water extractor. Out of view to the right are the ozone converter and the pack valve. 
Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft+IFf·io 

Air Cooling Systems Pressurisation systems are required because: 
Air can be cooled by: 
¦  Passing warm air over a cooler surface, a heat exchanger ­heat exchangers are most efficient when there is a large temperature difference between the hot and cold sides.  
•  Making it do work. The more work done, the more cooling that takes place.  
•  Allowing the air to expand -the more expansion the more cooling.  

Air conditioning packs use some or all of the three cooling methods in different proportions. They are either air cycle or vapour cycle systems. The system on which the examinations are based is the bootstrap or turbo compressor system. 
Air is cooled by passing it over a cold surface, making it do work or allowing it to expand. 

• At altitudes above approximately 10 000 ft there is insufficient air pressure to force the oxygen required into 
our lungs to allow us to breathe normally 
• The percentage of oxygen in the air at altitudes above approximately 10 000 ft is too low 
• At altitudes above approximately 10 000 ft the air density is insufficient to force the oxygen required into our lungs 
to allow us to breathe normally 
• There is insufficient oxygen present at altitudes above approximately 10 000 ft 
Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
Bootstrap Systems 

The bootstrap system can be used with either a mechanical blower or a turbine engine. It is an air cycle system which relies on compression, expansion and work done by the air itself to reduce the temperature ofthe charge air. 
The core of the bootstrap system is a compressor driven by a turbine which, in turn, is driven by the charge air. The cooling is by heat exchange with ram air and by making the charge air do work across a compressor and turbine assembly. The compressor and turbine assembly is used in the other air cycle systems and is referred to as a "cold air unit' or an "air cycle machine". 


Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft+ifff·io 
Function and Operation 
The Flow Control Valve reacts to the charge air density to ensure the correct mass of air enters the system. 
The bypass valves are also referred to as, and effectively are, temperature control valves. They are connected to temperature sensors either in the cabin or in the outlet duct leading to the cabin, and react automatically to maintain the selected temperature by splitting and mixing hot and cold air. 


Bypass valves control cabin temperature 

Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft+Milo 
Function and Operation continued 
The air entering the cooling unit of the system passes through the primary heat exchanger (also referred to as a pre-cooler) where, in the cruise, it gives up temperature to ram ambient air without effective loss of pressure. On the ground and during slow flight the cooling flow is provided by pack fans. These fans can be electrically driven or driven via gears from the GAU. 
The stream of cooler air from the primary heat exchanger enters a second bypass valve where it is mixed with a little of the charge air that bypassed the pre-cooler. The output ofthe second bypass valve is directed into the eye of the centrifugal compressor of the Cold Air Unit (GAU). 


The primary heat exchanger cools the air 
A second bypass valve opens to allow some air to bypass the second cooling stage 

Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft.Hf·llo 
Function and Operation continued 
The CAU consists of a centrifugal compressor driven by a turbine. The compressor pressurises the air, and so heats it. It then passes over a secondary heat exchanger or intercooler and is directed onto the edge ofthe turbine. The intercooler produces a large drop in charge air temperature but only reduces the charge pressure slightly. 
As the air passes through the turbine it loses energy in two ways, firstly by doing work to rotate the turbine which, in turn, has to drive the compressor and secondly as it expands across the turbine. As it leaves the turbine the air pressure has been reduced again and the temperatures will be very low, close to freezing. The major part of the cooling, however, took place across the secondary heat exchanger. 
The air flowing from the cold air unit can be below the dew point, so an ice screen is fitted to prevent ice impacting in the water extractor. Alternative systems add some of the hot air to this flow to prevent any moisture freezing. 


Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft.f:ii/lo 
The Bootstrap 
The term 'bootstrap' is a shorthand expression for a device or system that starts and runs with no apparent external power supply used. The name comes from the American term to 'pull yourself up by your own bootstraps'. The bootstrap in this case is the turbo-compressor element, as the compressor is driven by the turbine rather than being driven by an external prime mover. 
Water Extractor 
A water separator is located in the outlet from the CAU. 
After passing through the turbine the air is still swirling and the heavier molecules of water are thrown to the outside by centrifugal action. The water extractor consists of a woollen sock over a metal frame. The water is soaked up by the sock and the air flows on through to the cabin. The water is dumped overboard through a drain. If the water extractor starts to freeze warm air can be directed on to the unit. 
The combination ofthe turbo-compressor and the water extractor reduce the relative humidity to something like 15% which allays corrosion caused by condensation. Very few aircraft control humidity it is normally limited rather than controlled. 


The bootstrap is the CAU and the intercooler 

Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
ft+Milo 
Plenum 
The streams of air at different temperatures are mixed in a plenum, or mixing chamber before entering the cabin. The air temperature in the plenum chamber will be higher than that required in the cabin because there will be a small, final cooling process as the air expands into the cabin. 
Temperature Control on the Ground 
Air and vapour cycle systems will work on the ground as long as they have a source of charge air. If the main engines are not running, one pack can be operated using bleed air from the Auxiliary Power Unit (APU). 
If the APU is not available, it may be possible to connect the aircraft to an external ground conditioning unit. The ground unit supplies conditioned air which is distributed throughout the aircraft by the aircraft systems air conditioning ducts. 
Temperature Control in Flight 
The desired cabin temperature is selected on the flight deck controller, and then the system will schedule cooled, ambient and/or 'hot trim' air as necessary to achieve the desired temperatures. You can see the hot trim air and the relevant trim valves shown in amber on the next page. 

Air Conditioning Ducts 
The air ducts are made from: 
¦ 
Stainless steel for use with high pressure or high temperature air. 

¦ 
Light alloy for use with intermediate pressure or temperature air. 

¦ 
Plastic or fibreglass for use with low pressure or temperature air. 



If the air pressure in the high-pressure duct drops an amber warning light will illuminate, as this can indicate a burst duct. 
System Protection 
A shut off valve (isolation valve) isolates the air conditioning system from the engine in the event of contamination or fire. To ensure that the conditioned air is not delivered to the aircraft at excessive pressures or temperatures, a pressure reducing valve and temperature thermostat are fitted in the inlet manifold to the air conditioning ducting. 
Cabin Heating and Cooling Systems 
Cargo Compartments 
On most modern transport aircraft, the cargo compartments are also conditioned. This allows for the carriage of pets and livestock. However, the cargo compartments are supplied through separate isolation valves so they can be turned off. This means they lose conditioned air supply and so will become very cold. Nevertheless, they will remain pressurised. 
Recirculation Systems 
In modern aircraft recirculation fans fitted within the cabin and driven by AC electric motors force the conditioned air to flow back through the aircraft before it is exhausted to atmosphere. This helps to reduce the load on the air conditioning packs and also reduces the need to take so much bleed air from the engines, so saves fuel. 
Pressure and mass flow controller 
Ground connection 
Cabin ambient air Outflow vale 
Recirculating systems save fuel and reduce costs 



Lesson 72 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
AGK 72 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Pressurisation Systems 
An aircraft that flies at high altitude reduces its fuel consumption for a given air speed and avoids bad weather. However, while the percentage of oxygen remains constant at 21 %, any increase in altitude results in a drop in pressure. This in turn affects the amount of oxygen absorbed when breathing in. Lack of oxygen affects thought processes and the degree of consciousness ­hypoxia. 
Hypoxia is avoided by pressurising the cabin. Rather than expressing cabin pressure in Pascals or hPa we find it more useful to use the term cabin altitude. 
Pressurisation is achieved by feeding conditioned air into the cabin through the air conditioning packs and restricting its flow out. The pressure is controlled by a large valve fitted in the outside skin of the pressure hull called the outflow or discharge valve. The valves are often duplicated for safety. 
ft.ihi·-l o 
liy 

Restricted flow of conditioned air to atmosphere 
Pressurisation avoids hypoxia The cabin is pressurised by restricting the outflow 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
ft+i.i+lo 
Maximum Operating Altitude 
The maximum operating altitude of an aircraft is not entirely or solely related to pressurisation systems, but the EASA Learning Objectives mention it in the section on pressurisation, so we will touch upon it here. 
There are three main factors that determine the limit. 
The first is the maximum certified altitude as listed in the Aircraft Flight Manual -this is a structural limitation, defined by the pressurisation loads on the fuselage. 
Thrust limited altitude is determined by quite literally having sufficient thrust for manoeuvre and climb. 
Buffet limited altitude is that at which a 1.3g loading from turning, manoeuvring or turbulence may be safely flown without experiencing low speed stall or Mach buffet. 
The maximum operating altitude will be the lowest of any one of those three factors. 


Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Differential Pressure 
As the pressure in the cabin increases the difference between the cabin pressure and the ambient pressure will increase. This difference is called the differential pressure. The maximum differential pressure for a transport aircraft is a structural limit and usually between 7 and 9 psi. 
This graph shows aircraft altitude against cabin altitude and can be used to find the differential pressure. It can also be used to find the maximum altitude at which an aircraft can fly at given a cabin altitude and differential.

Differential Pressure -example 1 
The following example explains use of the graph. 
Example: 
Find the aircraft maximum altitude while maintaining a cabin altitude of 6000 ft and a differential of 4 psi. 
Solution: 
Locate the 6000 ft mark on the bottom axis ofthe graph. Trace a line vertically upward until it intersects the diagonal 4 psi differential line. Trace a horizontal across from the point of intersection to the vertical axis and read off the altitude, 17 000 ft. 
Similar questions can be asked based on tabular information. In­service defects like a crack in a window may impose a reduced differential pressure. The next example demonstrates how to use the tables.

Differential Pressure -example 2 
Example: 
Because of a crack in the windscreen the maximum differential pressure for an aircraft is restricted to 7 psi. If a cabin altitude of 4000 ft is to be maintained use the table to determine the maximum operating altitude. 
Solution: 
This is a standard atmosphere table. The only columns we are interested in are the first column, height, and the third column headed lb/in2. 4000 ft can be seen to equate to 
12.691 psi. If the maximum differential pressure is to be 7 psi then the greatest altitude will be the one where the pressure is 12.691 -7 = 5.691. This is 24 000 ft. 
The method can of course be worked in reverse to find the maximum cabin altitude given an aircraft altitude and differential pressure, or to find the differential pressure given the cabin and aircraft altitude.

~ysrem uperauon m r-11gm Climb 
During the climb the ambient pressure around the aircraft decreases and the cabin ambient pressure must also decrease. If the cabin goes up more slowly the aircraft will slowly pressurise. If the cabin is to climb then, in this phase of flight, the outflow valves allow more air out of the cabin than comes in. If operated correctly the cabin should arrive at its cruise altitude at the same time as the aircraft arrives it cruise altitude. 
This diagram shows the cabin rate of climb compared to the aircraft rate of climb for a well managed climb. The cabin has arrived at 8000 ft just as the aircraft arrives at its cruise altitude of 36 000 ft. 
A calculation can be made to establish the cabin rate of climb required to match the maximum aircraft rate of climb. 
Example: 
An aircraft takes 26 minutes to climb from an airfield at 2000 ft to a cruise level of 31 000 ft. What cabin rate of climb would raise the cabin altitude to 8000 ft in that period? 
Solution: 
The cabin has to go from 2000 ft to 8000 ft in 26 minutes. This needs a cabin rate of climb of 6000 7 26 = 231 ft/min

Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Cruise 
When the cabin altitude pressure (Zc) is at its selected cruise value the rate of discharge from the discharge valve decreases to equal the inflow from the mass flow controller. A constant pressure will be maintained in the cabin. This is called the isobaric range. 
This diagram shows a typical flight profile with the aircraft altitude in red and the cabin altitude in green. The cabin is raised to a fixed altitude of 8000 ft and minor variations in aircraft altitude are absorbed, the maximum differential pressure never being exceeded. 
Descent 
If the cabin discharge valve is moved towards the closed position the cabin pressure will increase, so the cabin altitude will decrease. The cabin altitude is descending and the aircraft is de­pressurising. Depending on the type of controller the aircraft will either be fully de-pressurised 500-1000 ft above the airfield or just after touch down.

Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
The Pressure Hull and System Components 
Air transport aircraft fuselages are designed and built as a pressure hull. The pressure hull encloses the flight deck, passenger cabin and cargo holds. It does not include the undercarriage bays. All pipes, cables and control runs that pass through the skin of a pressure hull have to be sealed against leakage of air. 
All external doors and hatches that give access into the pressure hull are also sealed. Most modern cabin doors are the plug type that, on opening, first move into the cabin. If any door or hatch is not properly closed and locked the flight crew is alerted by an amber light and also, in some cases, by an audible warning.

Safety valves

Hold wiring looms Dischargevalve 
The pressure hull does not include the undercarraige bays 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Outflow/Discharge Valves 
The outflow or discharge valves are electro-pneumatically operated. During normal operation the valves are automatically controlled by a flight deck pressure controller. The controller directs pneumatics to open or close the valves to maintain the selected cabin altitude, Zc, or the selected rate of climb or descent of the cabin, Vzc. 
Failure of automatic control requires the valves to be positioned manually by the crew. During manual operation the valves are operated by electric motors. 
Manual operation during climb and descent is time consuming. During the cruise the system stabilises requiring little effort. 
During flight the valves move between fully open and fully closed to control the pressure. 
Dump Valves 
Dump valves may be fitted to enable a rapid rate of reduction in cabin pressure when needed. An emergency descent may necessitate a faster than normal reduction, for example. Or it may be used to help clear smoke from a cabin fire. Dump valves are normally electrically operated, and may also be linked to a weight on wheels (WOW) switch to ensure that the cabin is completely depressurised on the ground. 
ft+M+lo 



operated pet control valve valve Cabin 
Bellows 

Ambient sensing port Ambient air 
The outflow valve can be called the discharge valve 
The outflow valves are partly open in the cruise 
If the differential pressure exceeds the maximum by 0.5 psi the normal control system has failed and the safety valves have operated 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Safety Valves 
Aircraft are fitted with two safety valves to protect the fuselage structure from over pressurisation in the event offailure of the normal control system. If the cabin pressure exceeds the max differential by 0.5 psi the safety valves open and control the cabin at this higher pressure differential. Safety valves may also be referred to as excessive differential pressure relief valves. 
Inward Relief Valves 
Inward relief valves limit negative differential pressure in an emergency descent 
Aircraft structures are not designed to withstand negative differential pressure, where the ambient pressure is greater than the cabin pressure. To prevent this happening, inward relief valves or negative pressure relief valves are fitted which open at a negative differential of 0.5 psi. 
Inward relief valves limit negative differential pressure in an emergency descent 

Blow Out Bungs 
To prevent the cabin floor from collapsing due to a differential pressure occurring between the cabin and cargo holds, plastic blow out bungs are fitted in the cabin floor over the length of the cargo bay. In the event of a decompression in either the cabin or the cargo bay the bungs will be blown out allowing the pressures to equalise. The bungs are also known as pressure equalising panels. 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Cabin Pressure Control Systems 
There are two types of cabin pressure controllers pneumatic on older aircraft and electronic on more modern aeroplanes. 
All pressure controllers have sensing elements that receive inputs of cabin pressure, rate of change of cabin pressure and atmospheric pressure. They can operate in either automatic or manual mode. The normal mode ofoperation is AUTO the manual mode is only usually used ifthere is a system failure. 
A cabin pressure controller operating in AUTO will regulate cabin altitude, cabin altitude rate-of-change and differential pressure. 
ft+iitii=fo 

Pneumatic Controller 
On the older pneumatic systems the cabin altitude and rate of change of cabin pressure is manually selected on the controller by varying the load on pressure sensors which then controls the operation of the outflow/discharge valves automatically. Indications of the selections are displayed on gauges in the cockpit. 
The pneumatic controller uses air pressure to control the position of the discharge valve, thus controlling the rate of outflow from the cabin. In the event of failure of the controller the pilots can assume manual control of the outflow or discharge valve. 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Flight Deck Indications 
The control and monitoring of the pressurisation and air conditioning system is normally carried out from one dedicated panel. A typical pneumatic control panel and combined cabin VS I/differential pressure/cabin altitude gauge called the triple gauge are shown in this diagram, both from a BAe146. 
The pressurisation control panel controls the discharge valves in both automatic and manual operating modes. The operating mode is selected by a push-button MODE switch and is normally set to AUTO. 
In the automatic mode the desired cabin altitude and flight altitude, the barometric correction, and the desired rate of climb and descent are set using the knobs at the bottom of the panel. The AUTO dial reflects the selected altitudes and the barometric correction which has been set. With AUTO selected the discharge valves automatically open and close to maintain the selected rate of climb to the selected cabin height. There is automatic over and under pressure protection. 


Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Flight Deck Indications continued 
The associated triple gauge shows the actual rate of change of cabin altitude, the differential pressure and the cabin altitude. 
If MAN is selected on the mode switch automatic control ceases and the MAN rotary control above the MODE switch becomes active. This is used to inch open and close the discharge valves to maintain the required cabin altitude, the discharge valve position being shown just above the rotary control. 
To the left of the discharge valve position indicators a second rotary switch operates in either AUTO or MAN to close individual discharge valves or, by selecting DITCH, to close them both. DITCH closes the outflow valve(s) to minimise water ingress following a landing on water. Automatic over and under pressurisation protection is also available in manual mode. 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Electronic Controllers 
Electronic control allows automatic control of the system in normal operation. 
There are two sources of electrical power that can operate the outflow valve, AC power for normal operation and DC power as a backup. This dual power source means a separate dump valve is not required. 
This diagram shows a typical modern control panel, actually from a B737. 
The departure airfield altitude is set in the window marked LAND ALT before take-off. With the FLT/GRD switch set to GRD the outflow valves are full open and the cabin is unpressurised. When the switch is selected to FLT before take off the outflow valves move towards close, slightly pressurising the cabin to improve passenger comfort and to prevent any surge in pressure. Ifthe take-off is conducted with the packs off they are selected on as the aircraft climbs through 500 ft or at the first power change. 
Having been set with the flight level and cabin altitude required, the controller acts in AUTO as a pressure controller deciding the rate of cabin climb and descent as it accesses the flight profile from the air data computer. 
CAB . 3 3 814192/93i94losos\5606 4681/6 80 FLT 18 20 22 24 26 28 30 32 34 36 
ALTITUDE X 1000 FEET -MAX PRESS SCHEDULE (7 8 PSI) 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Electronic Controllers 
During the climb the controller will bring the cabin up to its selected altitude. This can be faster than the 500fpm, but at no time must this exceed the maximum rate of 1800 ft/min. 
About 1000 ft before the cabin reaches its set altitude a cruise relay trips and the controller acts as an isobaric controller unless the maximum differential is exceeded when the controller acts as the max differential controller. 
For descent the cruise relay trips out and a descent relay trips in to schedule the descent to the selected LAND ALT. The controller programs the cabin to land at a slightly higher pressure than ambient so the aircraft lands pressurised. On touch down the ground logic switch tells the controller to start depressurising the aircraft when the FLT/GRD switch is selected to GRD. 
CAB . 3 3 814192/93i94losos\5606 4681/6 80 FLT 18 20 22 24 26 28 30 32 34 36 
ALTITUDE X 1000 FEET -MAX PRESS SCHEDULE (7 8 PSI) 
This aircraft lands pressurised 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Electronic Controllers 
If the aeroplane begins to descend without tripping out the cruise relay an amber 'OFF SCH ED DESCENT' light illuminates. 
In the event of the automatic system failing the controller will illuminate an amberAUTO FAIL light and selection to standby is required this action illuminates a green STANDBY light and extinguishes the AUTO FAIL light. Alterations in cabin or flight altitudes now have to be input manually, cabin rate of climb or descent is controlled by the cabin rate selector. 
In the event of standby failure the controller is selected to manual. 
In manual the only control the crew has is over the position of the outflow valve. It can be either opened or closed to increase or decrease the cabin height. In manual the outflow valve will not automatically open to de-pressurise the aircraft on landing. This means the doors cannot be opened until the outflow valve is opened manually. 
A ram air inlet is used for emergency pressurisation in the event of the loss of conditioned air.

Under manual control the aircraft will not automatically de­pressurise on landing 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
Decompression 
There are three categories of decompression, normal (6 to 10 seconds), rapid (4 to 6 seconds) and explosive (0 to 3 seconds). The time quoted is the time it takes the cabin pressure to equalise with the outside pressure. 
An explosive decompression will be accompanied by dust and mist in the air, rushing air in the cabin and, obviously, a drop in pressure. The drop in pressure sucks up the dust the mist is formed by condensation as temperature and pressure drop. 
The aircraft systems will at: 
¦ 
10 000 ft cabin altitude, give the flight crew a visual and aural CABIN ALT warning -the warning altitude. 

¦ 
13 850 ft cabin altitude, illuminate the AUTO FAIL light on the control. 

¦ 
14 000 ft cabin altitude, automatically present the passengers oxygen masks in the half-hung position. 


The pilots must put on their oxygen masks, manually close the outflow valves and initiate an emergency descent. 

Definitions Cabin altitude: cabin pressure expressed as a altitude. 
Cabin vertical speed: change in cabin pressure expressed as rate of change of cabin altitude. Normally known as cabin rate of climb or descent. 
Differential pressure: the difference between cabin pressure and ambient pressure normally expressed in psi. 
Ground pressurisation: pressurising the cabin on the ground for testing purposes. This is normally a procedure performed by maintenance staff. 
Cabin Pressurisation Systems 
New developments 
New aircraft such as the B787 save fuel by bleeding very little air from the engines. Electrically driven blowers supply air for the air conditioning system which is becoming widely known as the environmental control system (ECS). Further savings occur through not having to waste energy cooling or decreasing the pressure as the electric motor rpm is adjusted to maintain the desired pressure and flow rate.

Ice and Rain Protection 
EASA Regulatory Requirements 
EASA regulations state that operators must establish procedures to be followed when ground de-icing and anti-icing and related inspections of the aircraft are necessary. It prohibits a take-off unless the external surfaces are clear of any deposits, which might adversely affect the performance and controllability of the aeroplane, except as permitted in the Aeroplane Flight Manual. Some manuals state that the aircraft can take-off with a degree of hoar frost on the underside of the wings. 
EASA regulations prohibit a pilot from starting a flight under known or expected icing conditions, unless the aeroplane is certificated and equipped to cope with such conditions. 
ft+ii1-lo 

In Flight Icing Conditions 
In flight icing conditions are categorised by the amount of ice that forms and the speed at which it forms into light, moderate and severe.Icing conditions are said to exist in flight if the TAT is below +10°c either with visible moisture in the air or if the visibility is below 1500 m. Ice detection systems will warn that icing conditions have been encountered. Many systems work better if they are selected on before icing is encountered. 
All ice and frost must be removed before flight unless the Flight Manual permits some to remain 
Ice and Rain Protection 
Types of Icing Frost or Hoar Frost 
Frost on the ground forms mainly at night as the heat radiates from the earth. In flight frost or hoar frost forms when a cold airframe descends through warm air. It is most persistent on cold soaked aircraft on the ground in conditions of high relative humidity. 
Runback Icing, +10 to -3°C 
This forms in flight when warmer rain hits a cold airframe. The aircraft's speed is sufficiently high to prevent it freezing on impact so the water runs back and freezes as a clear glaze. 
Glaze or Clear Ice, -3 to -S°C 
This forms all over the airframe as a clear glaze. Supercooled water droplets impact the surface, a small part freezes and releases latent heat, the remainder runs back and forms what is known in meteorology as clear ice. 
If supercooled water falls as rain it forms clear ice on the airframe in the same way as it does in flight. This is called freezing rain. 
ft+1I1-lo 

Rime Ice, below -S°C 
This is formed where supercooled rain droplets hit the airframe and freeze on impact trapping the air. It has a milky white granular appearance. 
Mixed Icing 
This forms as a combination of both rime and glaze ice making an opaque surface. This happens when the aircraft is at an intermediate temperature between the glaze and rime figures. 

Ice and Rain Protection 
Ice Detection 

Vibrating rod ice detector head
Ice detection systems only warn that icing has been encountered. The frequency of the warning will indicate the severity of the conditions. 
Vibrating Rod Systems 
The most modern system is the vibrating rod, which is mounted on the outside of the aircraft. The rod is vibrated at 40 KHz, and when ice forms on the rod, it slows down which illuminates the ice warning light and activates a heater within the rod. This melts the ice so the rod returns to its 40 KHz extinguishing the warning light until ice forms on it again. Ifthe light remains on constantly the ice detector is faulty. 
You may well see these types of detectors referred to in EASA exams as Rosemount Ice Detectors, as the company that makes the Rosemount air temperature probe also make ice detectors. 
Ice and Rain Protection 
ft+ii1-lo 
Pressure Operated Detectors 
A pressure operated detector uses an aerofoil cross section tube protruding into the airflow. Several small holes are let into the leading edge, there are fewer and smaller holes in the trailing edge. This forms a pitot static system in which the pitot (ram) air pressure holds a relay open. 
In icing conditions the holes on the leading edge ice up before those on the trailing edge. This causes the pitot air pressure to fall, allowing the static pressure to close the relay and give an icing warning. At the same time power is applied to the heater element causing it to heat and melt the ice, so the ram air pressure is re-established and the relay broken, de-activating the ice warning light. 


Ice and Rain Protection 
Serrated Rotor Systems 
The serrated rotor system uses an electric motor to turn a rotor that has grooves cut into it. The rotor turns next to a fixed knife. As ice forms on the rotor the knife cuts it off. Because the motor is flexibly mounted it moves under the torque reaction, which switches on the ice detection light. 

Fuselage 
Ice and Rain Protection 
Hot Rod Systems 
The hot rod or black rod is the oldest system, and only gives a visual indication that icing conditions exist. An aerofoil section rod is mounted below the pilot side screen angled back. The rod is painted black and contains a heater element. When ice forms on the rod the pilot can heat it removing the ice then observe and time how quickly it takes the ice to reform. A light that illuminates the rod allows the pilot to use the system at night. 

Ice Detection Lights 
Ice detection lights are mounted into the fuselage or wing leading edges, and illuminate the leading edge ofthe wings for visual inspection in flight. 
Ice and Rain Protection 

Airframe Anti-Icing and De-Icing 
Ice build-up can be dealt with either by anti-icing systems, which prevent ice accretion, or by de-icing systems which remove ice build-up from the airframe and engine intakes. Some systems do both. 
De-Icing Paste 
Light aircraft without any fixed method of removing ice as it forms can have de-icing paste applied to the leading edge of the wings, fins and tailplane before take-off. Although it is called de-icing paste, a better description would be anti-icing paste as it provides short term protection only, in case the pilot inadvertently enters icing conditions. 


Ice and Rain Protection 
ft+W+lo 
Thermal Systems 
Typical thermal anti-ice system
Thermal systems, as the name suggests, heat the area requiring 
ice protection. The heat is normally provided by electrically heated mats or hot bleed air. 
Modern jet aircraft use thermal anti-icing systems for the leading edge of the wings and tail. These use large quantities of hot bleed air to heat the inner surface of the leading edge. The air escapes through outflow holes under the wings or into the nacelles. 
Hot air systems require a shut off valve and temperature overheat sensor so that the system can be isolated in the event of excess temperature before structural damage is done. 
These systems will overheat without a flow of cooling air across the wings, so if selected on the ground, a time delay of 12 seconds will elapse after the wheels lift off before the system starts to operate. Because they rely on engine bleeds there is a performance penalty ifthey are used. 
Heated mats are occasionally used for airframe anti-icing but only on small areas and usually on light aircraft. 

Ice and Rain Protection 
Pneumatic System 
Piston engined and turbo-prop aircraft use pneumatic de-icing systems. Attached to the leading edge of the wings, fin and tailplane are rubber boots consisting of a series of separate tubes, which are inflated and deflated either on demand or according to an automatic cycle. 
The act of inflating the tubes cracks the ice, allowing the slipstream to carry it away. One has to be careful, however, that the boots are operated when the ice is between 0.5 and 1.5 cm thick. If operated before 0.5 cm the ice can form a shell around the inflated profile of the boots and render them useless. If operated after 1.5 cm the ice can be too strong to break off. 
The wing section controls the design of the boots, ifthe inflation tubes run around the leading edge they are described as chord wise, if they run along the leading edge they are described as span wise. Thick wing sections use chord wise tubes thin wing sections use span wise tubes. To ensure that there is static electrical bonding between the wing and the boot the outer layer is made from neoprene rubber. 
Ice and Rain Protection 
ft+ii&fto 
Pneumatic System 
If the boots are not being operated they have a vacuum pressure applied to suck them on to the wings. When selected on, the vacuum stops and a positive air pressure is applied to inflate the tubes. 
For light aircraft all the tubes inflate at the same time. For large aircraft the boots operate in sections, for example A, B, C. To maintain balance section A will inflate on both wings at the same time cracking the ice. They will then start to deflate with vacuum pressure as section B starts to inflate and so on until the cycle is complete. 
For light aircraft the pilot operates a push once switch as required. If there is a single cycle it typically takes 34 seconds. For larger aircraft there are two speed settings, fast for heavy icing and slow for light icing, shown below. 
Fast cycle Heavy icing 26 sec. interval Slow cycle Light icing 206 sec. interval 
The tubes are inflated to a pressure of 18 psi. At 8 psi a green light comes on and remains on to show the boots are inflated until the pressure drops again below 8 psi at which point it goes out.

Ice and Rain Protection 

Engine Anti-Icing and De-Icing Jet Engine Intakes 
Nose cowl 
Jet engine air intakes are heated by a small amount of hot air passing through a sealed or perforated duct. Some manufacturers provide continuous anti-icing because the intake lip is an aerodynamic stagnation point some allow engine anti­icing to be selected on and off. 
Hot air anti-icing may also be provided for the inlet guide vanes. The first stage of the compressor may be protected by hot air from 
Nose cone 
the nacelle and guide vanes anti-icing systems being vented through the engine. 
The use of engine anti-icing reduces engine performance partly because the bleed air is taken off the side of the engine and partly because introducing warm air into the inlet reduces mass flow. Many manufacturers consider that the penalty is minimal and give no performance correction figures. 

Ice and Rain Protection 
Turbo-Prop Intakes 
Intakes of turbo prop aircraft are usually heated by electrical elements. The stagnation zone on the lip and 'breaker strips' on the external surface of the intake are provided with constant anti­icing. Other areas have intermittently heated elements to provide de-icing. 
Ice and Rain Protection 
Ice Diverters 
Some turbo-props have ice diverter ducts to allow ice broken off at the intake to exit harmlessly to atmosphere rather than being drawn into the engine. 
Many turboprops and some jets are designed with the engine oil tank in the intake casing. The hot oil just below the skin reduces intake icing. 

Ice and Rain Protection 
Propeller Anti-Icing and De-Icing 
Electrical Propeller De-icing 
Ice is likely to form on the inner third of the back of the blade because of the slower rotational speed at this radius and the lower air pressure. To combat this, electrically heated boots are fitted around the blade's leading edge. Sometimes a double boot is used to cover up to 50% ofthe blade. The electrical power is transferred from the static supply to the rotating propeller via a slip ring and brushes 
The action of the heater mat is to loosen the ice's grip and allow centrifugal force to fling the ice outward. Beyond 50% of the blade length the higher rotational speed prevents the ice from adhering to the blade. 
To reduce the electrical load the power is cycled from the inner boots to the outer boots. Ifthe blades are in even pairs then power is applied to one pair at a time and this also helps keep the propeller in balance. For three and five bladed propellers all the blades are heated at the same time. 
When the system is cycling lumps of ice occasionally hit the fuselage, this causes no damage but does distress the passengers! Often a doubler plate is fitted at that point on the fuselage to minimise the chance of damage from flying ice.

The propeller spinner is normally electrically anti-iced rather than de-iced because ice tends to stay there once it is formed.

Pitot and static vents are electrically heated continuously to prevent ice forming and blocking the vent holes. An amber warning light will illuminate on the flight deck if a pitot heater unit fails.

Ice and Rain Protection 
Windscreen De-Misting 
A fan is provided to blow air up onto the front windows for rapid de­misting. 
Rain Repellent 
On light aircraft rain is removed from the windscreens by the curved shape of the screens and the slipstream. For some larger air transport aircraft, while the slipstream plays a part and wipers are fitted as standard, bleed air is blown up outside of the screen to help remove the rain. Most larger aircraft, however, are fitted with rain repellent systems. 
Rain repellent is very viscous oil, which is sprayed on to the outside of the front screens and is used with the wipers to improve visibility in heavy rain. The repellent is applied as a measured dose and when sprayed will cloud vision for up to 15 seconds. 

The rain repellent fluid originally used contained chlorofluorocarbons (CFCs). It was both toxic and an ozone depleting agent, so the Montreal protocol banned its production and use in 1996. Despite this minor setback, new aircraft are still being fitted with rain repellent systems (electrically isolated), and the industry has recently developed some CFC free replacement fluid, most notably Donaldson Le Bozec, who have certification for several Boeing and Airbus types. 


Lesson 74 
Fuel Systems 

AGK74 
Fuel Systems 
Fuel Systems 
The job of the fuel system is to provide a suitable storage and delivery system such that fuel is delivered to the engine at pressures, flow rates and temperatures that meet the requirements of the engine. Light piston engine systems tend to be simple while turbo-jet systems are more complicated. 
This section considers the storage and delivery of fuel up to the engine high-pressure pump or carburettor, the types offuel, their grades and characteristics and the regulations covering refuelling and defuelling. 
Fuel Tanks 
Airframe fuel tanks can be of the rigid, flexible or integral type. 
Rigid tanks, usually made of aluminium alloy but not forming part ofthe aircraft structure, are used in the wings of light aircraft and in some larger aircraft as fuselage tanks. Their main disadvantage is the weight penalty. 
Flexible tanks are rubber bags attached to the aircraft structure by cords or buttons. Though lighter than rigid tanks their main disadvantages are that they are easily punctured, and that ridges can form in the bottom trapping water. 
ft.ihbf o 

Integral tanks are usual on large modern aircraft. They are made by sealing the aircraft's wing structure to make the tank this saves weight and allows the use of areas that would otherwise not be practicable. 
Larger integral and rigid tanks incorporate baffles to prevent the fuel sloshing about too much during manoeuvres. Baffle check valves prevent the movement offuel towards the wing tip. 
CS25 requires an expansion space of at least 2% of the tank volume when the tank is full. 
Rigid fuel tanks are used in the fuselage of large aircraft Flexible fuel tanks are used in light aircraft Integral tanks are used in the wings of large aircraft Baffles are fitted in larger fuel tanks 
Fuel Systems 
ft+i.bl o 
Light Aircraft Systems 

The fuel tanks in light aircraft are normally in the wings which can, Balance pipe High winged aircraft's 
of course, be located either above or below the aircraft's engine. 
High winged aircraft use a gravity feed to get the fuel to the 
carburettor, low winged aircraft use a mechanical fuel pump to 
draw the fuel from the tanks. As this could fail in flight an auxiliary 
electrical pump is fitted in parallel with the mechanical pump. 

Fuel Systems 
Vapour Locking 
As aircraft fly higher the ambient pressure drops, this allows the fuel to more easily change state from a liquid to a vapour. Iffuel changes into a vapour in a pipe, or if vapour enters a pipe, it can create a vapour lock which will reduce or stop the flow offuel. 
In order to prevent vapour locks a light aircraft can use an auxiliary fuel pump to increase the fuel pressure in the pipe. 
ft.lhbl o 

Venting 
The tanks are vented to allow air to replace the fuel as it is drawn off. 
A few light aircraft are vented by a ram air system. The increase in pressure above the fuel helps to reduce evaporation and aids fuel transfer. 
Most light aircraft tanks are vented to static pressure. This is normally done by leading the vent pipe out to the under surface of the wing into the high energy air. Most vent pipes of this type have a forward facing chamfer to gain some pressure from the ram effect. 
Tanks are vented inwards to allow air to replace the fuel pumped out 
Fuel Systems 
Fuel Feed 
Fuel is drawn off from a point above the bottom of the tank to prevent sediment or water entering the engine. This means not all the fuel in the tank can be used, and the remainder is referred to as unusable fuel. 
Water drains are located at the lowest point of the tank these allow a sample to be removed for inspection before flight. Fitted around the fuel draw off pipe is a coarse gauze mesh filter or finger screen to prevent large foreign objects from entering the supply system. The fuel is then passed through a low-pressure filter unit, sometimes referred to as a gascolator, that also incorporates a water drain. 
Finger screen 
Water drain

There may be unusable fuel in the bottom of a fuel tank 
Fuel Systems 
Large Aircraft Systems 
Large multi-engine air transport aircraft fuel systems have more regulatory requirements placed on them this is reflected in their design. Provision must be made to allow the fuel to continue to feed to the engines in the event of system failures. 
The 8737 system is shown in this diagram. It is typical of a modern jet transport fuel system. It has three tanks, one in each wing and one in the fuselage. Each tank has two low pressure (LP) fuel pumps in it and a variety of sensors and back-up systems. The LP fuel system feeds engine driven high pressure (HP) fuel pumps at the engines. 
There are at least two LP booster pumps in each tank often with two power sources

Fuel Systems 
Trim tanks 
Some aircraft employ a trim tank in the horizontal stabiliser to optimise the CoG and save fuel in the cruise. Once established in the cruise fuel is pumped rearwards into the trim tanks moving the CoG rearwards. Either before or during the descent the fuel is moved forward again to improve longitudinal stability. 
Low Pressure Fuel Pumps 

On larger air transport aircraft each fuel tank has two LP pumps these may also be referred to as LP Booster Pumps. Booster pumps are low pressure centrifugal pumps, supplying the fuel at between 20 and 100 PSI. 
The pumps are electrically powered from the aircraft's 11 SV AC supply. Each pump in a pair is supplied from a different AC busbar so a single generator failure will only affect one pump in each tank. Some aircraft, but not the B737, have a backup DC supply. 
To reduce stress at the wing roots of an aircraft, fuel is fed from the inboard tanks first. In the B737 system shown, the pumps in the centre tank operate at a higher pressure than the wing tanks, this ensures that the centre tank feeds the engines first.
