Fuel Systems 
ft+Mi._o 
Suction or Bypass Valves 
In normal use, the LP pumps ensure that the fuel is pressurised up to the engine's high pressure pump (HP) in order to prevent vapour locks. If the LP pumps fail suction or bypass valves allow the HP pump to draw the fuel from the tank and still give 75% of its power rating. In modern jets there may be a power reduction and a risk of flame out at altitude. Some aircraft have an altitude restriction which will clearly affect range and may affect terrain clearance in mountainous regions. Bypass valves are shown on the B737 fitted in the fuel supply pipe downstream of the LP pumps in the wing tanks. 
A pressure sensor is fitted just before the engine HP pump and activates a warning light in the flight deck if inlet pressure to the pump is reduced. 


Bypass valves allow fuel to flow if the LP pumps fail 
Fuel Systems 

Jet Pumps 
Jet pumps can be used to move large volumes of fuel within the fuel tank system from tank to tank or to keep a booster pump box full of fuel. They make use of the venturi principle, where a small volume offuel ejected under pressure passing through a venturi will draw with it a large volume of the fuel surrounding it. 
The 8737 shows one jet pump which uses No.1 Tank forward LP pump output pressure to scavenge fuel from the centre tank after the main LP pumps have been turned off. The fuel scavenge shutoff valve opens automatically when both centre tank LP pumps are turned off. 
To booster pump box 


Fuel Systems 
Engine Fuel Shut-off Valve 
The engine fuel shutoff valve is DC operated from the hot battery busbar. It shuts off LP fuel supply to the engine when either: 
¦ 
the appropriate engine fire handle is pulled or 

¦ 
the engine start lever is placed to CUTOFF. 


When shut the associated indication on the fuel panel comes on. 
FUEL VALVE CLOSED 
Fuel Tank Pressurisation 
Fuel tanks are pressurised in order to prevent vapour locks forming in the supply pipes after LP pump failure. Pressurisation also reduces the amount of vapour given off by the fuel. 
Some large aircraft use bleed air from the engines to pressurise their tanks to 5 PSI above ambient. An overpressure relief valve vents excess pressure to atmosphere. 
Other aircraft, including the B737 series, use ram air through the vent system to pressurise the fuel. The ram air intakes are located on the underside of the wings. 
ft.iiiifrlo 

Fuel Tank Venting 
Fuel tanks are vented to prevent excessive positive or negative pressures damaging the tanks. This is particularly important in the wing tanks because they are integral to the aircraft structure, if the fuel tank is damaged the wing is damaged. 
On some large aeroplanes the fuel tanks may be vented through air intakes on the underside of the wing. 
On the B737 the vent system feeds to vent surge tanks at the wingtips. These contain a pressure relief valve which will pop open and subsequently stay open if the vent system contains pressures too far above or below ambient. 
Vent Surge Tanks 
Vent surge tanks are located at the extreme tips ofthe wings and are integral to the aircraft structure. Normally empty, they collect fuel overflow into the vent lines during attitude changes and allow it to drain back into the main tanks. If the fuel overflow is too much for the vent surge tanks they vent the excess to atmosphere through outlets at the wingtips. 
Fuel Systems 

Crossfeed Valve 
The crossfeed valve is used is to rectify a fuel imbalance between the wing tanks by feeding both engines from the same tank, or to feed the remaining engine from both tanks following an engine failure. 
The crossfeed valve must be closed for take-off and is normally closed in flight. If it is necessary to correct a fuel imbalance the crossfeed valve is opened by a selection on the overhead panel, the LP pumps in the wing tank with less fuel are turned off. Procedures to correct a fuel imbalance must be monitored by both pilots. 
This diagram shows a fuel imbalance being corrected. The centre tank is empty and the LP pumps have been turned off, the corresponding Low Fuel Pressure lights are on. The No.1, left, tank has more fuel in it and will be used to supply the engines until the wing tank contents are equal.

Fuel Systems 
One of the problems with jet fuels is that at temperatures below -40°C solid wax-like particles begin to form. To counter both waxing and the possibility water in the fuel could form ice crystals which might block the low-pressure filters, jet aircraft systems usually heat the fuel at the engines just before the fuel filters. 
Heating can be from oil/fuel heat exchangers that use hot engine lubricating oil, or from air/fuel heat exchangers using use hot engine bleed air. The oil/fuel heat exchangers are called Fuel Cooled Oil Coolers (FCOCs), they heat the fuel and cool the oil. 
Control of fuel temperature is usually automatic but can be manual in response to a warning lamp. On modern aircraft the pilots can monitor fuel temperature on the ECAM FUEL page. In the fuel tanks the fuel can be heated by using the excess heat from the hydraulic system. Here the returning hydraulic fluid is passed through a heat exchanger, located in the bottom of the tank below the level of the unusable fuel. Should a leak occur the leak would be from the high-pressure hydraulic fluid into the fuel tank. This is a less dangerous situation than fuel leaking into the hydraulic system. Fuel in fuselage tanks is not heated deliberately, the problem here is usually keeping the fuel temperature down. 
Fuel temperature control is usually automatic 


The flight crew on transport aircraft are given some or all of the following indications: 
¦ Fuel quantity -indicated either as volume or weight. On older aircraft the contents of each tank or group of tanks was displayed on individual gauges, modern aircraft use a totaliser gauge which shows either the aircraft's total fuel load or the load in selected tanks. There is also a separate fuel low level warning system. 
¦ 
Fuel temperature -this is more important for turbine engined aircraft due to the likelihood of the kerosene becoming waxy. 
¦ 
Fuel flow -indicated as pressure, weight per hour, volume per hour or as a percentage of power. Informs the crew about the engines fuel consumption. 
¦ 
Filter light -illuminates when the fuel filter starts to block or clog. 



Lesson 75 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 

AGK75 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
Fuel Contents Gauging 
The range obtainable from a fuel depends on its calorific value, or energy content. For any specific fuel the energy available depends on fuel mass, and it is more important to know the fuel mass rather than the fuel volume. 
Early aircraft used a mechanical float operating a mechanical gauge to give a volumetric indication of the tank contents. This is only accurate when the aircraft is level and at a constant temperature. It is, however, a cheap, simple and reliable system. Modern fuel gauging systems include a low level warning to warn the pilots there is around 30 minutes flight remaining. 
Resistive System 
In the resistor system the mechanical linkage is replaced with an electrical resistor and a gauge operated by DC. Any change in the fuel level causes the float arm to move a wiper across the resistor, resulting in a varying signal being sent to the gauge. The system, although cheap and simple, is again limited as it only gives indication of volume and indications can fluctuate when the aircraft is manoeuvring. 
ft.iht.l o 

A major drawback for volumetric indication is that the fuel contracts when it is cold and expands when it is hot, so a variation in temperature alters the contents reading. This effect is particularly noticeable on the first flight of the day. If the aircraft were filled up the night before the fuel would have been dense. 
As the temperature rises and the fuel expands it occupies a larger volume and can vent on to the manoeuvring area. 
Fuel mass can be obtained from fuel quantity if the specific gravity (SG) is known. This can be calculated, or there may be an adjustable SG compensator in the gauging system. 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
ft+iff-lo 
The Capacitance System 
The capacitance system eliminates this problem by effectively 
weighing the fuel on board and displaying the quantity offuel as a 
mass or weight. 

To gauge 
The capacitance unit or capacitor consists of three concentric 
tubes one inside the other. The outer tube is an earth screen, the 
inner tube at low potential and the innermost at high potential. In 
this diagram the earth screen has been omitted for clarity. The 
general term for a substance between the plates of a capacitor is 
dielectric, the dielectrics here are fuel and air. As fuel has roughly 
twice the capacitance of air the amount of capacitive reactance 

indicates how much of the tube is covered by fuel. This is a Reference or 
compensating unit 
volume measurement. 

Capacitance systems are preferred as they indicate fuel mass not volume 
Fuel has twice the capacitance of air 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
ft+itf-lo 
The Capacitance System continued 
Fuel, however, has a different capacitance for different densities. The capacitance increases as density increases. The reference unit, which is in the unusable fuel and therefore always remains submerged, measures the actual capacitance for the fuel in the tank. This data allows the gauge to convert the output from volume to mass of fuel. Several tube units are located in each tank, wired in parallel for redundancy. Any change in density will still affect the volume in the tank, but the mass or weight displayed will be the same. 
If there is water in the tank it will lie at the bottom and cover the reference unit. Pure water has a co-efficient of capacitance eighty times that of air and forty times that of fuel. The gauging system interprets this as a massive calorific value and shows the contents off the scale at maximum. 
Fuel gauges only indicate the usable fuel, and electrically operated gauges will always go to zero when the power is removed or if the system fails. Electrical gauges can work through a ratiometer or a galvanometer. Both need external power. The ratiometer will read correctly if power fluctuates, the galvanometer will not. 

To gauge 

If there is water in the tank it will read full scale full Fuel gauges always read zero after failure 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 

Manual Measuring Systems 
Although modern aircraft have sophisticated fuel computers and capacitance systems, fuel reconciliation checks on the ground require some basic old-fashioned methods. Two examples of these are drip sticks and magnetic sticks. 
Drip Stick 
A drip stick consists of a hollow tube with a small hole in the side just below the top at the bottom is a tap. When not in use the drip stick is locked up inside the tank. To take a reading the tube is drained, then lowered until fuel starts to drip steadily from the tap. This indicates the correct position for a reading to be taken from the junction of the stick and the wing. Ifthe hole is below the surface of the fuel a steady stream of fuel will run from the tap. Most transport aircraft will have multiple sticks in each tank -for example, the 737-300 had five in each wing tank. 


To be absolutely certain of the contents the tanks still need to be dipped 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
ft+itf-lo 
Magnetic Sticks 
Magnetic sticks differ by using a float with a permanent magnet located around a sealed tube, inside the tube is a measuring stick, again with a magnet. The stick is unlocked and lowered until the float supports the stick and the reading is taken in the same manner as with the drip stick, as shown in this animation. 
With both types of stick the crew need to consider the attitude of the aircraft, as any deviation from level in pitch or roll means that the fuel is also not level. This is achieved on some aircraft by a small spirit level for each parameter located inside the landing gear well for ease of access on the ground. The attitude is calibrated in degrees of pitch and roll, and a correction is calculated from the data tables for that aircraft. A grid table for each aircraft will determine how many of the drip sticks or floatsticks will need to be pulled for each tank. 
Further data tables give both volume and mass equivalents for a given reading on the sticks, using an average specific gravity value for the fuel. Typically such a chart would give litres, US gallons, Imperial gallons, kilograms and pounds.

ft+Mirlo 
Fuel Flow Gauging 
Fuel flow can be used to monitor the performance of a jet engine. Fuel flow integrated over time is used for comparison with the fuel quantity gauging system as an indication offuel used. 
Fuel flow can be measured either as a volume flow or a mass flow. Of the two, mass flow is preferred as the calorific value ofthe fuel is proportional to its mass, not its volume. The flow meter is fitted after the Fuel Control Unit, between the HP cock and the engine.

ft+Hito 
Fuel Jettisoning 
Air transport aircraft that have a Maximum Landing Mass (MLM) significantly lower than the Maximum Take Off Mass (MTOM) must have a fuel jettisoning system. The system must be capable ofjettisoning enough fuel within 15 minutes to allow the aircraft to land at MLM following a take-off at MTOM. The 8737, like many short-haul aircraft, does not have a jettison system. 
When fitted, jettison systems operate from the refuelling gallery pipe. Selecting fuel jettison opens a valve or valves and the fuel is pumped out using the LP booster pumps. 
A jettison limit switch ensures that sufficient fuel remains on board for take off, climb to 10 000 ft and cruise for 45 minutes. Occasionally an auxiliary jettison control is fitted to allow a second and separate selection to continue the jettison below these limits. 
The diagram shows a typical fuel system with jettison in progress through a refuelling gallery pipe. 
Other than in an emergency, jettisoning must not be carried out below an altitude at which the fuel will not evaporate before hitting the ground, in precipitation and over built up areas. 
Bleed ram air T ssurization 

Most big aircraft have fuel jettison systems The main jettison system retains an automatic reserve 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
fti+H+lo 
Refuelling 
Light aircraft are refuelled through filling points on the top surface of the wings. This is called overwing or gravity refuelling. Air transport aircraft are refuelled and defuelled via an under wing connection. The fuel is forced in under pressure, so it is called pressure refuelling. The maximum pressure for this is 50 PSI the maximum suction for defuelling is -5 PSI. 
Pressure refuelling allows automatic operation, where the amount of fuel to be added or defuelled is pre-selected. The refuelling valve will automatically close when the pre-set is achieved. Tank sequencing can also be automatic. A Volumetric Top Off unit (VTO) will also close the refuelling valve when the tank is full. 


The maximum pressure for pressure refueling is 50 PSI 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
ft+Mirlo 
Fuel Sampling 
Fuel has to be checked, before the first flight of each day, to ensure that it is free from contamination from water and sediment. This is done by taking a sample offuel from each tank in turn. A clear container is used the fuel must be clear and bright. 
¦ 
Clear means that the sample is clear of sediment, free of water or foreign matter such as fungal growth. 

¦ 
Bright means that when the sample is held up to the light it is a clear even colour -cloudiness can be caused by particles of water reflecting and refracting the light or air. Air clears upward quickly, water clears downward over a period of time -this water is said to be in suspension, entrained or dissolved. 


If doubt exists over a sample of kerosene, which could be pure water, place a small quantity ofwater into a clear tube and mark its meniscus then add the sample to be checked. Ifthe sample is water then the meniscus will move up the container, if it was fuel then the water and fuel will separate and there will be a meniscus between the two. 
Water detecting paste or paper can be used. This is attached to a rod and lowered into the fuel but kept clear of the bottom of the tank, it must not be kept in the fuel for more than 10 seconds. If water is present then the paper or paste will change colour, normally to green.

Refuelling Precautions 
Refuelling is said to have started when the filler caps have been removed and continues until they are refitted. 
A 6 metre radius refuelling zone is taken from the open refuelling poinUconnection and any vents. 
Aircraft are refuelled before standing overnight to prevent condensation forming in the tanks. Full tanks present a fire risk, and empty tanks present an explosion risk. 
Before refuelling starts the aircraft must be bonded to earth and to the refuelling equipment. 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
ft+iiif+lo 
Fuel Types 
The two major types of fuel used in aviation are Aviation Gasoline (AVGAS) for piston engines and Aviation Kerosene (AVTUR) for turbine engines. Diesel engine aircraft use AVTUR. 
Before looking in detail at each fuel, it pays to look at some of the terms used. 

Volatility  The ability of a liquid to change into a vapour, it is affected by temperature, pressure and surface area.  
Gas Pressure  The ambient pressure above the liquid fuel, which prevents the liquid changing into a vapour. A fuel with a high vapour pressure will boil off at a lower altitude.  
Flash Point  The lowest temperature at which there is sufficient vapour to support a momentary flame.  
Fire Point  The lowest temperature at which there is sufficient vapour to support a continuous flame.  
Auto Ignition Temperature (AIT)  The temperature at which the fuel will self ignite.  
Viscosity  The resistance to flow, viscosity increases with a decrease in temperature.  
Waxing  Affects kerosene fuels, as the temperature drops the fuel takes on the consistency of wax.  
Freezing Point  The temperature at which the last crystal of frozen fuel melts.  

Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
Aviation Gasoline (AVGAS) 
Aviation gasoline is graded according to its octane rating the higher the octane rating the greater the resistance to detonation or knock. 
Octane rating is expressed as two numbers, e.g. 100/130 the lower number corresponds with the resistance to knock with a weak mixture, the higher number with a rich mixture. The greater the fuel's resistance to detonation, the higher the engine's working pressure can be. The fuel grade is normally referred to by the first number, e.g. 100 grade. 
Gasoline has a high saturation vapour pressure so is more volatile than jet fuel. It has low freezing point (-60°C), a low flash point (-45°C), and a Specific Gravity of 0. 72. 

Grades 
There are three grades of AVGAS in use, these are: 
¦ 
80 grade. This is coloured RED for identification and is used in older low powered engines. 

¦ 
100 grade. This is coloured GREEN for identification and is used in higher-powered engines. 

¦ 
100 LL. This is coloured BLUE to identify that it is a Low Lead fuel, which is used in high powered engines to reduce fouling. 100 LL is manufactured to DERD 2485 and has a freezing point of approximately -60°C 



The colour difference between these fuels denotes octane rating. 
The lead used in aviation gasoline is tetra-ethyl lead. 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
MOGAS 
Some lower powered piston engines are able to legally operate on car fuel, motor gasoline -MOGAS. It is prohibited for use in commercial aviation. The general safety considerations that apply to aircraft with more than 25% MOGAS in their tanks are: 
¦  Aircraft using MOGAS must not fly above 6000 ft.  
•  Aircraft using MOGAS must not fly if the fuel tank temperature is 20°C or above.  
•  Aircraft using MOGAS are more likely to suffer carburettor icing and vapour locking, due to increased volatility and water content.  
•  Use of MOGAS can affect seals and hoses, so use of MOGAS is recorded in the Maintenance Log.  


Aviatiation Kerosene (AVTUR) 
Aviation kerosene has a Specific Gravity of 0.8. It is generically known as AVTUR, Aviation Turbine fuel. 
Kerosene has a low gas pressure so is less volatile than gasoline, it has a higher flash point (60°C). The comparative disadvantages are that the kerosene has a higher freezing point and it becomes viscous and waxy as it cools down. 
In order to lower the freezing point and to prevent waxing, pure kerosene is mixed with additives to produce the two common jet fuels: JETA which has a freezing point of -40°C and the more widely used JET A1 which has a freezing point of -47°C. 
Kerosenes range in colour from light straw to clear. 
Fuel Types and Contents Gauging 
Extra Additives 
Most civilian aircraft have mechanisms to heat the fuel to prevent ice crystals forming and blocking the filters. Some military aircraft require Fuel System Icing Inhibitors (FSII) to be added to the basic fuel. These combine with water particles in the fuel and prevent ice crystals from forming. JETA1 with FSII is also used in rotor craft operations. Biocides can also be added to suppress fungal growth and kill off micro-organisms in the tanks.

Wide Cut Fuels 
To overcome the ignition problem, older American fuels used a mixture of 70% gasoline with 30% kerosene this lowers the fire point. These are referred to as wide cut or wide range distillates. 
They must be treated as gasoline for safety reasons. If a wide cut fuel is used in an engine that has been set up for pure kerosene, then the engine fuel control unit (FCU) must be adjusted for the change in specific gravity. Wide cut fuels are not approved in EASA operated aircraft.

Gas Turbine Fuel Specifications 
Two organizations have taken the lead role in setting and maintaining specifications for civilian aviation turbine fuel, the American Society for Testing and Materials (ASTM) and the United Kingdom Ministry of Defence (MOD). Many other countries issue their own national specifications for jet fuel which are very nearly, or completely identical to either the ASTM or MOD specifications. The two major jet fuel specifications are: 
¦ 
ASTM D 1655 The specification for two kerosene-type fuels ( Jet A and Jet A-1) and a wide-cut fuel ( Jet B ). Jet A is used for almost all domestic commercial aviation flights in the United States. 

¦ 
Defence Standard 91-91 The UK MOD specification for Jet A-1, which is used for most civil aviation fuels outside the United States, Russia and its former satellite states. 


There are only minor differences between the DEF STAN 91-91 JetA-1 andASTM D 1655Jet A-1 specifications.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+1&11o 
Fire and Smoke Detection and Suppression 
The prospect offire on board an aircraft in flight is very serious. The first line of defence is in the design and the materials used to construct the aircraft. The second line of defence is to detect the fire early enough for it to be fought and suppressed successfully. 
There are three methods of detecting a fire: 
¦ 
Visually. 

¦ 
By detecting smoke. 

¦ 
By detecting high temperature. 


As the old saying goes "there is no smoke without fire". People are excellent smoke detectors, especially a plane full of passengers. Places that are permanently staffed, such as flight decks and passenger cabins, do not require smoke or fire detecting units. These are called Class A compartments. 

Automatic Systems 
There is a requirement for duplication of automatic smoke and fire detection systems. Each detector is able to operate independently but they are normally operated in parallel, known as the double loop system, to prevent spurious warnings. Both detectors have to register the smoke or fire before the warning is given. To achieve this each pair of smoke detectors share a common air supply and two firewires or capillary tubes run side by side to form two loops for each fire detector. This makes use of an "AND" logic gate. 
A fault protection circuit inhibits the system when a detector line is connected to ground (earthed). Aircraft fire detection and suppression systems are DC powered. 
Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+ii1-lo 
Smoke Detection 
Smoke detection is required in: 
¦ 
Unmanned compartments that the crew can access during flight, such as avionics bays. These are called Class B Compartments. 

¦ 
Cargo bays and holds that are not accessible have smoke detection and remotely operated fire extinguishers and are called Class C Compartments. 

¦ 
Class D Compartments, cargo bays with increased fireproofing. The difference from a Class C compartment is that, if a fire does occur, it will not affect the structure of the aircraft and so is left to burn. 

¦ 
Class E Cargo compartments on aeroplanes used only for the carriage of cargo. 

¦ 
Galleys. 

¦ 
Toilets. It is mandatory to have a smoke detector unit in each toilet compartment. The warning can be given on the flight deck or a cabin crew station. 


Smoke detectors are required in galleys, toilets and class B, C & D compartments 

Ion Detection Systems 
Ion detection systems contain a small quantity of radioactive material that bombards the oxygen atoms in the air with alpha particles to create ions, charged gas molecules, in a small chamber. 
The ions that have been created allow a small current to flow through the air from one side of the chamber to the other. When smoke particles are present they absorb the alpha particles without ionising and cause the current to fall. The fall in current is sensed and a warning light or audio alarm is set off. 
Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+1I1-lo 
Optical Systems 
The second, and probably most common, system relies on the idea that smoke particles scatter light. This is the labyrinth smoke detector. 
The unit is located in an air extraction duct where a fan draws a constant flow of air through it. A beacon lamp is located upstream of the 'labyrinth' with a photoelectric cell at the other end. 
Under normal circumstances the labyrinth prevents the light from the beacon lamp reaching the photoelectric cell. In the event of a fire starting in the protected area smoke and fumes are given off. As these pass through the labyrinth they allow the light to reflect and refract on to the photoelectric cell creating a current. Detection of this current sends a fire warning signal to the flight deck. 
To test the smoke detector the pilot closes the test switch. This operates a relay switch that routes the power to a test lamp located behind the photoelectric cell. With test selected the lamps are wired in series so the complete circuit is tested.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Fire Detection 
Areas that have fire detection systems are: 
¦ Jet engine bays. 
• APU bays. 
¦ Main gear bays. 
Jet Engine Zones 
Jet engine bays, nacelles or pods are divided into three zones: 
Zone 1 is the area surrounding the hydraulic pumps, ancillary gearbox, engine lubrication oil reservoir, fuel control unit and so on. This is the most likely place for a fire to start and is the only zone that has fire detection and remotely operated fire extinguishers. To reduce the chance offire spreading the zone is kept at a lower pressure than the other bays by a ventilation system. 
Zone 2 is the area where the compressor blades could touch the engine casing and create a metal fire. This would be indicated via the engine vibration monitoring system. This zone is hotter than Zone 1 
Zone 3 is the hottest Zone, as it is the area surrounding the engine and jet pipe aft of the combustion chamber. Zone 3 is separated from Zone 1 by a stainless steel fire bulkhead. 


Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+ii1-lo 
Pipes in Hot Zones 
If a combustible fluid or gas has to pass through a hot zone then it must be routed through a double skinned pipe (a pipe inside a pipe). 
Auxiliary Power Units 
APUs have their own fire detection and protection systems, and, as they can be left running unattended and during the refuelling operation, the protection system is automatic. In the event of a fire the APU will shut itself down and a fire warning will be activated. 
APUs can be divided into zones in the same way as the main engines, but in most cases the whole bay in which the APU is located is classed as Zone 1 . 

Wheel Bays 
Main gear bays are given overheat detection systems in case an overheated brake unit causes a fire. If there is a fire warning there is no extinguishing system available, the checklist or Quick Reference Handbook (QRH) will call for speed to be reduced below the gear operating speed, VLO, and the gear to be lowered to blow the fire out. 

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+H+lo 
Resistive Fire Detection 
A fire detection system is made up of two independent loops of firewire supported by a central structure, either a piece of thicker wire or a more rigid tube. To avoid false fire warnings two loops are used. The two loops are usually referred to on control panels as Loop A and Loop B, and both have to be activated for a fire to be reported. 
A cross section of each resistive fire wire shows a fine central electrode surrounded by filler material inside a capillary tube. The two ends of the wire are brought together at a junction which is linked to an arrangement ofdiodes called a diode bridge or a "Wheatstone bridge". The capillary tube is earthed to the aircraft's structure. 
When the system is switched on a 28 volt DC electrical potential will be present in the central core but as this is an endless loop no current will flow. When the area to be protected is within its normal operating temperature range this is how the system will remain.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Resistive Fire Detection continued 
As the temperature increases in a fire the resistance of the filler material drops. It is sometimes described as having a negative co-efficient of resistance, and there is an increased leakage of current to ground via the capillary tube. This flow of electricity unbalances the Wheatstone bridge, which allows a signal to be sent to the appropriate logic circuit. 
If the fire wire is crushed in any way and the central electrode earthed it can cause a false fire warning. 
When system test is selected the detection loop is earthed out and a continuous red light illuminates on the appropriate panel the aural warning sounds. The test is for the electrical continuity of the system. It does not test for heat operation.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Capacitance Fire Detection 
The fire wires in the capacitance system look identical to those in the resistance system. In this system, however, the central electrode is connected to a charging and measuring unit. The charging unit charges the central electrode for a set length of time then discharges it into the measuring unit, where its capacitance is compared with a reference value. 
In a fire the capacitance of the system increases as the temperature increases, a positive co-efficient of capacitance. The measuring unit senses this and sends a signal to the logic circuit. 
Once again, if the fire wire is crushed it will earth out but now the loss of capacitance will prevent a fire warning from being given. A fault protection circuit inhibits the detector line. This is called a no fault fire detection system. 
Capacitance systems are generally preferred to resistance systems on the basis that no fire warning is better than a spurious one. A fire will probably show itself in other ways. The system test, when operated, alters the capacitance and a continuous red light illuminates on the appropriate panel. The aural warning sounds.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+W+lo 
Systron Donner Fire Detection System 
The Systron Donner Fire Detector is a gas pressure activated system. It consists of a responder unit that incorporates two pressure switches and a capillary tube with a fine central core of titanium hydride. Around the core and acting on one of the pressure switches is 7 psi of helium gas this is referred to as the averaging gas. This pressure holds an integrity switch closed. 
Any drop in pressure will cause the switch to open, giving a failed signal. In a fire situation the titanium hydride gives off hydrogen gas, this raises the pressure to 40 psi, which closes the alarm switch. 
If the capillary tube is crushed the system will operate provided that there is a gas path to the responder unit. The system test checks the responder unit's electrical circuit and also tests the sensor by heating it electrically from the test circuit.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
Overheat Sensors 
Many aircraft have both fire and overheat warnings originating from the same engine fire detection system, the distinction between warnings being only one of temperature. 
Separate overheat sensors are used in ducts carrying hot bleed air, typically for airframe anti-icing, landing gear bays and occasionally in engine zone 3, around the jet pipe. 
Overheat sensors work on the principle of differential expansion as heat is applied. A tube which expands easily has a pair of low expansion rate spring strips inside (bi-metallic strips), each with a contact. These remain open until the outer tube gets hot. As it expands it stretches and pulls the springs together to close the contacts and make the warning circuit. If this type of detector is used for fire detection, a delay circuit is used to avoid false alarms due to vibration. The detectors are wired in parallel. 

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
ft+ii&fto 
Thermocouples 
Thermocouples are loops of wire made oftwo different metals joined end to end. One join is placed where the temperature is high, the hot junction, and one in the cockpit where the temperature is low, the cold junction. The difference in temperature causes a current to flow through the loop of wire. 
If there is a fire the current flow will increase for those thermocouples with their hot junction in the fire. The difference in current between these and a reference thermocouple is enough to operate a relay that, in turn, activates the warning system. 
The weakness with this system is that should the fire burn through the interconnecting cable, the system will fail completely. Testing is carried out by heating one of the thermocouples. 

Cold junction in the cockpit 
Hot junction in the exhaust

Fire Warnings 

When the detecting units sense a fire, a signal is sent to the flight deck. The warning given will be one or more of these: 
¦ 
Red flashing attention-getters. 

¦ 
A warning bell. 

¦ 
A continuous red light on the central warning panel or, on an EFIS aircraft, a master fire warning light in front of the pilots. 

¦ 
A continuous red light in the appropriate panel or fire handle. 

¦ 
ECAM and EICAS warning messages. 


The engine fire warning consists of a warning light for each engine and a single alarm bell common to all engines. 
Attention getters and aural warnings can be cancelled but the continuous red lights will remain on until the fire is out. 

Master Fire Warnings Once the bell is silenced the checklist for the appropriate fire warning is actioned. When the fire goes out the master fire 
Most aircraft have a master fire warning light in clear view of the warning and the specific fire warning lights will also go out.
pilots. The diagram shows the lights on the glareshield panel of a B737. When any fire warning is set off the master fire warning light will illuminate and a bell will sound. Pressing the light silences the bell. 

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Delayed Fire Warnings 
Certain fire and smoke warnings may be suppressed at critical stages of flight such as just after rotation and just before landing and announced only when the aircraft is in a safer situation. This prevents the pilot being distracted from the primary task of flying the aeroplane.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
Cargo Bay Fire Protection 
Cargo bays use optical smoke detectors, several to each cargo bay. The system shown here is from a B737NG. There are two fire bottles, each with nozzles and squibs to discharge into either bay. The green lights under the DET and FAIL lights indicate the integrity of the squibs. 
The cargo fire warning consists of a bell, the master fire warning light, a continuous red FIRE light on the panel above and amber DET lights indicating which sensors have gone off. 
Pulling and rotating the arming switch will arm the squibs ofall the extinguisher nozzles that discharge into the affected bay the green ARMED light will illuminate under the first DSCH switch.

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Engine Fire Extinguisher Operation 
The engine fire controls are usually located on the overhead panel. An engine fire is detected by the firewire loops sensing an overheat. It is indicated by the master fire warning light and bell and by a continuous red warning light in the fire handle of the appropriate engine. 
The first of the immediate actions is to close the throttle, then turn the fuel and ignition off, then pull the fire handle. 
Pulling the fire handle isolates the engine from: 
¦ 
Fuel by closing Aircraft (LP) and Engine (HP) fuel valves. 

¦ 
Hydraulics by closing shut off valves. 

¦ 
Electrics by opening the GCB and excitation breaker. 

¦ 
The pneumatic system by closing the bleed air shut off valves. 


Pulling the lever also arms the firing circuit to the extinguisher squibs. In some aircraft the audible warning may silenced. 
To operate the extinguisher the handle is turned. Referring to the diagram, if the fire was in N°1 engine the N°1 handle would be pulled and then turned to the left to fire squib A. 


Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Engine Fire Extinguisher Operation continued 
If one bottle fails to extinguish the fire then, normally after 30 seconds, the second bottle can be fired. In this case turning the handle to the right would fire squib C. Even if one bottle has put out the fire the pilot may discharge the second bottle into the engine bay as a precaution just before landing. 

Test functions are available for each engine fire warning system. When selected the fire handle will be illuminated and the audible warning will sound. The overheat warnings can also be tested. Three green lights at the bottom right hand corner of the panel show the integrity of the squibs and firing circuits. 
The OVHT DET switch in the top left corner of the panel can be selected to A to make Loop A the active fire detection loop or to B to make Loop B active. Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
APU Fire Protection 
The APU fire warning is on the same panel as the main engine fire warnings and works in a similar way except that there is only one extinguisher bottle that can be fired by turning the fire handle either way. 
When a fire is detected theAPU automatically shuts down. The APU fire warning light and the master fire warning light illuminate and the fire warning bell sounds. 
On the ground the APU fire warnings are repeated with a warning horn in the main wheel well and a flashing APU fire warning light. The aural warnings can also be silenced and the fire extinguisher activated from a panel in the wheel well. 
In the 8737 both main engine and APU fire detection systems use Systron Donner gas pressure activated detectors. 

Toilet Fire Extinguishers 
Fitted in the toilet compartment under the sink unit is a small fire bottle which has two nozzles, one directed into the waste bin and one under the sink. The nozzle is sealed with a low melting point metal which when heated melts and automatically discharges a fire suppressant chemical. This used to be Halon (Freon), a chemical that depletes the ozone layer. Production was banned globally in 1994. Nowadays the suppressant is likely to be heptafluoropropane (C3F7H), a 'clean agent'. 
A placard mounted inside the waste bin door has silver spots, these turn black as the temperature rises to indicate how hot the fire was. 
The fire extinguisher system is not connected to the smoke warning system in the toilets. 
Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
Brake Fires 
Brake fires can occur after an overweight landing, a rejected take­off, or as a result of bad handling. Brake fires are fought by the airport Fire Section using a water mist which has the effect of reducing the temperature of the brake unit without causing too much thermal shock to the wheels. Do not fight brake fires with hand held extinguishers. Do not approach the brake units from the side because ofthe risk of explosion. 

Jet Pipe Fires 
Fires in this area are rare nowadays as most high bypass engines only have very short jet pipes. If there is a jet pipe fire on start up, there are normally no indications other than from the ground crew jumping up and down, and from excessive TGT. The actions vary from type to type but all involve cutting the fuel supply by turning the fuel and ignition off most involve pulling the fire handle. Some types recommend re-engaging the starter to blow the fire out, but if the fire handle is pulled no air is available for the engine starter motor. 



Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
Fixed Fire Extinguishers with Squibs 
Fixed fire extinguishers consist of bottles made of copper or steel alloy filled with a fire-suppressant. Attached to the neck is an operating head. 
A single operating head will only deliver the fire-extinguishing agent to one area. To save weight by reducing the number of fire bottles carried a dual-operating head may be used so one bottle can protect two areas. Fixed extinguishers are used for the suppression of fire in the jet engine bays, APU bay, cargo bays and toilet areas. 
The preferred fire-extinguishing agent used to be BCF, as we have seen. Now it will be a 'clean agent' chemical suppressant. 
Fixed fire bottle 

Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 
Squibs 
With the exception of those used in the toilet fire all fixed fire bottles are discharged with squibs, little explosive charges. 
The suppressant is stored in the bottle as a liquid under pressure, sealed in by a piston in the operating head. A small black powder charge, the squib, is fitted into the operating head and wired to a switch on the flight deck. Operating the switch will cause electricity to detonate the squib, pushing the piston down and allowing the liquid to escape, changing into a gas in the process. Heads that are accessible have a small pin which will protrude from the base of the head indicating when the bottle has been fired in the normal way. 
Where the operating heads are not clearly visible a indication that the squib has been fired and the extinguisher discharged is displayed to the on the flight deck. 
Squib Test 
To test the firing circuit a small electrical current is passed through the squib. A green light on the test panel indicates that the circuit is serviceable. 

Over Pressurisation 
To prevent the bottle from exploding when subjected to excessive heat a pressure relief valve is fitted to the bottle. This consists of a frangible disc that ruptures and dumps the contents overboard. As it does so it blows out a green plastic disc leaving a red bowl visible. 
Fire and Smoke Detection Systems 

Classification of Fires 
Fires are classed by type. There is no common European classification, the following conforms to ISO 3941: 
Class A  Fires involving ordinary combustible materials which are best attacked with water to douse the flames and lower the temperature.  
Class B  Fires involving flammable liquids like fuel and paint that need to be smothered. Electrical fires.  
Class C  Gas fires.  
Class D  Burning metal.  
Class F  Cooking fats/oils in appliances.

Lesson 77 
Oxygen and Breathing System 

AGK77 
Oxygen and Breathing System 
Oxygen Systems 
Oxygen is carried in pressurised air transport aircraft in case of emergencies such as cabin decompression or smoke and fumes on the flight deck. This is called supplemental oxygen. Supplemental oxygen for the flight deck is always gaseous and stored in cylinders. 
Passenger supplemental oxygen is usually chemically generated, the exceptions being on some Boeing aircraft, the B747 and B777, which use cylinders of gas for the passenger supply as well as the crew supply. 
Oxygen is also carried in the cabin for medical purposes. This first aid oxygen is always in portable bottles. 
Safety Precautions 
Care must be taken with the use of oxygen systems. Control valves should always be operated slowly and contact with grease must be avoided as it results in a chemical reaction that can lead to a spontaneous fire or explosion. For the same reason smoking should not be allowed if oxygen systems are in use. 
ft+IF·Ho 

Safety disk 
l 
Over pressure valve 
Oxygen equipment cannot be lubricated with grease 
Oxygen and Breathing System 
Gaseous Oxygen 
Gaseous oxygen is stored in steel cylinders. European cylinders are painted black with a white shoulder and neck American cylinders are all green. 
Gaseous oxygen is stored in the cylinders at a pressure of 1800 psi. This is reduced to an intermediate pressure of 80-100 psi and then to mask pressure of 8-10 psi. Oxygen cylinder content is sensed as a gas pressure and displayed as either a pressure in psi or bar, a volume in litres or remaining volume in eighths. Flow rates, when shown, are in litres a minute. 
Ambient temperature and the rate of replenishing can affect the volume of gaseous oxygen in the cylinder. To even out the temperature of the gas as it flows into a cylinder a thermal compensator is fitted to adjust the charging pressure. 
The thermal compensator consists of a tube with copper wire bristles in it. As the gas flows through the tube, the copper bristles even out the temperature through conduction. 
ftjff·•­

To stop an over pressurised oxygen cylinder exploding in an aircraft, a bursting or safety disc is fitted. When the pressure exceeds a predetermined limit the safety disc will rupture allowing the contents of that cylinder to escape to atmosphere. As it does so it will blow out a small green convex plastic disc leaving a red concave bowl visible it would also be indicated on the flight deck by a drop in the system contents.

Oxygen and Breathing System 
Large Aircraft Diluter Demand Regulators 
Because of their complexity the diluter demand system regulators are only fitted at flight crew stations. They are called diluter demand systems because they mix air with oxygen in differing proportions, depending on cabin altitude, and because they only supply oxygen when the wearer of the mask breathes in, on demand. 
Depending on selection air is mixed with oxygen up to a cabin altitude of 32 000 ft at which point 100% oxygen is supplied. The system is able to exclude the cabin air and supply 100% oxygen on demand. In an emergency it is also able to supply 100% oxygen at a higher pressure as a continuous flow. 
The regulator has three controls: 
¦ 
A selector with three positions -Normal, Emergency and Mask Test. 

¦ 
A 100% oxygen selector with two positions, ON and OFF. 

¦ 
An ON/OFF selector to isolate the regulator from the supply.

Oxygen and Breathing System 
Components and Operation 
The heart of the unit is the pressure regulator, which is made up of a chamber and demand valve. The chamber is split in two by a diaphragm. One side of the chamber is vented to cabin pressure. The other (the oxygen side) is linked to the supply via the demand valve and the user's mask. In the oxygen side of the chamber a pressure relief valve is fitted to protect the user. The three­position selector is connected to and acts on the diaphragm the other side is connected via a bow spring to the demand valve. 
As the user inhales a partial vacuum is created in the chamber, deflecting the diaphragm to the right. This causes the demand valve to be lifted off its seat allowing oxygen to flow in. As the pressure rises in the chamber the diaphragm is moved to the left closing down the demand valve, having the effect of regulating the pressure supplied to the user. On selecting 'ON' oxygen flows up to the demand valve. 
ft.ifid o 

Normal Operation with 100% Selected Off 
The wearer of the mask on breathing in will cause a partial vacuum in the mask and through to the chamber. This demand, as well as unseating the demand valve allowing some oxygen into the chamber, will also draw in cabin ambient air via the air metering valve. The user will then breathe cabin air enriched with oxygen. As the cabin altitude increases, the barometric capsule will progressively close the air inlet and open the oxygen port, increasing the proportion of oxygen. 
At 32 000 ft the air-metering valve will close the air supply completely, so that the user is on 100 % oxygen. Remember that, at 33 000 ft, sea level conditions can be maintained by breathing 100 % oxygen. 
Air mix becomes 100% oxygen at 32 000 ft cabin altitude 
Oxygen and Breathing System 
Normal Operation with 100% Selected On 
Iffor some reason the flight deck air were contaminated with smoke, but the cabin ambient altitude was below 32 000 ft the user would get a mixture of contaminated air and oxygen. By selecting 100 % the air inlet port is closed and supplementary oxygen valve opens to increase the flow as the user demands it. This is because the air metering valve will still be reacting to the lower cabin altitude. 
A selection of 100% oxygen excludes cabin air 
Emergency Selection 
On selecting emergency at any altitude the diaphragm will be forced over to the right, displacing the demand valve. The regulator will supply a 100 % continuous flow of oxygen at a higher pressure than the 'NORMAL' demand supply. 
Test Mask 
The test function is only used for testing the mask. It operates at a higher pressure than the emergency selection and would become painful if breathed.

Flight Crew Oxygen Mask Stowage 
The masks are stored in special stowage units at the flight crew stations. The masks are extracted by squeezing the two red tabs on the front of the mask which stick out of the stowage unit. This releases the doors of the stowage allowing the mask to be removed and simultaneously supplies oxygen under pressure into the straps. 

Oxygen and Breathing System 
ft++·+•­
Flight Crew Oxygen Masks 
Flight crew oxygen masks must be capable of being fitted with one hand. Many aircraft use masks with inflatable elasticated tubes as straps. 
The inflated straps stretch out to form a 'basket' larger than the average head which allows the mask to be put on one handed. As the red tabs at the front of the mask are released oxygen pressure is removed from the straps and the elastic shrinks to fit neatly on the head. 
The masks are fitted over normal headsets. They incorporate microphones to allow two way communication. 

Oxygen and Breathing System

Oxygen and Breathing System 
Passenger Oxygen Delivery Systems 

Controls and Indicators 
Passenger oxygen may either be deployed automatically by barostatic sensors that detect cabin altitude rising through a preset level, usually 13 300 ft to 14 000 ft, or by selection from the cockpit. Typical controls and indicators are shown on the panel shown here, actually from a B737. 
The pressure in the crew oxygen system is shown on the left. The guarded switch on the right is left in NORMAL for automatic mask deployment or, alternatively, the pressure sensors can be overidden by the crew and masks can be deployed by lifting the guard and selecting the switch to ON. When passenger oxygen is on the amber light comes on. 

Oxygen and Breathing System 
Chemical Oxygen Generators 
Air transport aircraft (other than the large Boeings) use chemical oxygen generators to provide passengers with oxygen in an emergency. 
The generators are located in the Personal Service Units (PSU) above the passengers' heads. The generators contain a solid charge block made of Sodium Chlorate and Iron Powder mixed together. Once the charge block is ignited it will burn continuously for 15 minutes producing more oxygen than it needs to support its own combustion. This excess of oxygen passes through an internal filter before being supplied to the user's mask at a temperature no more than 10°C above the ambient temperature. To save wasting oxygen and allow for cooling, a reservoir bag is fitted before the mask.

Oxygen and Breathing System 
Chemical Oxygen Generators continued 
Although the masks will drop to the 'half hung' position automatically or when commanded from the flight deck the charge block will only ignite when a passenger pulls the mask down. This action pulls a lanyard which electrically fires an igniter. Both the mask release and the igniter use 28 Volts DC from the battery busbar (vital services busbar). 
The generators come in several sizes to supply 2, 3, 4, 5 or 6 masks. Each generator has a black strip and a white strip painted around it. The white paint is heat sensitive and will change colour to black if the unit is ignited. The generators have a shelf life of five years. 
The EU-OPS requirement is that there must be 10% more masks installed than seats fitted, to provide for infants or children not occupying a seat.

Toilet Masks

Gaseous Passenger Oxygen Systems

There are two masks in each toilet supplied by chemical generators 
Oxygen and Breathing System 
First Aid Oxygen 



First Aid, or therapeutic oxygen, is a mixture of air and oxygen that is given via a facemask from a bottle. Cabin crew are trained to administer it, nearly always to passengers with in-flight medical problems. The bottle discharges at approximately 3 litres a minute. 
EASA require that enough first aid oxygen is carried for the greater of 2% ofthe passengers, or one passenger for the entire time the cabin altitude is to be above 8000 ft. There must be at least two first aid oxygen bottles on board. 
Bottled oxygen is provided for medical use

Protective Breathing Equipment (PBE) for the flight crew is the 
normal oxygen mask with goggles fitted over the top. The goggles can be ventilated from the oxygen mask, if required. Flight crew PBE requires an intercom system to be fitted.

Oxygen and Breathing System 
Cabin Crew 
EU OPS requires PBE for cabin crew to cover at least the eyes, nose and mouth, and to provide 15 minutes oxygen supply. In reality it is normal for cabin crew to be provided with smoke hoods which cover the entire head. 
Some smoke hoods use a filtration system to remove toxic smoke from the air. The more common type have a continuous flow of 100% oxygen provided from a small bottle of gas. The oxygen is activated by pulling a toggle. 



Lesson 78 
Basic Electrical Theory and Circuits 

AGK78 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
ft+IF·Ho 
Basic Electrical Theory 
In order to understand the flow of electricity we need to start by looking at the construction of atoms -the basic building units for all materials. An atom consists of a nucleus in the centre which contains positively charged protons and neutrally charged or uncharged neutrons. These are surrounded by orbiting electrons which are negatively charged. 
An electrical current occurs in a conducting material, such as metal or carbon, when an excess of electrons is induced into the circuit by a battery or generator. These excess electrons push out existing electrons from adjacent atoms and these displaced electrons push out electrons in the next atom -and so on, setting up a flow of electrons all the way down the wire. 
The flow of electricity is therefore the flow of electrons from negative to positive. Unfortunately, before the process had been completely understood, a convention had been adopted to regard the flow as being in the other direction. Thus it is important to distinguish between the convention, positive to negative, and the reality, negative to positive. 

Electricity flows from negative to positive but by convention we say that electricity flows the other way round 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
ft.ifid o 
Basic Electrical Theory 2 
One way of considering how the movement of electrons becomes an electric current is to liken them to ball bearings in a tube, as shown here. If a ball bearing is pushed into the tube on the left all the balls in the tube will be moved along one and the ball bearing at the other end will fall out. 
This action is almost immediate but the actual movement of a single ball or atom is small. It does not matter how long the tube is, the result will always be the same. An electric current moves at approximately 3500 miles per second and it would take about 7 seconds for an electrical pulse to travel around the world. 
Electricity is able to flow through most metals, carbon and water. These are known as conductors. Materials which do not allow a flow of electrons, such as rubber, plastic and glass are known as insulators. 
Some substances have a conductivity sitting between that of conductors and insulators these are known as semi-conductors. The behaviour of semi-conductors can be modified by impregnating them with minute quantities of other elements by a process known as doping. Semi-conductors are used for amplification, switching and energy conversion. We will see some of this in later chapters. 

The flow of electricity is virtually instantaneous Conductors allow the flow of electricity, insulators do not 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
ft+it·•­
Electrical Circuits 
The circuits discussed in this chapter are Direct Current, known as DC, meaning that the current flows in one direction only. As previously mentioned, conventional flow is from positive to negative. 
A source generating electricity such as a battery or generator must be connected to both the 'out' and 'in' side of an electrical system -it cannot push out more electrons unless the generating source is supplied with new ones. In the ball bearing analogy more ball bearings would need to be provided to keep the flow going. A circuit must therefore be closed with the returning electrons supplying the generating source with a new supply. This diagram shows the components of a simple electric circuit and a circuit diagram with the relevant electrical symbols. There is a battery, conductors (wires) an on/off switch and a light bulb (the resistance load).

A circuit must be formed to allow the electricity to flow 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 

Electrical Circuits 2 
It can be seen from the electrical diagram that with the switch open as shown the electrons cannot flow back to the battery. The switch is 'open circuit' and the light bulb will not illuminate. Closing the switch (closed circuit) will complete the circuit and the electrons will flow, illuminating the bulb. A closed circuit is said to have 'continuity'. An open circuit has loss of continuity and the 


loads will not work. The build-up of electrons in a battery which causes the current to flow is called the electro-motive force (emf) and is measured in volts for which the symbol is V. Emf is the driving force in a circuit. Alternative terms include electrical pressure, E, potential difference or voltage.  +  .----­-/---~ Switch  
The flow of electrons is called the current and is measured in amps for which the symbol is A. Current is referred to in formulae with the letter I. For example, the statement 'the current is six amps' could be written as I= 6A.  Resistance load Electrical potential is measured in volts Current is measured in amps

Electrical Circuits 3 
In an electric circuit the conductors and electrical components resist the current flow. The resistance to electrical flow is measured in ohms. Resistance in formulae is represented by the letter R and the symbol for ohms is 0. 'The resistance is eight ohms' becomes R = 80. 
A relationship exists between current, voltage and resistance. The higher the voltage for a given resistance the higher the current, the higher the resistance for a given voltage the lower the current. This can be expressed as a formula known as Ohm's Law: 
V = IR 
The formula can also be turned around to read: 
V V 
= or R = R 
This diagram is a simple way of remembering Ohm's Law. To find current, I, cover it up and you are left with V over R to find voltage, V, cover it up and you are left with IR to find resistance, R, cover it up and you are left with V over I. 

We can ask questions using this formula Example: The current in a circuit is 2A and the resistance is 60. What is the voltage drop? Solution: V = Ix R = 2 X 6 = 12V Example: The voltage of a circuit is 1.5V and the resistance is 0.50 Solution: = V+R = 1.5 + 0.5 = 3A 
Resistance is measured in ohms 
A 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
n +e+•o 
Resistors 
All materials resist the flow of electricity to some extent. Even conductors such as copper wire can be made into resistors by making the wire very thin. Resistance can also be increased by increasing the length of a wire double the length and you double the resistance. 
Most metallic resistors are also sensitive to temperature, resistance increasing as temperature increases, although carbon and non-metallic insulators behave in the opposite way. Materials that increase their resistance with an increase in temperature have a positive temperature co-efficient, so we could express that as R proportional to T. If the material has a negative temperature co-efficient, such as the carbon semi-conductor mentioned above, this could be expressed as R proportional to 1 /T, an inverse relationship. This change in resistance with temperature is used in aircraft systems for temperature measurement, and for the control of cabin heating. 
As well as the conductors in the circuit any electrical device, such as lights or a motor will create a resistance to current flow. Sometimes special resistances are placed in the circuit to alter current flow. These resistors may have a fixed or variable value. 

1 0 20 30 
The resistance of a wire depends on its thickness and length 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
ft+HUo 
Series and Parallel Circuits 
Resistors in a circuit behave differently depending on how they are connected. If they are connected one after the other, in series, they behave as one big resistor and the total resistance can be found simply by adding all the little individual resistances together. 
In this diagram the total resistance is 1 + 2 + 3 = 6 ohms. Notice 

that the total resistance is greater than any individual resistance.  
You might recall that in our first example we said a 12V circuit with  
a resistance of 6 ohms would have current of 2A. We can test that  1 0  20  30

the voltage drop across each resistor

for the second, the drop  =  2x2  =  4V  
for the third, the drop  =  2x3  =  6V - 
and the total  =  12V

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits

Series and Parallel Circuits 2 
Cheap party lights have the bulbs connected in series. The problem is that, although the wiring is simple, a failure of any single bulb breaks the circuit and all the lights go out. Each bulb then has to be tested in turn to find the one that failed. Series 
circuits are seldom used for aircraft electrical systems. 
A better way of wiring lights is to have a supply wire and a return wire and to connect each bulb between the wires, as shown here. This is called placing the bulbs or, to use the more general term, loads in parallel. 

Now the failure of any single bulb leaves the rest of the circuit unaffected and the failed bulb can be identified. 
In series the total resistance is always bigger than any individual resistance

A parallel circuit with only two resistors can use an alternative formula derived from the first to find the total resistance 
= 
The inverse of the total resistance is the sum of the inverses of the individual resistances 
In parallel circuits the total resistance is always less than any individual resistance 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 

Power 
When a current flows through a resistor it generates heat, sometimes so intense that it is visible as light as in the case of our party lights. This heat is work done or power lost in the circuit. The power is measured in watts, for which the symbol is W, and can be found from the formula 
Power= IV 
that is to say the product of current and voltage. As we already know that V = IR we can replace the Vin the formula with IR to find an alternative and more common expression 
Power= 12R 
Thus if we go back to our very first example where the current was 2A and the resistance 60 we can find that the power lost in the circuit was 
= 12R 
= 22 x6 
= 24 Watts It is useful to note that, as power lost is the product of the square of the current and the resistance, it would make sense to reduce the current and increase the voltage if we wanted to reduce the power loss over a fixed resistance. This is why the overhead power lines have a very high voltage, some 500 000 V, which is reduced to a lower voltage before it reaches our houses it reduces power loss in transmission. The opposite is also true, more power is lost in high current conditions. 

More power is lost in high current conditions 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
ft.iiiiWo 
Meters 
Ammeters, which indicate current, and voltmeters, which indicate emf, are very similar in operation. Both detect current flow using a coil moving in a magnetic field, the difference is that whereas ammeters are wired in series with the circuit and have a very low resistance voltmeters are wired in parallel and have a very high resistance. 
This means that the current through the voltmeter varies very little as the main circuit resistances change. 
Although the voltmeter detects current it is calibrated in volts. 

Both ammeters and voltmeters detect current 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
Meters continued 
An aircraft's supply voltage is kept constant. As the loads or resistances are switched on and off the current flow will change. Ammeters are sometimes called load meters because they indicate the load in the circuit. 
Ammeters may have the needle in the centre to show current in either direction or may have the needle resting on the left to show total current flow in one direction only. These two types are known as zero centre and zero left ammeters. 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 
Electric Fields 

An accumulation of electrons creates a static charge on a body, static because it is not moving. The unit of charge is a coulomb, roughly equal to 6.21 x 1018 electrons. 
A static charge influences the area around it. A negative charge would attract positively charged objects and vice versa. It produces an electrical field. The SI units of electric field strength are newton's per coulomb or volts per metre. 
Electric field is a separate concept to a magnetic field (which you have already met in your study of instrumentation). The combination of the electric field with the magnetic field produces electro-magnetism. The electro-magnetic force thus produced is known as a Lorentz Force, after the Dutch scientist Hendrik Lorentz (1853 -1928). 
An aircraft picks up a static charge as it flies through, rain, clouds, snow, dust and other particles. This build up is known as precipitation static. The amount of build up depends on the nature ofthe particles, dry snow and ice particles found in cirrus clouds produce substantial build up as does flying near thunderstorms. 


The unit of electrical charge is the coulomb 


Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 

Electric Fields continued 
If left alone the static would cause interference and noise on the intercom and radios. To alleviate this problem the designers use a combination of bonding links and static wicks. The bonding ensures that all metal parts of the aircraft are connected electrically, ensuring that there is no difference in the electrical potential between one part of the aircraft and another. With no potential difference there is no emf, so no current can flow. 
The static wicks, mounted on the trailing edges, usually the control surfaces, discharge the build up to atmosphere. The wicks also need the continuity of bonding, to ensure that all parts of the aircraft can efficiently discharge their static. 
Wicks play no part in arresting or diverting lightning strikes. In fact, they must be inspected following a lightning strike to ensure that they are still effective in discharging the static. 
Static wicks should be inspected as part of the pilot's walk around. 


Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits 

Electric Fields continued 
Static electricity also builds up during re-fuelling/de-fuelling as a result offriction caused by the fuel passing through the pipe. To prevent such build up the aircraft and refuelling vehicle need to be electrically bonded. To achieve bonding aircraft is connected to an earthing point on the ground, the truck is connected to earth by either a bonding strap or by conductive tyres, the hose nozzle is bonded to the refuelling point and finally the truck is bonded to the aircraft before refuelling commences. 

Static wicks should be inspected during a pilot's walk around 

Basic Electrical Theory and Ci rcuits

Direct Current Electrics 
Everything we have considered so far applies to both Direct Current (DC) circuits where current flows in only one direction and Alternating Current (AC) circuits where it washes backwards and forwards. Now we are going to consider DC circuits in more detail. 
DC circuits have the advantage of simplicity and DC electricity has the advantage that it can be stored ready for use when required in a battery. The simplest aircraft will have only DC supplies, 


although most have both DC and AC. Direct current can be obtained from batteries from DC generators or from AC generators, through a rectifier which converts AC to DC. AC · 1/\ f\ f\ f\ f\ f\ f\ f\
J YYYYYYY\ 

generators with a DC output are commonly called alternators. \TVVVVVV\ 
DC Generator Alternator 
DC circuits are simpler than AC circuits 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Batteries 
Batteries store electricity to allow it to be used when required. The battery is used for starting and to provide an emergency supply in flight in case of generating system failure. They are made up of a number of cells, each ofwhich consists of two plates of different material, separated by a liquid or gel electrolyte solution. The chemical action ofthe electrolyte on the different plates erodes or changes the material allowing an electron flow from one to the other, making one terminal negatively charged and the other positive. 
The voltage provided by an individual cell depends on the material ofthe plates and the particular chemical used. 
Batteries can be made up of either primary or secondary cells. If electrical current is continually drawn from a cell the chemical action between the plates eventually ceases and the emf decays. The cell is said to be discharged or more colloquially we say the battery is flat. Primary cells cannot be recharged whereas a secondary cell can. 
In a secondary cell battery the chemical process is reversible by applying a voltage slightly higher, about 112%, of the battery voltage and producing current flow back into the battery. 
ft.iiflo 
Lead (PbO) negative plate 

Lead peroxide (Pb304) positive plate 

Batteries store electricity using chemical reactions The recharging voltage is 112% of battery voltage

Primary Cells 

In a primary cell the positive plate is carbon and the ammonium Positive terminal chloride gel electrolyte is encased in a zinc shell which also acts as the negative plate. This is a normal torch battery. A primary 

Wax seal cell produces 1.5 volts. As they cannot be recharged, primary cells have a limited application on aircraft. 
Ammonium chloride electrolyte
