Carbon rod 
Primary cells are not rechargeable 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Secondary Cells 
There are three main types of secondary cell batteries used on aircraft, and these three are the types currently examined by EASA. The lead acid battery, just like a car battery, is used on smaller aircraft. Larger and modern transport aircraft are fitted with nickel cadmium (NiCad) batteries and even later aircraft use lithium ion (Li-ion) batteries. Battery technology is changing almost daily, and it is certain that EASA will add to those three types in subsequent editions oftheir learning objectives (LOs). 
Strictly speaking we should differentiate between batteries and cells. A cell is an individual unit that produces electricity. It has a low output voltage ranging between 1.2v and 4v depending on battery type. If you require a larger voltage then add more cells until you achieve the desired voltage. A collection of cells joined together in a single container is known as a battery. 
The capacity of a battery refers to how much current it can supply for what duration. This is achieved through cell plate area large plates, large capacity. Capacity is expressed in Amp Hours (Ah). A 10 Ah battery is capable of supplying 1 amp for 10 hrs or 10 amps for 1 hr or any multiple thereof, in theory at least. 
Terminals 
Casing 
Lead dioxide (positive plate) Sulphuric acid Lead (electrolyte surrounds (negative plate) the plates) 
Secondary cells are rechargeable 
Lead acid batteries give 2.2 volts per cell 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Lead Acid Batteries 
The traditional lead acid secondary cell has a lead peroxide positive plate and a lead negative plate with a dilute sulphuric acid electrolyte. The plates and electrolyte are contained in a plastic or hard rubber case. 
The voltage of a fully charged lead acid cell with no loads on it is 
2.2 volts. If the cell is then connected to a circuit with a load the voltage will drop to 2 volts. This is called the on load voltage. 
As a battery discharges the sulphuric acid turns both plates to lead sulphate, the strength of the acid reduces and the cell voltage drops. The only way to positively determine the state of charge of a lead acid battery is to measure the Specific Gravity (SG) of the electrolyte. This is done with an instrument called a hydrometer. 
Some fluid is drawn into the glass tube of a hydrometer and the value of SG is read off against a scale indicated by a 'float' within the tube. Fully charged it should be between 1.25 and 1.30 a low state of charge is 1 .20 to 1.24 and fully discharged is 1.17. 
If a lead acid battery is left discharged for a long period of time the sulphation of the plates makes the battery unserviceable. Lead acid batteries cannot be stored in a discharged state, they need to be maintained and regularly recharged. 
ft+iifto 


DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Nickel Cadmium (NiCad) Batteries 
Alkaline or NiCad batteries have a nickel cadmium positive plate and nickel oxide hydroxide negative plate with a potassium hydroxide gel electrolyte. This type of battery is lighter and stronger than lead acid batteries, has longer life but costs more and only produces 1.3 V off load or 1.2 Von load per cell. They do, however, produce a relatively constant voltage as they discharge. 
The advantages of NiCad batteries are: 
• 
They have a wider temperature operating range. 

• 
They hold a constant voltage until almost completely discharged. 

• 
They don't spill acid. 

• 
The charge rate is fast. 

• 
They can be stored discharged. 

• 
Sturdiness due to their metal castings. 



ft·iiifl!o 
The one real problem with NiCad batteries is that if they are recharged too quickly their temperature rises. This reduces the internal resistance which, in turn, leads to an increase in current flow. This increases the temperature again and the cycle continues until the battery electrolyte boils and the battery is damaged. This is called thermal runaway. 
Thermal runaway does also occur in lead acid batteries but it is less common and less severe. 
Too rapid a recharge can lead to thermal runaway 


DC Electrics and Magnetism 
ftiHflo 
Lithium Batteries 
There is a wide range of batteries using lithium. Two types, lithium-ion and lithium-polymer are currently used in aircraft. Lithium batteries have the advantage of high energy density meaning they have a high energy for a given weight. They can operate over a wider temperature range than Ni-Cad batteries. In the early days they could not deal with large currents but now are comparable if not better than lead acid batteries. 
However, they are more expensive, and the charging rate must be carefully controlled as they are more prone than Ni-Cad cells to thermal runaway. One runaway cell affects neighbouring cells leading to explosion and fire. The resulting fires are particulary hot. That the electrolyte is flammable adds to the hazard. In its first year the B787 suffered several problems associated with the Li-ion batteries. The image opposite shows the battery removed from JA829 J after thermal runaway. Extinguishing or containing such fires in flight is difficult. Serious incidents involving Li-ion batteries seem to be increasing and at least one aircraft has crashed with the loss of both pilots. Aircraft Li-ion batteries must be in fire resistant cases vented to atmosphere in an attempt to minimise the hazard. Clearly the more batteries the greater the hazard. 


DC Electrics and Magnetism 
fti+Eflo 
Lithium Batteries continued 
Another problem with some types of lithium batteries is damage to the casing, what appears to be a simple dent can can cause serious internal damage. 
Because of the fire risk, lithium batteries pose a threat to aircraft, not only the aircraft batteries but most portable electronic equipment carried by passengers use lithium. 
There are restrictions on the carriage of lithium ion batteries, although most phone and laptop batteries are small enough to be unrestricted. However, spare batteries must be cabin luggage as they are not permitted in the hold. 
At the time of writing, lithium-polymer is not currently used in large aircraft but is found in new self-launching gliders and drones where it's extra lightness is a bonus. 


DC Electrics and Magnetism 
ft.Mflo 
Battery health 
With most batteries performance improves with temperature but high temperature degrades their life. Heavy current flow also degrades life: if high currents are required on a regular basis use a larger capacity battery. Temperatures above 25°C seriously degrade the life of Li-ion batteries, so they must be kept cool. Battery charging circuitry must be designed to manage the above issues so that the batteries are safe. 

DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Battery Checking 
As a pilot you will not be involved in checking the charge of a battery with a hydrometer. Instead you will use the aircraft voltmeter to compare the off load and on load voltage of the battery. A typical lead acid battery has 12 cells at 2.2 Veach, giving an 'off load' voltage of around 26 V. 
A known or rated load is applied to the battery and the voltmeter monitored for about 15 seconds during which the rated voltage of the battery, typically 24 V, should be maintained. When the load is switched off you would expect the voltage to recover. 
If the battery is almost fully discharged the voltage will drop during this time under load. If one cell within a battery is dead, the voltage will either be reduced or zero. 
You should also be aware that battery performance reduces in cold weather. In extremely cold weather as their internal resistance increases their current flow reduces. Lead acid batteries are affected more than NiCads. 
Battery performance reduces in cold weather 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
ftii!Hfl 
Charging 
The primary source of DC voltage on the aircraft comes from engine driven generators. These produce either 14 or 28 volts DC, 112% of the battery voltage. If connected to the battery the generators will cause a flow of current into the battery, reversing the chemical process and constantly recharging the cells. 
To reduce the chances of thermal runaway NiCad batteries are recharged through a constant current charging device, this prevents the current rising if the internal resistance falls. 
Sensing devices may also cut off the charging power: 
¦ 
When the battery charge is deemed satisfactory. 

¦ 
When there is a battery overheat condition.

In case of a battery internal short circuit. 




Charging voltage is higher than battery voltage

Battery Ratings and Connections 
Aircraft battery voltages are either 12 or 24 V DC. To achieve these values a number of cells are connected in series within the battery. A battery is rated by its voltage and its capacity to provide a certain value of current for an hour. This is called its Ampere/hour, orAmp hour capacity (Ah). 
A battery might be described, for example, as 24 V and having a 50 ampere hour capacity. This means it operates at 24 V to supply 1 A for 50 hours, 2 A for 25 hours or 50 A for one hour. Connecting a number of batteries either in series or parallel affects the total voltage and capacity. 
Batteries connected in series give the same ampere hour capacity but the voltages are summed together. You might like to use 'Series Sums the voltage' to remember that. In the lower diagram in figure 2.5 the total voltage would be 72 V, and the total current would be 50 Ah. If the three batteries were all rated at 50 Ah then we would still have 50 Ah in total.

Single Pole or Dipole 
You will recall that for electricity to flow a closed circuit connecting the outlet and return to the electrical power source is required. Wooden aircraft require wires to and from the batteries to the electrical systems to complete the circuit this is called a dipole or two pole system. Metal aircraft make use of their structure for the return connection. This is a single pole, or earth return system with the negative terminal of the battery connected to earth. 
This diagram illustrates the two systems, the G symbol represents a generator wired in parallel with the batteries. Note also the symbol for a battery, which consists of a number of cells -thus far we have only seen a single cell battery in our diagrams. 
+ 

Adipole system 

Asingle pole (earth return) system 
Modern aircraft use a single pole, negative earth system 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Single Pole System 
The single pole system has the advantage of cutting out half the required wiring but the disadvantage that chafed wires can short to earth disabling systems and creating a fire risk. 
Busbars 
Rather than being connected to individual systems as in a car, aircraft batteries and generators are usually connected to a busbar, a heavy copper bar, and the systems or loads are in turn connected to that. Each load typically has one connection to the busbar and another to earth, this means they are wired in parallel and failure of one component should not affect the supply to another. 
An ammeter between the battery and the busbar indicates whether the battery is either discharging as it powers the busbar or being charged by the generator. An aircraft may have more than one busbar to distribute the electrical power.

DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Interpreting Ammeters 
When we look at instruments it's not simply a matter of, 'what is it reading?' We should be asking: what does this mean? Is it what I expect at this stage of flight? 
The ammeter in the previous image is a relatively large reading that we might expect immediately after engine start. 
The image opposites shows ammeters in 3 situations. 
Situation (a) indicates a small charge going into the battery, This would be expected a few minutes after start once the battery has re-charged after start and should persist throughout the flight. 
Situation (b) indicates an abnormal situation -there is no charge going into the battery. It is not possible to state the cause without further investigation. Cross reference to the voltmeter can provide further clues. 
If the volts were 28v, it would suggest that the battery has become disconnected meaning there is no access to battery in case of a generator failure. 
If the volts were low, say 26 v, it would suggest that the generator output had dropped and might well fail completely. In either case, it would be wise to consider landing as soon as practicable. 

Situation (c) indicates a heavy load on the battery-for whatever reason the generator is not functioning or is not on line. Cross reference to the voltmeter/ generator failure light should provide further clues. If you are unable to remedy the situation, you have electrics for as long as the battery will last. Land as soon as possible. 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Electricity and Magnetism 
A permanent magnet is surrounded by a magnetic field which can be easily seen with iron filings. The path taken by the magnetic flow -normally called the magnetic flux -is indicated by the lines of the iron filings. We can see that the flux field runs from a little way in from the ends of the magnet, not at the extremities. We already encountered this behaviour in the Instrumentation part of the course. We can produce the same effect with electricity as it flows through a conductor to create an electromagnet. The advantage of an electromagnet is that, because the current can be controlled, the strength of the magnet can also be controlled. Increasing current flow increases the magnetic field. 
PAGE 77 23 ~
en ¦ 


DC Electrics and Magnetism 
The Corkscrew Rule 
Lines of magnetic force around a wire carrying an electrical 
...... 
current will rotate in the same direction as a corkscrew which is moving in the direction of the current. This means a current carrying wire has a circular magnetic field around it. 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Coils 
If the wire is looped again and again to create a coil then the resulting magnetic field increases in strength and the field around the electromagnet will be like that shown here. Increasing the number and thickness of the coils increases the strength ofthe field. 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Core 
To further improve the electromagnet we insert a soft iron core, known as a ferromagnetic core, to concentrate the inner field. Now we have a strong controllable electromagnet. 
DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Solenoids 
As children we have all played with magnets and know that the North and South poles of a magnet are attracted to their opposite pole but repelled by a like pole. If the iron core is not central within the coil when the current to the electromagnet is switched on it will be attracted to the centre of the coil. This movement of the core allows the electromagnet to be used to move switches and valves. In this form it is called a solenoid. 
Solenoids are often used to control hydraulic and pneumatic valves and heavy duty electrical switches. The solenoid shown in this animation is being used to move a valve actuator. When current is applied to the coil windings, the core is drawn into the coil against spring pressure, moving the actuator link. When the current is cut, spring pressure returns the actuator to its original position. Solenoids can be designed to hold valves or switches either closed or open in the event of power failure. 

Spring Moving core 
Actuator link 


Solenoids operate very low torque valves and switches

Dolls-Eye Indicators 
Electromagnets can also be used to control dolls-eye magnetic indicators. When current is applied to the electromagnet it attracts a permanent magnet in the indicator causing it to rotate against 
spring pressure. When off, the white side of the dolls-eye shows to attract attention. Magnetic indicators have the advantage of not being as fragile as bulbs and are typically used to indicate rotary actuator positions.

DC Electrics and Magnetism 
Relays 

Electromagnets that are used to switch other electrical circuits are called relays. Here the magnetic field ofthe electromagnet attracts the contact breaker against spring pressure to hold the circuit open. 
Relays are typically used to remotely switch low to medium current circuits, sometimes switching several at once. 
Relays can be designed to fail with the points open or closed.

DC Motors and Generators 

AGK 20 
DC Motors and Generators 
Motors 
We have seen that passing a current through a wire creates a magnetic field that can in turn be used to make solenoids and relays move. What we have yet to discover is how to create continuous motion, in other words a motor. The purpose of a motor is to convert electrical energy into mechanical energy. Motors can be either DC, as we shall see in this chapter, or AC, which we shall meet later. 
A rotatable coil of wire with a current passing through it has a magnetic field. When it is placed in between the poles of a permanent magnet the two magnetic fields attract and repel each other to create torque, a turning force on the wire. This process is known as electromagnetic induction. The inductance and voltage in a coil depend upon the thickness of the wire, the number of turns in the wire, and the strength of the magnetic field, all of which we shall soon examine in greater depth. 
ft+IFfdo 
A current passed through a wire in a magnetic field creates a force 

A conductor is positioned in a magnetic field perpendicular to the magnetic field lines. If a current is passed through this conductor: 
• a force will be exerted on the conductor. 
• the intensity of the magnetic field will decrease . 
• there will be no effect unless the conductor is moved. 
• the current will increase. 
DC Motors and Generators 
Fleming's Left Hand Rule 
The direction in which the coil moves is dependent on its position within the magnetic field and the direction of the current flow through the loop. An easy way to find the direction in which the loop moves is to apply "Fleming's left hand rule". 
However, another force, called a Lorentz force, is also produced, which is perpendicular to the current and perpendicular to the magnetic field. The direction of the force is demonstrated by Fleming's right hand rule, which is explained later in the chapter. 
John Fleming was an English physicist who developed many practical methods of determining the effects of magnetic fields. Fleming's Left Hand Rule says, using the First finger of the left hand to point in the direction of the magnetic Field (North to South) and mlddle finger to point in the direction ofthe current flow (I) through the loop, the thuMb will point in the direction the of Motion of the coil.

Fleming's left hand rule Ml ddle finger points in direction of current flow (I) 
First finger points in direction of magnetic Field 
ThuMb points in directionof Motion 


n,,,._DC Motors and Generators 
Commutators 
Left to its own devices the coil would end up stationary and at right 
Carbon
angles to the magnetic field. The trick is to cut the current just 
Brush 
before it reaches this position and, as it continues to rotate past it, then supply current again for another half rotation in the same direction so the rotation continues. The device that does this is called a commutator. 
A commutator is a conductive ring in two pieces. Electrical contact is made with it through brushes, small blocks of solid carbon held against the commutator by springs. With the commutator in the position shown in here the current will flow through the coil (correctly called an armature) causing it to rotate. As it reaches the 90° position the brushes are in the gap between the commutator segments, current is cut but angular momentum keeps the armature turning to make the contact again. 
The maximum torque or twisting moment is found where the coil lies parallel with the magnetic field. 
In a motor the armature carries the current 
DC Motors and Generators 
Complete Motors 
Complete motors use many turns of wire and multi segment commutators so the current is always flowing through the wires in the maximum torque position. 
Current flow 
DC Motors and Generators 
ft.iiMo 
Field Coils 
Field coils are wound round the iron cores to create electromagnets rather than use weaker and uncontrollable permanent magnets. 
The torque developed by a motor is linked directly to the Lorentz force produced. Lorentz force is governed by the strength of the magnetic field around the conductor, the current flow in the the conductor and the length of the conductor within the field. In practical terms the length is increased by having many windings. 
The rotating part of a motor or generator is referred to as the 'rotor' and the static part as the 'stator'. 

The field coils produce the magnetic field 
DC Motors and Generators 
Field Windings Series Wound Motors 
Careful design can create motors for different purposes. To create a motor with high starting torque we would need high field strength initially. It makes sense, then, to put the field coils in series with the armature. Series wound motors need heavy field wiring to take the high currents. 
A disadvantage is that without anything to slow them down series wound motors can easily over-speed. Typically they are used as engine starter motors and for operation of retractable flaps and slats, systems requiring high initial torque.

Series wound DC motors, the most common, have high starting torque 
DC Motors and Generators 
ft+Hilo 
Shunt Wound Motors 
Parallel or shunt wound field windings have the advantage that the current flow through them is low and constant thus creating a constant speed motor, a disadvantage is that they produce low torque and cannot take much mechanical load on start-up. Shunt wound motors are used to drive cooling fans and a device we shall meet later, a rotary inverter. 


DC Motors and Generators 
Compound Wound Motors 
The obvious compromise between the two is to make a motor with both shunt and series wound field wires to try to gain the advantages of both types. The compound wound motor uses shunt windings to create 60% to 70% of the magnetic field and series windings for the rest. Compound wound motors are ideal for applications that require a wide range of torques. 
Split Field Motors 
Motors can be made to run in both directions by having two sets of field coils, one wound clockwise and one anti-clockwise. Switching the current from one to the other reverses the polarity of the field and thus the direction ofthe motor. These are called split field motors. 
ft.i=Hfo 


Compound wound DC motors offer a wide range of torques Split field DC motors are reversible 
DC Motors and Generators 
Problems with motors 
Electric motors are extremely reliable machines however a couple of known faults exist. 
Rotor bearings are the weakness of motors their failure can lead to seizure and/or mechanical breakup. In addition, if a motor were to seize, the lack of back emf would result in very high current flow which presents a fire hazard. 
As mentioned earlier, series wound motors must have a load on them or they they will over speed. In theory they would accelerate to an infinitely high speed. In practice they disintegrate before they get there, once again presenting a fire risk. 
ft.Mfo 


DC Motors and Generators 

Actuators 
Whilst solenoid actuators are ideal for low torque operations 
electric motors are the better choice to drive high torque actuators. Electrical actuators fall into two categories: rotary and linear actuators. 
Rotary Actuators 
Rotary actuators are small reversible electric motors which typically operate through 350°. Limit switches at each end of travel turn the motor off and change the direction ofthe field ready for the motor to drive the actuator back on it next selection. They are powered by series wound motors for the torque and are used to open fuel cocks and butterfly type valves. 


Rotary actuators use reversible series wound DC motors Limit switches open to remove power to the actuators 
DC Motors and Generators 
Linear Actuators 
Linear actuators use a DC motor turning a screw jack to give precise control over the actuator position. Solenoid operated brake systems avoid over-run when power is turned off, and friction clutches are fitted to protect against mechanical overload. Limit switches cut power to the actuator motor when the limit of travel is reached. Linear actuators may also be referred to as inching controls. 
Linear actuators are reversible DC motors with series field windings for instantaneous torque. A typical application for inching controls would be as electrical trim or electrical flap actuators. 
Electric or hydraulic trimming Jack 
Linear actuators are called inching controls Linear actuators use series wound reversible DC motors 
DC Motors and Generators 
Generators 
So far we have seen that if we pass a current through a wire in a magnetic field we get movement. The reverse is also true ifwe move a wire through a magnetic field a voltage is induced in the wire. 
This means that ifwe turn a motor armature with a drive from an engine we generate a voltage. A generator, then, is a motor working backwards a motor is a generator working backwards.

DC Motors and Generators 
Fleming's Right Hand Rule 
The relationship between the motion, the field and the induced current in a generator is described by Fleming's right hand rule the relationship for motors is Fleming's left hand rule. Remember to use the right hand for generighters. 
The size of the induced voltage depends on the strength of the magnetic field, the number of turns of wire and the rate at which the lines of magnetic flux are cut. The same controlling factors make the construction of a simple generator just like a simple motor. 
Fleming's right hand rule

Flemings right hand rule applies to generators 
DC Motors and Generators 
Magnets 
Some of the simplest generators rely only on permanent magnets for the field these have the advantage that the field is present as the generator starts to turn. This method can only be used for small, low output devices as the magnetic field is small. If electromagnets are used for the field an excitation current can be drawn from other sources such as the battery these are separately excited systems. 
More commonly the generator relies on residual magnetism in the iron core to provide a small magnetic field on start up. Once current begins to be generated this can be directed through the field coils to increase the output. This is a self-excited system. Self-excited generators sometimes lose their residual magnetism because of excess heat, shocks or inadvertent reversal of the excitation current. Residual magnetism can be restored by briefly passing a current through the field coils in the correct direction. This is called field flashing. 
Field coils Armature

DC Motors and Generators 
Parallel Wound Generators 
Putting the field coils in parallel with the output voltage helps initially as large variations in the output current as systems are turned on and off will have only a small effect on the current through the many fine windings of the field coils. 
Resistance changes in the main circuit will still, however, have a discernible effect. As more electrical items, wired in parallel, are turned on, more 'paths' are opened for the current to flow through and the main circuit resistance decreases. If the main circuit resistance decreases, the output current or load in the main circuit will increase and the current through the shunt field coils decreases. This will, in turn, decrease the output voltage. 
The parallel wound generator is sometimes referred to as a shunt wound generator. 

0 IL (Load CurrentOutput) 100% 
The voltage of shunt wound generators decreases as more services are switched on 

DC Motors and Generators 
Series Wound Generators 
An alternative system places the field coils in series with the electrical loads. As the field windings now have to take the full output current they must be made of fewer turns of heavier wire. 
In a series wound generator an increase in the main circuit current also increases the field current. Now we have the reverse of the parallel wound system, as load increases field current increases and output voltage also increases. 
The voltage of series wound generators is hard to control and they are not used in aircraft but, as with motors, the series wound system can be mixed with the shunt wound to create a compound wound system. 
Output voltage 

0 IL (Load CurrentOutput) 100% 
The voltage of series wound generators increases 
DC Motors and Generators 
Compound Wound Generators 
The combination or compound wound system combines the characteristics of both the series wound and parallel wound coils to produce a steadier output voltage with load. The fall in output voltage ofthe parallel coil generator as load increases is balanced by the increased voltage of the series wound generator as load increases. The series windings carry a large current so they are few and made of heavy wire the parallel (shunt) windings consist of more windings ofthinner wire. The generators fitted to aircraft are either compound or shunt wound. 
Although the generator output voltage is fairly stable it is still not quite constant enough to use. The next thing to consider is fine control of voltage voltage regulation. 
Shunt field coil 
Output voltage 
0 IL (Load CurrentOutput) 100% 
Combination wound generators are used to provide constant voltage with changing load 
DC Motors and Generators 
Carbon Pile Regulators 
The working part of a carbon pile regulator is a pile of carbon discs whose resistance varies with compression. When compressed the carbon discs make better electrical contact and resistance is decreased. 
At high RPM or low load conditions increasing voltage across the voltage coil increases the pull against a spring which reduces pressure in the carbon pile. This reduced pressure causes the resistance of the pile to increase thus lowering the current in the shunt field which accordingly reduces the output voltage. 
As the resistance of carbon decreases with temperature, it also needs to be cooled this is accomplished by enclosing it in a finned ceramic tube which acts as a heat sink. 
Notice that the regulator is wired in series with the field coils and that, because the regulator acts to reduce the voltage, failure of the regulator would cause the output voltage to increase.

DC Motors and Generators 
Vibrating Contact Regulators 
An alternative form of voltage regulator for low current systems on light aircraft is a vibrating contact regulator. As the generator voltage increases the voltage coil causes the points to open, decreasing the field current, decreasing the output voltage and allowing the points to close. The points will open and close from 50 to 200 times a second. 
Vibrating contact regulators are unsuitable for high output systems as the points might fuse together with the heat generated. Some vibrating contact systems have a current regulator combined with the voltage regulator so that either high current or high voltage will open the points. When either the current regulator or voltage regulators breaker points are open the other regulator will be disarmed. 
Once again the regulator is wired in series with the field coils and, because the regulator acts to reduce the voltage, failure of the regulator would cause the output voltage to increase. 
Modern aircraft with DC generators are fitted with electronic voltage regulators. Generators are rated by supply voltage and current capacity. 


Vibrating contact regulators might be used in light aircraft

DC Motors and Generators 

Parallel Operation and DC Load Sharing 
If two or more generators are installed, perhaps on a twin-engined aircraft, and the voltages are nearly the same the output can be fed to a busbar and then out to the individual components. The generators are then operating in parallel. 
Once connected to the busbar the individual generators must have balanced output voltages or one will do more work than the other. To achieve this load sharing an extra equalising circuit is added between the return lines to the generators. 
The equalising circuit shown here adds two special coils called the equaliser coils which are wired in series with each of the voltage coils and two calibrated resistors. The resistors can be fitted in the negative or, with slight variation, to the positive side ofthe circuit. The equalising circuits are then called negative and positive lead paralleling respectively. The resistors may be special resistors or a series winding of the generator. 
DC generator's output voltage is balanced to balance loads 
DC Motors and Generators 
Load Shedding 
The equalising circuit depends upon the voltage drop across the calibrated resistors. If all generators are supplying the same current the voltage drop across each resistor is the same. If the current is different from either generator, there will be a greater voltage drop across the resistor of the generator supplying the higher current. 
If number 1 generator is taking more load than number 2, the return side of number 1 generator will be at a lower potential than the return side of number 2, leading to a current flow through the equalising circuit from 2 to 1. This acts on the voltage regulators to reduce the voltage of number 1 generator and increase the voltage of number 2 by varying the excitation currents to the field coils restoring the balance. In a steady state no current will flow in the equalising circuit. 
If the equalising circuit were to fail then any generator with a higher output voltage would supply significantly more current. Where multiple generators are installed each generator would have its own ammeter to allow the pilot to monitor correct operation of the equalising circuit. 
If either generator were to fail it should be isolated and non­essential electrical services turned off to reduce the load on the remaining generator. This is called load shedding. 



The equalising circuit acts on the voltage regulators Load shedding is required if a generator fails 
DC Motors and Generators 
Generator and Busbar Protection 
None of the regulating systems discussed so far will protect the generator or the busbar from faults in the system. Each aircraft generator will have an ammeter installed indicating output current in amps. Fluctuating output current and a flickering generator warning light can indicate imminent generator failure. Automatic protection is provided by relays and control breakers that disconnect the generator from the busbar, remove the supply to the generator field coils and provide a fault indication to the pilot. 
Feeder Fault 
A feeder fault to the busbar, that is to say a generator supply fault, can result from a flux imbalance between the voltage coil and the series winding coils. Protection systems would: 
¦ 
Open the generator field current relay. 

¦ 
Open the main relay of the generator breaker. 

¦ 
Open the balance circuit of paralleled generators. 

¦ 
Operate a generator OFF line indicator light. 




DC Motors and Generators 
Reverse Current Protection 
In a two generator system, a differential relay is fitted which ensures that the voltages are almost equal before the generators are paralleled. Once paralleled, any faults will remove one of the generators from the busbar to prevent it being driven as a motor against the direction of the engine. Protection against a reverse flow of current from the battery through the busbar to the generator is also provided. 
The first step to avoiding this is to maintain the generator voltage at a higher level than the battery voltage for 24 V batteries the generator output voltage would be maintained at 28 V. 
The second step is to consider what would occur if the generator output voltage became lower than the battery voltage. Simply put, the battery would feed to generator which would start to act like a motor, rapidly draining the battery. To prevent this we include a reverse current relay to disconnect the generator when either a reverse current flow is detected or the generator output become less than 27 volts. This often incorporates an additional switch to allow the generator to be manually disconnected from the busbar and is referred to as the generator breaker or cut-out. 

Generator voltage is higher than battery voltage 
DC Motors and Generators 
Reverse Current Protection continued 
Generator breaker contacts are held open by spring tension when the generator is off line and closed by a solenoid when the switch is made and generator output is adequate, generally at half a volt over battery voltage. A generator warning light indicates the generator cut-out is open. 
A reverse current circuit breaker (RCCB) may operate to disconnect the generator if the reverse current relay fails. 
If a zero centred ammeter is fitted to show if the battery is charging or discharging then generator failure would be indicated by the ammeter indicating a discharge with the generator warning light on. If a voltmeter calibrated in volts is used it will show the voltage of the electrical system both before the cut-out closes as, for instance, 28 V and after the cut-out closes when it will show battery voltage of 23 V or 24 V. 

DC Motors and Generators 
Thermal Trips 
When working hard the generators get very hot. In flight the generators are normally cooled with ram air. If the cooling is insufficient there may be thermal trips fitted which turn off the field current. If the generator is taken off line because ofoverheating it should be allowed to cool for a while before bringing it back on line. 

Starter Generators 
Some aircraft are designed to take advantage of the fact that the construction of a DC motor and generator are the same by using a single machine to function in both roles. 
Initially the machine works as an engine starter motor. Once the engine is running the machine is selected to operate as a generator. Using the same component for both tasks reduces weight. 
Starter generators are only practical on relatively small engines as the energy required to start an engine is related to its size. Small turbine engines may be able to use the battery for a very limited number of start attempts however, large turbine engines require a greater power source, normally a pneumatic starter powered from the APU or an external air trolley.

DC Power Distribution 
Grouping services on busbars makes load shedding a relatively simple operation. Aircraft systems are classified as vital services, essential services or non-essential services. Each group is powered from separate busbars so that power to groups of electrical services can be simply controlled.

ft+i.+l o 
DC Power Distribution 
The vital services are powered from the battery busbar, this is also called the "Hot Busbar" or "Direct Busbar". This busbar is directly connected to the battery and is always powered. 
Aircraft fitted with DC generators may require some AC power for instruments and avionics. The AC supply is obtained from an inverter, a device that turns DC to AC. 
The generators are connected to their busbars via the Generator Control Breakers (GCB). The N°1 and 2 DC busbars can be connected together by operation of the Bus Tie Breaker (BTB) either for parallel operation or to power the whole system from one generator. The BTB is occasionally referred to as the changeover relay. 
Provision is made on some aircraft for external ground power to feed the busbars. Closing the BTB with ground power supplied would allow all the services to be powered on the ground. Bringing an engine generator on line automatically trips the external power relay. This diagram shows a typical DC power distribution system for an aircraft. 
Voltmeters and ammeters are fitted so that the electrical system can be monitored. Warning lights indicate if a generator is off line or a busbar is not powered.

means that the circuit breaker cannot be held in to re-make the circuit with the malfunction still present. Non-trip free circuit breakers, which are not permitted in aircraft, can be held in to re­make the circuit. Neither can be reset until the fault is cleared.

DC Power Distribution and Circuit Protection 
ft.iff+l o 
Bi-Metallic (Thermal) Circuit Breakers 
These circuit breakers are operated by a bi-metallic strip, two strips of metal bonded together. The heating effect of the current causes the metals to expand by different amounts and this causes the strip to bend. Excessive current will bend the strip so much that a latch is moved to trip the circuit breaker and literally break the circuit. 
Magnetic Circuit Breakers 
A possible disadvantage of bi-metallic circuit breakers is that the heating effect takes time. Magnetic circuit breakers provide a quicker response. Similar to the thermal circuit breakers, magnetic circuit breakers trip a mechanical latch. Magnetic breakers can also be used to sense direction of current so may be used as reverse flow breakers. 
Fuses 
Most fuses are made of a waisted strip of zinc alloy. The heating effect of excessive current causes the alloy to melt and break the circuit. An extra 10% of fuses must be carried in flight as spares. 
Slow Blow Fuses or Current Limiters are made of a waisted strip of tinned copper. This has a high melting point, which means that it will stay intact in the event of a high transient overload but melt and break the circuit if there is a continuous overload. 

Rating 
Fuses are rated in amps: a fuse rated at 10 amps should be able to carry a continuous load of 10 amps without failure. In practice current flow fluctuates and fuses suffer from fatigue so a fuse of a higher value may be used. For example, if a circuit has an expected flow of 10 amps then a 15 amp fuse would be reasonable. 
Risks 
One of the reasons for a high current is a short circuit, which means a part or all of the circuit has been by-passed reducing the overall resistance of the circuit. If you consider Ohms law, V = IR it is clear that if the resistance goes down then current flow increases creating heat which may lead to fire. Then look from a power perspective Watts = IV. If current goes up then power consumed by the circuit goes up. A short circuit could easily overload the generator causing generator failure/fire. 
In case you are wondering about the voltage, it remains constant by the action ofthe voltage regulator. However, switching of high loads may lead to transient spikes in voltage while the regulators attempt to regain control. Most circuit breakers and fuses can cope with transient changes provided they are not excessive. 
DC Power Distribution and Circuit Protection 
ft+M+lo 
Risks continued 
Circuit breakers and fuses are part of the safety system, respect what they tell you: the current flow was excessive! You are permitted one attempt to reset a circuit breaker or replace a fuse. When replacing a fuse it is important to use a fuse of the correct rating otherwise there is a fire risk. 
Warning Lights 
On most modern aircraft lights and indicators are designed to be off when the system is operating normally. This is sometimes referred to as a 'black panel' as lights only come on if the unusual happens. Lights are generally, but unfortunately not exclusively, colour coded as shown opposite. 
Filaments of the bulbs are tested by pushing in on the light lenses or by a master test switch to show a complete panel of lights.

Toggle and Momentary Switches 
Toggle switches are the most basic, and probably the most familiar, of them all. The panel shown here has a variety of switches in it. We need not concern ourselves yet with the function of this panel -we will meet it in a later chapter. The switch marked GALLEY is a simple toggle switch, which would stay 'latched' in either position once moved there. The switch to the left of that, marked BAT is another toggle switch, but this time guarded to prevent inadvertent operation. Most guarded switches like this will revert to the design default position when the guard is closed, whether that is OFF, ON or any other available selection. Some toggle switches incorporate a second latch, requiring the actual switch to be pulled out slightly before operating, again to prevent accidental operation. 


DC Power Distribution and Circuit Protection 
Toggle and Momentary Switches continued 
Guarded switches are often colour coded. A black guard shows that the switch can be moved from one position to the other freely. A red guard warns the pilot that once operated the action cannot be reversed. Generator disconnect switches discussed later have red guards. 
To the right of the GALLEY switch is a push switch marked RESID VOLTS. This is a type known as a 'momentary' switch, which needs to be held in against spring pressure to complete -or indeed break -a circuit. 
A red guarded switch means that once operated the action cannot be undone 

DC Power Distribution and Circuit Protection 
Switchlights, Rocker and Rotary Switches 

Many modern aircraft use switch lights, which are push switches with integral lights to show the selection made. These are very common on autopilot control panels, for example. 
Rocker switches are very common in cars, but rather less so in aviation. They combine the function of a toggle and a push/pull switch. They can also be illuminated from within to indicate the status of the switch, as you can see in this image. 
Another type of switch is the rotary switch, used where a toggle switch would not have enough positions available. Two rotary switches are depicted in the illustration on the previous page, above the bottom row of switches that we have already met. Slightly different rotary switches are also to be found bottom right in the image here.

····-· --····-··--, ..-········,, .··-····--··-.····­
Switches 
Microswitches are used extensively in aircraft. An actuating rod into the switch moves contacts to make or break a circuit. They might be used, for example, to show the status of a panel or door. They are often used on the undercarriage legs to show 'Weight On Wheels' (WOW) status. A schematic of an NC microswitch is shown in this illustration. 
A proximity switch serves a similar function to a microswitch, indicating the position of an item -for example an undercarriage leg. Rather than physical contact, the proximity switch works by the object interrupting a magnetic field. This can be by pulling together two blade-like contacts, known as a 'reed switch' or by solid state (no moving parts) where an inductance loop is influenced by a ferrous component on the other half of the item being monitored. 
Thermo switches, as the name implies, work on temperature. A change in temperature will result in a change of the switch position. These switches are not normally operated by the crew, as they often serve to automatically isolate an overheated component, in much the same way as a circuit breaker. 
Time switches are those that react to a time that has been programmed into them -think central heating controller and you have it. The time used could be the actual time, or perhaps an

AC Electricity 


AGK 22 
AC Electricity 
AC Electricity 
Whilst DC electricity is a constant flow of electrons in one direction along a wire, AC electricity is produced when electrons move backwards and forwards along a wire. It could be produced by an AC Generator or via a DC circuit which is converted to AC by an inverter. 
This diagram shows how AC voltage varies with time, the current flow in an AC system is constantly changing both in magnitude and polarity. The frequency of an AC current is expressed as the number of complete cycles a second. One cycle a second is a Hertz (Hz), and three full cycles are shown here. 
As voltage fluctuates evenly between peak positive and peak negative the average voltage is clearly zero. This tells us nothing of value as the peak voltages could be very high or very low and still the average voltage would be zero. To get around this problem of description the voltages are squared to make them positive, then averaged, then the square root is taken to provide the Root Mean Square (RMS) voltage, a more meaningful indication. The heating effect ofthe RMS AC voltage is the same as the equivalent DC voltage. The RMS voltage is, because of the maths of a sine wave, 0.707 times the peak voltage. UK mains electricity is 240 volts RMS, so the peak voltage is: 
240 -a-0.707 = 340 volts. 
ft.ihi·-l o 
+150 V 

Voltage 00 

-150 V 
AC electricity comes from alternators 
RMS voltage is 0.707 times peak voltage and has the same heating effect as the equivalent in DC 
AC Electricity 
AC Generation 
You can see here a simple AC generator and its output with a DC generator for comparison. In each case the wire loop rotating in the magnetic field, the rotor, has a voltage induced in it. In the DC generator the segmented commutator ensures that the pulses of current flowing out to the circuit are of the same polarity (positive or negative). In the AC generator the commutator is replaced by slip rings which continuously collect the current induced in the loop. The current changes from positive to negative as the sides of the wire loop pass the North and South poles of the stationary electro-magnet, the stator. In practice there are many loops of wire wound on the rotor to increase the alternator efficiency. 
It is equally acceptable, and more common in aircraft, to feed a low DC current via brushes and slip rings to the rotating coil (the rotor), thus producing a rotating magnetic field which induces AC electricity in the stationary coils (the stator). An AC generator that has a built in rectifier rendering the output DC is known as an alternator. Most cars have alternators. An AC generator producing just one output is known as a single phase generator, though there are not many of these. Aircraft can use single phase, three phase or alternators. We shall meet alternators next followed by three phase. 
ft+i.i+lo 

· 1/\ f\ f\ f\ f\ f\ f\ f\
J YYYYYYY\ 
\TVVVVVV\ 
DC Generator Alternator 
The rotor rotates, the stator is fixed 
AC Electricity 
Alternators 
AC generators are more efficient than DC generators, so they are sometimes combined with an internal rectifier and used to power DC systems. They are more efficient because they produce voltage at a lower RPM than a DC generator they have a better power to weight ratio and, because they use a slip ring rather than the split ring commutators we met in the last lesson, they produce virtually no arcing and sparking. They therefore produce much less interference in the form of noise through radio and intercom circuits, and do not wear out as quickly, making for reduced maintenance costs. Generators like this are called alternators, and are extensively used in light aircraft and helicopters, as well as being almost universal now in cars. 
ft.IH~f o 

Which of these statements is correct? 
• The frequency of an AC current is expressed as the number of complete cycles a minute, one cycle per 
minute is a Hertz (Hz) 
• The voltage of an AC current is expressed as the number of complete cycles a second, one cycle a 
second is a Hertz (Hz) 
• The frequency of an AC current is expressed as the number of complete cycles a second, one cycle a 
second is a Hertz (Hz) 
• The frequency of an AC current is expressed as the number of half cycles a second, one half cycle a second 
is a Hertz (Hz) 
AC Electricity 
Three Phase Output 
Larger aircraft use AC generators that produce an AC output. These generators have three outputs, called a three phase supply. 
Stator

This animation shows a three phase AC generator with a coil -0 permanent magnet rotating between three sets of induction coils, so effectively three AC generators in one. As the rotor end passes a stator coil, the magnetic field of the rotor moves closer to, then away from, the stator. This changing magnetic field induces an AC current in the stator. The stator pairs are at 120° from each other, thus producing three AC supplies which are 120° out of phase, each phase being tapped from a pair of stator coil windings. 
The animation shows a simple brush less three phase AC generator, it lacks a method to control output voltage. The three phase output is shown in the illustration below. Electrical services can use one, two or three phases at once. 
AC Electricity 
Phase 
In electrical terms, phase has two meanings depending on context. 
The first is the definition of the position at a point in time on a waveform. For example, at a given time, what is the phase angle and what is the amplitude. 
The second is an expression of relative displacement between waves having the same frequency. In three phase circuits there are 120 degrees of phase difference between each phase. This makes sense as 360 degrees divided by 3 equals 120 degrees. 
ft+++~,­

Phase Shift 
Phase shift occurs when the phase angle of a wave is displaced from its original position. It doesn't change the frequency it is more of a shift in position. 
We will see more of phase relationships when we consider AC capacitors and inductors. 
AC Electricity 
Calculating the Frequency 
Aircraft use a frequency of400 Hz, as the motors and generators using or producing those frequencies are considerably smaller and lighter than domestic devices that use 50 or 60 Hz. 
The frequency of the induced AC current will be the rate of revolution of the rotor but divided by 60, as frequency is expressed as cycles a second and rate of revolution is Revolutions Per Minute (RPM). If two magnets are used, two pole pairs, the frequency is doubled, if three are used it is tripled. 
Putting this concept into a formula, we get: 
RPM x Number of pole pairs
Frequency(Hz) = 
60 
ft+M+lo 

Example: An AC generator has four pole pairs at 6000 RPM. What will the frequency be? Solution: 
RPM x Number of pole pairs 
Frequency (Hz) = 60 
6000 X 4
Frequency (Hz) = 
60 = 400 Hz 
Frequency is RPM times pole pairs divided by 60 

AC Electricity 
Self-Excited Brushless AC Generators 
The permanent magnet rotor of the AC generator in the animation on page 4 is usually replaced by a DC powered electro-magnet. Varying the excitation current through the field windings of the electro-magnet varies alternator output voltage. 
The DC supply for the field windings can come from either the battery (separately excited), or from the generator itself, via a rectifier (self-excited). If the excitation current comes from the battery then it follows that a flat battery will result in the generator not operating. This makes the second format, self-exciting, the most common. 
This diagram shows the components of a brushless self-excited AC generator. The voltage regulator controls the DC current to the exciter, which in turn controls the AC current induced. The AC is rectified to DC to provide a controlled excitation current in the generator field coils. The permanent magnets on the stator induce a small amount ofAC in the rotor coils as the generator first starts to turn, and this makes the generator self-starting. 
A typical three phase aircraft AC supply will run at 115 V RMS.

AC Electricity 
Advantages of AC Generators 
The main difference in construction between the DC generator and the AC generator is that, in an AC generator, the windings in which the output current is induced, the armature, are on the stator with the field windings on the rotor. With a DC generator the opposite is the case, the field windings are on the stator and the induction windings of the armature are on the rotor. 
AC generating systems have several advantages over DC systems: 
¦ 
They are more flexible in use. 

¦ 
They are lighter than DC generators. 

¦ 
They have a better power to weight ratio. 

¦ 
It is easy to change the voltage with transformers. 

¦ 
It is easy to convert AC into DC, if required. 

¦ 
They are brushless, so require less maintenance. 

¦ 
They produce a voltage at a lower RPM than DC generators. 


ft.i=hf+lo 

Because of this most piston engined aircraft use alternators where all, or nearly all, of the 14V AC output is converted to DC through a rectifier and controlled with a voltage regulator to feed the aircraft systems and charge the battery. 
Large aircraft use AC generators for the main AC circuits that also supply DC services through rectifiers. Some smaller gas turbine engines, however, still use DC generators as they have an additional function as starter motors -in this case DC current is fed from the battery to the generator which works backwards as a motor. 
AC advantages: better power to weight ratio, voltages can be changed 
Most piston engine aircraft use alternators rather than DC generators 
AC Electricity 
Star Connections 
Now we have three phases, effectively three separate AC supplies, we have to consider how we wire them up. A star connection, shown in this diagram, is used for alternators. 
Each pair ofstator coils is connected at one end to a common star, wye or neutral point and at the other end to the output, through a load and then back to the star connection via a return line. Loads can also be placed between any two outputs to use a different, higher RMS, voltage. The voltage between a single output, a live line, and the return is called a phase voltage the voltage between two live lines is called the line voltage. 
The RMS line voltage is the sum of two out of phase stator coil supplies which is .3 (or 1.73) x the RMS phase voltage, therefore the line voltage is higher than the phase voltage. In aircraft applications the phase voltage is 11 Sv, so the line voltage is 115 x 
1.73 = 200 V. The line current is equal to the phase current as the loads are placed in the live lines, not in the return. 
ft+M+lo

AC Electricity 
Star Connections continued 
Although multiple phases can be used most aircraft loads are single phase loads and therefore placed between the live line and the return. If the loads are kept fairly equally distributed between the phases, as when one current is positive the other two are likely to be negative, the current tends to flow only along the live lines and the return line current is fairly low. 
If a single phase of an AC generator fails, perhaps because of a broken wire or open circuit, then the current in the other two phases will not be balanced and the flow down the return line will increase. Ifthere is a short between a phase and the return line, line to earth, or between two phases, line to line, the current will markedly increase leading to overheating in the stator coils. In short, a failure in one phase of a star wound (connected) system affects all phases. 
Domestic electricity makes use of the star winding. High voltage power is three phase but is split into single phases for small groups of houses. The domestic plug has a live wire in to the plug, a live wire out of the plug and, in the UK, an earth line which literally uses the earth to connect to the wye point of the supply. The theory is that, ifthe loads are balanced in the street then no current flows through the earth line. Many countries dispense completely with the earth connection.

Capacitance and Capacitors 
Capacitors are used to store an electrical charge. They are made by placing a non-conducting material, called a dielectric, between two conducting plates. Applying a voltage to one plate builds up a charge on that plate, positive or negative depending on the voltage, and a matching opposite charge on the other. If the capacitor is disconnected once the charge has been built up it will hold the charge until a circuit is created again allowing it to 

discharge. The plates have a relatively large surface area so it  
takes a little time for the charge to build up. The discharge is  A one farad capacitor with one volt applied to it will store one  
quicker.  coulomb of energy.  
The amount of electricity a capacitor can store is called its  
capacitance and is measured in Farads. A capacitor in a circuit is  
shown by this symbol, similar to the symbol for a battery but with  
two lines ofthe same length.

Capacitance is directly proportional to the area of the plates and inversely proportional to the distance between them. The value of the dielectric is expressed as a number derived from comparing the electrostatic field of the material, with that of a vacuum and called the dielectric constant (K). Capacitors are used in aircraft fuel gauging systems, where the value of K for fuel is about 2 compared to 1 for air. 
Capacitors act differently when fitted to DC and AC circuits. In a 
DC circuit the capacitor acts like a switch. Once charged there will be no further flow in the circuit and any other loads will cease to operate. 
AC Electricity 
Capacitance and Capacitors 3 
A capacitor in an AC circuit will build up a charge as current flows into it in one direction, then discharge and build up an opposite charge as the current reverses. Loads in the circuit will still work. The maximum charge across the plates occurs when the supply current is zero, just before it reverses, and the maximum discharge current occurs when the supply voltage is zero. This is odd, because normally voltage and current are in phase and high voltage means high current. The implication is that, in a purely capacitive AC circuit, the capacitor has the effect of shifting the current and voltage goo out of phase. Strictly, current leads voltage by go0 . 
Current leads voltage with capacitors in the circuit 
AC Electricity 
Capacitance and Capacitors 4 
In reality there is always some resistance in a circuit and it can never be purely capacitive. This means the current never quite leads the voltage by the full 90°, all we can say in reality is that current leads voltage in a capacitive circuit. 
This effect of capacitance in an AC circuit is called capacitive reactance and, as it opposes current flow, it acts like resistance and is measured in ohms. Capacitive reactance is also affected by frequency. Capacitive reactance is inversely proportional to frequency. So as frequency rises capacitive reactance reduces and current flow will increase. 
Another way of putting it is that capacitors act as low resistances to high frequencies and high resistances to low frequencies. A mnemonic helps here, Cows Love Really Happy Farmers for Capacitors have Low Resistance to High Frequencies. 
Capacitors act as high resistance to low frequencies 
AC Electricity 
Inductors and Inductance 
We know that as a magnet is inserted into a coil ofwire a current is induced in the wire. What we have yet to consider is that the induced current will have its own magnetic field which, depending on its sense, would either encourage the initial movement or oppose it. Judicious use of Fleming's left and right hand rules will show that the induced current creates a field that opposes the movement this is Lenz's law. The induced current always flows in such a direction as to oppose the change that is giving rise to it. The opposition can be viewed as an opposing voltage, called a back emf. 
In any AC circuit the collapse of the current before it reverses direction and the subsequent build up will produce a change of the magnetic field and a back emf will be induced. This back emf will be small in a simple circuit but larger if many coils and windings are included. The effect of the back emf, of inductance, is to resist current flow. 
Inductance will be a feature of all AC circuits which include coils such as motors, generators, electro-magnets and transformers. 
Inductive components mounted close to each other can induce electrical currents in each other. This undesirable induction, known as interference, can cause problems. 
The induced current always opposes the movement 
AC Electricity 

Inductors and Inductance 2 
The unit of inductance is the Henry (L). If an emf is induced of 1 volt with a current flow of 1 amp an inductance of 1 L (Henry) is produced. The effect of inductance is called inductive reactance and act like resistance in that it affects current flow. Inductive reactance is measured in ohms. 
An inductance is shown in a circuit by this symbol. 
7JJJJJJ 
Notice the similarity to the symbol for resistance, we shall use this later to remind us that inductors in series and parallel behave the same as resistors. 
We would expect the maximum back emf when the rate of change of current in a circuit is at its greatest. This is where the current is zero, reversing direction from one way to the other. The minimum back emf is where the rate of current change is least. The effect of inductance is not dissimilar to the effect of capacitance except here current lags behind voltage as shown in this illustration. 
Inductors are also frequency sensitive but this time reactance is directly proportional to frequency high resistance at high frequencies and low resistance at low frequencies, the opposite of capacitors. 


Inductance is measured in Henrys Induced current lags behind the voltage Inductors create high resistance to high frequencies

As we have seen the effects of capacitive reactance and inductive reactance are opposite the suspicion grows that capacitors and inductors are complementary and that one can be used to balance the effect of the other. Most circuits have both, and of course they will have resistance as well. 
Capacitive reactance leads current flow, resistance is in phase with it and inductive reactance lags behind current flow, so there cannot be a straight addition of the three. 
The total resistance to current flow in an AC circuit with capacitive and inductive reactance is called impedance and is the vector sum of all three.

The total opposition to current flow in an AC circuit with a combination of reactance (capacitive and inductive) and resistance is called impedance. AC Power 
We know that power equals voltage multiplied by amps. So for a simple resistive only circuit (no capacitors or inductors) we can plot a graph of voltage, current and power as below. Power is at a maximum when volts and amps are also at their maximum values. 


Resistive circuit 
AC Generator and Motors 
ft+ii+io 
AC Power2 
In a circuit which contains a capacitor or an inductor, the voltage will be out of phase with the current. If we plot voltage and current for an out of phase circuit, then we can see that the peak power is lower. 
The maximum power is no longer the product of voltage and current at their maximum but the product of lesser values. This means that power in a capacitive (or inductive) circuit is reduced. Obviously the answer to this is to balance a capacitor with an inductor to bring the current and voltage back into phase if we achieved this we would have a resonant circuit, a circuit where the impedance is totally resistive. 


AC Generator and Motors 
AC Power 3 
Assuming, for the moment, that we have failed to achieve resonance then the impedance of the circuit will be made up of the resistance and the reactance, both capacitive and inductive. 
The work done by the generator overcoming resistance is useful or real power. The work done overcoming reactance is wasted effort, called reactive power or reactive load. Reactive loads waste power. This can be represented by our familiar vector diagram. 
The ratio of useful work (useful power) to total work (total power) is a measure of the efficiency of a circuit. This is called the power factor the power factor is also the cosine of the angle between the real power and the apparent power. 
In practice, this all means that if an AC motor (which has many inductive windings) has a power factor of 0.45 and it is required to put out 500 Wat 25 V, then instead ofjust: 
500 W + 25 V = 20 A What we actually need: 
20 + 0.45 = 44.4A This would suggest a strong case to fit a capacitor in the circuit to increase the power factor.

Generator Power Ratings 
AC generators are rated to show the total power available in kilovolts A (kVA). The measured output elements are kVA and kVAr, reactive load. 
Typical generator output would be from 30 KVA up to 90 KVA for the largest aircraft. 
ft.iflo 

Example: An AC generator rated at 90 KVA is operating in a circuit with a power factor of 0.8. What will be the useful output? Solution: The useful output is 0.8 x 90 = 72 KW. We can use the KW unit for real power.

AC Frequency Control 
Frequency Wild AC Generators 
AC generators will be frequency wild if there is no control of rpm. Outputs from such generators cannot be paralleled to a common system. 
Frequency wild generators are usually fitted to more simple aircraft where the primary electrical supply is DC. Their output is 208 V, 22 KVA with a frequency range from 280 to 400Hz. This is used directly for engine and prop anti-icing and rectified to DC for the other systems. 


Such a system can only be used for systems which rely upon resistance only and are not affected by frequency, basically heating and non-fluorescent lighting. 

AC Generator and Motors 
Constant Speed Drive Units 
Systems that are primarily AC need frequency and voltage control. To produce a constant frequency generator a Constant Speed Drive Unit (CSDU or CSD) is used. This consists of a hydraulic/mechanical unit with its own hydraulic oil supply. As well as providing the hydraulic power for operation the oil in the CSDU is used for cooling the unit. The CSD is connected to the engine by a dog clutch release mechanism known as the CSD disconnect. 
As engine speed or load on the generator changes the CSD alters its output torque to keep the generator running at the same speed and constant frequency. The frequency is stabilised to between 380 Hz and 420 Hz, quoted as 400 Hz, from a typical rotation speed of 6000 RPM. 

Engine 
CSD disconnect 
As inductive reactance is sensitive to frequency, a constant frequency output is required 
Generator Quill shaft 400Hz 

AC Generator and Motors 

Constant Speed Drive Units continued 
High CSD oil temperatures, low pressures and low oil levels indicate an impending failure of the CSD. If any of these occur a warning light in the cockpit alerts the pilots to disconnect the CSD. 
The CSD disconnect can be operated at any time during flight and at any engine RPM but ideally the engine should be at idle to reduce the shock loads. A warning light indicates the CSD disconnect has been operated. 
The CSD disconnect may be operated on the ground if the engine is running above minimum ground idle rpm. Once operated in flight the disconnect can only be reset on the ground by the engineers with the engine stopped. In some installations and for some particular malfunctions an automatic CSD disconnect is provided. 
The final protection for the CSD is a quill drive with a waisted section that is designed to shear if the generator seizes and overloads the drive. 
Engine 
CSD disconnect 
Once disconnected the CSDU can only be reset on the ground 
Generator Quill shaft 400Hz 
AC Generator and Motors 
More modern designs have a CSD mounted in the same casing as the generator, in which case it is known as an Integrated Drive Generator (IDG). The IDG used on the Boeing 737NG is cooled by both fan air and a fuel/oil heat exchanger. It has an automatic CSD disconnect for high oil temperature. There is also a cockpit warning and a manual disconnect for low oil pressure. The A320 family use an IDG with a fuel cooled oil cooler. This IDG has a manual disconnect for high oil temperature or low oil pressure. 
If oil is visibly seen leaking from an engine the source needs to be identified and isolated if possible to reduce the fire risk. The usual suspects are engine oil, hydraulic oil or CSDU/IDG oil. It might be possible to identify the source by observing the instrumentation for each of the systems. Both the hydraulic pump and the CSDU/IDG can be isolated. An engine oil leak can only be stopped by shutting the engine down. 
A combination generator and CSDU is called an IDG 


A VSCF drive takes the variable output of a frequency wild generator, rectifies it to DC then converts it again to constant frequency AC with an inverter. The VSCF drive was developed for the B737-300 to -500 series and was intended to improve reliability. In practice it has proved less reliable than the CSDs that preceded it and has been replaced on the B737 NG with an IDG. 
Paralleling AC Generators 
The reactive load on a generator is affected by the frequency and by the rated output in KVA which is in turn affected by voltage. The real load (KW) is affected by the rated output and the reactive load. It can be seen that the KW, KVA and KVAR are interconnected and any adjustment in frequency will affect both KW and KVAR. If generators are to be paralleled frequency is adjusted by adjusting the CSD output torque to balance the real load and then the reactive loads are trimmed by adjusting the energising current from the voltage regulator. 
In summary: 
¦ 
Real Load -adjusted with CSD Torque. 

¦ 
Reactive Load -balanced by energising current. 


Frequency adjusts real load & reactive load is controlled by voltage 
AC Generator and Motors 
Auxiliary Sources of AC Power 
In the event of loss of an engine driven generator in flight, either through generator or engine failure, there are other ways of providing AC power. 
Auxiliary Power Units (APUs) 
An APU is a small gas turbine engine fuelled from the aircraft main fuel supply that can provide power to drive a three phase generator to produce normal 115/200 volts AC at 400 Hz. It is capable of supplying all the essential electrical loads on its own. On older aircraft it cannot be paralleled with the aircraft's generators as the APU has no CSDU and the turbine speed can vary. On modern, constant speed, APUs the APU generator can be paralleled. 
APUs are normally started electrically. Some aircraft such as the B737 have independentAPU batteries for starting others such as the A320 start the APU from the main aircraft batteries. 
As well as providing back up electrical power in flight the APU can be used to power the aircraft systems on the ground prior to engine start. 
Constant speed APUs can be paralleled 


Hydraulic Drive Units 
Some aircraft such as the B737 are fitted with constant speed hydraulic motors that can, in emergency, be used to drive an AC generator. Such generators produce the same 115/200 V 400Hz AC output but at a reduced power rating. They are not designed to run all of the electrical systems, only the essential AC busbars. 
AC Generator and Motors 
Ram Air Turbines (RATs) 
Emergency generators can be driven directly by Ram Air Turbines but, more usually, the RAT is used to power a hydraulic system and the hydraulic system powers the emergency generator. The RAT, therefore, powers both flying controls and essential electrics. As an example, the A320 family of aircraft have a RAT which, in the event of loss of all generators, deploys to pressurise the blue hydraulic system. A hydraulic drive unit on that system drives an emergency generator which powers the AC and DC essential busbars. You can take a closer look at the RAT using the 3D model on the next page. 
Inverters 
AC power for essential services can be obtained from DC by either static or rotary inverters. The oldest type of inverter, the rotary inverter, is essentially a DC electric motor which drives an AC generator. More modern static inverters use solid state electronics to do the same thing. 
While the input to an inverter is invariably 28 V DC, the output voltage varies according to need. The two most common outputs are 115 V AC as an alternative means to supply a bus bar normally supplied by an engine driven generator or 26 V AC to supply an instrument bus bar.

pump sensor 
Inverters convert DC to AC 
AC Generator and Motors 
AC Motors 

DC motors are ideal for high torque applications and applications where variable speed control is needed. In other situations more reliable AC motors can be used. 
There are two main types, the synchronous motor and the induction motor. The synchronous motor runs at a speed that is related to its supply frequency and thus maintains speed as load varies. The induction motor tends to lose speed as load increases but in its two or three phase forms has the advantage that it is inherently self-starting. 
Synchronous Motors 
Do you recognise this animation? This is a three phase motor, exactly the same as the three phase alternator except now the current is fed into the stationary field coils causing the rotor to rotate. Motor speed is therefore directly related (synchronous) to voltage frequency if not overloaded. Under higher loads, the rotor falls behind the rotating magnetic field reducing efficiency, torque then reduces in turn and the motor slows and quickly stalls. 


AC Generator and Motors 
Synchronous AC Motors continued 
Synchronous AC motors are used for low torque, constant speed applications such as the drive to a tachometer. They may be single or three phase. 
As they are not self-starting, an inductive system is incorporated to bring the motor up to synchronous speed when it then becomes a solely synchronous motor. On occasions a separate DC supply is used to create an electro-magnet on the rotor. 
Synchronous AC motors are low torque and constant speed 
Synchronous AC motors are not self starting 
The speed of a synchronous motor depends upon the frequency ofthe supply and the number of pole pairs (pairs of rotors) and is obtained by dividing the AC frequency by the number of phases ­this give revolutions per second, so to get RPM we must multiply by 60. You should recognise this formula as a rearrangement of the formula used for generators. 
Frequency (Hz) x 60 
Synchronous speed (RPM) = ----'-...;._'---­Number of pole pairs
