AC Generator and Motors 
Induction Motors 
The induction motor differs from the synchronous motor in that instead of using a magnet as a rotor, coils of wire are used. This diagram shows an induction motor. The six black dots represent three coils shown in cross section in reality there would be many more. 
The changing electromagnetic field produced by the stator coils induces a current in the wire which in turn produces its own magnetic field. The magnetic field of the rotor is attracted to the rotating electromagnetic field ofthe stator so the rotor ends up chasing the stator field. 
As the rotor speed builds up towards the speed of the stator field the relative movements of the fields reduce and thus the induced emfand ultimately the torque that makes the rotor turn reduces. This means that the rotor can never turn quite as fast as the stator field. The difference between the two is called the slip speed and it will increase as the torque load on the motor increases. 
Increasing slip speed caused by high torque loads increases inductive reactance and therefore reduces motor efficiency in terms of the ratio of power in to power out. This means induction motors are at their most efficient under light loads. The induction motor is commonly used for fuel and hydraulic pumps, gyro rotors, gyro torque motors and AC actuators. 


Induction motors run slower than synchronous speed, the difference is the slip speed 
AC Generator and Motors 
Induction Motors 2 
Induction motors may be single or three phase but single phase motors will not be self-starting. A curiosity of mathematics means that only two of the phases of a three phase motor need to be reversed to reverse the direction of the motor. Consider a three phase supply running ABCABCABC, if we reverse A and B we get BACBACBAC. 
Rather than use loops of wire, induction motors often use a series of copper or aluminium bars connected to circular metal endplates, each opposite pair act like a closed single loop coil. This is called a squirrel cage motor. 
The diagram is stylised and only shows two loops. In reality, once again, there would be many more. A more realistic representation is shown on the next page.

If a three phase induction motor suffers an open circuit fault on one of the phases then it will continue to run but at only about half speed unless the loads are very heavy when it may stop altogether. Once stopped, it will not be possible to re-start the motor whilst the fault persists. If a short circuit occurs between

AC Generator and Motors 
Delta Connections 
You will recall that generators use a star connection where the return line is required because unless the loads are exactly equal in each phase there will be an unbalanced current. When the loads can be made permanently and exactly equal, for instance where the alternator is used 'backwards' as a motor, the return line can be dispensed with and the phases connected by a delta connection. 
With this connection line voltage is the same as phase voltage but line current must be more than phase current as each line is connected to two phases and two loads. In fact the line current is .3 x phase current or, if you prefer, 1. 73 x phase current. Thus we can say that, in a delta system, voltages are equal but line current is greater than phase current whereas in a Star system currents are equal and line voltage is greater than phase voltage. Use the symbols V ~V to remember that voltages are equal in a delta system, once you have that everything else can be figured out. 
If a single phase on a delta connection is earthed all phases will be earthed. As we have observed an open circuit in one phase will mean the motor continues to run but more slowly. 
Delta connections are used in motors, rotary rectifiers and transformers. 


Line voltage 

Phase voltage 

VtN, delta wound voltages are equal

AC Devices 
Transformers 
Some systems on the aircraft may require lower AC voltages than the generated 115/200 V AC supply. Aircraft instrument systems often work off a 28 V AC supply. To achieve this transformers are used to power busbars at reduced AC voltages. 
Transformers use the principles of electromagnetic induction to increase ( step up) or decrease ( step down) AC voltages. Ifthe voltage increases the current must reduce and vice versa as the power out must be the same as the power in, assuming no power lost in the transformer itself. 

Transformers step up or step down AC voltages 
AC Devices 
ft.ii+lo 
Transformers continued 
Transformers have two sets of windings, the primary input windings and secondary output windings, arranged on a laminated metal core. Note that the two sets of windings are not electrically connected in anyway. 
An alternating current in the primary windings induces a magnetic field in the core which in turn induces an alternating current in the secondary windings. The output current and voltage will depend upon the proportion of windings between each coil. More coils on the secondary windings than the primary will step up the voltage, fewer will step it down. The relationship is straightforward, twice as many turns produces twice the voltage and, of course, half the current. 
Transformer cores are made up of laminated sheets in order to reduce the eddy currents which are induced at right angles to the field and will have a heating effect. 
The transformer is particularly useful if you have to transmit electricity a long way. Very high voltages can be used with correspondingly low current and consequently low power loss. The heating effect of electricity is l2R, remember. 
Transformers can be multi phase, and in a three phase system the transformers will be delta wound as the loads are predictable and can be balanced. 

Twice the turns, twice the voltage but half the current Three phase transformers are delta wound 
AC Devices 
ft+it+lo 
Auto Transformers 
If a step up or step down in voltage is required in a low current circuit then an auto transformer may be used. Primary and secondary coils are combined in one coil and the output current is tapped off at the appropriate number of turns. The step down is over a wide range, but the step up is usually limited to around 110­115% of the input. 
Secondary
Simpler and lighter in construction than a normal transformer, this type is usually only used for lighting in aircraft. 
Primary 

AC Devices 
Rectifiers 
A rectifier allows current to flow easily in one direction but not in the other direction. Rectifiers in normal circuits can be semiconductors or valves called diodes. 
Semiconductor diodes are made from silicon or germanium 'doped' or made impure with other materials such as boron. Doped crystals can either have an excess of electrons in which case they are known as N or negative type material or a shortage of electrons, called electron holes, in which case they are called P or positive type material. If N and P type materials are placed next to each other current can flow from N to P, negative to positive, but not from P to N. This creates a PN diode, the most common rectifier. 
The circuit diagram symbol for a diode is shown here and it would permit conventional current flow in the direction of the arrow, in this case from left to right. 
.I 

.I (\ (\
r

Input Output 
Rectifiers allow current to flow in only one direction

ft.ii+lo 
Rectifiers 2 
Neither valves nor semi-conductors are suitable for circuits with very high currents. In these cases an AC motor can be used to drive a DC generator to produce the same effect. This is a rotary rectifier. 
The simplest form of rectification is half wave this can be done with a single diode. 
Full wave rectification of a single phase using four diodes provides a better DC output.

AC Devices 
Zener Diodes 
Most diodes will break down and be permanently damaged by high reverse current flows. The zener diode is designed to prohibit reverse current flow to a point and then to allow it. Once reverse current flow is permitted the voltage across the diode remains close to constant for a wide range of reverse currents. This allows the zener diode to be used as a voltage stabilising device. The symbol for a zener diode is: 
Zener diodes are used for voltage stabilisation 
ft+H+lo

AC Devices 
Transistors 
Transistors are semiconductors. They are made from three layers of doped silicon or germanium. A layer of P type material is sandwiched between two layers of N type material. In normal circumstances the two PN junctions created would act as back to 
N p N
back rectifiers allowing no current flow in either direction. 
AC Devices 
ft.Mflo 
Transistors 2 
In this case, though, the P material is only very lightly doped, and 
N p N
therefore very weak, so it does initially let much of the current through, about 98% of the electrons. 
2% of the electrons, however, cannot get past the PN boundary and build up in the Player preventing further electrons crossing the NP boundary. Now the current is restricted and in as little as 0.00000005 seconds can be shut off entirely. 


AC Devices 
Transistors 3 
The trick is to allow the electrons in the P layer, properly called the base, to escape down a third wire. Typically removing one electron from the base allows 50 electrons to cross the NPN junction. This means that small fluctuations in base current can be translated to large fluctuations between the other two terminals, the collector and the emitter. 
In this format the transistor can act as an amplifier. If, on the other hand, the current into the base is regarded as being either on or off, perhaps digital pulses, then it will act as a switch on the main circuit. Remember that the function of the base is to control the current through the transistor. 
N p N 
The function of the base is to control the current 
AC Devices 
Transistors 4 
The circuit symbol for a NPN transistor is shown here. 
NPN 
AC Devices 
Transistors 5 
Reversing the materials produces a PNP transistor. An easy way to remember which transistor is which is that the PNP has the arrow Pointing in, whereas the NPN has the arrow Not pointing in. 
Transistorised or semiconductor rectifiers are also known as solid state rectifiers. They are the type fitted to modern aircraft. Transistors are low current devices and can be easily damaged by exposure to large voltages and currents. 
PNP 
Transistors are low current devices 

AC Devices 
AC Power Distribution 
Modern aircraft systems are designed so that, in flight and following a failure, the most important electrical services can be supplied from either a single engine driven generator or an APU. 
AC is distributed through a number of busbars which allow services to be prioritised into vital, essential and non-essential services. Vital and essential service busbars have more backup systems to ensure the power supply is not interrupted. Be aware that the terms vital, essential and non-essential are concepts and the names given to bus bars vary with aircraft. 
28 V DC services are powered from the AC generators through Transformer Rectifier Units (TRUs) which reduce the voltage and rectify it to DC in order to power the DC bus bars and charge the battery, like this charger we met earlier. In an emergency the battery can power the essential AC bus bars through an inverter as well as the DC bus bars and the vital DC bus. 
, ------1 --------.--.. I 
240 VOLTS AC I SUPPLY 
A domestic battery charger -240 V AC in, 14 V DC out.

AC

+I I I I I

.-oc-. 
AC Devices 
Ground servicing bus bars 
Modern aircraft have ground servicing or maintenance bus bars. If external ground power is available, a momentary push button will power those bus bars for utility outlets such as cabin lighting, refuelling, hydraulics to open cargo door, battery charging etc, without powering the rest of the aeroplane. This function is overridden when the normal busbars are powered. 
There are two ways of designing an electrical system in normal operation. It is possible to have several generators feeding a common busbar, the parallel system, or to have a single busbar for each generator, the split busbar system shown here. 

AC Devices 
Split Busbar System 
In a split busbar system each engine driven generator feeds an AC busbar. The busbars are only connected in the event ofa generator failure or, possibly, on the ground. Because the generators are not operated in parallel their voltage and frequency can vary slightly and they need not be in phase. 
The generators are connected and disconnected to the busbar by operation ofthe Generator Circuit Breaker (GCB) The GCB can be operated manually by the crew and automatically by the generator control unit (GCU). 
A bus tie breaker (BTB), also called a changeover relay, is fitted between the AC busbars. It is operated by a bus power control unit (BPCU) with inputs from an air/ground logic switch. 

When closed on the ground it allows both busbars to be powered either by external power or by the APU generator. It can also be closed in flight to allow one generator to power both busbars after a failure. 
In a split busbar system each generator supplies its own busbar 
AC Devices 
Split Busbar System continued 
Number 1 and Number 2 AC busbars normally provide DC power to the Number 1 and Number 2 DC busbars through TRUs. The Essential AC services can be fed from either the Number 1 AC busbar or from the battery through an inverter. Essential DC services can be fed from either the TRU or the battery. The battery becomes the final back up for both AC and DC essential services and, when fully charged, should be good for around 30 minutes operation once all other power sources have failed. 
Essential services were defined earlier as those required for continued safe flight in an emergency. They would include flight instruments, control actuators and avionics and may be either AC or DC. 
Vital services are DC and powered directly from the battery busbar, usually called the hot battery busbar because it cannot be disconnected. The A320, for example, has two hot busbars, powering engine and APU fire extinguishers, the parking brake, slide warnings, etc. 
Non-fluorescent lighting to the cabin and flight deck is usually 28 V AC, with a 28 V DC standby. Fluorescent lighting (450 V AC) is used for flight deck storm lighting and also cabin lighting. 

Vital services are DC, often from isolated batteries 
AC Devices 
Paralleled Systems 
In a parallel system in normal operation all the generators are connected to an AC tie or synchronising busbar. 
Before a generator can be paralleled the voltage and frequency must be within limits. In older aircraft the voltage and frequency were checked manually, on modern aircraft the process is automatic. If all the requirements of voltage, frequency and, sometimes, phase relationship are not met the generators cannot be paralleled. 
A three generator parallel system is shown here. Each engine generator is connected to its load busbar via a GCB that can be paralleled through bus tie breakers to a synchronising or tie busbar. The tie busbar has few or no services on it. Its purpose is to connect the power supplies. The DC systems have been left out for clarity. 
r.t GCB 
r.t 8TB 
Paralleled generators supply tie busbars 
AC Devices 
Paralleled Systems continued 
During normal operation the BTBs are closed and each of the generators is connected to the tie/synchronising busbar. The load is shared equally by all generators in this system. In the event of a generator failure the AC tie busbar would continue to be powered but the GCB and the 8TB for the failed generator would be tripped. 
On large four engine aircraft, such as the 8747-400, two independent parallel busbars may be provided with provision to connect them together via a split system breaker (SSB). On the ground there is provision for two external power sources to be connected to the aircraft and two APU generators, powered by a singleAPU, may also be fitted. 
Normal operation is in parallel with the SSB closed but the SSB can be opened in the event of a generator failure in flight or, on the ground, to allow half the aircraft to be powered by external power and half from internal. This diagram shows the arrangement. Once again the DC busbars have been omitted for clarity.

Split System Breaker (SSB) 
AC Devices 
Advantages/Disadvantages 
The disadvantage with the split system is that there is a momentary loss of power on the AC busbars as the system switches between sources this does not occur in parallel systems. In parallel systems, the load is shared between the generators and if one generator fails the others take the load without interruption. 
However the parallel system is more complex requiring extreme precision of generator rpm and phase relationships between generators. 
As aircraft have developed, the most vulnerable systems have become the computers. They are DC powered and can be protected from momentary power interrupts by the batteries. As the complexity of parallel systems is no longer required most modern aircraft use split systems.

AC Devices 
Protection and Generator Control 
Generator Control Unit (GCU) 
A GCU is provided for each of the aircraft's generators. The GCU monitors power quality, it consists of a number of relays that are sensitive to generator voltage and frequency. Protection is provided for: 
• 
AC generator over voltage . 

• 
AC generator under voltage 

• 
Under-frequency 

• 
Over-speed (high frequency) 

• 
Excess current 

• 
Differential fault (internal short) When a fault is sensed the GCU automatically opens the GCB to remove the generator from the busbar and trips the exciter relay to 


cut the power to the generator field and stop the production of electricity. Faults from the CGU are placed in memory. 
The GCU also receives inputs when the fire handle pulled in the event of engine failure or fire. When the fire handle is pulled the GCU operates to trip the GCB and exciter relay for that generator. 

Excitation Breaker 
GCU 
, 
Over volts Under volts Excess current Under frequency 
F.-GCB 
Over frequency 
\.. ~ 


Generator failures are often transient and, once the failed generator has been isolated, the cause of the fault can be determined. It is often possible to subsequently close the exciter relay, confirm the generator is once more operating within limits then manually close the GCB to bring it back on line. 
AC Devices 
Bus Tie Breakers and Transfer Busbars 
Bus Tie Breakers have different roles in split and parallel systems. In a split bus system the BTB is normally open as the generators cannot be paralleled. If only one power supply is available the BTB automatically closes so that both the left and right systems are powered. 
The role of the BTB in a split system is sometimes filled by special transfer busbars, which will normally be supplied by their individual generators, but switch automatically to the remaining source in the event of a power failure. In a parallel system the BTBs are normally closed and the load busbars are connected to the tie or synchronising busbar. 
If there is a generator fault on a parallel system the GCU will take the generator offline and also open the BTB between that generator's load busbar and the tie busbar. Once it has been established that a generator fault exists rather than a busbar fault, the BTB can be remade and the load busbar powered from the tie busbar. If the problem is a phase relationship defect, then only the BTB will open, as the generator is serviceable, and can therefore operate a busbar, but it cannot be paralleled. 

Supply/Busbar Failure 
We need to distinguish between generator and busbar failure, in particular the outcomes of each. 
As we just saw, in the parallel system if a generator failed it also led to a temporary loss of output from its busbar because it was isolated from the rest of the system. Why do we need to isolate the busbar? Put simply, it might be that the generator failure was caused by the busbar. Let's say the busbar has developed a short circuit thereby drawing a massive current from the generator causing it to under volt or over load. If the bus bar were not isolated then the issue would transfer to the other generators possibly leading to a total electrical failure. 
In the above case the crew would need to locate the fault and if possible isolate it before attempting to restore power. If they are not able to locate the fault then the busbar and its services are lost. 
However, if the fault can be traced to the generator then, in most cases, another generator can supply that busbar and no services are lost. It becomes clear that a busbar fault is a much bigger issue than a generator fault. 
AC Devices 
Load Shedding 
In the event of generator failure it is normal, particularly on older aircraft, to have to turn off non-essential electrical services. This is known as load shedding. Most modern systems have some form of automatic load shedding device, typically disconnecting the galley power or passenger entertainment. 

Summary 
A generator fault on a split busbar system will cause the GCU to: ¦ open the GCB, disconnecting the generator from its busbar. 
¦ 
trip the exciter relay, cutting its output. 

¦ 
close the BTB in order to power both main busbars from the main generator or, depending on type, switch the transfer bus. 


A generator fault on a parallel system will cause the GCU to: 
¦ open the GCB, disconnecting the generator from its busbar. 
¦ trip the exciter relay, cutting its output. 
¦ open the 8TB, isolating the busbar of the failed generator from the tie busbar. 
AC Devices 
Cockpit Indications and Controls 
Cockpit indications and controls vary widely. Early systems gave full manual control to the crew and had extensive indications on the Flight Engineer's panel. Later systems are more automatic and designed for two pilot operation. The example we are going to look at is from the B737 series, not the NG which has quite limited indications, but from the earlier 300/400/500 series. 
The B737 has a modern split distribution system with two AC generators and an APU generator. Each generator supplies its own load busbar and a transfer busbar. In the event of a generator failure the transfer busbars will switch to the remaining operating generator. The controls are all located on the left overhead panel, above the Captain's head. 
The top panel allows the pilots to check the output of the DC and AC supplies. The left hand two meters are for DC current and voltage with a rotary switch below the meters to select the source to be displayed. The right hand two meters are for AC frequency (in cycles per second, shown as CPS) and voltage. Once again, the rotary switch below the meters is used to select the source. There is a battery switch, a galley power switch and a RESID VOLTS button which, when pressed, shows the residual voltage on the AC supplies. 

AC Devices 
Cockpit Indications and Controls 2 
The middle panel holds the controls and indicators for the CSDs. Indicator lights show low CSD oil pressure or high oil temperature. A central switch determines whether the gauges at the bottom of the panel show input oil temperature or the rise in oil temperature in the CSD or Variable Speed Constant Frequency (VSCF) drive. A STANDBY POWER SWITCH controls the power source for essential AC and DC systems. Red covers protect the CSDNSCF disconnect switches. You will also see the caution that the drive can only be reconnected on the ground once disconnected in flight. 
AC Devices 
Cockpit Indications and Controls 3 
The panel opposite shows the AC generator load in amps and allows the ground power to be selected ON or OFF. Below that is the BUS TRANSFER switch, normally left in AUTO, and a set of four amber indicator lights that show when a transfer bus or main AC bus is unpowered or blue captions to show when a generator is not supplying the bus. A further blue APU GEN OFF BUS light shows when the APU is running but it is not supplying a generator. 
A row offour switches manually connect and disconnect the main and APU generators from each generator busbar. OFF trips the generator field and disconnects the GCB, ON excites the field and closes the CGB when the power quality is correct. 




AGK 25 
Logic Circuits and Computers 
The Binary Number System 
We use numbers between zero and nine to count. Once we get beyond nine we start with one and zero again, which we call ten, and count up to nineteen. After nineteen is twenty, or two tens and no units and on to twenty-one, two tens and one unit, and continue on through hundreds, thousands and millions. This is the decimal system, or numbers based on ten, and derives directly from the numbers of digits on our hands. 
Other number systems exist. Digital computers work with numbers based on multiples of two, the binary system. Just as we have no single symbol for ten, relying instead on a one and a zero, the binary system has no single symbol for two or above. The table shows the effect as we count up. 
In the table below, we can see that a series of ones and zeros have been used to represent a specific number in binary form (101101 ). That number is 45, as it consists of no 64, one 32, no 16 and so on. 
64 32 16 8 4 2 
0 0 
ft.ihil o 

Decimal  Binary  Meaning  
0  0  No unit  
1  1  One unit  
I  2  10  One two, no unit  
I  3  11  One two, one unit  
4  100  One four (two squared), no twos, no unit  
5  101  One four (two squared), no twos, one unit  
I  6  110  One four (two squared), one two, no unit  
I  7  111  One four (two squared), one two, one unit  
8  1000  One eight (two cubed), no four, no twos, no unit  
9  1001  One eight (two cubed), no four, no twos, one unit  
10  1010  One eight (two cubed), no four, one two, no unit  

Binary counting only involves two digits

The Binary Number System continued 
By the same method, we can see that a binary number written as 111101 would equate to a decimal number of 61, as shown in the table below. 
64 32 16 8 4 2 
0 
The nice thing about this is that all decimal numbers can be reduced to a sequence of ones and zeros. Electrically ones and zeros are very easy to deal with as they can be represented as the positions of a switch, on or off. The very earliest computers used mechanical switches to reproduce the ones and zeros but nowadays millions of small transistors are used instead on an integrated circuit using different voltage levels to represent the ones and the zeroes. 
ft+i.i·lo 

Counting Devices 
The basic counting device is called a "flip-flop". This is a semiconductor with two equally stable states which we could equate with 1 and 0. If a pulse is fed into it when the state equates to Oit will change to 1 and if one is fed in when it is at 1 it will change to 0. 
lfwe start counting with a string of binary zeros representing a string of flip-flops and input a pulse into the first column, units, then 0000 changes to 0001. Of course, unless something is done, the next pulse in will simply change the 1 back to Obut ifwe make the flip-flop output a pulse to the next column as it does this the next digit will change to 1. 
This means two pulses in becomes 0010, which is 2 in binary. The next pulse changes the first flip-flop to 1 again so three pulses is 0011. The fourth changes the first column to zero sending a pulse to column two which also changes to zero and sends a pulse to column three which changes to 1. Now we have four pulses reading 0100, four in binary, and we can theoretically continue counting up to the processing limit of the computer. 
A flip-flop has two equally stable states 
Logic Circuits and Computers 
Bits and Bytes 
In computing an individual binary digit is called a "bit" and a series of "bits" makes a "word". The number of "bits" in a "word" is governed by the computer itself, most modern computers have words of 32 or 64 bits and are therefore called 32 or 64 bit computers. Do not confuse "bits" with "bytes". A "byte" is a unit of storage capacity, the space taken to store a single character, and a "word" may be stored in several "bytes". Computer memory is expressed in units of Megabytes, millions of bytes, and Gigabytes, thousands of millions of bytes. 
A bit is a binary digit, 32 or 64 bits make a word 
A byte is a unit of storage capacity

Logic Circuits and Computers 
Life of Electronic components 
We tend to think that as electronic goods have no moving parts they have infinite life: this is not the case they do indeed have a lifespan. What is more, that life expectancy is severely reduced by high temperature and humidity. 
With regard to temperature life is affected by the 10°C -twice law. For example, if a component had an expected life of 80 000 hours at40°C, then when exposed to 50°C (10°C higher) the life expectancy is reduced by half to 40 000 hours. Computers are essential to modern flight, so cooling methods must be employed to keep the aircraft computers within their design temperature range. 
Humidity is also an issue. Mould and corrosion are more likely to cause damage when humidity exceeds 60%. 


Computer life expectancy is affected by temperature. If a computer has a life expectancy of 40 000 hours at 30° C, what would be the life expectancy at 40° C? 
•••  30 000 hours 10 000 hours 20 000 hours  
•  80 000 hours  



Lesson 26 
Principles of Internal Combustion 
AGK 26 
Principles of Internal Combustion 
ft.ih·l o 
Internal Combustion Principles 
Conventional aircraft are powered either by piston or by gas turbine engines. Although the two types look very different, they are both internal combustion engines. In other words, they both convert the chemical energy of the fuel to heat energy. 
As its name implies, an internal combustion engine burns fuel with air internally. In a piston engine, heating the air increases pressure within a cylinder to produce power. In a gas turbine engine heating the air increases its volume, and hence its velocity, and this is used to produce power at a turbine and thrust from its exhaust. 
Internal combustion engines are much easier to understand if you have first grasped the underlying physical principles. It's important that you learn them -not least because some exam questions will directly test your knowledge ofthe physics. So before we examine each engine type in detail we must revisit some elementary principles. 
Combustion 
Combustion 

Principles of Internal Combustion 
ft+i.·l o 
The Properties and Behaviour of Gases 
Boyle's Law 
Boyle's Law describes the relationship between the volume and pressure of a gas. 
The volume of a given mass of gas is inversely proportional to its pressure at constant temperature. Put simply, if a gas is compressed its volume will decrease. If pressure is reduced the volume of the gas will increase. Expressed as a formula: 

Where P = pressure, V = Volume and the absolute temperature of the gas remains constant. 
In practice the situation is a little more complex because any change in pressure will also affect the temperature ofthe gas. 


Principles of Internal Combustion 
ft+IH·lo 
Charles' Law 
Charles' law describes the relationship between the temperature and volume of a gas. 
The volume of a given mass of gas at constant pressure is directly proportional to its absolute thermodynamic temperature. In other words, if the temperature ofthe gas is increased (and its pressure remains constant) it will expand to occupy more volume. 
Expressed as a formula: 
Provided that the pressure remains constant. 


Principles of Internal Combustion 
The Combined Gas Law 
To accurately describe the behaviour of a gas we must combine Boyles' and Charles' laws. 
The true relationship between the pressure (P), temperature (T), and volume (V) of an ideal (hypothetical) gas can be expressed as the formula: 
TPV = a constant 
This allows us to state the following: 
¦ 
If the volume of a gas is kept constant, its pressure will increase with temperature. 

¦ 
If the volume of a gas is reduced, its pressure and temperature will increase. 

¦ 
If the volume of a gas is increased its pressure and temperature will reduce. 

¦ 
If the pressure of a gas is kept constant, its volume will increase with temperature. 



Conservation of Energy 
The law of conservation of energy dictates that energy can be neither created nor destroyed. So in a perfectly isolated system, energy may be converted from one form to another, but it will never be lost. 
This has important implications for the energies contained in a flowing gas. In this context the sum of the pressure, heat and kinetic energies of a given mass of gas must remain constant. 

Principles of Internal Combustion 
ft.iff·l o 
Bernoulli's Equation 
Bernoulli's theorem applies the law of conservation of energy to a gas. It states that the sum ofthe pressure, potential and kinetic energies per unit volume is constant at any point in a non-viscous incompressible fluid in steady subsonic flow. Expressed as an equation:

From this we can make a couple of deductions about the properties of a flowing gas. When gas flows through a restriction (a convergent duct) it must increase its velocity to maintain the same mass flow. Conversely when gas flows into a divergent duct its velocity must reduce. Hence: 
¦

Principles of Internal Combustion 
ft+H·lo 
Mechanisms for Heat Transfer 
So far we have considered an ideal gas flowing in perfect isolation 
from its surroundings (an adiabatic system). But in practice energy, in the form of heat, is lost to, and gained from, external sources such as the engine components. Energy may also be lost through mechanical inefficiency caused, for example, by friction from bearings. 
Piston and gas turbines engines are no exception. The heat of combustion is transferred to the air and to the engine components. There are three mechanisms by which heat can be transferred from one mass to another: 
Convection 
Heat is transferred indirectly through the circulation of a warm gas or liquid. 
Conduction 
Heat energy passes directly from molecule to molecule when two bodies touch. 
Radiation 
Heat energy radiates as electromagnetic waves from a relatively hot body. These are then absorbed by another body. 

Principles of Internal Combustion 
fti+h·-lo 
Newton's Laws of Motion 
Newton's laws are fundamental to our understanding of how a piston or jet engine generates thrust and how the mass of gas inside these engines behaves. It's very important to learn these laws and to understand that they apply not only to the engine's moving parts but also to the air moving through the engine or the propeller disc. Air has mass and therefore possesses inertia and momentum. 
Newton's 1st Law 
A body will continue in a state of rest or uniform motion in a straight line unless acted on by an external force. 
Newton's 2nd Law 
The rate of change of momentum is proportional to, and takes place in the direction, of the applied force. 
Newton's 3rd Law 
Action and reaction are equal and opposite.

Principles of Internal Combustion 
Thrust 
The ultimate aim of all aircraft engines is to produce thrust. Piston and gas turbine engines generate thrust by accelerating a mass of air rearwards. Gas turbine engines produce thrust by directly accelerating the air. Piston engines produce power which is then converted into thrust by a propeller. One type of gas turbine engine (the turbo-prop) also produces power to drive a propeller. 
Thrust is the force which moves an aircraft through the air and we have already learned that force= mass x acceleration. 
So the thrust produced by a propeller or jet engine depends on two things: the mass (amount) of air it propels rearwards and the acceleration it imparts to the air. 
ft+it·h·lo 

Its important to understand that the acceleration refers to the increase in speed above that of the air flowing into the engine. So, as an aircraft's forward speed increases, a given mass of air must be given a greater acceleration to produce the same level of thrust. The formula for thrust is: 
F = M x (Vjet -Vrnght) 

where F is thrust, M is the mass of air, Vjet is the speed of the air exiting the gas turbine or propeller and V t1ight is the air speed. 
Principles of Internal Combustion 
Power 
The modern unit of power is the watt, but the older unit, horsepower, is still commonly used. One horsepower equals 746 
W. There are four principal measurements of horsepower: 
Indicated Horsepower (IHP) is the theoretical power produced by a reciprocating engine. It is calculated from the pressure developed in the cylinders but takes no account of the work that must be done within the engine. 
Friction Horsepower (FHP) Friction horse power is the work done within the engine in other words, the work required to drive the engine's mechanical components. 
Brake Horsepower (BHP) or shaft horsepower is the power delivered to, for example, the propeller shaft. It derives its name from the brake drum dynamometer originally used to measure it. BHP is IHP minus the power lost through friction and compression. 
Thrust Horsepower (THP) is the power which is successfully converted into thrust. In a piston engine no useful work is done until the propeller has produced thrust by propelling air rearwards. Propellers are never 100% efficient so the power finally converted into thrust will be less than the BHP. 
ft.iihi-l o 

Efficiency 
The more work that an engine has to do to power its own internal components the less efficient it will be as a power source. The mechanical efficiency of an engine is expressed as the ratio of useful power produced to the theoretical power produced. Modern piston engines are about 80% efficient. 
Engines are also described in terms of their thermal and volumetric efficiency. These concepts are explained in detail later.

Piston Engine Principles of Operation

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
ft.lhi·-l o 
Piston Engines 
Both diesel and spark ignition engines can be referred to as "piston engines". Both types of engine are used in modern aircraft designs and both develop power in the same way. 
Piston engines produce power by converting the chemical energy contained in the fuel to mechanical energy. The fuel is burned with air to release its energy in the form of heat. The heat is absorbed by the gas which experiences a marked increase in pressure energy. This pressure energy is converted to mechanical energy by a piston. Mechanical energy drives the 
Converted to Converted to Converted to
propeller which accelerates air rearwards to produce thrust. 
Chemical Heat Pressure Mechanical energy energy energy energy 


Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
ft+i.i+lo 
The Spark Ignition Engine 
Air is the working fluid of all internal combustion engines. In this context the term "air" covers all the various forms ofgas encountered in the engine. Atmospheric air is sucked in but when mixed with fuel, it is called "the mixture". The gas produced from burning the mixture is usually called the "products of combustion" or "exhaust gas". 
Principle of Operation 
Gasoline fuel (petrol) is mixed with air. The mixture is introduced into a cylinder through an inlet port. The inlet port is then closed by an inlet valve. The cylinder is sealed at the other end by a moveable, but gas-tight, piston. 
The mixture is ignited by an electric spark plug. As combustion takes place the hot air attempts to expand but is confined by the cylinder. This causes a marked increase in pressure which forces the piston down the cylinder. 
The piston is connected to a crankshaft that converts its linear (up and down) motion into rotary motion. The up and down motion of the piston is more accurately described as reciprocal movement hence the term "reciprocating engine". 


Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
ftiihi=hlo 
Principle of Operation 
The crankshaft is either connected directly to the propeller, or indirectly through a gearbox. The propeller accelerates air rearwards which creates an equal and opposite reaction known as thrust. 
The piston and crankshaft arrangement is analogous to a bicycle. By increasing the force applied to the pedals, the cyclist increases the power supplied to the rear wheel. In the same way the increased force exerted by the pistons is translated into increased power to drive the propeller.

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
ft+++~,­
The Theoretical Four-Stroke Cycle 
To produce one power stroke, the engine must perform 3 additional strokes to allow mixture into and out of the cylinder, and to pressurise the mixture. This gives a total of four strokes to complete a full cycle. The four strokes, in their order of operation, are as follows: 
¦ 
Induction: the mixture must be drawn into the cylinder. 

¦ 
Compression: the mixture is compressed to maximize the pressure produced by combustion. 

¦ 
Power: The piston is driven down the cylinder to produce mechanical power. 

¦ 
Exhaust: The waste gases must be expelled from the cylinder before the whole process can begin again. 


The four strokes are sometimes better remembered as suck, squeeze, bang and blow. They are more correctly known as the four-stroke cycle or the 'Otto cycle' named after its inventor, Nikolaus Otto. 
The term "stroke" refers to the distance the piston travels between its lowest and highest points within the cylinder. The topmost limit oftravel is called Top Dead Centre (TDC). The lowest limit is called Bottom Dead Centre (BOC). Both are important reference points for the operation of the valves and ignition. 

180° crankshaft rotation 
Top dead centre {TDC) Stroke Bottom dead centre (BDC) 

There are two other important points to note: 
¦ 
Ignition occurs between the Compression and Power strokes. 

¦ 
To move the piston through one stroke, the crankshaft must rotate 180°. 



Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
ft+M+lo 
The Induction Stroke 
The inlet valve opens at TDC just as the piston begins to travel down the cylinder. As the available cylinder volume increases the internal pressure (and temperature) reduces causing the mixture to be sucked into the cylinder. As the piston reaches the bottom of its travel, BOC, the inlet valve closes to seal the chamber. 
An engine that induces the fuel and air in this way is called a normally aspirated engine.

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
The Compression Stroke 
The compression stroke begins as the piston starts to travel up the cylinder. Both the inlet and exhaust valves are closed so the pressure and temperature ofthe mixture increases as the volume in the cylinder reduces. At TDC the fuel is ignited by the spark plug. 
Combustion 
Combustion takes place between the compression and power strokes. The mixture is ignited by an electric spark produced by one or more spark plugs located at the top of the cylinder. 
In the theoretical cycle combustion occurs instantaneously whilst the piston is at TDC. Theoretically then, there is no change in volume during combustion. This is why piston engines are sometimes known as constant volume engines. Combustion in such a confined space produces a very large increase in pressure and temperature.

The Power Stroke 
The rapid rise in pressure causes the piston to be forced down the cylinder. This is the only stroke in the four stroke cycle when power is produced. 
As the available cylinder volume increases, the pressure and temperature of the combusted gas reduces. The average pressure exerted on the piston during the power stroke is called the Mean Effective Pressure (MEP). This value is used to calculate the theoretical power of the engine. 
The power stroke is sometimes referred to as the 'firing expansion stroke'.

The Exhaust Stroke 
At BDC, just as the piston begins to rise, the exhaust valve opens. The rising piston causes the pressure and temperature in the cylinder to increase. The products of combustion are expelled from the cylinder through the exhaust valve and out to atmosphere. 
Two revolutions of the crankshaft are required to complete the four stroke cycle.

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
ft+iitii=fo 
The Practical Four Stroke Cycle 
An engine designed to operate the theoretical four stroke cycle would produce some power but it would be very inefficient. 
Air has mass and therefore possesses inertia and momentum. It doesn't want to flow into or out ofthe engine, but once it is flowing it will tend to continue to flow. 
The force that makes the air flow comes from the pressure changes produced by the changing volume of the cylinder. But close to TDC and BOC there is very little linear movement of the piston even though the angular rotation speed of the crankshaft remains unchanged. This is known as the ineffective crank angle and marks the periods where there is little change in cylinder volume and therefore little change in cylinder pressure. 
A further problem with the theoretical cycle is that it assumes that the combustion process is instantaneous. In reality it takes time to completely burn the mixture. Consequently it takes time for the combusting gas to reach maximum pressure in the cylinder. 
Ineffective crank angle 
Small linear 
: movement of piston compared to large angular rotation of crankshaft at TDC and BDC. 


The practical four stroke cycle exploits the characteristics of ineffective crank angle and finite combustion time to maximise engine efficiency. It does this by altering the position at which the inlet and exhaust valves open and close (valve timing) and by adjusting the point at which ignition occurs (ignition timing). 
Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
Valve Timing 
Within the ineffective crank angle the piston can do little work on the cylinder gases. So the valve timing is adjusted to exploit the momentum of the gas and to overcome its inertia. 
As the exhaust stroke comes to its end, but before the piston reaches TDC, the inlet valve opens. This allows fresh mixture to enter the cylinder before the start of the induction stroke proper. 
The process of opening the inlet valve before TDC is known as valve lead. Inlet valve lead and lag both help to maximise the amount of mixture that enters the cylinder. Valve lead and lag is measured in degrees of crankshaft rotation. This is graphically expressed in this diagram, where the opening ofthe inlet valve on the induction stroke is seen to lead by 20°. The figures for valve lead and lag used here are purely representative, so you will not need to remember them for the exams! The complete version of this timing diagram, with all the strokes shown, is available at the end ofthis lesson.

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 
Valve Timing 5  
The period when both inlet and exhaust valves are open at the end of the exhaust stroke, and the start of the induction stroke, is called valve overlap as shown here. During the overlap period the action of exhaust gases flowing out helps to draw in the fresh mixture, a process known as scavenging.  Valve overlap• exhaust stroke  
A further advantage of valve lead and lag is the longer period available for valve operation. This reduces the speed at which the valves must work, which significantly reduces the stresses imposed on their operating gear.  
The exact point in the cycle at which each valve opens and closes is dictated by the shape of the cams on the camshaft. Because the cam profile can not be changed, valve timing remains unchanged regardless of engine RPM.  
Some modern car engines can vary the valve timing by the use of hydraulic, rather than mechanical tappets, but this system is not yet used on aero engines.  

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 


Ignition Timing 
It takes a finite time for the mixture to combust and thus reach peak pressure. If the spark is timed to occur at exactly TDC, the piston will already have travelled some way down the cylinder before peak pressure is reached. 
Again, the engine designer exploits the ineffective crank angle to increase efficiency. Ignition is timed to occur just before TDC on the compression stroke. Combustion is well under way by the time the piston begins its downward movement on the power stroke. 
In fact, combustion is completed, and maximum pressure produced, just as the piston leaves the period of ineffective crank 
Just before TDC TDC Just after TDC 
angle on the down power stroke -about 6-12° after TDC. This increases peak pressure and maximises the power generated on the power stroke. 

If this were allowed to happen, the maximum power produced by 
When the spark is timed to occur before TDC the ignition is said to the engine would reduce markedly with increased RPM. 

be "advanced". 

To overcome the problem, the ignition is further advanced as the 
While the rate of burning remains relatively constant, the speed at RPM increases. 

which the piston travels does not. At higher engine revolutions the piston is moving so rapidly that, if nothing is done to alter the timing of the spark, it will have already completed a significant portion of the power stroke before peak pressure is achieved. 

Piston Engine Principles of Operation 

Ignition Timing continued 
Likewise, as engine speed reduces, the ignition must be retarded. If it were not, in the extreme case, peak pressure might be achieved before the piston reaches the top of its travel. Not only would this be highly inefficient but it would also impose huge and potentially damaging forces on the engine components. It could even force the crankshaft to reverse its direction of rotation. There is a real risk of this happening during the very slow RPM associated with engine starting. During start the ignition is usually retarded to around TDC. 
These modifications to the theoretical cycle can be graphically displayed by the full timing diagram shown here. This relates piston movement, valve operation and ignition to the angular rotation of crankshaft. As stated earlier, the angles shown in the figure are representative only. Each engine design will have its own particular timing values.

Lesson 28 
Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
AGK28 

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
ft+IHfh!o 
Multi Cylinder Engines 
We noted earlier that one ofthe factors determining engine power is the mass of mixture ignited in the cylinder. The greater the mass that can be combusted, the greater the power. 
The obvious way to increase mass would be to increase the size of the cylinder. But in the four-stroke cycle power is produced for only 25% of the time. The other three strokes represent power that must be used for "housekeeping" tasks. 
In practice cylinder size is increased but, more importantly multiple cylinders are used. Multiple cylinder designs have another benefit -they produce smoother power delivery. Four stroke aero-engines normally have at least four cylinders. 
A four cylinder engine is designed so that each cylinder is on a different stroke, ensuring that a power stroke is occurring at all times. 
Because a complete otto cycle takes two revolutions of the crankshaft, 720°, the firing interval of an engine is found by dividing 720° by the number of cylinders. A six cylinder engine would produce a power stroke every 120° of crankshaft rotation. 

Classification of Engines 
Engines can be classified according to their design characteristics. Most commonly by the: 
¦ 
Number of cylinders. 

¦ 
Cylinder arrangement. 

¦ 
Method of cooling. 

¦ 
Type of fuel and air induction system. 

¦ 
Propeller drive arrangement. 

¦ 
Some of the more common engine arrangements are described on the following pages. 



Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
ft.iiflo 
In-Line Engines 
The cylinders are positioned in line. These engines can be air or 
r
liquid cooled. The number of cylinders is often restricted to four or 
six to ensure adequate air cooling and to limit the overall length of the engine. 
In-line engines are often inverted to provide greater propeller tip clearance with the ground. This can give rise to problems with oil pooling in the cylinders. 


Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
ft+itfto 
Radial Engines 
As the name suggests, in a radial engine the cylinders are arranged radially around a central crankshaft. Radial engine designs have the advantage of being short, and offer a better power to weight ratio than other engine arrangements. 
Radial engines require an odd number of cylinders, to maintain a smooth power output by firing every other cylinder in order. A five­cylinder radial would fire in the order 1, 3, 5, 2, 4 and so on. The engine shown here has seven, arranged in a single bank. More cylinders could be added by using multiple banks. The ultimate radial piston engine design had 28 cylinders arranged in four banks of seven. 
Radial engines are air cooled and were often the preferred arrangement for larger aircraft. However, their relatively large frontal area can incur a significant drag penalty. Like inverted in­line engines, oil can pool in the lower cylinders when the engine is not running. 


Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
The 'V' Engine 
The 'V' engine is effectively two in-line engines, set at an angle to each other and joined by a single crankshaft. This arrangement allows the designer to increase the number of cylinders, without increasing the engine's overall length, and representing a dramatic saving in weight due to the much shorter crankshaft than an in-line engine with the same number ofcylinders. 

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
ftiiifto 
Horizontally Opposed Engines 
Horizontally opposed, or "flat" engines are the most common multi cylinder arrangement used in light aircraft. The cylinders and pistons are arranged either side of a common crankshaft, offset from their opposite number. 

This arrangement produces a short, rigid engine that sits easily within a streamlined nacelle, so reducing drag. Four, six and eight cylinder arrangements are common. They are usually air cooled but some designs may have liquid cooled cylinder heads. The internal arrangement is shown here. 



Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
Major Components of a Spark Ignition Engine Cylinder and Cylinder Head 
The cylinder comprises two parts, the barrel and the head which are bolted together. A gasket between the two faces ensures a completely gas tight seal. The barrel is usually made of steel and the head of alloy. Air cooled engines have fins on the barrel and head to help to disperse heat. Liquid cooled engine have galleries incorporated within the casting to allow a flow of cooling liquid around the engine. 
Spark Plugs 
Aero-engines always incorporate two spark plugs per cylinder. This in-built redundancy increases safety. It also ensures more efficient combustion because the mixture is simultaneously ignited at either side of the cylinder. This produces a quicker and more even burn. 


Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
ft.Mflo 
Pistons 
Aluminium alloy pistons fit tightly in the cylinder. The top of the piston is called the crown. The piston is tapered slightly towards the crown to allow for thermal expansion. The crown itself is shaped to ensure optimum combustion conditions. The lower part of the piston is called the skirt. 
Compression rings are fitted around the top of the piston to prevent gases from escaping around the skirt. They are made from centrifugally spun cast iron. Carbon in the cast iron makes the rings self lubricating and the natural springiness of the material ensures a gas tight seal. 
Oil control rings are used to ensure that oil is spread evenly over the inner surface of the cylinder. An oil scraper ring is fitted to the 


piston skirt to draw excess oil back into the crankcase.  
Connecting Rod  Most aero engines have connecting rods and piston pins made  
The piston is connected to the crankshaft by a connecting rod. A  from steel aluminium alloys or titanium are also used for weight  
gudgeon pin, also known as a piston pin, connects the rod to the  saving in modern engines.  
piston at the "small end". The rod is attached to the crank pin of  
the crankshaft at the "big end". The connecting rod is usually 'H'  
shaped in cross-section for extra strength and lower weight.  

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 

Crankshafts 
Crankshaft
A crankshaft lies between the two halves of the crankcase and is 
2 x Throw = Stroke

supported in bearing journals. Shell type main bearings are used --I to minimise friction.

Crankshafts need to be very strong and are made from heavy 
Balance weight 

steel alloys. They are sometimes hollowed to save weight and to Crankpin
Crankweb 
allow cooling, lubricating oil to flow through them. 
The distance between the centre of the crankshaft journal and the centre of the crank pin is known as the crank throw. 
The amount of crank throw determines the distance the piston moves up and down in its cylinder. In half a revolution i.e. from TDC to BDC or from BDC to TDC, the piston will move by two throws. The piston will move twice the distance of the crank throw. This distance is known as the stroke. 
Reducing the stroke saves weight because the cylinder, connecting rod and crank throw are all shortened and therefore have less mass. This is why aircraft engines tend to be short stroked.

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 


Valves and Valve Gear  
The Valves  
The inlet and exhaust valves are poppet valves. They are usually  
mounted in the cylinder head. The valves close under spring  
pressure and are opened by the camshaft via the valve operating  
gear. The inlet valve is normally larger than the exhaust valve.  
The poppet valves are made from a special steel alloy designed to  
withstand the very high thermal and mechanical stresses imposed  
by high speed, high temperature operation. The valve seat is  
carefully machined to ensure a gas tight seal at the port.  
To assist cooling, the exhaust valve is hollowed and partially filled  
with sodium. At high temperatures the sodium melts and flows up  
and down the stem transferring heat from the valve face to the  
valve stem.  
Helical springs located around the valve provide the force to hold  To achieve this, the springs are different diameters, one sitting  
the valve face firmly closed against the valve seat. The springs  inside the other. To prevent mutual interference they are counter  
are retained by a cup and tapered collets.  wound, meaning they are coiled in opposite directions. Counter  
Valve bounce  winding also serves to minimise any tendency of the valve to rotate in operation.  
When a spring vibrates at its resonant frequency it loses its ability  
to hold the valve closed: this phenomenon is known as valve  
bounce. Valve bounce can be eliminated by fitting two springs,  
each having different resonant frequencies.  

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
Valve Gear 
Valve gear is the collective term used to describe the components that operate the valves. A hinged lever, the rocker arm, moves down. This forces the valve down, against the pressure of its springs, to open the port. When the rocker arm moves up, spring pressure returns the valve to its fully seated, closed position. 
The rocker arm is operated by a push rod that rests on a camshaft driven by the crankshaft. The profile ofthe cams on the camshaft is carefully set to ensure that the valves open and close at precisely the right point in the four-stroke sequence. 
A tappet is fitted between the camshaft and the push rod. This helps to translate smoothly the movement of the cam into the reciprocating motion of the push rod. The assembly is adjusted so that when the valve is fully closed a small gap exists between the top of the valve stem and the rocker pad. This "tappet clearance" allows for thermal expansion. 
The correct tappet clearance ensures that the valves close completely at all operating temperatures. An excessively large clearance will prevent the valve from opening fully. An excessively small clearance may prevent the valve from closing properly at high temperatures. 
Rocker arm 
Rocker pad 
Clearance Adjusting nuts 
Valve stem PushRod 
Valve seat Tappet 

Incorrect tappet clearance can cause a significant reduction in performance. Many modern engines are now fitted with hydraulic tappets which automatically adjust themselves to cater for thermal expansion. They operate using engine oil as their hydraulic medium, and require no pre-set tappet clearance. 


Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
Engine Power 

Air is the working fluid of the engine. The more that can be induced into the cylinder, the more power the engine can produce. 
"Normally aspirated" engines rely on the difference between atmospheric pressure and cylinder pressure to draw the mixture into the cylinder, but atmospheric pressure reduces with altitude. At height, less mixture will be drawn into the cylinders so the engine will produce less power. This limitation can be partially overcome by forcing air into the cylinder using some form of mechanical compressor. Engines which use such systems are described as being "supercharged". 
The pressure of the mixture is measured in the inlet manifold, just before it is introduced into the cylinder. This value is displayed on a Manifold Absolute Pressure (MAP) gauge. The unit of pressure normally used is inches of mercury, (in Hg). 
Engine Take-off idle 


Engine 

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
Engine Power continued 
MAP directly correlates with the average pressure produced by combustion (MEP) and is therefore a good indicator of the power being produced by the engine. The pilot controls MAP and therefore cylinder pressure with the throttle lever. The lever operates the throttle valve (butterfly valve) which controls the amount of air or mixture delivered to the engine. 
When the engine is stopped the MAP gauge displays atmospheric pressure which, in ISA conditions, is 29.92 in Hg. 
When the engine is started the MAP will drop. On a normally aspirated engine MAP would always indicate slightly less than atmospheric pressure, even with the throttle fully open when running. A supercharged engine can produce higher than atmospheric MAP.

Calculating Power 
Power is the rate of doing work and is shown by the formula 
P _ Force x Distance ower -Time

The force produced on the piston is equal to the pressure ' P'

(MEP) in the cylinder during the power stroke acting on the area 'A' of the piston crown. The distance the piston moves is the length of stroke 'L'. 
Distance over time is speed, which in this case equates to RPM. Because, on a single piston engine, only one power stroke is produced for every two revolutions of the crankshaft the RPM must be divided by 2 to give the effective number of power strokes 'E'. 
Multiplying these factors together gives the theoretical power produced in the cylinder. If the engine has multiple cylinders an additional factor 'N' for the number of cylinders must be applied. A mnemonic to help remember the five factors is PLANE.

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
Piston Engine Efficiency 

Thermal Efficiency 
The greatest losses occur because of the engine's poor thermal efficiency. Thermal efficiency is expressed as the ratio of the work done by the engine to the mechanical equivalent of the heat energy released by combusting the fuel. 
A modern piston engine is about 33% thermally efficient. Most of the heat energy is lost to the atmosphere in the exhaust. The remainder heats up the engine components. Thermal efficiency is independent of altitude but increases with increased compression ratio. 


Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 
Compression Ratio 
When the piston is at TDC the fuel/air mixture is compressed into a very small space between the top of the piston and the top of the cylinder. This is known as the clearance volume. 
When the piston is at BOC the volume of space containing the mixture the "total volume" is very much larger. The difference between the clearance volume and the total volume is called the swept volume. The compression ratio is the ratio of total volume to clearance volume. 
For aero engines, compression ratios typically fall between 7:1 and 9:1. High compression ratios improve thermal efficiency. However, there is a practical limit to the maximum achievable compression ratio. If it is too high, the rise in temperature caused by compression can lead to detonation which is discussed later. 
The swept volume multiplied by the number of cylinders gives the "displacement". In other words: 
Displacement= piston area x stroke x number of cylinders 
To compensate for their shorter stroke, aero engines have a relatively large piston area. A "squared" or "square cage" engine describes a design where the cylinder diameter equals the stroke. 

Clearance Total Sweptvolume volume volume 
.. 
lTDC BDC 

Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 

Volumetric efficiency is the ratio of the mass of the mixture induced into the cylinder to the mass of air (at standard temperature and pressure) which would otherwise fill the swept volume. 
Increased volumetric efficiency results in increased engine power. The most obvious way to increase volumetric efficiency is to force more mixture into the cylinders by supercharging. 
For a normally aspirated piston engine, three main factors influence volumetric efficiency: 
¦ 
The ease with which the mixture can enter the cylinder. 

¦ 
The density of the air. (Affected by altitude, temperature and humidity) 

¦ 
The ease with which the exhaust gases can leave the cylinder. 


Inlet flow will depend on the smoothness of the inner surfaces of the inlet manifold, the shape ofthe manifold itself, the efficiency of operation and timing of the inlet valve, and any restrictions caused by the throttle butterfly valve. 
Outlet flow will depend on the design of the exhaust system and the operation and timing ofthe exhaust valve. Backpressure in the exhaust pipes will hinder the flow of exhaust gases from the cylinders. 

On a normally aspirated engine, Volumetric Efficiency (VE) increases with: 
¦ 
Altitude: because the reduced air density results in a drop in exhaust back pressure, which increases mass flow into the 

cylinder, and therefore density in the cylinder, but only ifthe MAP remains constant. Note if the MAP does not remain constant then the VE will reduce. 

¦ 
RPM: because of the increased momentum of the gases. 



A modern normally aspirated piston engine typically achieves a volumetric efficiency of about 80%. 
Piston Engine Design and Efficiency 

The Effect of Volumetric Efficiency on Engine Power 
In normally aspirated engines the maximum obtainable MAP will reduce with altitude because of the reducing atmospheric pressure. Maximum power will be obtained at low pressure altitudes and power will reduce with altitude. 
The improvement in volumetric efficiency caused by reduced exhaust back pressure is evident in flight when using a normally aspirated engine and a variable pitch propeller. 
If the aircraft is climbed initially at less than full power, for example at max continuous power, both MAP and RPM can be kept constant using the throttle and RPM levers. Under these circumstances the power available would initially increase with altitude until the throttle is fully opened and the selected MAP value can no longer be obtained. 
Specific Fuel Consumption 
The overall fuel efficiency of a piston engine is measured by its Specific Fuel Consumption (SFC). SFC is the mass of fuel required to produce a given amount of power for a given period of time. The SI units of SFC are kilograms per kilowatt hour. A low value of SFC indicates a more fuel efficient engine.

Piston Engine Lubrication and Cooling 
AGK 29 

Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft.ih·l o 
Engine Lubrication Systems 
The moving parts in an engine must be lubricated with oil to reduce friction and wear. This is achieved by injecting or spraying a thin film of oil between them. The outside layers of oil cling to the metal surfaces allowing a central layer of molecules to move effortlessly in an oil "sandwich". 
As well as its primary lubricating function, engine oil has a number of secondary functions. 
Cooling. Oil helps to cool the engine by absorbing heat and dispersing it to atmosphere. 
Cleaning. Oil cleans the engine by removing deposits and metal flakes which are then trapped by an oil filter. 
Protection. Oil protects the components from corrosion. 

Hydraulic operations. Oil can be used as the hydraulic medium Low pressure could be due to a poor pump, or a low oil quantity. Ato operate valve gear and variable pitch propellers. 

low quantity would also run at a higher temperature as the amount 
Engine oil temperature and pressure are both very important of oil is insufficient for adequate cooling of the engine. 

indicators of the condition of the engine. There is a relationship between oil pressure, temperature, and quantity. Pressure is achieved by the work of the pump, and temperature is controlled through the oil cooling system. 

Piston Engine Lubrication and Cooling 
ft+i.·l o 
Aero Engine Oils 
An important characteristic of oil is its viscosity. Viscosity is the resistance of the fluid to flow. It reduces with increased temperature. 
High viscosity oil makes the engine more difficult to turn over but guarantees a good film in the bearings. Low viscosity oil makes the engine easier to turn over. But if it is too low, the oil film can break down, allowing metal to come into contact with metal. 
Aero engines normally use a mineral oil designed specifically to maintain an acceptable level of viscosity over the full range of operating temperatures. A numbering system is used to indicate viscosity the higher the number, the higher the viscosity. 
Aero engine oil must 
¦ 
maintain suitable viscosity over a wide range of temperatures and pressures, 

¦ 
have a low evaporation rate, 

¦ 
inhibit corrosion, 

¦ 
not react with the materials with which it comes into contact, 

¦ 
discourage the formation of sludge. 



To meet these requirements, additives are introduced to the base mineral oil. Oil containing additives is known as "compound oil". It may be identified by letters preceding the viscosity number. For example W80 orAD100. 
"Straight" oil has no additives and is used on new engines to reduce the running-in time. 
Some piston aero engines use modern synthetic multi-grade oils. Synthetic oil is produced from chemical compounds which don't occur in crude oil. 
No oil can indefinitely resist the effects of operating in such a harsh environment. Eventually irreversible damage is caused by oxidisation, the corrosive by-products of combustion, water vapour contamination and the repeated mechanical shearing which occurs in the lubricating film. This is why it is essential to change the oil according to the schedule laid down by the engine manufacturer. 
As well as incurring damage some oil is normally lost through burning. Nevertheless, the oil level should be checked regularly to ensure that oil consumption isn't excessive. Abnormal or excessive oil burning may be indicated by blue or grey smoke in the exhaust. Worn piston rings, cylinder or piston can all result in oil being burned in the combustion chamber. 
Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft+IH·lo 
Types of Lubrication System 
Engines may be classified according to the way in which the engine oil supply is stored. There are two methods: the "dry" and "wet sump" systems. 
In a dry sump system oil is stored in a tank mounted remotely from the engine. In a wet sump system the oil is stored in the bottom of the crankcase, the "sump". 


Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 

Dry Sump Systems 
Oil is drawn from the tank by an engine driven pump. A suction filter is fitted just before the pump. The pump forces oil through a second, high-pressure, filter to various strategically placed jets and drillings servicing the major lubrication points. 
The pump provides considerably more oil pressure than is normally necessary. This ensures that sufficient pressure can be guaranteed even under extreme conditions. A pressure relief valve regulates the system pressure. 
Highly loaded points, such as the big end bearings, are fed with high-pressure oil at about 60-70 PSI. The oil is injected at the crankshaft main bearings and passes into a gallery inside the crankshaft to emerge at the big end bearings. As it leaves the big end bearings some of the oil is flung onto the cylinder walls. 


Lightly loaded components such as the camshaft are fed with low­pressure oil via a pressure-reducing valve. 
The oil then falls to the sump, where it is collected by a scavenge pump. This pumps the oil back to the oil tank, via an oil cooler. The scavenge pump is more powerful than the high-pressure pump, to ensure that oil doesn't pool in the sump. 

Piston Engine Lubrication and Cooling 
ft.iff·l o 
Dry Sump System Components Oil Tank 
The oil tank contains a reserve of oil to allow for fluctuations in demand, thermal expansion, minor leaks and frothing caused by aeration. 
Oil returns from the engine via the oil return line (or "scavenge line") and spills onto a de-aerator plate. This helps to separate out any air bubbles which may be suspended in the oil. The air escapes to the sump via a vent, and eventually to atmosphere via an oil breather. 
The de-aerated oil drops into the tank which contains baffles to prevent the fluid from slopping and surging during manoeuvres. 
Most oil tank designs incorporate a hot well. The hot well is a perforated metal cylinder covering the outlet to the pressure pump. During starting, when the oil is cold and at its most viscous, oil is drawn first from the hot well. The cold viscous oil in the main part of the tank can only flow very slowly through the perforations into the hot well. Consequently the hot well tends to refill with hot oil returning from the engine. This quickly establishes a good circulation of less viscous oil during and immediately after start. 
Eventually all the oil in the tank warms to operating temperature and is able to flow freely through the hot well. 

Coarse Filter 
Coarse filters are fitted before the pressure and scavenge pumps and are respectively known as the suction and scavenge filters. The filters clean the oil by trapping any dirt, metal particles and carbon carried by the oil. The filters are fitted before the pumps to protect the pumps from damage. They are removed during servicing and the contents examined by engineers to asses the wear on the engine components. 
Magnetic Plugs (Chip Detectors) 
Removable magnetic plugs ("chip detectors") are fitted in the oil return lines. Ferrous particles from the engine or gearbox are caught by the magnets. Analysis of these particles can provide a very accurate indication of wear and impending component failure. This analysis is used for engine condition monitoring. 
Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft+M·-lo 
Oil Pump (Engine Driven Pump) 
The oil pumps are engine driven and normally use a spur gear system. The scavenge pump usually has twice the capacity of the pressure pump. Pump speed varies with engine RPM so pump output will increase at higher engine speeds and has to be regulated by a pressure relief valve. 
Relatively low oil pressure, especially at high RPM, may indicate a worn oil pressure pump. 
Pressure Relief Valve 
The pressure relief valve is fitted in parallel to the pump to regulate its output pressure. Excessive output pressure causes the spring-loaded valve to open and dump oil to the suction side of the pump. 
Pressure Filter 
The fine high-pressure filter removes very small particles that might otherwise damage the bearing surfaces. The filter is made of very fine felt, reinforced by wire gauze. The felt surface is deeply corrugated to increase the filtration area. A bypass valve is fitted to ensure that sufficient (unfiltered) oil continues to lubricate the engine if the filter becomes blocked or clogged. 

Air Cooled Oil Cooling 
An oil cooler is fitted in the scavenge line between the scavenge pump and the oil tank. It comprises a matrix of small galleries with fins attached. Air is drawn through the intake and passes through the matrix absorbing the heat from the oil and exhausting it overboard. An anti-surge valve allows oil to bypass the cooler whilst it is cold and viscous, or in the event that the oil cooler becomes blocked. 
Shutters are sometimes used to control the amount of air passing over the oil cooler. These manually operated doors restrict the flow of cooling air and so prevent the oil from being cooled excessively. 
Excessive cooling can cause "oil coring" in which the oil tends to congeal in the matrix and block it. If this occurs the anti surge valve will operate sending un-cooled oil directly back to the oil tank. The result will be a sharp rise in oil temperature that can only be corrected by closing the shutters! 
In a dry sump system the oil cooler is fitted in the return line and in wet sump systems the cooler is normally fitted in the pressure supply to the engine. 
Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft+H·lo 
Oil Temperature Gauge 
An oil temperature gauge measures the temperature of the oil in the supply line after the cooler and before the pressure pump.
