Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft++h·-lo 
Wet Sump Systems 
Wet sump systems use the same components as dry sump systems except that there is no scavenge pump, scavenge line or oil tank. Instead the oil is stored in the sump, which is a reservoir formed by part ofthe crank case. 
As well as pressurised oil feeds, the action of the crankshaft splashing through the oil helps to lubricate the working surfaces. This is known as splash lubrication. 
Comparison of Wet and Dry Sump Systems 
The principal advantage of a dry sump system is that the oil is contained in a separate oil tank. This ensures a supply offiltered and cooled oil to the pump under all flight conditions. By contrast the oil in a wet sump system disperses around the engine during inverted flight. This can very quickly lead to oil starvation. 
Because the wet sump system uses splash lubrication, excessive oiling can occur at high RPM. Furthermore, the oil used for splash lubrication hasn't been filtered or cooled. 
In a wet sump system, depending on the shape of the crankcase, it may be necessary to carry a greater quantity of oil than is necessary for lubrication to ensure an adequate supply to the oil pump. 

Checking the Oil Level 
In a dry sump system the oil tank quantity must be checked immediately after the engine is shut down. This is because oil in the tank can, after a while, start to drain back into the engine and give a false low reading. 
In a wet sump system the oil level in the sump should be checked about 20 minutes after shut down to allow time for all the oil to drain back to the sump. 
Hydraulicing 
Oil leaking into the engine and particularly into the engine cylinders can lead to a problem known as hydraulicing. This usually only occurs in inverted and radial engines. Oil can pool in the inverted cylinders between the piston crown and the cylinder head. Any attempt to start in this condition can cause severe damage as the piston forces the incompressible oil against the cylinder head. 
To prevent damage from hydraulicing it is common practice to briefly remove the spark plugs from the lower cylinders to allow the oil to drain out. At the very least the engine should be turned manually before starting to check for signs of hydraulicing. 
Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft+M·-lo 


Engine Cooling Systems 
Lubricating oil contributes significantly to engine cooling, but oil alone is insufficient to dissipate all the heat. An additional cooling method is needed. 
Piston engines can either be air or liquid cooled. Liquid cooling is inherently more efficient, but spark ignition aero-engines are usually air-cooled to save weight and to reduce the number of working components. 
In an air cooled engine, an air inlet directs air over the engine. Deep fins on the cylinders increase the external surface area available for cooling. Cylinder baffles control the flow of air to ensure that each cylinder is evenly cooled. 
(increase cooling)

Hot air exhausted'·-.: to atmosphere after cooling engine 
Piston Engine Lubrication and Cool ing 
ft+it·h·lo 
Temperature Measurement 
To operate efficiently the engine is designed to run at high temperature -but not so high as to cause fuel detonation and unacceptable thermal stresses on the engine. 
The highest temperatures occur around the cylinder head which is why cylinder head temperature is often measured and used as an indicator of engine health. 
Cylinder Head Temperature (CHT) sensors may be fitted to all the cylinders but usually only one is attached to the (normally) hottest, cylinder. The hottest cylinder is usually the rearmost, as it is the last to receive the flow of cooling air. CHT is measured by a thermocouple which requires no electrical supply. The thermocouple produces a voltage which varies directly with temperature. This is used to drive a CHT display in the cockpit. 


Piston Engine Lubrication and Cooling 
ft.iihi-l o 
Temperature Control 
During normal operation, cylinder head temperature is affected 
by: 
¦ 
The amount of power being produced. 

¦ 
The aircraft's speed (i.e. the mass flow of cooling air). 

¦ 
The temperature of the cooling air. 

¦ 
The ratio of the mixture. 


Of these four factors the pilot can control three. Mass flow is related to the aircraft's forward speed. The heat generated by the engine depends on the power produced and the mixture is controlled by the mixture lever. 
A "lean" or "weak" mixture will cause an increase in CHT because it burns more slowly and has more time to transfer heat to the components. 

The engine is most likely to overheat at low speed and high power, for example during an extended climb. It is equally important to ensure that the engine is not overcooled. Overcooling can result in thermal shock which can cause cylinders to crack. Overcooling is most likely to occur at high speed and lower power settings. 
Some engine installations are equipped with automatic or manually operated cowl flaps to regulate the flow of cooling air to the engine. The cowl flaps are opened for a full power climb and closed for a low power descent -for example in a high speed descent at idle power.

Piston Engine Ignition Systems

Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
ftiih\·lo 
Starting and Ignition Systems 
Modern piston engines are equipped with an electric starter motor powered by the battery. During the start sequence the motor drives the engine, either via a gearbox or by engaging a toothed gear on the engine flywheel. 

As soon as the engine starts, an overrun clutch disengages the drive from the motor. An amber starter warning lamp illuminates whilst the drive gear is engaged. If the light remains on for more than 30 seconds after engine start the starter motor may have Belt driven 
alternator
failed to disengage. The engine must be shut down. 
As the engine begins to rotate it drives one or more magnetos which generate High Tension electricity to power the spark plugs. 

Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
Ignition 
The purpose of the ignition system is to provide sparks of sufficient strength and duration at precisely the right moment in the cycle to ensure efficient combustion. 
The critical components of the ignition system are duplicated, thus ensuring that any single failure does not result in a total loss of engine power. Aero engines usually use engine-driven magnetos to generate the high voltages required for the spark plugs. Some modern aero engines use a combination of one magneto and one electronic system, such as plasma ignition. 
ft+i.i·lo 
Left hand Right hand cylinders cylinders 
Left or No1 magneto Right or No2 magneto fires upper spark plugs LH fires upper spark plugs RH cylinders and lower spark cylinders and lower spark plugs RH cylinders plugs LH cylinders 
Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
Principle of Magneto Operation 
The magneto is an AC generator, transformer and distribution system providing high voltage current to the spark plugs. It is independent of the aircraft battery and electrical system and, like all electrical generators, works on the principle that current will be caused to flow in a coil of wire when it is passed between the poles of a magnet. 
The generator section of the magneto comprises a rotating permanent magnet surrounded by a U-shaped stator made of soft iron. A primary winding, also called the Low Tension (LT) circuit is wound round the stator and comprises a few turns of thick wire. A secondary winding, also called the High Tension (HT) circuit is then wound over it. The secondary winding contains many turns affine wire and so acts as a step up transformer. As a result, any electrical flow in the primary winding will induce a higher voltage in the secondary winding, albeit at lower current. 
The permanent magnets are rotated by a drive from the engine. As they rotate they induce a alternating current in the primary and secondary windings. Both windings also produce a magnetic field that surrounds them. 
ft+IFf·io 


Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
Principle of Magneto Operation 2 

The primary winding circuit is continuously broken and remade by Th Primaty and 
Fl111 Secondary a contact breaker. This has two effects: Windl1191 
¦ 
It converts the continuous flow of alternating current into a series of pulses. 

¦ 
The collapse of the magnetic field around the primary winding produces a surge of energy in the secondary winding. 


The overall result is a series of very high voltage low current 
SpnPlugl 
pulses of electricity which are fed to a distributor and then through High Tension (HT) leads to the spark plugs. 
The HT leads and sometimes the spark plugs are screened to reduce electrical interference. 
The collapsing magnetic field caused when the contact breaker points open has one undesirable effect. It induces a current in the primary winding which is sufficiently strong to produce arcing at the contact breaker points. This arcing can damage the breaker points. A capacitor or condenser is fitted in parallel with the contact breaker to prevent arcing and to provide a rapid collapse of the primary current. It also helps to ensure that the highest possible voltage is induced in the secondary circuit. 
Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
Principle of Magneto Operation 3 
The distributor distributes the electrical pulses to each spark plug in the appropriate sequence. It comprises a rotor arm driven by the engine and a distribution block which contains a series of electrodes each linked to a separate spark plug. HT current from the magneto is fed to the rotor and passes to each spark plug in turn as the rotor comes into contact with its associated electrode. 
The HT current is sufficiently large to jump the gap between the spark plug's electrodes, creating a powerful spark as it does so. The spark occurs at the spark plug just as the contact breaker points open. 
The engine drives the contact breaker and the distributor rotor via a spindle. So the speed of the rotor and the speed of operation of the contact breaker vary directly with engine RPM. The spindle always rotates at half the crankshaft RPM. 
ft+ifff·io 


Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
Controlling the Ignition System 
Each magneto is controlled by its own ignition switch. The switch isolates the magneto's ignition circuit by connecting the primary circuit to Earth. In this condition its is said to be "grounded". The ignition switch lead is called the 'P' lead. 
The ignition switch must be closed to earth the circuit. When the switch is open, the ignition circuit is live and current is free to flow in the primary circuit whenever the contact breaker is closed. 
This switching method works in the opposite sense to a normal electric switching system to provide fail-safe protection in flight. With this design, a break in the switch's wiring will not cause the ignition system to fail. 
The system is anything but fail-safe on the ground because a disconnected wire in the switch circuit may result in a permanently live ignition circuit! 
There is a further problem with this arrangement. Most light aircraft use a single-pole, earth-return electrical system. This means that it only needs one disconnected switch wire to touch any part of the airframe for the magneto to switch off. 
ft+Milo 

Additional Advantage of Two Spark Plugs 
The additional spark plug in each cylinder helps to accelerate the combustion process because the flame front spreads out from both plugs simultaneously. This causes a quicker rise to peak cylinder pressure and thus a noticeable increase in power. This is why engine RPM drops slightly when each magneto is switched off during the pre-flight magneto check. 
Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
Magneto Checks 
The ignition system is completely duplicated, so both halves of the circuit must be checked. The pre-flight magneto check comprises two elements: the "mag drop" check and the "dead cut" check. 
The "mag drop" check is carried out twice, but at different power settings and for different reasons. The first check is carried out just after starting the engine with the RPM set at about 1000-1200 RPM. Each magneto is switched off in turn. The RPM should drop but the engine should continue to run smoothly. This proves the operation of the magneto switches and confirms that, at low RPM, one magneto will keep the engine running. 
The second "mag drop" check is carried out at higher RPM, normally about 75% of maximum. Again each magneto is switched off in turn. During this check the RPM drop must fall within specific limits and the engine must continue to run smoothly. A successful check indicates that, if one magneto were to fail, engine power would not be significantly affected. 
A complete dead cut would indicate a failure of one half of the ignition circuit, whilst no drop in RPM would imply that one half of the ignition system is not being earthed. Excessive RPM drop and rough running indicates an ignition problem. 
ft.Hf·llo 

A drop of around 300-400 RPM accompanied by rough running is typical of a malfunctioning spark plug. 
A malfunctioning ignition switch could leave the circuit live after engine shut down, so it is essential to carry out a dead cut check before shutting down. With the throttle at idle, both ignition switches are briefly turned off. If the system is serviceable the engine will begin to shut down but will recover immediately the magnetos are switched back on. If a dead cut doesn't occur it's likely that at least one magneto is "live" -a potentially dangerous condition. 
Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
The Need for Pressurised Magnetos 
Magnetos rely to some extent on the insulating properties of air to isolate internal components. Consequently magnetos designed for high altitude operation are normally pressurised to reduce the possibility of internal arcing. 
Auxiliary Spark Augmentation 
The magneto's output voltage is directly proportional to its rotational speed. It is driven by the engine via a gearbox which increases its rotational speed to guarantee sufficient voltage is produced, even at idle RPM. 
During start, when the engine is turning very slowly, the system needs some help and, for reasons we discussed earlier in this chapter, it must also ensure that the spark is sufficiently retarded. There are three methods of achieving this. 
ft.f:ii/lo 

Impulse Magneto 
Smaller, lower powered, engines use an impulse coupling magneto on either or both magnetos. The coupling comprises a spring-loaded clutch mounted between the drive spindle from the engine and the magneto shaft. As the engine begins to rotate the drive spindle winds up the spring-loaded clutch. When the clutch releases the spring unwinds rapidly spinning the magneto much faster than the engine is turning it. 
This occurs once every half revolution of the engine and ensures that sufficiently strong sparks are generated, even when hand starting. The same mechanism also retards the ignition during its operation. 
Once the engine has started, pawls within the mechanism move out under centrifugal force to prevent the coupling from operating. 
Piston Engine Ignition Systems 
High Tension Booster Coil 
The output from the magneto can be augmented using a booster coil. High Tension booster coil systems are commonly used on larger engines. 
The HT booster coil induces a large current in a secondary winding by collapsing the magnetic field surrounding the primary winding. Current is fed from the aircraft battery to power the primary winding. A trembler interrupter in the coil interrupts the primary winding circuit to produce a continuous stream of high voltage impulses in the secondary (HT) circuit. 
This voltage is then fed to a trailing brush on the distributor rotor arm. The amount of trail is set to ensure the spark is sufficiently retarded for starting. This system is also known as the "shower of sparks" ignition system. Once the engine has started the HT booster coil is switched off. 
ft.M\lo 

Low Tension Booster Coil 
The Low Tension (LT) booster coil uses aircraft battery power to increase the output from the magneto. Boosted battery power is fed directly to the primary winding ofthe magneto itself to improve its slow speed power generation. 
In this system a second set of contact breaker points in the LT booster coil circuit ensure a sufficiently retarded spark. The LT booster coil is switched off once the engine has started. 
Piston Engine Ignition Systems  
Spark Plugs  
The High Tension (HT) leads from the distributor supply each spark plug with about 30 000 volts at relatively low current.  
The plug comprises an insulated electrode running through its centre and a smaller electrode attached to the base of the spark plug body. The main body of the plug is metal, and connects to earth when screwed into the cylinder. A small but precisely set gap separates the two electrodes. When HT voltage is applied to the centre electrode a strong spark is generated between the two electrodes. This ignites the fuel air mixture. The size of the gap is important and must be accurately set before the plug is installed. If it is too small the spark may be weak. If it is too large there may be no spark at all.  Body forms electrode  Spark jumps across gap Centre electrode If gap is blocked plug is said to be fouled  
Occasionally oil, carbon or lead deposits from the fuel can block the gap causing a failure to spark. This is known as plug 'fouling'.  
Spark plugs have a designated operating temperature range. Within this range the plug is designed to reduce the risk offouling. Fouling is much more likely to occur if a plug of the wrong temperature specification is fitted.  



Lesson 37 
Piston Engine Fuel 

AGK37 
Piston Engine Fuel 
ft.ihil o 
Piston Engine Fuel 
Characteristics of Aviation Gasoline (AVGAS) 
A piston engine relies on harnessing the energy released when fuel is burned with air. For this to be effective the fuel must have a number of characteristics: 
¦ 
It must contain a significant quantity of energy. 

¦ 
It must be able to form vapour-but not too readily. 

¦ 
It must be able to burn quickly but controllably, giving a manageable rise in pressure that can be efficiently translated into the movement of the pistons. 


The quantity of energy contained in the fuel is measured by its calorific value, which is the amount of heat obtained for a given mass of fuel. The specific gravity ofAVGAS is typically 0.72 at ISA and at sea level, which means that it is less dense than Jet A 1 (AVTUR) at around 0.8. 
The way in which the fuel burns depends upon a number of factors. For example, under optimum conditions, aviation gasoline burns rapidly but consistently with a flame rate (the speed at which the flame propagates) of about 60-80 ft per second. This produces the predictable and uniform rise in pressure which is used to drive the piston. 


Piston Engine Fuel 
Detonation 
Under abnormal conditions the mixture may detonate rather than burn. Detonation is the extremely rapid spontaneous combustion of part of the mixture after burning has begun. The result is a rapid, violent and uneven rise in pressure in the cylinder with flame rates in the order of 1000 ft per second. The shock of detonation can often be heard as a knocking sound within the engine. Detonation is often referred to as "knocking". 
Most of the energy produced by detonation is not absorbed by piston movement but is instead given up as heat and shock. Consequently power output reduces markedly and the cylinder temperature rises rapidly and unevenly, producing local hot spots and inflicting physical damage to the piston crowns. The engine runs rough. Prolonged detonation will eventually cause the piston crowns to fail under the load. 
ft+i.i·lo 

The four principal causes of detonation are: 
¦ 
Fuel with poor anti-knock qualities. 

¦ 
Incorrect (excessively weak) mixture ratio. 

¦ 
Anything that unduly raises the temperature or pressure of the mixture before it is burned (i.e. low RPM with high MAP). 

¦ 
High engine temperature. 


Piston Engine Fuel 
Pre-Ignition 
Detonation should not be confused with pre-ignition. Pre-ignition is the ignition ofthe mixture before the spark plug has fired. Detonation occurs after ignition. 
Pre-ignition is caused by local hot spots within the cylinder caused, for example, by small carbon deposits. It is most likely when the cylinder is excessively hot or excessively contaminated with carbon deposits. The primary cause of pre-ignition is an excessively weak mixture. The symptoms of pre-ignition are the same as for detonation: loss of power, rough running and overheating. Pre-ignition usually worsens with increased RPM whereas detonation usually reduces as RPM increase. 
Pre-ignition has one unique characteristic -the tendency for the engine to continue to run for a short period after the ignition has been turned off. This is called 'running on'. 
Controlling Detonation 
Modern aviation gasoline has very good anti-knock qualities produced by carefully controlling the hydrocarbon content of the fuel and by using additives. 
ft+IFf·io 

Octane Rating / Grade {Anti Knock) 
Fuel is assigned a number to indicate its resistance to detonation. The number is sometimes also known as the anti-knock value. 
The number indicates the percentages of the two pure spirits, iso­octane and heptane used to produce the same detonation characteristics of a particular fuel under test. Each has widely differing anti-knock characteristics. !so-octane has very good anti-knock qualities, heptane does not. For example an 87 octane fuel would have the same anti-knock qualities as a mixture of 87% iso-octane and 13% heptane. The higher the octane rating/grade of a fuel the higher its resistance to detonation. 
AVGAS normally has a two-figure octane rating which describes its anti-knock properties for weak and rich mixture settings respectively. AVGAS is normally rated at 100/130 or 115/145. The AVGAS specification is D Eng RD 2485. Octane numbers above 100 are called performance numbers. 
Piston Engine Fuel 
Additives 
By far the most effective additive for controlling detonation is tetra­ethyl lead. Unlike the fuel used in cars, lead is still added, albeit in much reduced quantities, to aviation gasoline. The most common fuel used for piston engine aircraft is 100 LL (lower lead). 100 LL is dyed blue and has replaced the 100 fuel which is coloured green. Both 100 and 100 LL have the same resistance to detonation despite the lower lead content of the latter. Some older piston engines were designed to run originally on 80/87. This fuel is dyed red. 

Other Factors Influencing Detonation 
Although fuels are selected for their resistance to detonation they can still detonate! Anything which raises the temperature or pressure of the mixture will increase the likelihood of detonation. Carburettor heat at high power settings, overheated cylinders, and high power settings at low RPM all increase the risk of detonation. 
Not surprisingly, engines which use high compression ratios or which are supercharged require a fuel with very good anti-knock qualities. 
If fuel ofthe specified octane rating or grade isn't available, you may operate the aircraft temporarily using a fuel with a higher rating but never use one with a lower rating/grade. 
Piston Engine Fuel 
The Combustion Process and Mixture Ratios 
The ratio of air to fuel which ensures complete combustion is about 15:1 by mass. This ratio is sometimes referred to as the "chemically correct mixture", "ideal ratio" or "stoichiometric mixture". Stoichiometric comes from the Greek words for "element" and "measure", so in this case it refers to the chemical elements in the fuel/air mixture. 
When a chemically correct mixture is ignited, all the hydrogen and carbon in the fuel combine with all the oxygen in the air for complete combustion. The products of stoichiometric combustion are carbon dioxide and water vapour, coupled with an increase in the temperature of the inert nitrogen gas. 
The chemically correct mixture cannot be used for normal operations because the high combustion temperature would incur the risk of detonation. 
With the mixture set about 10% richer, the excess fuel absorbs some ofthe latent heat and cools the charge below the detonation value. Rich mixtures also burn quickly. Both factors help to reduce the risk of detonation. 
In practice not all cylinders receive exactly the same mixture so to err on the safe side a 15% rich mixture is used at normal cruising power. Typically, mixture strengths vary from 8:1 (rich) to 20:1 (lean). 
ft+ifff·io

Fuel 


Piston Engine Fuel 
The Combustion Process and Mixture Ratios continued 
At high power settings (high combustion pressure and temperature) the risk of detonation increases. When high power is required the mixture is further enriched to about 20% rich to obtain the necessary cooling. 
At take-off the mixture may be as much as 30% rich. Apart from its cooling function, this excess fuel has no other use. It isn't fully burned and is discharged in the exhaust as a combination of fuel vapour and carbon monoxide. Very rich mixture causes black smoke in the exhaust. It can form carbon deposits in the cylinder and lead to fouling of the plugs. 
Ironically, a weaker mixture will also result in cooler burning, not only because less fuel is being burned per power stroke but also because the rate of burning slows down. 
With extremely weak mixtures the mixture may still be burning as the inlet valve opens. This can lead to "popping back" in the inlet manifold. 
ft+Milo 

A weak mixture will produce cooler burning gas but, paradoxically, it will result in higher engine temperatures. This is because the slower burn gives more time for heat to be transferred to the body of the engine. So a lean mixture will result in an increased CHT but a reduced exhaust gas temperature. 
The slower burn resulting from a weak mixture results in reduced power but has the advantage of reducing fuel consumption. A 20% lean mixture is enough to achieve the best specific fuel consumption. 
Finally, at very low idling RPM the momentum of the exhaust gas is much reduced and consequently not all of it is scavenged from the cylinder. The remainder dilutes the incoming fresh mixture and effectively over-weakens it. Consequently the mixture must be enriched as idle RPM is approached. 
Prolonged ground running at idle with a rich mixture can cause spark plug fouling. To prevent this, the engine should be run at about 1000-1200 RPM. 
Piston Engine Fuel 
Practical Use of Mixture 
If your aeroplane is fitted with a carburettor (discussed in the following chapter) you can manually change the mixture to meet the requirements for various stages of flight. In the exam you could be asked about mixture settings particularly with reference to the graphs shown over the next two pages. Notice that one graph the x axis goes from a rich to weak mixture and the other weak to rich. Take care! 
In truth the graphs are not quite the same, the one with the single line is a more theoretical curve with fixed rpm and manifold pressure and the one with multiple curves is a more practical graph. So the numbers are in approximately comparable locations. 
Let's start with positions 1 and 2, in the practical graph where rpm and manifold pressure change, there is very little between the achievable power of positions 1 and 2. The slightly richer position 1 is preferred for take-off, probably to ensure freedom from detonation. Position 2 gives slightly more power at the expense of increased temperature and risk of detonation. Note that position 2 has a significantly lower specific fuel consumption so position 2 might be used when you needed to high-speed cruise. Some books refer to positions 1 & 2 as max power rich and max power lean respectively. 
ft.Hf·llo

In addition, this is where the temperatures are at their highest -not good for engine fatigue! So, we need to find somewhere cooler than position 4.

Practical Use of Mixture continued 
The multiple curve graph clearly shows that point 5 gives the best economy -look at the specific fuel consumption. However, there are two risks associated with this position, first the burning is so slow that it can ignite the incoming gases resulting in backfiring which causes a loss of power and damage. Second, if you fail to enrich the mixture prior to advancing the power very bad detonation occurs, causing loss of power and damage. Position 5 is known as 'best economy lean side of top'. This is because the method of setting this mixture is to find the peak EGT (top) and then lean off further to reduce the temperature by a set value. 
Point 3. Refer to the specific fuel consumption curve, notice there is very little difference between positions 5 and 3 but position 3 is free from the worst excesses of position 5. So, position 3 is the preferred economy setting. It is known as 'best economy rich side of top' because you find it by leaning to achieve peak EGT (top) and then enrich the mixture reducing the temperature by a specified value.

Piston Engine Fuel 
Volatility, Viscosity & Vapour Locking 
When referring to fuel, volatility is a measure of how readily it will change from liquid to vapour, in other words, how easily it will vaporise. Oddly, this is a bad thing and a good thing: we don't want fuel to vaporise in the tanks or delivery pipelines but we do need it to vaporise readily so that it will burn. 
Of the two fuels we use in aviation, gasoline and kerosene, gasoline is the most volatile and particularly prone to vaporising in pipelines if they become either hot or evacuated. The vapour forms a bubble which sits at the highest point in the pipe, and can cause serious blockage to fuel flow. This is known as a vapour lock. To prevent such a hazardous situation submersible fuel pumps are mounted in the fuel tank to ensure the fuel is always delivered under positive pressure. This strategy is employed in both gasoline and kerosene fuel systems. 
Gravity feeding in the climb is a problem in some jet aircraft. The reducing atmospheric pressure with altitude causes the fuel to vaporise which is sometimes given the term 'foaming'. The result is that the engine(s) could be starved of fuel. In this situation the pilots will need to determine the 'gravity feed ceiling' which, could be as low as FL 150. If using JET B which vaporises more readily than JetA1 then the ceiling may be even lower. In one of the Airbus aircraft the 'gravity feed ceiling' is FL 100 when using JET 
ft+Milo 

To prevent vapour locking, which can lead to fuel starvation, a common type of limitation imposed by the Minimum Equipment List (MEL) in large transport aeroplanes for complete boost pump failure is: 
• 
Minimum fuel temperature limitation 

• 
Maximum operating altitude limitation 


• Extra reserve fuel has to be carried 
• No fuel may be carried in the affected fuel tank

Piston Engine Fuel 
Volatility, Viscosity & Vapour Locking continued 
Viscosity is a measure on internal resistance within a fluid its value represents resistance to flow. An old way of measuring viscosity was to place the liquid in a cup known as the Ford cup (as shown opposite). The cup has a hole in the bottom for the fluid to flow out. Once the cup was filled, scientists timed how long it took for the cup to empty. The longer it took, the higher the viscosity. This simple method failed to take account of many variables such as temperature and pressure, so nowadays more sophisticated techniques used. 
Fuel needs to be easily pumped so must be of a low viscosity. While gasoline presents problems with vaporisation, jeUdiesel fuel (kerosene) has problems with viscosity, particularly at low temperatures when the fuel steadily reverts to a wax rendering it unpumpable. In high-flying long-range jets fuel temperature is monitored and methods of heating are required.

Engine Fuel Pump 
Fuel is delivered from the tanks to the engine by an engine driven 
pump, often backed up by an electrically driven pump for redundancy. The engine driven pumps may be of the spur gear type, as we already met in the chapter on hydraulics, or they may be of the vane type. 

Carburettors 
ft.ifid o 
Engine Fuel Pump continued 
The vane type pump works by rotating a vane within a housing of variable volume. By reducing the volume available the pressure of the fuel is increased. To achieve this the vanes are designed to slide across each other around the point of rotation. You can see in the illustration that Chamber B, currently on the supply side, is considerably larger than Chamber D on the pressure port. Carburettors 
The Basic Float Carburettor 
Throttle butterfly Utube principle 

Principle of Operation maintained Vent to atmosphere
l l
by float 
Filtered fuel is pumped into a chamber in the carburettor. The level of fuel in the chamber is regulated by a float which uses a needle valve to shut off the fuel supply when the fuel reaches the correct level. 
A piped outlet from the fuel chamber forms a U-tube which exits in the carburettor's "throat" or "choke". When the engine is stopped the fuel levels in the chamber and the U-tube are the same because both are exposed to atmospheric air at the same pressure. 
When the engine is running air is drawn in through the inlet and 

accelerates as it passes through the throat. Because of the Fuel delivered under pressure from aircraft fuel tanks
venturi effect the pressure in the throat drops. As a result fuel is drawn up the U-tube and into the air flowing through the throat. 
The nozzle is designed so that the fuel is finely atomised as it mixes with the air. A calibrated restrictor known as the "jet" regulates the amount of fuel flowing up the U-tube. 
Carburettors 
ft.ifid o 
Principle of Operation continued 
Throttle butterfly 
As engine RPM increases, the speed of the air flowing through the throat increases causing a further drop in pressure. This causes more fuel to be drawn through the nozzle. In this way the mixture of fuel and air is naturally regulated at all power settings. 
Some points about this basic principle of operation need further emphasis: 
¦ 
The throttle butterfly valve controls the amount of air passing through the throat. If the valve is opened, the flow of mixture to the cylinders will increase and the engine will accelerate. 

¦ 
When the fuel level drops in the float chamber the float will also drop. This opens the fuel inlet needle valve and allows more fuel to flow into the chamber. 

¦ 
The fuel is pumped under pressure to the carburettor to 



prevent it vaporising in the supply line, causing vapour lock. The carburettor's basic principle of operation is relatively straightforward but, as we shall see in the following sections, this isn't quite the whole story. 
Carburettors 

n +e+•o 
Additional Fuel Metering Requirements 
The simple system we have so far described takes no account of air density and ignores the fact that the fuel flow from the nozzle doesn't vary linearly with the amount of air flowing through the throat. 
In practice this simple system would result in a progressively richer mixture as engine speed and/or altitude increases. Furthermore, because of the ram air effect in the inlet, the mixture would become progressively weaker with increased airspeed. 
The system also fails to provide the special mixture settings required at very low and very high power settings. So a practical carburettor must have a number of additional refinements. 
Throttle butterfly 
l 
Carburettors 
ft+HUo 
The Pressure Balance 
At high airspeeds the intake pressure will rise because of the ram effect of the air entering it. This increases the pressure in the throat which causes a drop in the fuel flow from the U-tube resulting in a weaker mixture. To avoid this problem a pressure balance duct connects the intake and the float chamber. 
The duct ensures that the pressure in the intake and float chamber remain the same. 
.., The purpose of the pressure balance duct is to ensure that the correct mixture ratio is maintained across a range of airspeeds and flight conditions. 
high forward speed 

Carburettors 


The Diffuser 
Airflow to diffuser
The fuel flow from the nozzle will increase with increased airflow through the throat but the relationship isn't linear. In fact, doubling the mass flow through the throat would cause a fourfold drop in pressure and a correspondingly large addition offuel, resulting in an excessively rich mixture. The solution to the problem is to fit a diffuser immediately after the main jet. 
Air from the pressure balance duct is fed though a perforated tube called the diffuser. Fuel enters the diffuser from the main jet. At idle RPM the levels in the diffuser and float chamber are almost equal. 
When the engine is running, the fuel level in the diffuser falls because of the slight restriction caused by the jet. This uncovers some ofthe perforations in the diffuser allowing air from the pressure balance duct to flow in. This reduces the pressure at the nozzle, so reducing the rate of fuel flow. 
r 
The purpose of the diffuser is to maintain the correct mixture The perforations in the diffuser are very precisely sized to ensure ratio across a wide range of engine speeds. 
that the correct mixture ratio is maintained across the whole speed range of the engine. 
The diffuser has an added advantage. The increased mixing of air and fuel aids atomisation, helping to produce a good emulsion of air and fuel. 
Carburettors 

n +e+•o 
Mixture Control 
Air density decreases with altitude. So the mass of air passing through the throat will reduce with altitude. This results in a progressively richer mixture as the aircraft climbs. 
To compensate for the barometric effect of changing air density a mixture control valve is fitted. The valve is either manually controlled or operated automatically by an aneroid capsule. 
There are many designs for mixture control. The example shown here regulates the flow of air from the pressure balance duct to the diffuser thus varying its effectiveness. 
Alternative methods include connecting the float chamber to the venturi to lower the float chamber pressure, or using a needle to vary the size of the jet. In all variants the systems adjust the fuel flow -not the air flow. 
Automatic systems invariably have a manual override. Manual 

mixture control levers are coloured red. 
The mixture control is not just used to compensate for reduced air density. It is also used to set a leaner mixture, for example during an economy cruise. This can produce a significant reduction in fuel consumption. 
r 
The purpose of the mixture control is to adjust the mixture to take account of changes in air density and to reduce fuel consumption. 

Carburettors 
Slow Running Jet 
Idle fuel flow 
At very small throttle openings the pressure drop in the venturi is negligible, resulting in an insufficient flow offuel from the main jet. 
-r~ 
The solution is to insert an additional jet specially designed to cater for low power conditions. 
The slow running jet is placed just at the edge of the almost closed butterfly to take advantage of the strong pressure drop in the constriction caused by the valve. This jet is designed to provide a rich mixture at idle. 
As the butterfly opens the constriction disappears. With no venturi effect the fuel ceases to flow from the slow running jet. 


The system usually includes a cut off valve linked to the mixture control lever. The cut off valve ensures that all fuel flow to the engine ceases when the pilot shuts down the engine. The shut off valve is operated by moving the mixture control lever rearwards to the "Idle Cut Off' (ICO) position. 
The purpose of the slow running jet is to ensure a sufficiently rich mixture at low RPM. The purpose of the cut off valve is to assist engine shut-down. 

Carburettors 
Power Enrichment 
At high power settings a power enrichment system is required to provide the rich mixture needed to cool the cylinders. 
There are two ways this can be achieved. A variable size main jet can be used or, more commonly, a second jet is brought into play by a cam linked to the throttle. 
At high throttle settings the cam forces down a piston which allows fuel to flow to the power jet. The power jet shuts off during all but high power settings and for this reason it is sometimes confusingly referred to as an economiser system. 
The purpose of the power jet or "economiser" is to provide a rich mixture at high power settings to assist cylinder cooling. 
lo... 
ft.iiiiWo 
Power jet cam operated by pilots throttle 
Carburettors 
The Accelerator Pump 
When the throttle is opened rapidly the air flow to the engine increases almost instantaneously. But there is a slight delay before reduced pressure in the throat causes more fuel to be added to the mixture. This can cause the engine to hesitate or even stop -a phenomenon known as "weak " or "lean" cut. The solution is an accelerator pump. 
The throttle lever is connected by a suitable system of links to a small piston type pump in the carburettor. When the throttle lever is moved rapidly forward the accelerator pump injects a quantity of fuel directly into the venturi. 
Accelerator pump jet cam operated by pilots throttle 
Power jet cam 
Carburettors 

Induction and Carburettor Icing 
Causes 
The carburettor throat has one potentially disastrous flaw. As well as inducing a drop in pressure it also produces a drop in temperature. A further temperature reduction is caused by fuel evaporating as it emerges from the nozzle. At low power settings, with the throttle butterfly forming a tight constriction, the drop in pressure and temperature is more pronounced. In the worst case the temperature in the throat can drop by as much as 25°C. 
It is a common mistake to assume that carburettor icing only occurs at low ambient temperatures. A much more relevant factor is the amount of moisture in the air. 
At moderate or high levels of humidity ice will form in the intake duct or throat whenever the temperature drop is sufficient to reduce the intake temperature below zero. 
Ice formation in the intake and throat restricts the amount of air that can enter the engine, causing a marked reduction in engine power. Ice may sometimes form around the throttle butterfly, causing it to stick. If this happens when the butterfly is closed, for example during a prolonged descent, the result could be rather embarrassing. 


Carburettors 
Causes 
The risk of carburettor icing varies principally with humidity, ambient temperature and throttle position, as shown in this illustration. 
With a Relative Humidity (RH) in excess of 60% carburettor icing may be expected in virtually all flight conditions at ordinary ambient temperatures. So, when flying in fog or cloud, i.e. in saturated air, intake heating must be used. Mist (visible moisture in the atmosphere) generally implies a relative humidity of 95% so again, intake heating is prudent. 
With an ambient temperature of 18°-25°C and a relative humidity of only 30%, serious carburettor icing is likely if the throttle is closed for prolonged periods for example, during a prolonged descent or whilst taxying. Carburettor icing is not confined to cold conditions. In fact, the most dangerous conditions occur when cruise or descent power is set on relatively warm days with moderate or high humidity. 

Risk of carburettor icing in air free of cloud fog or precipitation Risk is maximum in cloud fog or precipitation.

Carburettors 

Symptoms 
The symptoms of induction or carburettor icing may include some or all ofthe following: 
• 
Loss of RPM . 

• 
Reduced performance . 

• 
Reduced inlet manifold pressure . 

• 
Decreased Exhaust Gas Temperature (EGT) . 

• 
Rough running . 

• 
Jammed throttle . 


In extreme cases ice accretion can cause the engine to stop altogether. On a fixed pitch propeller engine the first sign of carburettor icing is likely to be an unexplained drop in RPM. On variable pitch installations the RPM will initially remain constant but the MAP will reduce. 

Carburettors 
--------------------------------~ 
Carburettor heating systems offer reasonably good protection against icing. Whenever carburettor icing is likely the pilot manually selects an alternative air source which uses hot air drawn from a heat exchanger wrapped around the exhaust system. The alternate air source is usually unfiltered so care must be taken when using it on the ground -especially on dusty or grassy surfaces. 
Using heated air has five potential disadvantages: 
• 
It reduces engine power . 

• 
In cold ambient conditions the hot air may raise the temperature of air in the venturi from a point well below that likely to induce icing, to the optimum temperature for icing to occur! 


¦ 
Using carburettor heating to clear ice which is already well established may cause further problems as the ice breaks off and is ingested by the engine. 

¦ 
The use of heated air at high power settings can cause detonation. 

¦ 
Because hot air is less dense it will enrich the mixture. 


The carburettor heat system must be checked before flight. This is done by checking for a temporary drop in RPM (usually around 100-200 RPM) whilst carburettor heat is selected on, with the engine running at about 75% of max RPM. 


Carburettor heat on Carburettor Carburettor 'unfiltered air' air box heat valve 

If carburettor heat is selected with ice present, then the engine will continue to run rough for a brief period, and then smooth out. If no ice is present then you can expect a drop in power as you are unnecessarily using warm, so less dense, air. 


Lesson 33 
Fuel Injection Systems 

AGK 33 

Fuel Injection Systems 
Fuel Injection 

Fuel injection systems are replacing the traditional carburettor on 
Throttle
modern piston engines. Injection systems provide even and 
valve 
reliable fuel delivery, better icing protection and quicker throttle 
I 
Metered fuel 
response. 
delivered with air 
Air 

Because injection systems deliver fuel under pressure to the inlet engine they don't need to use a venturi in the air intake. This makes them much less susceptible to air intake icing. 
There are two types of fuel injection system direct and indirect. Both deliver a carefully measured amount of fuel under pressure to the engine. Indirect injection systems deliver fuel to the inlets, usually just before the inlet valve. Direct injection systems inject Air 
inlet 
precisely measured quantities of fuel directly into the cylinders. Amongst other things this requires an extremely accurate timing mechanism to ensure that the fuel is delivered at precisely the right moment in the firing cycle. 
Most aircraft engines use the low pressure continuous flow indirect system because this eliminates the need for complex injection timing devices. The downside is reduced fuel efficiency. 
fuel jet 

Fuel Injection Systems 
Indirect Fuel Injection 
Continuous-flow injection systems deliver fuel continuously to the inlet ports. They pay no attention to intake-valve opening or crankshaft position. The system has five basic components: 
¦ 
An engine driven pump (EDP) which supplies fuel at pressure to the fuel air control unit. 

¦ 
An electrically driven pump which also supplies fuel to the fuel air control unit. 

¦ 
An injector assembly which adjusts the fuel flow according to throttle position and mixture setting. 

¦ 
A manifold valve which distributes fuel from the injector to the fuel nozzles. 

¦ 
Fuel nozzles which spray finely atomized fuel into the inlets of each cylinder. 


Fuel is delivered under pressure to the injector unit. The output pressure of the engine driven pump is determined by engine speed. Normally its output considerably exceeds the pressure required by the injector assembly so the excess pressure is dumped by a relief-valve. This arrangement ensures that the injector assembly is always supplied with sufficient fuel pressure throughout most ofthe engine's operating range. 

The electric pump also provides fuel under pressure to the injector assembly. It has two functions. Firstly it provides pressure in the event that the engine driven pump fails. Secondly, it is used to prime the engine prior to start. 
The injector assembly or "fuel air control unit" controls the amount of fuel directed to the manifold valve. It comprises a mixture control valve and a metering valve. 
Pressurized fuel enters the assembly where it first encounters the mixture control valve. This valve is directly controlled by the mixture control lever and determines how much fuel is delivered to the metering valve. 
The metering valve is connected to, and controlled by, the throttle assembly which is also directly connected to the throttle butterfly valve. When the throttle lever is moved towards full power the throttle butterfly opens and so also does the metering valve to allow more fuel to flow. The ratio of movement ofthe metering valve to the movement of the throttle butterfly valve is precisely determined by the mechanical relationship of the operating gear. 
Fuel Injection Systems 
Indirect Fuel Injection (continued) 
It is important to appreciate that the mixture control valve is the only means of adjusting the otherwise pre-set ratio of fuel to air. 
Metered fuel from the injector is then fed to the manifold valve. The manifold valve has two functions. Its primary purpose is to divide the metered fuel between all the cylinders. Its secondary function is to provide a positive means of stopping all fuel flow when the mixture control is set to idle cut off. 
Fuel from the manifold valve is fed to each of the fuel nozzles through stainless-steel injector lines. The injector nozzle has a calibrated orifice in its upper chamber, controlling the amount of fuel injected. The lower portion of the nozzle is used as a mixing chamber. Here the metered fuel is combined with air to help to ensure that it is completely atomised before it enters the cylinder. Normally aspirated engines use ambient air. Turbocharged engines use boost pressure. 
Fuel injection systems are usually monitored by measuring fuel pressure at the outlet of the fuel manifold. Since there is a direct relationship between pressure and flow this sensor also serves to provide information on fuel flow. 
ft.ih=d o 
From fuel tank 

Fuel Injection Systems 
Direct Injection 
Larger more powerful engines, and diesel engines use direct fuel injection systems. In this system the fuel is delivered directly into the cylinder. Direct fuel injection produces better fuel efficiency. The subject is covered in detail in the section on diesel engines. 
Fuel Injection Systems 
Engine Priming Systems 
To help engine starting, particularly from cold, a very rich mixture is needed to ensure that sufficient fuel is vaporized On a carburetted engine neat fuel is usually injected into the inlet manifold. This is achieved in one of three ways:A manually operated plunger forces down the float in the carburettor float chamber flooding the carburettor.The throttle is exercised to activate the accelerator pump.A manually or electrically operated priming pump is used.Manual priming pumps are usually operated by a plunger type priming control. After use, the primer control must be locked off to prevent over fuelling. Injector systems automatically supply fuel as soon as the booster pump is switched on and consequently need no additional priming system. 
Care must be taken not to over prime and flood the engine. Excess fuel can cause a fire in the engine intake or carburettor, foul the spark plugs, or may wash the lubricating oil off the cylinder walls. Some checklists recommend continuing to crank the engine with a carburettor fire, to draw the flames in to the engine where they can do less damage. Once the fire is extinguished the aircraft must then be fully checked for fire damage, of course. 


The Engine Air Induction System 
Air is drawn into the carburettor or injector through an intake air filter. The filter removes potentially damaging dirt particles. 
Air intakes are susceptible to impact icing caused by droplets of super-cooled water freezing as they impact the intake. The symptoms of intake icing are the same as for carburettor icing and can occur on both carburetted and fuel injected engines. Carburettor heat may not be able to clear intake icing. 
Fuel injected engines have an alternate air source which also bypasses the intake air filter. On both types care should be taken when using the unfiltered air source. 
Both carburetted and injected engines should be checked before flight for satisfactory operation using alternate air. However, unlike the carburetted engine, fuel injection systems will not show a drop in RPM when alternate air is selected. 
Fuel Injection Systems 
ft+M=b!o 
EGT Gauges 
Some engine installations also measure the temperature of the exhaust gases. Like CHT devices, Exhaust Gas Temperature (EGT) measuring systems use thermocouples. 
The temperature of combustion, and hence the temperature of the exhaust gases, is affected by the ratio offuel to air in the mixture. Consequently, EGT is often used as a method for accurately adjusting the mixture ratio in cruise flight. 
The highest combustion temperature occurs at an air/fuel ratio of 
15:1. Combustion is cooler at richer mixtures because of the cooling effect of the excess fuel. It is also cooler when the mixture is very lean, this time because of the excess air in the mixture. So, in theory at least, peak EGT coincides with the ideal air/fuel ratio of 15:1. 

Two cruise power settings can be set with the help of EGT.

the ideal power cruise ratio of 12:1.  Charge temperature reduced by excess fuel rich mixture

Lesson 34 
Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
AGK 34 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
ft+i&Ho 
Piston Engine Power and Performance 
There are three operational descriptions of power output: 
Take-Off Power. Take-off power is the maximum power the engine can produce. It cannot be used continuously and is normally limited by time or altitude. 
Maximum Continuous Power can be used continuously without any time limit. This power setting reduces physical stress and excessive temperatures, extending the fatigue life of the engine. 
Cruise Power. Cruise power is normally expressed as a percentage of maximum power. 
Most piston engine aircraft have a variety of cruise power settings, 
e.g. 60%, 65%, 70% or 75%. Maximum cruise power will be the same as Maximum continuous power. Your choice will depend on factors such as economy, range, speed, engine life or simply company policy etc. 
The power developed by a piston engine depends on the mass of mixture drawn into the cylinder "the charge". The charge mass depends on the density of the air and the pressure in the inlet manifold. At full throttle on a normally aspirated engine the pressure in the manifold will be just less than atmospheric pressure. 

Density varies with temperature, altitude and humidity, so the relationship between pressure and density is not constant. The most direct determinant of engine power is the density altitude. 
Density altitude is the altitude in the International Standard Atmosphere at which the air density would be equal to the actual air density at the place of observation. In other words it is the pressure altitude adjusted for any temperature deviation from ISA. 
Pressure altitude is the altitude displayed with 1013 hPa set on the altimeter. 
In ISA conditions sea level temperature is 15°C decreasing by 2°C per 1000 ft. 
The density altitude can be calculated approximately using the following formula: 
Density altitude= Pressure altitude +/-(the difference in ISA 
temperature x 120) 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
ft+iiflo 
Density Altitude continued 
Consider an airfield at an altitude 4000 ft. The OAT is observed to be 21 °c. The ISA temperature for this height should be 15°C ­8°C (2-0c per 1000 ft) i.e. 7°C. So the difference between actual and ISA temperature is 14°C. 
The density altitude is 4000 + (14 x 120) = 5680 ft. 
In other words, the maximum power produced by the engine at this airfield on this day will be equivalent to an engine operating at almost 5700 ft. 
Engines produce the most power when the air is cold, dry and dense i.e. in winter high pressure conditions at sea level. 
Piston engine power is translated into usable thrust by a propeller. So, when considering power output we must also take into account the efficiency ofthe propeller. 
At high atmospheric densities the engine produces more power, but it will also use more power to turn the propeller in the relatively dense air. The reverse is true at low atmospheric densities. Furthermore, for aerodynamic reasons, propeller efficiency varies significantly with airspeed. 


Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
ft+ifflo 
Superchargers 
On a normally aspirated engine the mass of charge entering the cylinder decreases with increasing density altitude. To maintain maximum continuous power as the aircraft climbs the pilot must progressively open the throttle. Eventually the point will be reached where the throttle can be opened no further. From this point on, power output will inevitably and steadily reduce. 
In fact, by 20 000 ft a normally aspirated engine will have lost 50% of its maximum available power. 
We can delay the point at which this drop in power begins by using an air compressor to artificially maintain the mass of charge air entering the cylinders. Or, to phrase it more accurately, we can increase the volumetric efficiency of the engine. 
The concept of compressing the inlet air is known as supercharging and the compressor is known as the supercharger. 
Superchargers are used in one of two ways. The most most common way is to boost the maximum power of the engine at sea level e.g. make a 1 00hp engine produce 11 0hp. These are know as 'ground boosted' superchargers and usually require the engine to be strengthened to withstand the greater combustion pressures. 

The other, less common, way is to maintain sea level power during the climb. These are known as 'altitude boosted' superchargers. 
While most of the syllabus refers to turbochargers, you are required to know the differences between superchargers and turbochargers, so let's take a brief look at superchargers. 
Early superchargers were driven by a geared drive from the engine, therefore they were said to be 'internally driven'. In the large radial engines of the 1940s superchargers could absorb as much as 70 hp which was a significant loss of energy. 
Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

Superchargers continued 
In this basic, single-stage, supercharger the fuel/air mixture is ducted to a radial/centrifugal compressor, also often referred to as a "blower impeller". This accelerates the mixture outwards via the divergent vanes. Once accelerated, the mixture then passes through a pair of diffusers where the air velocity is traded for pressure energy. This high-pressure fuel/air mixture is now directed to the cylinders. Such superchargers could be single speed, two speed or even variable speed. The various parts are labelled opposite and there is a 3D model on the next page for you to explore.

Altitude boosted superchargers 
The image opposite contains several interesting points. First, although it is an altitude boosted supercharger it can be used as a ground boosted system for short periods e.g. take-off. Second, you will notice that the power increases slightly until the rated altitude. During this phase the boost pressure is constant so the charge of air entering the cylinder remains constant, so why does power increase? Simply put, exhaust back pressure decreases with altitude providing improved scavenging during the exhaust stroke. 
The Rated Altitude is defined as the maximum altitude at which full rated manifold pressure can be maintained by the supercharger. This is also the altitude at which full rated power can be maintained. Beyond the rated altitude the power decays much as it does for a normally aspirated engine. 
On the graph opposite, note the difference of power between normal aspiration and supercharging at ground level. The difference indicates the power lost to drive the supercharger.

ft.Wflo 
Altitude boosted superchargers continued 
The image opposite shows various power curves for different boost and rpm settings. The purpose of this graph is to show the difference between Rated Altitude and Full Throttle Height. We have already said that Rated altitude is associated with rated power, but we may use lower settings to reduce stress on the engine to increase its life. Full Throttle Height is defined as the altitude at which a given Manifold Absolute Pressure (MAP) set can be maintained at a given rpm. The lower the MAP selected, the greater the altitude to which it can be maintained. Note also the odd mix of height and altitude in the definitions. 
Both Rated Altitude and Full Throttle Height are terms associated with engine driven superchargers. 
As soon as metallurgy found ways to deal with much higher temperatures engine driven superchargers became redundant, being replaced by turbo-superchargers, normally called turbochargers. Turbochargers are powered by exhaust gases. In other words, turbochargers recover energy from hot exhaust gases that would normally be lost.

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Centrifugal Impellers / Compressors 
Centrifugal compressors consist ofthree main components 

¦ 
an impeller Casing 

¦ 
a diffuser 

¦ 
a casing which houses both the impeller and diffuser.

Diffuser 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Centrifugal Impellers / Compressors continued 
The impeller rotates at very high speed (as much as 40 000 RPM). Air enters the "eye" of the impeller where it is picked up by the rotating vanes and propelled rapidly outwards from the centre by 
Diffuser Vanes
centrifugal force. As it moves from the eye to the circumference of the impeller the air increases in velocity, pressure and temperature. 
The air leaves the impeller tangentially and enters the diffuser vanes. These form a series of divergent ducts which trade some ofthe air's kinetic energy for pressure energy. 
The impeller and diffuser each account for about half the overall rise in pressure. The pressurised air is then ducted either directly to the inlet manifold or to the eye of a second impeller to generate a further rise in pressure. 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

Turbochargers 
The most common method of boosting manifold pressure is to use an externally driven supercharger, better known as a turbocharger. 
The principal advantage of a turbocharger is that it uses energy that would otherwise have been lost to drive its impeller. The energy in question is contained in the exhaust gases. 
A turbocharger unit comprises a turbine linked to an impeller by a short shaft. The turbocharger's bearings are lubricated by engine oil. 
Exhaust gas exiting the engine drives the turbine which in turn drives the impeller. 
The system uses exhaust gas, so it quickly becomes very hot. This means that it cannot be used to compress the mixture. So, unlike the supercharger, the turbocharger compresses inlet air only. Fuel is then added to the compressed air to create the mixture. 


Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Advantages and Disadvantages of Turbochargers Throttle and The turbocharger has a number of advantages over the internally mixture control driven supercharger: 
¦ 
No power is taken from the engine to drive the turbocharger. 

¦ 
The drive train is less complex and the whole system is lighter. 

¦ 
As engine RPM increase, the velocity of the exhaust gas will 



Exhaust increase so increasing the speed of the impeller and thus the system amount of boost. 
Turbochargers have one principal disadvantage. The turbine is driven by the exhaust gases, so it takes a short but finite time to spool up as engine RPM are increased. This can lead to a slight Turbocharger 
Intake
delay in delivering the extra boost required, a phenomenon known 
Air intake Alternate Manifold 

as "turbo lag." air intake 
A typical turbocharger installation is shown here. It is considerably smaller and lighter than a conventional supercharger. 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Controlling Turbocharger Output 
The amount of boost delivered to the engine must be controlled to prevent overboosting. On a turbocharger the boost level is controlled not by modifying the throttle butterfly position, but by controlling the speed of the turbocharger impeller. This is done by restricting the amount of exhaust gas directed onto the turbine. 
Very simple turbocharger control systems use a fixed restrictor and a pressure relief valve fitted in the induction manifold. This dumps excess pressure to atmosphere. When the valve operates it triggers an overboost warning light in the cockpit. 
On more sophisticated installations, a waste gate controller is used to direct some of the exhaust gas through a duct which bypasses the turbine. 

The waste gate is a butterfly valve positioned in the exhaust upstream of the turbocharger. By controlling the amount of gas directed to the turbine the output pressure of the impeller can be Note that: maintained. 
¦ 
The waste gate is fitted in parallel with the turbocharger. 

¦ 
The waste gate is upstream from the turbocharger. 



Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Controlling Turbocharger Output 2 
The waste gate is controlled by a hydraulic waste gate actuator. Engine oil is permanently supplied under pressure to the actuator and returns to the engine via a variable restrictor. 
When the variable restrictor is closed, pressure builds up in the actuator. This forces the actuator piston to move, closing the waste gate. All the exhaust gas is now directed to the turbine, causing it to accelerate. 
When the variable restrictor opens, the pressure in the actuator reduces. The piston returns under spring pressure and the waste gate opens. Exhaust gas is now directed through the bypass. Less gas reaches the turbine so it decelerates. 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Controlling Turbocharger Output 3 
In its simplest form the variable restrictor is controlled by a sealed aneroid capsule which is exposed to manifold pressure. 
When manifold pressure increases the aneroid capsule contracts. The restrictor opens, the piston returns under spring pressure and the waste gate opens. Turbocharger output is reduced. 
If the throttle is opened, pressure sensed by the aneroid capsule, drops. The aneroid capsule expands to close off the restrictor. Pressure builds in the actuator and the piston moves to close the waste gate. More exhaust gas is now directed to the turbine which increases speed. 
This process continues until a point of equilibrium is reached between oil and spring pressure in the actuator. 
Using this method the controller maintains a constant pressure in the inlet manifold up to the throttle butterfly. However, it takes an appreciable time for any drop in manifold pressure to be translated into an increase in compressor output. Consequently it takes a finite time to achieve a new power setting demanded by the throttle. This is the cause of turbocharger lag. 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

"'' 11.1""u.1 r,,11.11.uu'­
For a given power setting, the point will eventually be reached where the waste gate controller has completely closed the waste gate, in its attempt to sustain manifold pressure. Above this altitude manifold pressure, and thus engine power, will reduce. 
Critical altitude is reached when the turbocharger can no longer sustain the maximum outlet pressure from the turbocharger compressor. Critical altitude might be defined as the altitude at which the throttle reaches full open and the engine is still producing full rated power. This term is associated with turbochargers. Notice also that says, 'still producing' this is because the turbocharged engine can produce its rated power from ground level up to critical altitude. 
There is a further complication. Because all the exhaust now has to enter the turbine, exhaust back pressure increases which causes a reduction in volumetric efficiency. Engine power for a given value of MAP will decrease this is the opposite to what happens in an internally supercharged or in a normally aspirated engine. 
The image opposite combines the supercharger power curves with the turbocharger curves. In this case, the turbocharger is altitude boosted allowing the engine to maintain sea level power to its Critical Altitude above which power decays in the same manner as a normally aspirated engine. 
, ····} Power for ~.~ •~ • , ··· Supercharger

Engine 
10K 20K 30K Altitude 

Altitude boosted turbochargers are popular in the USA because many central states airfields are at high altitudes. For example, Leadville in Colorado has an elevation of almost 10 000 ft. The altitude boosted turbocharger provides the same take-off power at most airports. 
Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Operation During Various Flight Phases 
Engine Start: During start the waste gate is held open because of the spring pressure acting on the actuator piston. 
Idle Power:At idle power manifold pressure is low and there is little exhaust gas. The waste gate moves to the fully closed position. All available pressure is directed to the turbine but there is insufficient exhaust to achieve maximum compressor output pressure. 
Take-off: At take-off the waste gate is partially open. 
Climb: During a climb at constant power, the waste gate will tend to close as the air density decreases. Turbine RPM will increase. 
Critical Altitude: The waste gate is fully closed. The turbine is at maximum speed. 
Above Critical Altitude: The waste gate is fully closed, compressor outlet pressure cannot be maintained and the maximum value of MAP will reduce. 
In the simple system described above the waste gate controller is known as a single controller or absolute pressure controller (APC). The APC prevents turbocharger pressure from exceeding the design maximum pressure. 

A more sophisticated version ofthe single controller uses a variable pressure controller. A throttle-operated cam modifies the effect of the aneroid capsule to produce a manifold pressure that is related to power setting. 
Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

Dual Pressure Control Unit System 
The single controller system is inefficient. It is designed to maintain a constant discharge pressure from the turbocharger regardless of the actual MAP required. So at MAP values lower than maximum the turbocharger may be running faster than it needs to. 
To overcome this problem a second controller is normally added. This controller is sensitive to the difference in pressure across the throttle butterfly. In other words the difference between the compressor outlet pressure and manifold pressure. Its purpose is to eliminate any difference between outlet and manifold pressure. 
A diaphragm in the controller senses turbocharger outlet pressure on one side and manifold pressure on the other. The diaphragm is connected to a bleed valve. If manifold pressure drops below turbocharger outlet pressure, the diaphragm distorts and opens the bleed valve. Oil pressure drops and the waste gate opens until turbocharger outlet pressure reduces sufficiently to restore the diaphragm to its neutral position. 

Carburettor butterfly valve 


Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

Dual Pressure Control Unit System 
The differential controller controls the waste gate at all throttle positions except fully open. This last task falls to the density 

Carburettor controller. butterfly valve 
The density controller is sensitive to both temperature and pressure and prevents the turbocharger from exceeding the upper limit of manifold air pressure below the critical altitude at the full throttle position. 
The two controllers operate independently to control the turbocharger operation at all throttle positions. At less than full throttle the differential controller also reduces the possibility of an unstable operating state known as "bootstrapping" in which undemanded power changes can occur. 


Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
lntercoolers 
One of the drawbacks of any supercharging device is that the compressed air entering the engine is significantly hotter than ambient. This increases the risk of detonation. To overcome this disadvantage an intercooler is sometimes fitted at the induction manifold. 
Cold ram air is ducted from the atmosphere and passed over the induction manifold. Heat from the manifold air is absorbed by the ram air and exhausted overboard thus cooling the mixture. 

Engine Management 
The operating temperature of a supercharged engine is much higher than that of a normally aspirated engine. Consequently the oil and cylinder head temperatures must be monitored carefully. 
Power changes should always be made slowly and smoothly. Rapid throttle movement can cause serious damage. 
To prevent thermal shock when shutting down, the engine should be left to run at low power for long enough to allow its temperature to stabilise. Normally this period is significantly longer than that required for a normally aspirated engine. A turbocharger is particularly prone to damage ifthe engine is shut down before it has had time to cool. 
Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Engine Instruments 

Piston engine instrumentation may include the following: 
¦ 
RPM Gauge: which measures crankshaft RPM. 

¦ 
Manifold Absolute Pressure (MAP): which measures manifold pressure in inHg. 

¦ 
Boost Pressure Gauge: which measures manifold pressure in PSI. 

¦ 
Oil Pressure Gauge: which measures the oil pressure just after the pressure filter. 

¦ 
Oil Temperature Gauge: which measures the oil temperature between the oil cooler and the pressure pump. 

¦ 
Cylinder Head Temperature Gauge: which measures the temperature of the hottest cylinder. 

¦ 
Exhaust Gas Temperature Gauge: which measures the temperature in the exhaust gas stream. 

¦ 
Fuel Pressure Gauge: which monitors fuel pump outlet pressure on carburettor engines and manifold output pressure on injected engines, both being proportional to fuel flow. 

¦ 
Induction System Temperature Gauge: which measures the temperature at the carburettor inlet.
