Engine Controls 
Piston engine controls may include: 
¦ 
Throttle Lever, which controls MAP and engine RPM on engines with fixed pitch propellers by altering the position of the throttle butterfly valve. 
¦ 
RPM Lever: which is used on constant speed engines to set the engine/propeller RPM. 
¦ 
Mixture Control: which adjusts fuel flow from the carburettor or fuel injection system. 

¦ 
Carburettor Heat Control: which selects the alternate hot


' ' 
source of air for the carburettor. Carburettor hot air is unfiltered. 
¦ 
Alternate Air. Can be selected on injected engines if the normal filtered air supply becomes blocked. 
¦ Cowl Flap Lever: which adjusts the position of the engine cooling air inlet cowl flaps to regulate the Cylinder Head Temperature (CHT). 
¦ 
Ignition switch. In the OFF position the switch is closed. This earths the magneto's primary circuits. The circuit is connected to ground. 
¦ 
Engine Priming Pump: which is used to pump neat fuel into the inlet manifold to assist engine starting. 
¦ Engine Prime Switch: which controls an electric pump which injects fuel into the inlet manifold to assist starting. 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

FADEC 

Many ofthe engine controls mentioned above are not needed with Full Authority Digital Engine Control (FADEC). Throttle, mixture, IELECTRONIC CONTROL UNIT 11 cowl flaps and ignition can all be combined into one computerised 
ITOP SPARK PLUGI control system. FAD EC has been in existence for many years with gas turbine engines, but piston engines have been curiously slower in introducing this technology. Anecdotal evidence suggests that may be down to resistance to change in the GA pilot community, who did not want to be parted from their mixture and throttle controls. 
FAD EC reduces engine management to a single lever for each engine. The pilot simply selects the power setting required, and the computer schedules the right amount offuel and air, taking into account temperature, humidity, density and limitations. The system also times the ignition spark for the optimum effect. 


Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 


FADEC continued 

Full authority means just that -the computer relieves the pilot of any effort to do with engine management. It will shut the fuel off in IELECTRONIC CONTROL UNIT 11 an emergency. It will schedule exactly the right amount of fuel ­
ITOP SPARK PLUGI even to individual cylinders with a fuel injected engine. Best fuel economy is provided for the desired power setting. 
The electronic engine control (EEC/ECU) is the brain of the FADEC, but the system also needs redundancy for reliability. For that reason FADEC is invariably twin channel, with either channel capable of running its engine. For multi-engine aircraft each engine will have its own FAD EC. Full authority also means that the failure case is potentially hazardous -hence the twin channels. FADEC also needs -in fact must have -its own power supply independent of the aircraft electrical system. That can be a small permanent magnet alternator within the engine driven EEC/ECU, or may even be a battery pack with a longer life than the endurance ofthe aircraft. 

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 

Engine Handling 
Handling techniques for piston engines vary slightly with engine type but the underlying principles are applicable to all piston engine operations. 
Pre-start Inspection 
The fuel must be checked for water contamination. The water contamination check involves taking fuel samples from one or more drains situated at the lowest point in the fuel system (water is heavier than fuel). The sample is drawn off into a transparent container and visually checked for water. Any water in the sample is unacceptable. More sophisticated methods of checking for water contamination involve visual colour changes on a paste, paper or capsule. Fuel quality checks are carried out before the first flight of the day. 
On inverted or radial engines, the propeller should be pulled through by hand a number of times to check for possible hydraulicing. 
Starting Procedure and Precautions 
¦ 
If the starter warning light remains on for 30 seconds or more after start the engine must be shut down. 

¦ 
Positive engine oil pressure should be indicated within 30 seconds of engine start. 



After-start Checks and Testing 
¦ 
Individual magnetos should be checked after start to ensure that both ignition circuits are working normally. 

¦ 
Because heated carburettor air is usually unfiltered, the carburettor heat should be off when the engine is running on the ground. 

¦ 
Prolonged idling should be avoided because it causes excessive fouling of the spark plugs. 

¦ 
In cold weather the oil is very viscous and doesn't lubricate the engine properly. After start, the engine should be run at minimum power until the oil reaches a specified temperature. 



Procedure for Changing Power Settings 
¦ 
Throttle movements should always be made slowly and smoothly. To reduce the stress on the engine the power should be reduced to max continuous as soon as is safely possible after take-off. 

¦ 
For constant speed propellers the sequence for changing power is remembered by the phrases 'RPM leads power for power up. Power leads RPM for power down' or 'Rev up throttle back'. 



Shutdown Procedure 
¦ The engine should be allowed to cool by running at relatively

¦

Supercharging and Piston Engine Handling 
Engine Running Faults 

¦ 
An excessively weak mixture could cause high cylinder head temperatures. This increases the risk of detonation and/or engine damage. 

¦ 
Rough running could be caused by carburettor icing, moisture in the ignition circuit, problems with fuel delivery or uneven compression in the cylinder -for which there may be a number of possible causes. 

¦ 
Black smoke from the exhaust indicates an excessively rich mixture. Black smoke just after start may indicate over priming or an unlocked manual priming pump. 

¦ 
Blue smoke from the exhaust is caused by oil burning. This may indicate worn or broken piston rings. 

¦ 
High cylinder head temperatures during ground running or taxiing may indicate that the cowl flaps have been left closed. 







Lesson 35 
Diesel Engines 

AGK 35 
Diesel Engines 
Diesel Engines 
Design and Construction 
Diesel engines use the heat generated during the compression stroke to ignite the charge. This is why they are sometimes known as 'compression ignition' engines. Ignition occurs spontaneously, so there is no need for spark plugs, distributors and magnetos. 
The other main difference between diesel and petrol engines is the way in which the mixture is created. Diesel engines always use direct fuel injection whereas petrol engines may use a carburettor, direct or indirect fuel injection system. 
To produce a sufficiently high temperature for spontaneous fuel combustion to occur, diesel engines operate at much higher compression ratios than petrol engines typically from about 14: 1 up to about 28:1. This compares with a maximum ratio of about 
9:1 for a petrol engine. This higher compression ratio increases the power output from a given quantity of charge and improves the engine's thermal efficiency. 

Cylinder head 

Fuel injector 
Valve 
Block Glow plug Pre-chamber 
Combustion chamber Piston 
Diesel Engines 
Diesel Fuel 
Aircraft diesel engines use a form of kerosene (Jet A 1) for fuel. Gasoline can't be used because it detonates uncontrollably at such high compression ratios. Diesel fuel is heavier than gasoline -with a specific gravity of about 0.8 to 0.9. It is less volatile and has a higher flash point than AVGAS which makes it a safer and more stable fuel. Its only real disadvantage is that it is a poor lubricant. Consequently the fuel pump must be specially designed to cope with kerosene. 
Kerosene fuels are sometimes categorised according to their cetane number. This indicates the ignition delay which is the time interval between start of injection and the start of combustion. The higher the cetane number the shorter the ignition period. 

Advantages and Disadvantages 
The higher operating pressures require stronger construction which increases the weight of the engine. This has traditionally been a problem because weight is usually of paramount importance. However, the invention of strong, light alloys has allowed designers to create diesel engines with much less weight penalty. 
In consequence, modern relatively light diesel engines offer a number of advantages: 
¦ 
They are inherently more fuel efficient than gasoline engines. 
¦ 
Their heavier build makes them more robust, so they require less scheduled maintenance. 
¦ 
Their more robust build also makes them ideally suited to 
supercharging/turbocharging. 
¦ 
Without the need for an ignition system they are simpler and more reliable. 

¦ 
They use kerosene which is not only cheaper than AVGAS but is less prone to the risk of fire or explosion. 


Diesel Engines 
Principle of Operation 
The four stroke diesel engine operates in a manner very similar to the four stroke petrol engine. 
Induction 
During the induction stroke air rather than mixture is drawn into the cylinder. 
Compression 
The air is compressed. Its temperature and pressure rise significantly. The temperature rise is so great that it would quickly exceed the auto ignition temperature of the fuel. If mixture had been used it would detonate. So a diesel has no option but to introduce the fuel at the very last moment. This is why all diesel engines use fuel injection. 
At the very top of the compression stroke, at TDC, a precisely metered quantity of fuel is injected into the cylinder. It immediately ignites. Again this differs from the petrol engine where ignition begins before TDC. 

Power 
Compared to the power generated by a similar sized petrol engine the force exerted on the piston of a diesel engine is very much greater. This is because, at the point of ignition, the charge is very much more compressed and contained in a much smaller volume. 
Fuel is introduced right at the last moment, so there is no risk of detonation. Therefore, unlike petrol engines, diesels don't normally operate close to the correct stoichiometric ratio they operate much leaner. With a normally lean mixture and no risk of detonation, diesel engines don't require a mixture control lever. 
The power output of the diesel engine is controlled by the amount offuel injected into the cylinder (though some designs may also have a throttle butterfly). This differs from the petrol engine where power is set by adjusting the amount of fuel and air introduced into the cylinder. 
Exhaust 
The exhaust stroke operates in the same way as a petrol engine. Recent publicity about exhaust emissions from diesel cars has made them rather less popular than they might once have been. It is now generally accepted that diesel exhaust emissions are more damaging to health than those from petrol engines. 
Diesel Engines 

Diesel Fuel Injection Systems 
Direct fuel injection systems on diesel engines must cope with the 
r
very high pressures experienced in the cylinder. Typically the injectors must operate at anything up to 24 000 psi and still be able to deliver a fine mist of exactly the right quantity of fuel at exactly the right time. As with other injected piston engines, diesel engines do not require intake heating. 
Two types of injection system are used on aero diesel engines: 
¦ 
Direct Injection. In this system high pressure fuel is fed from a high pressure pump directly to each injector. 

¦ 
Common Rail. Here, a high pressure pump supplies fuel to a common rail from where fuel is distributed to each injector. 


Older diesel engines produce soot from the exhaust due to fuel droplets from the atomiser not burning completely. Most modern 


diesel designs use the common rail system because it results in more efficient combustion, cleaner emissions and quieter operation. One good example is the Thielert Centurion shown here.

Injectors 
The injectors are mounted in the cylinder head. They incorporate a mechanically operated spring-loaded poppet valve which opens momentarily whenever the fuel line pressure exceeds the opposing force of the spring.

Common rail systems comprise four major components: 
¦ 
A high pressure pump with a pressure regulator and metering valve 

¦ 
A common rail acting as an accumulator for high pressure fuel. 

¦ 
Electro-magnetically operated injectors. 

¦ 
An engine control unit (ECU) which monitors engine conditions and then precisely controls the pressure in the common rail and the opening and closing of the injectors. 


The high pressure pump supplies a constant output of high pressure fuel to the common rail. Unlike the injection pump in direct injection system it doesn't have to generate a series of pulses nor does it need to time the fuel delivery. Its sole purpose is to pressurise the common rail. 
The common rail acts as a reservoir which provides a constant supply of high pressure fuel to each injector. Excess fuel from the rail is returned via a cooler to the aircraft fuel system. 
The injectors are electrically controlled. A solenoid valve in each opens and closes on command from the engine control unit. 
The engine control unit monitors the key engine parameters including the speed and position of the cam and crankshafts to calculate and 
ft+tiio 
Pressure Pressuresensor 
Sensors 

adjust the output of the high pressure pump and the timing of the injectors. 
The great advantage of this system is that the two functions: generating high pressure fuel, and delivering metered quantities to the cylinders are now separate. This greatly simplifies the pump design.

Diesel Engines 
ft+i=tfio 
Glow Plugs 
Diesel engines can be difficult to start when cold. This is because in cold conditions the temperature rise on the compression stroke may not be enough to raise the air temperature above the auto ignition temperature of the fuel. 
To overcome this problem some diesel engines are fitted with glow plugs to increase the temperature in the combustion chamber. There are two types of glow plug: 
Sheathed Glow Plug. The sheathed glow plug contains an electrically heated coil encased in a sheath filled with magnesium oxide powder. The powder protects the coil from vibration and the damaging environment of the combustion chamber. 
Ceramic Glow Plug. The ceramic glow plug, shown here, comprises a special heating element with a very high melting point encased in silicon nitrite. Silicon nitrite is a particularly good heat conductor, so the preheating time is very short. The material can also withstand rapid heating for long periods of time. Consequently this type of glow plug is more heat resistant and longer-lasting than the sheathed type. 
Glow plugs are powered from the battery. A cockpit warning light illuminates to indicate their operation. 

Diesel Engines 
Power to Weight Ratio 
Compared to a gasoline engine of similar size the aircraft diesel engine has a lower power to weight ratio. 

Diesel Engine Cooling Systems 
Diesel engines require more cooling than their petrol engine equivalents. Although air cooling can be used, most modern designs use a liquid cooling system. 
Liquid cooling systems use a mixture ofwater and antifreeze. Coolant is pumped around passages within the engine and returned to a storage tank via a thermostatically controlled valve. An air cooled heat exchanger located in the intake helps to disperse the heat energy absorbed by the coolant. 


Lesson 36 
Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 

AGK 36 

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+1&11o 
Gas Turbine Engines 
Propeller driven aircraft are limited to a practical top speed of about 400 KT. Beyond this speed propellers are unable to impart a sufficiently large acceleration to the air to generate thrust efficiently. Remember that thrust force is the product of the mass of the air times the acceleration given to it. 
The only efficient way to achieve higher speeds is to impart a greater acceleration to the air. This is the purpose of the jet or "gas turbine" engine. 
Modern gas turbine engines produce huge quantities of thrust by accelerating very large masses of air rearwards. For example, on take-off a Rolls Royce Trent engine ejects 1000 kg of air every second! Gas turbine engines also produce power. Internal turbines continuously drive compressors and, of course, on turboprops they also drive the propeller, either directly or indirectly. 




Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+ii1-lo 
Creating Jet Thrust 
Like all aircraft propulsion systems, the jet engine creates thrust by giving momentum to a mass of air or gas. According to Newton's 3rd Law, if we expel a mass of air rearwards there will be an equal and opposite reaction forwards. 
Jet propulsion in its simplest form is seen when an inflated balloon is released. The rapid expulsion of air from the neck of the balloon results in an equal and opposite reaction force on the inner surface of the balloon. This force is called thrust and causes the balloon to be propelled forward. 
It is important to appreciate that this propulsive force is solely the result ofthe reaction to the mass of air exiting the balloon. In no sense is the balloon achieving any thrust by "pushing" against the air. If we released our balloon in the vacuum of space it would still move forward.

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+1I1-lo 
Simple Gas Turbine Engines 
A simple gas turbine engine generates thrust much like a balloon. By expelling air rearwards, it produces an equal and opposite reaction forwards. But unlike a balloon, gas turbines use heat, 
rather than tension, to expel the gases. 
As in a piston engine, four operations are carried out on the air: compression, combustion, expansion and exhaust. When all four processes occur continuously, as in a jet engine, this is called the Brayton Cycle. When they occur one after the other, as in a piston engine, it is called the Otto Cycle. 
Intake Compressor Combustion Turbines Exhaust chamber
Air is drawn in through an intake and enters a compressor. The compressed gas leaves the compressor and enters a combustion chamber where fuel is added and the mixture is burned. The hot gases generated by combustion then rapidly exit through the exhaust section. As they exit they pass through a turbine which extracts some of the energy from the gas stream to power to the compressor. 


Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Simple Gas Turbine Engines 2 
The aft section of the exhaust pipe usually forms a convergent duct known as a propelling nozzle or jet nozzle. This duct increases the speed ofthe gas flow exiting the engine. 
The amount ofthrust produced depends on two factors: the mass of air that is accelerated and the acceleration given to it. This can be expressed as an equation: 
Thrust = mass x velocity or T = MV 
Where M = mass flow expressed in kg/sand V = change in velocity through the engine, in other words, the difference between inlet and outlet velocity. So a more accurate rendition of the formula is: 
Thrust= M X (VJet -VFlight) 
Where M is the mass flow of the air, V Jet is the speed ofthe jet exhaust and VFlight is the speed of the air entering the engine (i.e. the forward speed of the aircraft) 
Notice from this formula that we can achieve the same thrust (at slow airspeed) either by accelerating a large mass of air relatively slowly or a small mass of air very quickly. 
Intake Compressor Combustion Turbines Exhaust chamber 

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+ii1-lo 
Simple Gas Turbine Engines 3 
If the propelling nozzle is choked (i.e. no further increase in velocity can be obtained unless there is a temperature increase), then the static pressure of the gas stream at exit increases above 
atmospheric pressure. This pressure difference gives what is known as known as pressure thrust. 
This thrust can be calculated using the equation: 
Pressure Thrust= A(p -PO) 
Where A is the nozzle area, p is the nozzle static pressure and PO 
Intake Compressor Combustion Turbines Exhaust
is atmospheric pressure. 
chamber 
Therefore, total thrust of this engine is: 
Thrust= M x (VJet-V Flight) + A(p -PO) 
Jet thrust and power must not be confused. Power is the rate of doing work and is calculated by multiplying force x speed. Just before the take-off roll begins the aircraft is stationary. The engine may be creating very high thrust but the aircraft is producing no power! In fact the EASA Learning Objectives (LOs) state that "thrust can be considered to remain approximately constant over the whole aeroplane subsonic speed range". 


Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+H+lo 
Pressure, Temperature and Velocity in the Working Cycle 
This diagram shows how pressure, temperature and velocity change in a typical turbojet engine. 
There are a number of important points to note: 
Compressor: The pressure increases as air travels through the compressor. Air cannot normally flow from a low to a high pressure area, so to ensure that flow is maintained, the entire unit is contained within a convergent annulus. The convergence is designed to keep the air at a near constant velocity, and thus overrides the tendency of the air to flow back to the intake. The convergence is achieved by either reducing the diameter ofthe outer casing, increasing the diameter of the hub, or a combination of both. 
Diffuser: A divergent duct is created between the compressor and combustion sections. This is the area of highest pressure in the engine. The primary purpose of the diffuser is to reduce the axial velocity of the air for combustion.

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 

Pressure, Temperature and Velocity continued 
Combustion Area: Combustion is continuous and takes place at approximately constant pressure. For this reason gas turbines are sometimes known as constant pressure engines. 
The pressure in the combustion chamber is lower than the pressure ofthe air exiting the diffuser. Otherwise the flow through the engine would reverse. 
The highest engine temperatures are generated in the combustion chamber. 
The exit from the combustion chamber forms a convergent duct. Gas velocity increases as it exits the combustion chamber and enters the guide vanes. 
Turbine: One or more stages in the turbine extract energy from the gas stream. The overall effect is a decrease in temperature, pressure and velocity. To ensure that the air can flow without the risk of being 'choked', the annulus in the turbine section is divergent. 
Exhaust Section: In the particular exhaust design shown on the right, pressure continues to fall whilst velocity rises slightly. As we shall see later, this is not necessarily typical of a modern exhaust system. However, this is the diagram currently shown in exam papers.

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Factors Affecting Thrust 
The most obvious way of increasing thrust is to increase engine RPM. The faster the engine turns the greater the mass flow through the engine. 
However, thrust depends on the mass of air being accelerated, therefore anything which affects the mass of air or its acceleration will affect the thrust produced. 
¦ 
Altitude. Air density varies directly with humidity, altitude and temperature so less thrust will be produced at high altitude and in hot and humid conditions. At altitude the reduction in density is the predominating influence, more than offsetting any benefit gained from the lower temperature. Above the tropopause where temperature is constant the rate of thrust loss increases. 

¦ 
Speed. Aircraft speed also affects the mass flow. At high speeds (greater than M0.4) the engine benefits from a ram effect. At these speeds air becomes compressible, producing an area of relatively high pressure in the intake. The ram effect more than offsets any reduction in thrust caused by drag. As pressure rises so does temperature, and eventually temperatures at the turbine will limit maximum thrust. On a turbo-propeller engine, increased air mass flow causes engine shaft power to increase with speed. 


¦ 
Ambient Temperature. Temperature affects the mass of air entering the engine and the operating temperature of the turbine. High ambient temperatures reduce thrust and engine power. On a very cold day the engine may produce more thrust or power than it or the airframe can safely take. Such engines are called 'flat rated'. Below a certain temperature fuel flow to the engine is restricted. Flat rated engines are limited at temperatures below ISA +15°C 

¦ 
Engine Bleed. Air taken from the engine to power other aircraft systems will reduce the mass flow through the engine and thus reduce thrust. 

¦ 
Intake Heating. Heating systems will reduce the air density and so reduce mass flow through the engine. 



Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+W+lo 
Factors Affecting Thrust continued 
Maximum thrust is produced when the aircraft is stationary on the ground "static thrust". This is because the difference between VJet and VFlight is at its greatest, as is the air density. The imperial unit of thrust is the pound (lb). The SI unit offorce is the newton (N). 
Like the pressure volume diagram for the piston engine we can create a graphical representation of volume against temperature. The area bounded within these lines represents the total work done on the air and corresponds to the thrust being produced. 
Burning more fuel would increase the temperature and accelerate the air faster. This would extend the combustion line (B-C) increasing the area and thus increasing thrust. But in practice it is very difficult to achieve higher temperatures because the jet engine is already operating close to the thermal limit ofthe turbine blades. The only practical option is to increase the capacity and compression ratio of the compressor section.

Introduction to Gas Tu rbine Engines 

The Drive for Greater Engine Efficiency 
Profit margins are thin in commercial aviation so huge effort has been devoted over the years towards making jet engines more efficient. Before we look at why they have evolved into their current designs we must first understand the causes of, and remedies for, gas turbine inefficiencies. 


Specific Fuel Consumption 
The overall efficiency of the engine is usually expressed in terms of its specific fuel consumption (SFC) and its propulsive and thermal efficiencies. SFC is the ratio of fuel mass consumed to produce a unit of thrust per unit of time. The lower the SFC the better. Engines with higher compression ratios have lower SFC. 
Modern jet transport aircraft cruise at much higher altitudes than piston aircraft. This is because a gas turbine engine works more efficiently at higher altitudes. As pressure altitude increases, air density reduces. As a result, the mass flow reduces, and so maximum thrust reduces with increased pressure altitude. 
Compressors work more efficiently at higher rotational speeds. Therefore, to maintain a given value of thrust as altitude increases, compressors must rotate faster. Less fuel is required to spin the compressor faster as the less dense air reduces the resistance to turning. 
There is an optimum altitude for a jet aircraft to operate efficiently in respect of its speed and thrust required. This altitude increases as aircraft weight reduces. Maintaining a constant TAS as altitude increases results in a reduction in fuel flow and SFC from sea level up to the optimum altitude. 
Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
ft+ii&1to

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Thermal Efficiency 
Thermal efficiency describes the efficiency with which fuel energy is converted to kinetic energy. Thermal efficiency is determined by combustion temperature and pressure ratio. A long trail of hot exhaust gas left in the wake of a jet airliner represents thermal inefficiency work has been done to heat the atmosphere rather than drive the aircraft forward. Gas turbine engines are about 30% thermally efficient. 

Propulsive Efficiency 
Propulsive efficiency describes the efficiency with which kinetic energy is translated into propulsive force. This is affected by the amount of kinetic energy wasted by engine. The turbulent energetic air left in the wake of a jet engine is an example of propulsive inefficiency. Work has been done to agitate the air rather than propel the aircraft. The propulsive efficiency of an engine is expressed as: 
work done 7 (work done + work wasted in the exhaust) 

At low speeds the high velocity exhaust from a turbo-jet wastes considerably more energy than the slow slipstream from a propeller. Propeller systems are therefore more efficient at low speeds. At higher speeds the situation changes. The exhaust gas velocity remains high but its speed compared to the relative airflow is much reduced. 


Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Propulsive Efficiency continued 
Jet engines become much more efficient as forward speed increases. Some modern engine designs can achieve propulsive efficiencies between 80-90%. 
These considerations result in different engine designs to suit different operational requirements. You will recall that thrust depends on the extra velocity added to the air stream. So to achieve very high forward speeds, military fast-jet aircraft require engines that produce a high-speed jet exhaust. 
At the more moderate speeds required by conventional airliners there is considerable scope for the engine designerto increase propulsive efficiency by reducing the kinetic energy given to the exhaust stream. In other words, by applying a relatively small acceleration to a large mass of air. 
This has another key advantage. Small accelerations can be produced by propellers or, in the case ofthe modern jet engine, by large fans driven by a jet core. The wake produced by the fan is cold and relatively free of turbulence and noise so the mechanical and thermal efficiency of the fan is an order of magnitude better than a turbojet. Hence the almost universal popularity of designs which use a fan or a propeller.

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Types of Gas Turbine Engines 
For historical completeness we will start with the single-spool axial flow turbo-jet. This type ofjet was typical of early designs and is really only efficient for very high speed flight. No commercial airliner uses this design. It is inherently inefficient at airliner cruise speeds and the very high velocity exhaust gases create unacceptable noise levels. 
Single Spool Axial Flow Turbojet 
The term 'spool' is used to describe an assembly comprising a 

compressor and turbine connected by a shaft. "Axial flow" Intake Compressor Combustion Turbines Exhaust chamber
describes the passage of air through the engine -directly from front to rear along the engine's axis. 
In this example three turbine rotors are connected by a single shaft to the eight rotors of the compressor to form a single spool. This simple design produces a very high velocity exhaust from a relatively small mass of air. Its small frontal area produces low drag for high speed flight. 

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Twin Spool Bypass Turbojet 
Bypass engines improve efficiency by giving a smaller acceleration to a larger mass of air. In this design some of the air bypasses the inner section, the core of the engine and is given only a relatively small acceleration by the Low Pressure (LP) compressor, the fan. 
To work efficiently the LP compressor must operate at a relatively low rotational speed. By contrast the High Pressure (HP) compressor in the core needs to rotate at high speed. 
To cater for these differing needs the two compressors are mounted on separate spools, each driven by their own turbine section. Notice that the HP compressor is connected to the HP turbine and the LP compressor is connected to the LP turbine. The slower rotating LP compressor requires less energy so it is powered by the rear LP turbine. The high speed HP compressor requires very high energy gas so it is driven by a turbine stage located immediately behind the combustion chamber. 
The proportion of air entering the bypass compared to that entering the core is known as the bypass ratio. In this particular design, the proportion of air bypassing the core is relatively small. This engine has a low bypass ratio. 
Twin-spool bypass turbojet 
Mass flow core 
Mass flow bypass duct 

The ratio is quite simple to calculate. A bypass ratio of 10: 1, for example, means that for every 10 kg of air through the bypass, 1 kg of air goes through the core of the engine.

Twin-spool bypass turbojet
Bypass engines have several advantages: 

¦ 
The bypass air does not need to be combusted to achieve its Mass flow core acceleration. Since no fuel is needed to heat the air, the design is inherently more thermally efficient. 

¦ 
The bypass air is given a smaller acceleration so its propulsive efficiency is greater at lower speeds. 

¦ 
The two compressor sections are more efficient because both run at their optimal speed. 

¦ 
As less air enters the core, this section is smaller and so reduces overall weight. 

¦ 
The bypass air shrouds the high velocity core air resulting in a significant reduction in noise.

Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Turbo-Propeller and Turboshaft Engines 
At airspeeds up to about 300 KT and at medium altitudes a conventional propeller is more efficient than a turbofan engine. So, for slow speed aircraft, gas turbine engines can be used to drive a propeller. This requires extra turbine stages which extract almost all the energy contained in the exhaust gas. These are "turbo-propeller" or "turboprop" engines. The same design logic can be used to extract power to drive a helicopter rotor. These are known as "turboshaft" engines. 
You may be wondering why these engines bother to use a gas turbine core at all. Remember that gas turbines operate on a process of continuous combustion and consequently have a much greater power-to-weight ratio than piston engine. 
Gas turbine engines rotate at very high speed so this type of turbo-prop design uses a reduction gearbox to drive the propeller in its optimum speed range. 

Centrifugal compressor turbo propeller arrangement 
Reduction gearbox to reduce propeller speed 

Shaft Horse Power+ Jet Thrust= Equivalent Shaft Horse Power (ESHP) 
Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Turbo-Propeller and Turboshaft Engines continued 
On early designs centrifugal compressors were used. These are still used on some helicopter turbo-shaft designs where overall engine length is an issue. 
Most of the energy is extracted by the turbines to produce torque measured as "shaft horsepower", the power delivered to the shaft driving the propeller or rotor. But some residual thrust may be obtained from the gases leaving the exhaust. The overall contribution of shaft horsepower and residual thrust is measured in units of Equivalent Shaft Horse Power (ESHP). 
This diagram shows an early turboprop engine with two centrifugal compressors on a single spool. The type is known as a "direct couple" engine because the single spool is mechanically connected to the propeller. This has some inherent disadvantages. The propeller produces significant rotational drag, which can lead to problems maintaining the flow of air through the engine when it tries to accelerate. For the same reason engine starting may be more problematic. 

Centrifugal compressor turbo propeller arrangement 
Reduction gearbox to reduce propeller speed 

Shaft Horse Power+ Jet Thrust= Equivalent Shaft Horse Power (ESHP) 
Introduction to Gas Turbine Engines 
Free Turbine Engines 
Twin spool turbo-propeller engines help to get around this problem. One of the most common types is the free turbine engine. The propeller is driven via the LP turbine allowing greater flexibility in operation of the HP spool. The mechanical link between the gas generator and the propeller turbine is removed, so each element is free to rotate at its optimum speed. This results in improved power generation and helps to relieve the load on the engine during start. 
The pilot's power lever controls fuel flow, gas temperature, RPM and torque of the gas generator. The propeller control lever controls the RPM of the free turbine. If a free power turbine overspeeds, it can result in bursting of the free power turbine disc. 

Free power turbine engine Free power turbine 
Drive to gearbox for helicopter rotor or propeller

Lesson 37 
Inlet and Compressor Design 

AGK37 
Inlet and Com pressor Design 
ftiihi-lo 
Air Inlets 
The air inlet or "intake" is technically not an engine component but is part of the airframe or engine installation. However, its function is so crucial to the engine's performance that we must consider it. The purpose ofthe inlet is ensure under all operating conditions that 
¦ 
the engine receives the correct quantity of air 

¦ 
air is delivered to the engine with the minimum energy loss 

¦ 
the airflow to the engine is uniform and free of turbulence 

¦ 
the compressor receives air at a uniform pressure, evenly distributed across the entire inlet area 

¦ 
the installation causes minimum drag. 






Inlet and Compressor Design 
Subsonic Inlets 
Subsonic aircraft use a short pitot-type inlet because this shape makes full use of the pressure rise caused by the ram air effect. The inlet comprises three sections: the lip, the throat and the diffuser. 
The lip is shaped to reduce the risk of flow separation in crosswinds and when operating at high incidence angles. The throat forms a slightly convergent duct which leads to the diffuser. The diffuser forms a slightly divergent duct to generate an increase in pressure at the compressor inlet. 
The inner surface is usually profiled as one smooth continuous curve to minimise the risk of generating turbulent flow. 
When the aircraft is at rest with the engine running, air is drawn in from directly ahead, abeam and even behind the plane of the intake lip. 
A significant crosswind across the face of the intake can disrupt the smooth inflow and lead to problems in the compressor. A "hot" or "hung" start may result. A similar problem can occur at the start of a take-off in a strong cross wind. Inlet instability can cause the engine to surge when it attempts to accelerate. The risk of surge is reduced by carrying out a rolling take-off. Strong in flight turbulence, particularly at lower in flight speeds, can increase the chances of surge or flameout. 
ftiihi-lo 
Pitot intake 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Subsonic Inlets -the Effect of Airspeed 
At low airspeeds, the airflow approaching the lip is larger in cross-section than the inlet. This steadily reduces as speed increases so that the two flows are equal by the time the inlet reaches its design Mach number. At low airspeeds high sideslip angles or a high angle of attack may cause intake airflow instability which can lead to an engine surge. 
A further problem may occur at low airspeeds and high power settings. The velocity ofthe air leading into the inlet may approach Mach 1, at which point the inlet becomes "choked". To alleviate this problem older designs sometimes incorporated secondary air doors around the circumference of the inlet. These open against spring pressure to admit more air into the inlet. Secondary air doors are uncommon on modern inlet designs because of their unacceptable noise levels. 
At high airspeeds the inflow may exceed the capacity ofthe engine so the excess air spills over the lip. Above about 0.85 Mach, shock waves may form as the air accelerates through the intake throat. 

ft.IFl-lo 
Low speed 
Design mach number 
High subsonic speed 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Subsonic Inlets -Effect of Inlet Condition and FOD 
The slightest change in inlet conditions can have a marked effect on the engine. Any damage to the lip or ice formation in and around the lip of the inlet can be a serious problem. Even small amounts of ice can disrupt the laminar flow of air into the engine and cause problems at the compressor. In extreme cases it can starve the engine of air, leading to combustion failure "flameout". Furthermore, serious damage can be caused to the compressor if large chunks of ice break off and enter the engine. For this reason the lip is heated, usually with bleed air from the engine. 
A large jet engine draws in very large quantities of air even at idling speed. This creates a suction zone which may extend for several metres around the intake. 
Debris, or even people, entering the suction zone can be drawn into the engine. Even very small debris can cause catastrophic damage to the fan, compressor and turbine blades. Foreign Object Damage, or FOO as it is more commonly known, is a serious operating hazard for jet engines, especially those which are mounted close to the ground in underwing pods. Strenuous efforts are made to keep the aircraft manoeuvring areas as free as possible from loose debris. 

All personnel involved with jet engine operations must be aware of the danger posed by the suction effect of the intake. On some aircraft a diagram showing the shape and dimensions of the danger area is painted onto the engine nacelle. 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
ftiiffi-lo 
Supersonic lntets 
At supersonic speeds the pitot-type inlet generates a severe shockwave at the lip which makes it impracticable for use at high speeds. 
The jet engine can only cope with air entering the compressor at subsonic speed. So at supersonic speeds the intake must provide some means of slowing the air without incurring a large drag penalty. 
The "multi-shock" inlet slows the air in steps using a series of small oblique shockwaves. There are two types of multi-shock inlet: 
¦ 
External/Internal compression intake. 

¦ 
Variable throat intake. 




Since the demise of Concorde, neither type of inlet is used in current civil designs. Concorde used a form of variable throat. More exotic designs such as the Lockheed SR-71 high altitude reconnaissance aircraft have an external internal compression intake which uses a prominent cone located in the mouth of the intake. 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Compressors 
The purpose ofthe compressor is to increase the mass flow through the engine by pressurising the air. During compression, energy (pressure energy) is added to the air and its volume is reduced. A well designed compressor will perform this work efficiently and minimise the load on its associated turbine. The less work the turbine has to do to drive the compressor, the more energy will be left in the gas stream to be converted into thrust. 
Compressor performance is usually described in terms of its pressure ratio. Pressure ratio is the ratio of absolute compressor delivery (output) pressure to absolute compressor inlet pressure. For example, if the outlet pressure was 65 psi and the inlet pressure was 14.7 psi then the pressure ratio is 65/14.7 = 4.42. So, the pressure ratio would be 4.42:1 The very latest axial flow compressors typically achieve a pressure ratio of 35: 1. 
Two types of compressor are used: the axial flow and the centrifugal compressor. 
An axial flow compressor is so named because air flows parallel to the axis of the engine. In a centrifugal compressor the air flows radially from the centre of the compressor to its perimeter which is why it is sometimes known as a radial compressor. 
ftiiii-lo 


Inlet and Compressor Design 
ft+Hi-lo 
Centrifugal Compressors 
Centrifugal compressors are similar in appearance and principle of operation to the impeller found in a supercharger. They consist of three main components: 
¦ 
An impeller 

¦ 
A diffuser 

¦ 
A casing which houses both the impeller and diffuser. 


The impeller rotates at very high speed (as much as 40 000 RPM). Air enters the "eye" of the impeller where it is picked up by the rotating vanes and propelled rapidly outwards from the centre by centrifugal force. As it moves from the eye to the circumference of the impeller the air increases in velocity, pressure and temperature. 
The air leaves the impeller tangentially and enters the diffuser vanes. These form a series of divergent ducts which trade some of the air's kinetic energy for pressure energy. 
The impeller and diffuser each account for about half the overall rise in pressure. The pressurised air is then ducted either directly to the combustion chamber or to the eye of a second impeller to generate a further rise in pressure. 

Inlet and Compressor Design 
Centrifugal Compressors continued 
It is important to appreciate that a centrifugal compressor uses mechanical rather than aerodynamic forces to compress the air. This has one very important implication. Centrifugal compressors cannot stall -at least not in any aerodynamic sense of the word. 
The clearance between the rotating impeller and the stationary diffuser section is very carefully controlled. If it were too small it would generate buffeting and if it were too large, pressure would be lost. 
To achieve a greater mass flow the impeller can be double-sided. Air is introduced simultaneously to the eyes of the front and rear impellers and is propelled into a single diffuser section. This design is known as a "double entry" impeller. 
The use of multiple stages to increase the overall compression ratio has proved problematic as the energy losses of directing the air back into further stages is excessive. For this reason, centrifugal compressors are usually limited to single stage, sometimes two stages. This places a severe restriction on the total compression ratio and overall efficiency. 
ft.f:ifilo 

Inlet and Com pressor Design 
ftiiii-lo 
Axial Flow Compressors 
The disadvantages of centrifugal compressors outweigh the advantages. Almost all large commercial designs use an axial flow compressor. 
Principle of Operation 
Like a centrifugal compressor axial flow compressors add kinetic energy to the air and then convert the kinetic energy into pressure energy. A series of aerofoil section blades or "rotors" mounted on a rotating rotor disc accelerate the air towards a set of stationary "stator" blades. The stator blades are arranged to form a series of divergent ducts which convert kinetic energy to pressure energy. Each set of rotors and stators is called a "stage". An axial flow compressor comprises many stages. 

Inlet and Compressor Design 
Principle of Operation 2 
As the air passes across each set of rotors, velocity, pressure and temperature increase. As it passes over each set of stators velocity reduces, and pressure and temperature increase. 
Across the multiple stages of the compressor the overall effect is a steady increase in pressure and temperature whilst velocity remains approximately constant. 
The stators have a second function. Just as in a conventional wing section the air flowing over the aerofoil shaped rotor is slightly deflected. In the context of a wing we would call this downwash. The stators correct the deflection to ensure that the air is presented at the correct angle to the next stage of rotors. 
The pressure rise across a single stage is usually only about 10% or 20%. ( 1.1 : 1 to 1.2: 1) The rise is small because both the rate of diffusion and the angle of deflection must be kept quite low otherwise the blades might stall or cause unacceptable levels of drag. This is why an axial flow compressor requires many stages to achieve a usable level of compression. Compressors usually achieve pressure ratios of up to 35:1 with outlet temperatures up to 600°C. 
Rotor Stator Velocity rises and falls 

Principle of Operation 3  
To keep the flow moving against the steady rise in pressure the entire compressor assembly is located in a convergent duct -the "air annulus". This means the rotors and stators at the high­pressure end of the compressor must be much shorter than those at its inlet. This diagram shows the overall arrangement for a multi-stage axial flow compressor.  Stator vanes  Rotor blade  Main drive shaft from turbine  
Reducing the outer casing diameter and increasing the compressor spool diameter also help to keep the air flowing against nature's tendency to flow from high to low. Rolls Royce delightfully refer to this as 'sweeping water uphill'.  
Intake casing  Variable inlet guide vanes  

Inlet and Compressor Design 
ft.iiifilo 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Compressor Blade Twist 
The speed and direction ofthe air approaching each compressor blade is determined by factors: the rotational speed of the blade speed (the "circumferential" or "tangential velocity") and the speed of the air flowing along the axis of the engine (the "axial velocity"). 
These two vectors are summed together to give the "absolute velocity" which is the velocity of the air in relation to the rotating blade. The absolute velocity determines the blade's angle of attack. 
These vector components are often depicted in a velocity triangle like the one shown here. 
The tips ofthe compressor blades have a higher tangential velocity than the roots, so the angle of attack on the blade will differ from root to tip. To maintain the optimum angle over its entire length the blade is twisted. The root is set at a higher angle than the tip. The amount of twist is known as the "stagger angle". 

Rotors Stators

Inlet and Compressor Design 
Compressor Aerodynamics 
The axial flow compressor has one disadvantage. Because the rotor is an aerofoil it is susceptible to stalling -in much the same way as a wing can stall. If a stage stalls the disruption to the airflow can cause further stages to stall. This can lead to an oscillating breakdown of flow "a surge", or even a complete reversal ofthe airflow through the compressor "a deep surge". 
Gas turbine engines have a very narrow operating band, limited by the air mass flow and the overall pressure ratio. Beyond this operating band the compressor becomes unstable. A well designed compressor provides an adequate safety margin between the normal and unstable operating regions. 
A "compressor map" is a useful way of graphically illustrating the operating margins. Under normal steady-state operation the compressor operates on the "working line". But, during accelerations, the compressor's operating point can move above the working line towards the region of instability. The area of instability is bounded by the "stability line". Here we see a typical compressor map. 
Notice that the safety margin is significantly smaller at low mass flows. 

Unstable area Safety margin 
0 

.:: 
~ ~ 
:::::I Cl) Cl) 
~ 
a.. 
60% 70% 80% 90% 100% 

Air mass flow 

Some compressor maps may show an "acceleration line" which sits between the working and stability lines. This shows the expected operating conditions as the engine accelerates. 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Variable Inlet Guide Vanes and Variable Stator Vanes 
Each rotor blade is an aerofoil, so its efficiency depends entirely on achieving the optimum angle of attack. Angle of attack is a function of the rotor's rotational speed and the axial speed of flow. At low axial speeds and high rotational speed the angle of attack will be high. The rotor blades are fixed to give an optimum angle of attack at the design operating speed of the aircraft and engine. Consequently, during engine start, with almost no forward speed component, the angle of attack on the first stage rotor blades could exceed their stalling angle. 
To prevent this from happening Variable Inlet Guide Vanes (VIGV) are fitted. These adjust their angle to ensure that air is directed onto the first stage at precisely the correct angle across all operating conditions. 
On some engines the same principle is applied to the stator vanes. Variable Stator Vanes (VSVs) ensure that the air leaving one compressor stage arrives at the next at precisely the correct angle -over the entire operating range ofthe engine. 

Inter-stage Bleed 
In large multi-stage compressors it is also sometimes necessary to include inter-stage bleed valves. During start, whilst the engine is attempting to accelerate, the valves open to vent excess pressure and increase the axial flow across all the stages. This reduction in pressure reduces the load on the compressor by reducing the blade angle of attack in the early stages. This effectively brings the compressor away from the stability line and towards the working line. 
Multiple Spool Designs 
Triple spool systems are now a common feature of modern jet engines. Multiple spool designs have two major advantages: they allow High Pressure (HP), Intermediate (IP) and Low Pressure (LP) compressors to run at their individual optimal speeds and they help to reduce the need for complex air control systems to ensure stable airflow. 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Compressor Stall 
Despite all these precautions compressor blades can and do stall. Blade stall can be triggered by 
• 
damage to the blades, usually caused by ingesting debris 

• 
ice formation on the blades 

• 
extreme incidence angles, usually caused by strong crosswinds on take-offor high sideslip angles. 


If the stall is severe the consequent aerodynamic instability can spread from one stage to the next until all the stages of the compressor stall. 
A stall will result in reduced air mass flow through the compressor and thus reduced thrust. Iffuel continues to be pumped into the combustion chamber at the same rate the gas temperature will quickly rise. The principal symptoms of compressor stall are 
• 
high Gas Temperature (EGT) 

• 
reduced thrust 

• 
rumbling or banging from the compressor 

• 
fluctuating RPM and fuel flow. 



Compressor Surge 
If the stall is sufficiently severe, compressor output pressure may fall below the pressure in the combustion chamber. Hot combustion gases now flow back through the compressor. This can trigger an oscillating flow pattern in the compressor as it alternates between being stalled and unstalled. The oscillation frequency is typically about 5 Hz and can be heard as a loud "burble". 
In an extreme case the compressor can experience complete flow reversal in which combustion gases flow all the way through the compressor. Flames may be seen exiting the intake. This phenomenon is known as "deep surge" and is often accompanied by a loud bang. 
A surge is possible even when the compressor is functioning normally. If too much fuel is pumped into the combustion chamber the combustion pressure may rise excessively causing flow reversal. This "over-fuelling surge" was common with very early jet engines on start up or if the pilot attempted to increase thrust too rapidly. 
Modern fuel control units have almost completely overcome the problem by carefully monitoring engine conditions and regulating fuel flow accordingly. 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Stall and Surge in a Centrifugal Compressor 
A centrifugal compressor is much less likely to suffer from surge but is not immune from the problem. Severe intake icing, damage to the impeller or over-fuelling can cause one. 
Centrifugal compressors cannot stall in any aerodynamic sense, but they can lose output pressure for the reasons given above. 
Avoiding Surge and Stall 
The underlying principle for avoiding compressor stall is to preserve the optimum intake conditions. 
Before flight check the front and rear faces of the fan blades and engine inlets for damage or ice. Ice on the blades must be removed with hot, low pressure, air before engine start. 

Avoid starting in strong crosswinds or tailwinds by requesting a push back into wind. In strong wind conditions and high thrust settings avoid rapid turns on the ground. Consider a rolling take off in strong crosswinds and avoid any flight manoeuvre which is likely to cause an extreme angle of attack at the inlet. 
If a compressor stall occurs, restore stable intake conditions and reduce combustion pressure. In other words return as quickly as possible to straight flight and close the thrust levers. Except in cases of severe damage to the blades these two actions will normally allow the compressor to clear itself. 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Axial Flow Compressor Construction 
The rotor blades are mounted on a disc which is driven by one of the turbine shafts. Stator blades are usually bolted to the compressor casing. The tips ofthe stators are often joined by a shroud to avoid gas leaks and energy losses around the tip, this also strengthens the blades and reduces vibration. 
Rotor blades are subjected to huge centrifugal forces because of their high rotational velocity. The blades themselves are usually loosely fitted into the disc using a "fir tree" root connection. The loose fit allows the blade to take up a position which depends to some extent on the centrifugal and aerodynamic forces acting on it. This reduces the stresses at the blade root. A bonus is that individual blades can be replaced more easily. 
The loose fit is the cause of the characteristic clicking or "tinkling" sound that a gas turbine makes when it is windmilling slowly on the ground. 
Fir tree root (with shank seals) 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
LP Compressor Blade Construction 
Fan blades are usually made from titanium, or often nowadays from composite materials, increasingly, carbon fibre composites. Some designs include a mid-span support called a "snubber" or "clapper" to prevent aerodynamic instability caused by the blades flapping and bending. 
Wide chord fan blades often avoid the need for snubbers. Composite blades are designed to be flexible enough to withstand birdstrike damage. 
Fan blade showing snubber Wide chord fan blade 
Inlet and Compressor Design 
Centrifugal Versus Axial Flow Compressors 
Axial and centrifugal compressors have advantages and disadvantages which are summarised here. 
A centrifugal compressor 
¦ 
uses an impeller which is relatively easy and cheap to manufacture 

¦ 
is less prone to surge 

¦ 
is less susceptible to foreign object damage 

¦ 
is shorter, and thus well suited to compact engine designs 

¦ 
is reasonably efficient over a range of operating conditions. 


When a small engine is required, APU for example, centrifugal compressors are more efficient than axial flow compressors which suffer from excessive leakage around blade tips. 
But it has significant disadvantages compared with an axial flow compressor 
¦ 
it has a low compression ratio, rarely more than 4.5: 1 compared to at least 35:1 for an axial flow compressor 

¦ 
for a given mass flow it requires a larger frontal area 

¦ 
it is less efficient than an axial flow compressor. 



But an axial flow compressor 
¦ 
is more expensive to manufacture 

¦ 
is vulnerable to foreign object damage 

¦ 
can suffer from aerodynamic instability. 



Axi-Centrifugal (Axi-Cf) Compressors 
Some aircraft engines capitalise on the benefits of both designs by using both types. In the usual arrangement an axial compressor feeds a centrifugal compressor. This design allows relatively high compression ratios from a relatively short engine. 


Lesson 38 
Combustor and Turbine Design 

AGK 38 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Combustion Systems 
The purpose of a combustion chamber or "combustor" is to mix fuel with the compressed air and burn it efficiently. The chamber must be carefully designed to convert the rapidly expanding volume of air into a steady stream of uniformly heated gas, flowing towards the turbines. This must be accomplished with the minimum loss of pressure and maximum heat release. Combustion efficiencies in the order of 98% are not unusual. 
Unlike a piston engine, combustion does not result in a sharp rise in gas pressure but occurs at constant pressure. This allows the combustor to be lightly fabricated. There are several designs but all of them comprise an inner chamber called the flame tube and an outer air casing. 
Almost all modern gas turbines use a single annular combustion chamber wrapped round the engine core. Older designs, such as the one shown here, used multiple chambers. The operating principle is the same for both. 
ftiihi=-lo 
Air casing 


Combustor and Turbine Design 
Requirements for Combustion 
A combustor must satisfy a number of important and sometimes conflicting requirements. It must: 
¦ 
Ensure high combustion efficiency and maximum heat release. 

¦ 
Provide combustion stability across a wide range of operating conditions including ingestion of rain and hail. 

¦ 
Promote reliable and consistent ignition during start. 

¦ 
Permit in-flight restarting when air is flowing through the chamber. 

¦ 
Ensure that the hot gas leaving the chamber does not exceed the temperature limit of the turbine blades. 

¦ 
Promote low emissions, low fuel consumption and high durability. 


ft+i.i+lo 

Principle of Operation 
Air from the compressor leaves the last stage at high speed. The final stator stage straightens out the flow and feeds into a diffuser section at a velocity of about 150 m/sec. The diffuser converts some ofthe air's kinetic energy into pressure energy allowing the gas to exit the diffuser at higher pressure and lower velocity (110 m/sec). 
Only about 20% ofthe air exiting the diffuser is introduced immediately into the flame tube this is sometimes referred to as the "primary air". The remaining 80%, the "secondary air", flows around the tube and is introduced after combustion has begun. 
Even after diffusion and separation the gas velocity at the combustion chamber inlet (snout) is still much too high (100 m/sec) for stable combustion. This is because kerosene burns with a flame rate of only 3 metres per second. Consequently the entrance to the flame tube incorporates swirl vanes or a "swirler" to force the air to swirl into a doughnut-shaped recirculation vortex. This slows the air sufficiently to allow continuous combustion. The area in which this recirculation occurs is called the primary zone. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Principle of Operation 
A fuel injector sprays fuel into the re-circulating air. The combination of spray pattern and turbulent air helps to atomise the fuel. An electric igniter plug starts the combustion process. Once combustion has begun it becomes self-sustaining, so the igniter is switched off. 
Detonation is not a problem in a constant pressure combustor, so air and fuel are burned at the stoichiometric ratio of 15: 1. This results in very hot combustion gases. There are two mixture ratios to be considered at this point in the engine. The first is primary airflow to fuel, our 15: 1 ratio. The second is total airflow within the combustion chamber to fuel, and of course would be a much weaker mixture, as much ofthat secondary air is not intended for combustion. Typically the combustion temperature may peak at 2100°C. This is far hotter than any current material can withstand so these very hot gases must be quickly diluted. This is the function of the secondary air. Secondary air enters through ports in the walls of the flame tube into the "secondary zone". 
The combustion temperature, and hence power output from the engine, is limited by the maximum gas temperature that the turbine section can withstand. This in turn limits the amount of fuel that can safely be burned in the combustion chamber. 
ftiihi=hlo 
Fuel 
Igniter
injector 
Nozzle guide vane 
Diffuser 
Primary zone 


The air in the secondary zone is still too hot to be fed directly onto the turbine blades so more is added in the last third of the flame tube, the "dilution zone". This reduces the temperature of the gases leaving the combustion chamber to between 1000°C and 1500°C. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Combustor Design and Construction 
Multiple Combustion Chamber Systems 
Multiple combustion chambers were the first type of combustor to be used on jet engines. Individual chambers are arranged around the engine but are interconnected to equalise the pressure across all the tubes and to ensure that the flame propagates to all chambers during engine start. 


Image Courtesy of Gary Brossett via the Aircraft Engine Historical Society, lnc. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
ft+++~,­
Tubo-Annular Combustion Chamber 
The tuba-annular or "canannular" combustor shown in this illustration is a hybrid design comprising a number of individual flame tubes fitted inside a single air casing. This design is lighter, more compact and easier to maintain. 

Combustor and Turbine Design 
ft+M+lo 
Annular Combustion Chambers 
The annular combustor comprises a single annular flame tube contained within an inner and outer air casing. It is shorter than a multiple tube design, typically only 75% ofthe length of its tubo­annular equivalent. This results in a shorter, stiffer and lighter engine with a smaller frontal area and gives a more even distribution ofthe thermal loads on the turbine. 
The wall area is reduced, so the amount of cooling air required is also reduced. This permits more air to be used in the combustion process which improves combustion efficiency and results in cleaner emissions. 
The principal disadvantage of the annular combustor is that it is more difficult to manufacture and is not as strong as the tubo­annular design. 
Annular combustors may be lined with ceramic tiles to protect the flame tube from the high temperatures. Alternatively the flame tube itself may be constructed from a laminate of material which allows cooling air to enter a network of passages in its walls. Air entering the chamber provides a protective barrier between the hot combustion gases and the wall of the flame tube. 
All types of combustion chamber have drain valves to remove unburnt fuel. 

Combustor and Turbine Design 

Reverse Flow Annular Combustor  
To create a shorter engine, a reverse flow combustor can be used. Air enters from the rear of the engine, rather than the front. Air exiting the compressor is first ducted around the combustor and then fed in from rear to front. This allows the designer to place the compressor and turbine sections much closer together.  
Forcing the airflow to reverse results in some loss of efficiency but the advantages of a shorter engine make this a desirable option for helicopters and small turboprop aircraft.  
Centrifugal compressor  I Nozzle HP turbine guide vane  

Combustor and Turbine Design 
Fuel Spray Nozzles 
Fuel is introduced to the combustion chamber through fuel nozzles mounted around the circumference ofthe air casing. The function of the nozzles is to introduce a finely atomised spray of kerosene or kerosene vapour into the turbulent air in the primary zone of the flame tube. 
The two principal types are pressure jets and air spray injectors. 
ft.f:hf+lo 

Pressure Jet Injector 
The simplest type of pressure injector is the "simplex" nozzle. Fuel is delivered under high pressure through a swirl chamber and then out through the nozzle to provide a continuous stream of finely atomised fuel. Although it works well at high power settings the design is inefficient at low RPM. 
A refined version, the "duplex" nozzle, delivers fuel under pressure to two orifices. The smaller orifice supplies fuel at low RPM, the larger orifice supplies fuel at high power settings. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
ft+M+lo 
Air Spray Injector 
The air spray injector channels air from the compressor through an inner swirl chamber and outer swirl vanes. An annular fuel nozzle sits between the two so that the fuel is ejected between two turbulent air streams. This creates a finely atomised spray which improves combustion, producing fewer carbon deposits and offering cleaner exhaust emissions. Spray injectors operate at lower fuel pressures than pressure jet injectors. 


Combustor and Turbine Design 
Fuel Vaporisers 
The most basic method of introducing fuel into the combustor is the fuel vaporiser. This very simple system comprises a U or T shaped tube. Fuel is forced through the tube to exit in the opposite direction to the air flowing into the flame tube. This produces reasonably good fuel atomisation. They are rarely used on commercial aircraft engines because of their relatively inefficient combustion characteristics. 


Which of these statements is UNTRUE? 
• The purpose of a combustion chamber or "combustor" is to mix fuel with the compressed air and burn it efficiently. 
• Combustion results in a sharp rise in gas pressure 
• The temperature of the gases leaving the combustion chamber are typically between 1000°C and 1500°C 
• Typically the combustion temperature may peak at 2100°c 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Ignition Systems 
Gas turbine engines burn fuel continuously so a separate ignition source is usually required only during the start. Dual ignition systems are always fitted for redundancy. 
Traditionally ignition systems were powered by the aircraft electrical system, either AC or DC. Modern aircraft use an engine driven permanent magnet alternator (PMA) which also supplies the FAD EC. This serves to make the engine electrics independent of the aircraft electrics. 
There are two types ofAC igniter. The high-energy (25 000 V) type operates in a similar way to the spark plugs of a piston engine. It uses a capacitance High Energy Ignition Unit (HEIU) to store, and the deliver about 60-100 sparks per minute during the start sequence. 
The surface discharge igniter works in much the same way but uses an iridium electrode and silicon carbide semiconductor to create a high intensity flashover from the electrode to the body. It requires only 2000 V. 
Two igniters are normally mounted on the air casing and project into the flame tube. 
ftjfifii=f­

The ignition system can work automatically with the engine starting system or independently. During automatic operation ignition ceases when stable combustion has been achieved. 
To reduce the risk of flame-out some checklists require the igniters to be switched to "continuous ignition" during take-off from wet runways. Continuous ignition may also be required for flight through heavy rain, flight in high ambient temperatures, in the vicinity of volcanic ash and during the approach and landing. 
The ignition system can usually operate at two different energy outputs. The high power (12 joule) output is used for short periods of operation such as high altitude starts and ground starting. The low power (3-6 joule) output is used for continuous ignition. 
DC ignition systems use a "vibrator" or "tumbler" mechanism to feed pulsed DC to a step-up transformer. An alternative system uses a transistorised chopper circuit rather than a mechanical vibrator. 

PAGE 72 78 ~

Combustor and Turbine Design en • 
Turbines 

The turbine is the most highly stressed component in the engine. LP Turbine Not only does each blade endure large centrifugal forces, sometimes as much as 66 000 G, but it must also cope with gas temperatures up to 1600°C, which is well above their melting point. This mechanical-thermal stress affects turbine blades as well as the wheels or discs to which the blades are attached. In normal operation the blades glow red hot. 
The turbine provides power to drive the compressors, front fan, and a drive shaft for ancillary components. 
In turbo-propeller or turbo shaft engines the turbine also powers the propeller or helicopter rotor blades. Turbo-propeller and turbo­shaft engines extract almost all the energy from the exhaust gas. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Principle of Operation 
A turbine assembly comprises a series of stages. Each stage comprises a set affixed nozzle guide vanes (stator) and a rotating disc comprising many turbine blades. The discs are directly coupled to their respective compressor to create a spool. The entire assembly is located within a divergent duct to ensure that it doesn't create so much back pressure that it might choke the engine. 
High velocity gas leaves the combustion chamber and enters the Nozzle Guide Vanes (NGVs). The vanes direct the gas at the right speed and in the right direction onto the turbine blades. This generates a force on each turbine blade which causes the turbine disc to rotate. 

Types of Turbine 
There are three types of turbine: impulse, reaction and a combination of the two the impulse-reaction system. 
Impulse Turbine 
The nozzle guide vanes form convergent ducts which accelerate the gas and reduce its pressure. The accelerated gas then strikes the crescent shaped turbine blades causing an impulse force which drives the turbine. 
Reaction Turbine 
The nozzle guide vanes form parallel ducts to change the direction of airflow only. There is no change to pressure or temperature. However, some reaction turbines might form slightly convergent ducts, in which case the air is accelerated slightly with a corresponding slight drop in pressure and temperature. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 

Impulse-Reaction Turbine 
Normally gas turbine engines use a combination of impulse and reaction turbine blades to generate rotation. The nozzle guide 
Nozzle Turbine Nozzle Turbine Nozzle Turbine 
vanes form a convergent duct. The turbine blade is shaped to capture impulse force at its root and generate reaction force at its tip. About half the turning force comes from the impulse and half from the reaction. 
It is important to understand that turning force is not derived from the aerofoil shape of the blade, only from impulse and reaction. So, unlike the axial flow compressor, the axial flow turbine cannot stall. 


Impulse blade Reaction blade 
Turbine blades and NGVs are twisted along their length. The twist ensures that gas flows with uniform axial velocity along the entire span of the blade or vane. The amount oftwist is defined by its "stagger angle" and is greatest at the tip. 
The task of a turbine is to extract energy from the gas stream. So, not surprisingly, there is an overall drop in temperature, pressure and velocity across a turbine stage. 

Combustor and Turbine Design 
Turbine Blade Design and Construction 
The combination of heat and stress causes turbine rotor blades to steadily increase in length during their operating life. This phenomenon is known as "blade creep" and sets a finite life for the turbine. 
Most turbine blades are cast from high temperature nickel alloy which is resistant to creep. They are sometimes coated with a ceramic thermal barrier coating. 
The most exotic blades are formed from a single crystal of nickel alloy. Single crystal blades are even more resistant to blade creep but are very much more expensive to manufacture. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Turbine Blade Design and Construction continued 
The blades operate in temperatures which far exceed the melting point of the materials from which they are constructed. Consequently, sophisticated cooling mechanisms are required. There are three principal methods: 
¦ 
Convective Cooling. Large vented cooling voids are created inside the blade to promote heat transfer by convection. 

¦ 
Impingement Cooling. Jets of cooling air are played onto the internal surfaces of the blade faces. Heat is transferred to the air which then exhausts from holes in the trailing edge of the blade. 

¦ 
Film Cooling. Internal air passages are fed with high pressure cooling air. The air exits through very fine holes drilled into the faces ofthe blade and forms a protective film of cooling air across the surface. 


In this context "cooling" air is a somewhat relative term. The air is drawn from the HP compressor and is approximately 700°C! 
Turbine blade tips are usually shrouded to prevent tip losses and are normally attached to the turbine disc using a fir tree root. This diagram shows a blade without its tip shroud. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Turbine Disc Creep 
The turbine disc itself is also prone to creep. Turbine disc creep is partially controlled by introducing inter-stage cooling using relatively cool air from the compressor. 
Multi-Spool Engine Designs 
In multi-spool designs the higher speed HP turbine is closest to the combustion chamber to take advantage ofthe highest energy exhaust gas. It powers the HP compressor. The LP turbine is located furthest downstream and operates at slower speed. The LP turbine powers either the front fan or, in turbo-props, the propeller. On triple spool designs the middle IP turbine is used to power the middle IP compressor. 

Active Clearance Control 
Active clearance control is a method for controlling turbine blade tip clearance in the turbine section. Bleed air is played onto the turbine casing. By adjusting the flow of bleed air the internal circumference of the casing can be very finely controlled. 
Combustor and Turbine Design 
Fatigue 
High Cycle Fatigue (HCF) occurs where a large number of small stresses, typically more than 10 000, lead to a component failure. In the context of gas turbines high cycle fatigue is mainly caused by high frequency engine vibration and can lead not only to turbine blade failure but also to the failure of range of components including fans and compressors. 
Low Cycle Fatigue (LCF) is caused by a lower number of larger stresses. LCF damage occurs to turbine blades following changes in stress and tensile strain and by thermal stress when the turbine speed, and consequently the load on the turbine, is changed. Low cycle fatigue is reduced by operating at cooler temperatures and minimising turbine load changes. 
The probability of both HCF and LCF failures can be calculated. This means that components can be lifed and replaced before a failure is likely. Modern alloys and careful component design help to reduce the chance of both HCF and LCF failures. 
This image shows a computer model of thermal stress on the fir tree root of a turbine blade. 



Lesson 39 
Exhaust and Thrust Systems 

AGK 39 
Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
ftiihtrlo 
Exhaust Systems 
The function of the exhaust system is to straighten, accelerate and direct the exhaust gases to maximise thrust. A second important task is to produce minimum noise. The design of the exhaust system significantly affects overall performance. 
In a turbo-prop engine the main function of the exhaust is to expel low energy waste gas from the engine. 
The exhaust system comprises exhaust cone, jet pipe and propelling nozzle. 
Gas from the engine turbine enters the exhaust system at speeds approaching 370 m/sec. If this velocity were maintained a lot of energy would be lost in friction between the gas and the inner surface of the exhaust pipe. 
For this reason a tail cone is attached to the rear of the turbine section. This creates a divergent duct which exchanges velocity for pressure. It also helps to prevent hot gas from flowing across the rear face of the rearmost turbine. The rear struts holding the tail cone also help to reduce any residual whirl in the gas stream. 

Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
Exhaust Systems continued 
Exhaust gas velocity is held at about 290 m/sec (Mach 0.5 relative to the exhaust system) until it reaches the propelling nozzle. 
The nozzle forms a convergent duct that converts pressure back to velocity. The gas stream in the propelling nozzle can accelerate to about Mach 1.0, beyond which no further increase in velocity can be obtained unless the temperature is increased. At this point the nozzle is "choked" and back-pressure builds up in the exhaust. 
The difference between the increased static pressure in the exhaust section and the air beyond, which is of course at atmospheric pressure, results in "pressure thrust" which adds to the thrust obtained from the momentum of the gas. 
Some designs maximise the pressure thrust by adding a divergent section after the throat. This is known as a convergent-divergent nozzle.
