Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
Noise Suppression 
Noise suppression is a high priority. Exhaust noise is caused by the violent turbulence and rapid shearing action associated with high speed gases exiting from the exhaust section. 
On medium bypass engines "mixer chutes" combine the high and low speed gases to produce a slower speed, quieter exhaust. On high bypass engines the bypass air is ejected around the high velocity core air, effectively sheathing it. 
Turbo jet engines are retrofitted with exhaust hush kits which extend the jet pipe. 
Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
ftiifftrlO 
The Danger from Jet Efflux 
The high mass flows produced by modern jet engines represent a serious safety hazard to ground personnel. The hazard area extends several tens of metres behind the engine -even at idle RPM. 
Exhaust efflux can also damage other aircraft or blow equipment across the servicing area. Extreme care must be taken not to approach the rear of a running engine. Pilots must exercise similar caution when applying thrust -particularly in congested areas. Problems most commonly occur when using higher than usual thrust settings to start moving or when initiating a tight turn. 
Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
Thrust Reversers 
Exhaust efflux is often used for one other important task ­providing reverse thrust on the ground. 
Thrust reversal is used on landing and for rejected take-offs to reduce the load on the wheel braking system and to reduce the length of the landing run. It is especially useful on wet or icy runways when the conventional braking action is reduced. Typically the maximum reverse thrust available is only about 45% of maximum forward thrust. 
Thrust reverse systems must be carefully designed to ensure that hot gas is not re-ingested by the engine (which might cause a surge) and that it doesn't damage the wing. 
In all thrust reversal systems part of the exhaust stream is reversed to create a retarding force. The hot turbine exhaust can be reversed by up to 130° to produce a strong retarding force. The cold stream can also be reversed, but through a smaller angle. Consequently cold stream reversal is not quite as effective. On high bypass engines normally only the cold stream is reversed. The three most common mechanisms are shown here. 
ft+M+lo 


Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
ftiH+lo 
Clamshell and Bucket Systems 
Clamshell doors and buckets produce reverse thrust by redirecting the core (turbine) exhaust air. Clamshell doors are normally operated by pneumatic actuators. Buckets are normally hydraulically operated. 


Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
fti+h+lo 
Cold Stream Reversal Systems 
Cold stream reversal produces reverse thrust by blocking and then redirecting the bypass air. Air driven screw-jacks extend blocker doors which seal off the bypass duct. They also drive back the rear section of the nacelle to allow the bypass air to exhaust through cascade vanes which direct the air forwards. 


Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
ft+M+lo 
Interlock Protection 
Inadvertent operation of reverse thrust in flight can and has caused catastrophic structural failures. Consequently these systems must be extensively protected by interlocks. There are normally three separate locks. One is operated from the flight deck, and the other two are operated and controlled by the engine. Amber caution lights on the flight deck indicate when the reverser doors are unlocked. 
The system is prevented from deploying until the aircraft is on the ground and the engine is at idle RPM. Full reverse thrust cannot be selected until the thrust reverser mechanism has fully travelled. 
Limitations on Use 
On some engines reverse thrust cannot be used at very low speeds because hot exhaust gases might be re-ingested into the engine. 


Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
Boeing System Operation 
On Boeing aircraft separate thrust reverse levers are mounted on the forward edge of the thrust levers. They are mechanically interlocked to prevent them being used until the main power levers are at idle. Whilst the thrust levers are fully retarded and the aircraft is on the ground the reverse thrust levers can be pulled up to activate the thrust reverse system and to control engine 
RPM. 
Reverse thrust levers 
Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
Airbus System Operation 
On Airbus aircraft the main thrust levers are used to select and control reverse thrust. Reverse thrust is activated and controlled by moving the levers back, through a 'gate' beyond the idle position. The reverse thrust range is protected by interlocks including a weight on wheels sensor. 
The thrust reversal system is either manually cancelled or cancelled automatically when the aircraft slows below a certain speed. 
Reverse thrust range 
Exhaust and Thrust Systems 
Exhaust Temperature Measurement 
Temperature measurement is an essential task in the gas turbine engine. The internal temperatures of the engine have a significant impact on the operating life and structural integrity of the turbine section. 
Excessive temperatures will shorten the life of the engine. They can also result in catastrophic failure ofthe turbine discs. Therefore any temperature exceedance, whether intentional or otherwise, must be recorded in the maintenance log. 
As the engine accelerates the EGT will increase with increased air mass flow and will decrease during deceleration. These temperature variations impose significant thermal stress on the turbine. Short haul aircraft, which perform many cycles every day, impose the most thermal stress on the engine. This high "fatigue cycle" means that the engines on short haul aircraft will require more frequent inspection. 
Temperature sensors are located at several points in the exhaust and turbine areas. 
The most common sensor is the thermocouple. Thermocouples convert heat into electrical current. Two wires of dissimilar metal, typically chromel and alumel, are joined at a "hot" and "cold" junction to form a loop. A small voltage is produced whenever a temperature difference exists between the two junctions. 



Cold junction in the Cockpit 
Current Flow 

The voltage is translated to a visual indication of temperature. 
Thermocouples are wired in parallel so that the failure of one does not cause a total failure of the temperature monitoring system.

Gas Turbine Systems

Gas Turbine Systems 
Auxiliary Gearboxes 
As well as producing thrust, gas turbine engines are also the principal source of power for all other aircraft systems. Auxiliary or "accessory" gearboxes typically provide power to drive the aircraft's hydraulic, electrical and pneumatic systems. 
Power is also provided to drive the engine's fuel and oil systems. The gearbox is usually mounted on the underside of the engine for easier maintenance. 
The power extracted is not insignificant. On a high bypass engine it can be as much as 370 kilowatts. 
The gearbox is powered by an accessory drive shaft which takes power from one of the compressor shafts. The drive may be transmitted through one or more internal gearboxes. 
The starter motor is usually mounted on the auxiliary gearbox and drives the engine through the accessory drive shaft. On a multi spool engine, this drive must be connected to the HP spool.

Non-essential components (but not the fuel or oil pumps) are usually connected to the gearbox via a quill shaft or "shear neck". This type of shaft incorporates a deliberate weak point which shears if the component seizes thus protecting the gearbox and drive system from excessively high loads. 

Gas Turbine Systems 
Oil Systems 
The main purpose of the lubrication system is to reduce friction and wear between the moving components of the engine. The most significant moving components are the ball and roller main bearings of the main drive shafts. Lubricating oil is also fed to the external drive gearboxes. 
Most gas turbine oil systems are self-contained and have their own oil reservoir, usually mounted on the fan or compressor casing. Oil is pumped around the system by engine driven pumps. Oil is then gathered by scavenge pumps and returned through filters and coolers to the oil reservoir to be used again. 
A typical Rolls Royce design is shown here.

This type of air sealing system results in aerated oil which needs to be conditioned before it can be reused. Air is separated from the oil using a centrifugal breather. The relatively heavy oil is thrown to the sides and gathered whilst the air is vented overboard. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
Types of Gas Turbine Oil System 
There are two main types of lubrication system: the pressure relief valve system and the full flow system. 
In the pressure relief valve system a powerful pump provides oil pressure to the bearing chambers. The pressure is controlled by a pressure relief valve. The system works well when bearing chamber pressure is relatively low and does not increase much with engine RPM. But it is unsuitable for the high bearing chamber pressures found in modern engines. 
In the more common full flow oil system, oil flow and bearing housing pressures are directly proportional to engine speed, this permits higher bearing pressures and ensures an optimum flow of oil to the bearings at all engine speeds. 

Gas Turbine Engine Oils 
Gas turbine engines have high speed rotating assemblies but no reciprocating parts. Internal temperatures and pressures are very high, but ambient temperatures at high altitudes are very low. It is not uncommon for engine oil to experience a temperature range from -40°C to 250°C in one flight. To address these conflicting requirements low viscosity (thin) synthetic oils are used. 
Low viscosity oil offers very little resistance to the rotation of the main shafts and helps to reduce the workload on the starter motor. 
Oil System Monitoring 
Engine oil temperature, pressure and quantity are monitored and are usually displayed in the cockpit. 
The pressure drop across the HP filter and scavenge oil filter are also monitored. An excessive drop could indicate debris in the system and an impending engine failure. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
Engine Bleed Air Systems 
Air is taken from several compressor stages to be used for various internal engine tasks and externally to supply hot or high pressure air to aircraft systems. Air tapped from the compressor is known as "bleed air". 
Bleed air is not without its disadvantages. Tapping air from the compressor reduces the mass flow though the engine. This increases the engine's operating temperature and reduces power output or thrust. It also increases specific fuel consumption. So when bleed air is selected the pilot may notice a reduction in thrust (EPR) or N1, combined with an increase in EGT and fuel flow. 
Because of their effect on performance, some external bleed air systems must not be used during critical flight phases such as take-off, approach and landing. Future engine designs may do away with bleed air altogether opting instead for more powerful generators to supply an "all-electric" aircraft. 


Internal Air Systems 
The main functions of the internal air system are: 
• 
Internal engine and accessory cooling. 

• 
Bearing chamber sealing. 

• 
Prevention of hot gas ingestion into turbine disc cavities. 

• 
Engine anti-icing. 



Generally, bleed air is taken from an appropriate compressor stage depending on the required task. Relatively hot HP air is used to cool the turbine nozzle guide vanes, the turbine blades and the turbine discs. LP air is used to cool the accessories. Air of differing pressures and temperatures is used to seal the bearing chambers and to control the flow of cooling air around the turbine. 
The internal air temperature is monitored by sensors in the engine. Excessively hot air will trigger an overheat warning caption. 
Engine anti-icing uses HP air. The hot air is fed to internal galleries in the intake lip and through the supporting struts of the front compressor bearing. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
Engine Fuel Systems 

The purpose of an engine fuel system is to provide the correct quantity of fuel at the correct pressure under all conditions of altitude, airspeed, air temperature, engine temperature, engine speed and power setting. Apart from the thrust levers, the fuel control system is fully automatic. 
The main components of an engine fuel system are the HP fuel pump, a Fuel Control Unit (FCU) and a high pressure shut off valve, or "pressure raising shut-off valve." All of these components are usually mounted on the engine. 
An electrically driven LP booster pump ensures a positive fuel feed to the HP pump at all times. 


Gas Turbine Systems 
High Pressure Fuel Pumps 
Two types of high pressure fuel pump are used on gas turbines engines. These are: 
¦ 
Spur gear fixed volume pumps. 

¦ 
Multi-plunger (multi-piston) variable volume pumps. 


Spur gear pumps are used for low pressure air-spray fuel burners. They are light and simple in operation. To allow for acceleration the pump delivers more fuel to the fuel control unit than is required. A relief valve returns excess fuel to the inlet side of the pump. 
The multi-plunger pump incorporates a swash plate. The stroke of the pump is limited by the angle of the swash plate, which in turn is adjusted by a control piston. The extension of the control piston is determined by inputs from the engine overspeed governor and the FCU. By moving the swash plate the stroke can be adjusted which in turn adjusts the pump output. At engine start the pump defaults to full stroke. 
Both types of pump use fuel to lubricate their moving parts. To avoid them overheating, the engine is shut down using the HP shut-off valve. This technique also avoids the risk of cavitation in the LP fuel lines. 

A pressurising and dump valve is fitted before the burner nozzles. On start up, this valve will allow fuel to the burners once the pressure is high enough for sufficient atomisation. On shut down it will close and dump the fuel in the line to low pressure, or return it to the aircraft fuel tanks. We have a 3D model of a fuel pump for you to view in more detail on the next page. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
Fuel Control Units (FCU) 
The purpose of a FCU is to ensure the correct ratio of fuel and air during all phases ofengine operation. In other words it must keep the engine on the working line and away from the stability line during acceleration. Each engine has a dedicated FCU. 
When the pilot demands more thrust the input from the lever is sensed by the engine speed governor in the FCU. The governor now demands more fuel to accelerate the engine and the FCU commands the control piston in the HP fuel pump to move. 
More fuel is delivered to the fuel spray nozzles so combustion temperature increases. This causes the gases to exit the combustor faster which increases engine speed. The result is greater mass flow through the engine and consequently greater thrust. 
An FCU moderates the fuel flow to the spray nozzles in all flight conditions. The amount offuel required for a given thrust setting will vary according to the mass of air entering the combustor. 

An FCU has inputs from: 
¦ 
The thrust lever position or angle. 

¦ 
Air inlet temperature and pressure. 

¦ 
Engine speed. 

¦ 
Exhaust gas temperature. 

¦ 
Compressor discharge pressure. 



An Acceleration Control Unit (ACU) limits fuel flow to prevent over fuelling when the thrust lever is advanced. 
An altitude sensing unit or "barometric pressure control unit", compensates for changes in intake pressure caused by airspeed or variations in ambient air pressure. 
The FCU automatically adjusts fuel flow the keep the engine within its RPM and temperature limits. 
There are several different types of fuel control unit with names reflecting their design methodology. These include the Power Management System (PMS), the Mechanical Engine Control Unit (MECU or MEC) and the Power Management Control System (PMCS). Their internal mechanisms are very complex but you will not be asked detailed questions about them. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
FADEC 
The old style hydromechanical FCU has now been mostly superseded by Full Authority Digital Engine Control (FADEC) units. FAD EC is the term used for the total system, including EEC, FMU, sensors, actuators and electrical wiring. FADEC units are duplicated and have at least two channels of communication for redundancy and have their own power supply. 
In a FADEC system the FCU is replaced by an Electronic Engine Controller (EEC) and a Fuel Metering Unit (FMU). 
The EEC is a digital computer which senses a wide variety of engine and aircraft parameters and sends control commands to the FMU. 
The input from the throttle is not a mechanical linkage. The throttle lever angle is converted to electrical signals in the throttle control unit, and then transmitted to the EEC. 
All input and output data is checked by both channels. 
The FMU regulates HP pump output pressure to deliver the correct amount of fuel to the engine. The EEC also sends control commands to the engine airflow control systems to prevent compressor stall and surge and to improve efficiency. FAD EC systems are single fault tolerant, where a single input data failure will not affect the normal operation of the engine. 
ft+iiif¦o 

The major functions a FADEC system are: 
¦ 
Automatic starting 

¦ 
Idle. 

¦ 
Acceleration and deceleration scheduling. 

¦ 
Fuel flow control for automatic thrust setting computations. 

¦ 
Fuel flow control for manual power settings. 

¦ 
To maintain sufficient HP pump output at low RPM to power the FMU's internal components. 

¦ 
Fuel shut-off when commanded by the pilot or the EEC. 

¦ 
Overspeed protection. 

¦ 
Overtemp protection. 

¦ 
Controlling compressor airflow using IGV's, VSV's and Bleed Valves. 

¦ 
Ensuring manifold fuel draining on shut-down and returning excess fuel to the tanks. 

¦ 
Active clearance control of the turbine. 


Gas Turbine Systems 
Progression from hydro-mechanical to dual FADEC 
As you might imagine the transit from the old, purely hydro­mechanical FCU described earlier to the modern FADEC was not one giant leap but a steady progression. The following list is given as a guide. 
¦ 
Hydro-mechanical -this was the first generation of fuel control systems used on the early jets. 

¦ 
Hydro-mechanical with a limited authority electronic supervisor. This was still a hydro-mechanical system but with electronic refinement which either reacted more quickly or made life easier for the pilot. The electronic refinement could be switched off and was not required for dispatch. 

¦ 
Single channel FAD EC with mechanical back up. Full engine control added to the refinements of the previous generation but still with the ability to revert to mechanical systems. It seems the ability of mechanical back up varies between aircraft with some the mechanical back up is limited and designed to get you to land as soon as practicable, while on others you can carry on. There is no dispatch with a failed FADEC. 


¦ Dual channel FADEC with no mechanical back up. This is the latest, the FADE Cs are in constant communication with one another, if one fails the other takes over with full function for the remainder of the flight. There is no dispatch with a failed FADEC. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
Fuel Oil Heat Exchangers 
Fuel Oil Heat Exchangers ("FOHE"), sometimes known as Fuel Cooled Oil Coolers ("FCOC"), use heat from the oil to warm the fuel. This has two beneficial effects. Hot oil is cooled before it returns to the engine and cold fuel is warmed before it reaches the HP fuel system. The oil in a FOHE is usually at higher pressure than the fuel. This helps to ensure that fuel does not enter the oil system in the event of an internal leak in the FOHE. Oil entering the fuel from a leak in the FOHE would cause oil to be burnt in the combustion chamber. Apart from the depletion of the oil the burnt oil could cause carbon deposits and uneven burning. 
Fuel warming is important because it reduces the risk of fuel waxing. For this reason a FOHE is usually located upstream from the HP fuel pump to guarantee that wax crystals do not clog the inlet to the pump. 
In some systems however, the fuel is heated by bleed air before the filters, and again by the FOHE after the fuel HP pump. The fuel in the FOHE will now be at a higher pressure than the oil, and an internal leak will result in fuel entering the oil system, giving a rise in the level in the oil tank. A more dangerous consequence of fuel in the oil system is that fuel will be admitted to areas of the engine intended to be free from fuel, with an increased risk of an engine fire. 


Types of Jet Fuel 
Jet fuel is a form of kerosene or paraffin oil. It may sometimes be referred to as jet kerosene orAVTUR, which stands for aviation turbine fuel. Jet kerosene has a specific gravity between 0.77 and 0.84. 
The most common jet kerosene specifications are Jet A and Jet A1. JetA is used in the United States while most of the rest of the world uses JetA1. 
Both JetA 1 and Jet A have a fairly high flash point of not less than 38° C. 
JetA and JetA1 may contain additives to inhibit oxidization and corrosion. Some may also contain anti-static agents. One of the most common additives is Fuel System Icing Inhibitor (FSII). 
A third type offuel, Jet B, is still occasionally used in Alaska and Canada, primarily because of its much better cold weather performance. This "wide-cut" fuel is much lighter, spanning the gasoline and kerosene boiling ranges. Because of its higher volatility it is more dangerous and is only exceptionally used for commercial operations. 
Gas Turbine Systems 
Fuel Waxing 
Jet A 1 has a lower maximum freezing point than Jet A. The freezing point of Jet A is -40°C, the freezing point of Jet A 1 is ­47°C. JetA1 'slower freezing point makes it more suitable for long international flights, especially on polar routes during the winter. Jet B freezes at -60°C. 
When it becomes very cold, jet kerosene will form waxy deposits, a phenomenon known as "waxing". Waxing is potentially dangerous because it can lead to clogged filters and pipes. 
Jet fuel does not become solid at one temperature. It contains many hydrocarbons each with their own freezing point. As the fuel cools, the elements with the highest freezing points solidify first, forming wax crystals. The remaining elements will form wax crystal as the fuel continues to cool. Consequently jet kerosene gradually changes from containing a few wax crystals, to a slush and then finally to near-solid wax. 
The freezing point ofjet kerosene is defined as the temperature at which the last wax crystal melts, when warming a fuel that has previously been cooled until wax crystals form. Thus the freezing point of fuel is well above the temperature at which it solidifies. 
Waxing is potentially dangerous because it can lead to clogged filters and pipes 



Lesson 47 
Gas Turbine Handling 

AGK47 
Gas Turbine Handling 
ft.ihbf o 
Engine Starting 
To start a gas turbine engine three conditions must be met: 
¦ 
There must be a flow of compressed air through the engine. 

¦ 
Fuel must be supplied to the combustor. 

¦ 
There must be a source of ignition. 


The exact ground start sequence will vary from type to type but will essentially follow the same steps. The sequence is mostly automatic and is initiated by selecting a start switch on for two to three seconds. 
This activates a starter motor which, on a multi-spool engine, turns the HP compressor. Shortly after rotation begins the ignition units are energised at their high power setting. At about 15% RPM the HP shut-off valve is opened, allowing fuel to enter the combustor. 

The HP shut-off valve is rarely called by its technical name. It is Light-up occurs shortly after the shut-off valve is opened, more usual to refer to it by the name of its control. 

indicated by a rise in EGT. 
On some aircraft the HP shut-off valve may be incorporated in the thrust lever and is opened by moving the lever past a latched or "gated" position. 
Gas Turbine Handling 
Engine Starting continued 
The starter motor continues to help the engine to accelerate until it reaches self sustaining speed (about 30% RPM). This is the point where the engine is generating sufficient excess power to accelerate itself to idle RPM without assistance from the starter motor. 
The igniters and starter-motor cancel automatically shortly after self-sustaining speed is reached. 
The engine will continue to accelerate under its own power until it reaches and stabilises at ground idle speed. At this point N2 will be about 60% RPM and the LP compressor (N1) will be at about 25% RPM. The start sequence is complete. 
The start sequence controller usually incorporates a time switch and an overspeed sensor. 
It is quite common to hear a low frequency resonance from the exhaust during start. This is normal and no remedial action is required.

Gas Turbine Handling 
ft.lhbl o 
Types of Starter Motor 
Two types of starter system are commonly used: 
¦ 
Electric. An electric DC starter motor drives the engine through a reduction gearbox. Power is normally transmitted to the HP compressor shaft through the radial drive shaft. 

¦ 
Pneumatic. As its name suggests, a pneumatic starter motor is driven by air. Compressed air is directed onto a turbine in the starter motor. The motor turns the HP compressor via the drive shaft. In this system the starter motor can use air ducted from a running engine or from the Auxiliary Power Unit (APU) or from an external air source. Most commercial aircraft have a pneumatic back-up start system. 



Gas Turbine Handling 
Engine Start Malfunctions 
There are a number of possible start sequence failures, the most common of which are: 
Nil Rotation. The compressor fails to turn. This can be caused by a sheared starter drive, no air to the starter, or no current to the starter. 
On multi-spool engines, the low pressure (N1) spool is not connected to the starter motor. A tailwind can cause the N1 rotor to rotate in a reverse direction. During an engine start with the low pressure rotor windmilling in a reverse direction, the rotor will decelerate, stop, and then start rotating in the normal direction. Under these conditions the start will be slower than normal, with a higher than normal EGT as the tailwind velocity increases. Under high tail wind conditions it may not be possible to start the engine, in which case the engine must be turned into wind prior to start. 
Dry Start. Fuel fails to flow to the engine. Light-up does not occur (no EGT rise) and the engine stagnates at low RPM, driven only by the starter motor. The start must be cancelled. The indications of a dry start are: 
¦ 
Low and stagnating RPM. 

¦ 
No EGT rise. 

¦ 
No fuel flow. 




Hung Start. If the engine lights up but fails to accelerate properly the starter motor will continue to turn the engine but, without assistance from the engine itself, the start will "hang" with the engine RPM stagnating at typically less than 20%. The start sequence must be cancelled. The indications of a hung start are: 
¦ 
Low and stagnating RPM. 

¦ 
Low fuel flow. 

¦ 
High EGT. 



A hung start may often be the result of low air pressure or voltage to the starter motor. Similar indications can be caused by a compressor stall or surge during the start cycle. 
Hot Start. Occasionally light-up is followed by a very rapid rise in EGT. This is known as a hot start. It is essential that you monitor the EGT during the start sequence and abort the start as soon as it is obvious that EGT will exceed its maximum limit. A hot start is usually caused by excess fuel in the combustor, sometimes as a result of a previous failed start attempt. The symptoms of a hot start are: 
¦ An excessively quick rise in EGT. 
Gas Turbine Handling 
ft.iffbl o 
Engine Start Malfunctions continued 
Wet Start. If the engine fails to light-up, RPM will stabilise at a low value while unburned fuel is sprayed into the combustion chambers. This is known as a wet start. The start sequence must be cancelled. The symptoms of a wet start are: 
¦ 
Low and stable RPM. 

¦ 
No EGT rise. 

¦ 
Some indicated fuel flow. 


If a wet start occurs the pilot must carry out a "dry run" or "blow out" before attempting a re-start. 

To dry run the engine, the HP shut-off valve is closed and the igniters are switched off. The engine is then run using the start switch. This rotates the engine sufficiently fast to blow out any fuel that may have pooled in the combustor. 
If a dry run is not carried out after a wet start, the passengers will be treated to a phenomenon known as torching when the excess fuel ignites causing spectacular flames to exit from the exhaust section! 
Gas Turbine Handling 
Airborne Relight 
An airborne re-light requires the same three conditions as a normal ground start: air flow, fuel flow and a source of ignition. 
The forward speed ofthe aircraft is usually sufficient to windmill the compressor at a high enough RPM to begin the start sequence. Re-light can usually be achieved simply by activating the igniters and ensuring that the HP shut-off valve is open. 
An airborne re-light will only be successful if there is sufficient air mass flow through the engine. This depends on airspeed and altitude. It may first be necessary to descend (usually below 25 000 ft) and slow down to ensure that the start is carried under optimum conditions. In other words, within the "re-light envelope". 
The maximum re-light altitude is usually defined as the altitude at or below which a satisfactory re-light may be obtained within a given speed range. 
ft+M+lo 

Engine Shutdown 
The engine should be allowed to run at ground idle for a period of time, to decrease the thermal shock. If this is not carried out, problems can occur with the rotating assemblies due to differing cooling rates within the engine. 
Gas Turbine Handling 
Gas Turbine Operation and Monitoring 
Before we describe the various indicators used to monitor engine performance and health we first need to look at the common notation used to describe the various parts of the engine. 
Numbering starts from zero at the front ofthe engine. Lettering denotes the parameter being monitored: P for pressure, T for temperature and N for rotational speed. The typical notation for a twin spool engine would be: 
¦ 
PO, TO: ambient pressure and temperature. 

¦ 
P1, T1: compressor inlet. 

¦ 
P2, T2: LP compressor delivery 

¦ 
P3, T3: HP compressor delivery ¦ P4, T4: Turbine entry. 

¦ 
PS, TS: HP turbine exit outlet. 

¦ 
PG, TG: LP turbine outlet. ¦ P7, T7: exhaust. 

¦ 
N1 : speed of the fan or LP spool. 

¦ 
N2: speed of the HP spool. 


ft.Hft o 


On a triple spool engine N2 describes the IP spool and N3 the HP spool. On more sophisticated engine installations many of these values are recorded and digitally stored for trend analysis. Trend analysis looks for undesirable trends in engine operation which could indicate a developing problem, and also to enhance maintenance planning. 
Gas Turbine Handling 
ft.i=h%l o 


Thrust Indications 
Engine thrust is indicated either by reference to N1 or by Engine Pressure Ratio (EPR). EPR is the ratio ofjet pipe pressure to inlet pressure. A fan engine usually displays integrated EPR which is the ratio of turbine discharge and fan outlet pressures to compressor inlet pressure. 
The amount of thrust produced for a given EPR value will reduce with altitude. However, given the same atmospheric conditions EPR will equate to the same level of thrust irrespective ofthe engine's condition or age. EPR is often used as a means of indicating the power set. In ground icing conditions EPR may over-read if the P1 sensor becomes blocked. This has been the cause of at least one fatal take-off accident. 

Thrust Values Compressor Inlet Pressure 
Various thrust limits are normally specified: 
¦ Max Climb: used for a routine all-engines climb. 
¦ 
Max Take-Off: this is the maximum thrust allowed. Its use is usually limited by time. 

¦ 
Max Continuous: the maximum thrust that can be used continuously. 

¦ 
Go-around: a thrust value close to, but less than, maximum thrust used for the go-around. 


Gas Turbine Handling 
Handling Considerations 
Gas turbine engines are most efficient when operating at high RPM and at constant thrust settings. 
Frequent changes in thrust (and hence operating temperature) increase engine wear and low cycle fatigue. The turbine is the component most sensitive to high temperature. To preserve engine life, when ambient conditions and payload permit, a reduced, "de-rated" thrust take-off procedure is often used. The disadvantage oftaking off with de-rated thrust is reduced performance. In other words there is a longer take-off roll and a slower climb to height than if full thrust is used. 
FAD EC equipped aircraft enjoy a higher degree of envelope protection than engines equipped with a conventional hydro­mechanical FCU. This normally includes protection from exceeding EGT. Engines without FADEC must be handled more carefully and their operating parameters must be more closely monitored during all flight phases. The same applies for an engine whose FADEC system has failed. Not all engines 'spool­up' at the same rate, with some exhibiting a delay between pilot application of throttle and developing the required thrust. 
ft+Mi._o 

Engine Speed 
Engine speed is usually measured as a percentage of maximum RPM. On multi-spool engines N1 can be used to indicate engine thrust. The actual thrust delivered for a given N1 value will, of course, reduce with altitude. Changes in temperature and humidity will also affect the relationship between thrust and engine speed. 
During flight the lowest speed the engine operates at is Flight Idle. Flight Idle is faster than Ground Idle which means that the engine will be quicker to accelerate. It will also be able to sustain greater quantities of bleed off-take before reaching its limiting temperature. 
Gas Turbine Handling 
Engine Temperature 
The temperature ofthe exhaust gases is a reliable indicator of the performance and health ofthe engine. EGT is often used operationally to adjust thrust settings and may indeed be the limiting factor in high ambient temperatures. 
Temperature may be measured at a variety of different positions but ultimately all relate back to turbine temperature. There are several different measurements in common use: 
TGT  Turbine gas temperature  
EGT  Exhaust gas temperature  
TIT/ITT  Turbine inlet temperature  
TET  Turbine entry temperature  
JPT  Jet pipe temperature  
T4/T6  Temperature at stage 4/6 in the engine  


Torque 
On a turboprop engine power output is usually measured in terms ofthe torque produced at the propeller. Torque is a turning force, usually indicated as inch pounds or as a percentage of total power. 
Torque is measured either by hydraulic or electronic sensors, usually at the reduction gearbox. Some systems use electronic strain gauge sensors. 
Vibration 
A jet engine has no reciprocating components, so its levels of vibration are usually very low. Consequently, raised vibration levels can indicate impending problems. High frequency vibration in particular can indicate serious damage. Vibration indicators display levels of vibration on a scale of relative amplitude. The signal is filtered to remove unwanted frequencies and amplified before being transmitted to the vibration indicator. 
Engine Overheat Indication 
Engine overheat sensors are positioned around the engine and in the outlets of the vents to atmosphere. Excessively hot internal air indicates a serious engine malfunction which is why engine overheat captions are usually coloured red. 
Gas Turbine Handling 
Interpreting Engine Indications 
Monitoring of engine health indications is clearly desirable. However you should be alert for possible unusual combinations of indications, and as to what the causes might be. It may be possible to identify and assess engine damage based on instrument indications alone. 
The range of possibilities is too large for any meaningful analysis here, suffice to say that there have been many examples offlight crew misinterpreting indications that should have been obvious ­though it is always easy to be wise after the event. Wrong engines have been shut down in flight, with catastrophic consequences. 
ft.iiiifrlo 

Engine seizure 
Engine seizure describes a situation where one or more ofthe engine rotors stop turning in flight. Seizure results from very severe engine damage, to the point where the vanes and blades ofthe compressor and turbine are mostly destroyed. The static and rotating parts lock up against each other, bringing the rotor to a halt. The most likely cause of seizure is the ingestion offoreign objects. Other causes may be a failure of the lubrication system leading to bearing failure which eventually break up. Depending on the nature of cause, the seizure may be a gradual or rapid event. The rapid event can be startling, in April 1985 ice broke off the fuselage of an American Airlines B727 and went into the engine resulting in a rapid seizure that was sufficient to rip the engine off its mounting. The aircraft landed safely. 
Symptoms of engine seizure in flight may include vibration, zero rotor speed, yaw, unusual noises and the failure of other systems driven by that engine. 
As most seizures result from ingestion of foreign objects, such events can be reduced by good housekeeping procedures and good maintenance. In the above incident, the ice was caused by a leak in another system. 
Gas Turbine Hand ling 
Auxiliary Power Units (APU) 
The high power-to-weight ratio of gas turbine engines make them ideally suited for use as APUs. 
An APU generates pressurised air and electrical power to allow an aircraft to operate independently of ground support equipment. It can also provide back-up power in the event of in-flight engine failure. When ambient conditions dictate that the main engine bleeds are switched off, it can also provide air for cabin pressurisation and conditioning. An APU is often a critical element of the certification for extended twin engine operations, ETOPS. 
An APU draws fuel from the aircraft's main fuel supply, usually through a DC powered booster pump to remove reliance on external power. Air supply to the APU is either from an actuated door, as on the B737, or through a permanently open inlet, often at the base of the fin. It is usually started by a small electric starter motor powered from the aircraft's main batteries. Occasionally a dedicated APU battery is fitted. The unit is usually located in the rear fuselage so that its exhaust presents no danger to passengers, crew and ground staff. 


This photograph shows a typical APU installation. 

Gas Turbine Handling 
APUs continued 
Although the APU's generator's output is usually much less than any of the main engine driven generators it can be paralleled with them and is sufficiently powerful to meet the demands on the essential AC Busbar. 
Most AP Us may only be started below 25 000 ft. However, once started some units can operate up to 40 000 ft. Like all gas turbines, the power reduces with altitude. Some units are not cleared for air start (e.g. 8747). 
Apart from starting and shut-down, APU operation is almost entirely automatic. The engine is automatically protected from overloading. Internal sensors monitor the unit and will shut it down in the event of: 
• 
Fire warning . 

• 
Excessive exhaust gas temperature . 

• 
Low oil pressure . 

• 
High oil temperature . 

• 
Airspeed and/or altitude limits are exceeded . 

• 
APU overspeed ( 110% ) . 

• 
High bleed air temperature .

Lesson 42 
Propellor Systems 

AGK 42 
Propellor Systems 
ft+IH+-lo 
Propellers 
The purpose of a propeller is to convert engine power into thrust. Turbojets deliver thrust by accelerating a small mass of air to very high speeds but propellers do so by accelerating a larger mass of air to lower speeds, and this makes a propeller driven aircraft more efficient at providing thrust at lower speeds and medium altitudes than turbojets. Propellers can be mounted either as pusher or tractor propellers, occasionally both. 


Propellor Systems 
ft.ii+lo 
Reduction Gearboxes 
Most aircraft piston engines operate at comparatively low speeds, often less than 2500 RPM. Under these circumstances the propeller can be driven directly from the engine. Turboprop engines, however, turn at much higher speeds, typically 10 000-15 000 RPM, speeds that are so high that, ifthe engine was directly linked to the propeller, most of the blade would be supersonic, and the centrifugal forces would tear the blades from the hub. Consequently turboprops and a few pistons use a reduction gearbox to drop the propeller RPM down to manageable speeds. The gearbox is usually shaft driven and uses epicyclic gears to effectively transmit the torque whilst reducing the RPM. 
Gas turbine engine
Torque shaft 


Propellor Systems 
ft+it+lo 
The Combined Airflow 
A propeller blade can be considered as a small rotating wing producing lift in exactly the same way as does an aircraft wing. This can be seen clearly ifwe cut a slice through a propeller blade and look at the aerodynamics. 
This section of the blade has a rotational velocity, a product of the propeller RPM and the radius at the place where we cut the slice. It is also moving forward through the air at a speed that is the sum of the aircraft speed, TAS, and the airflow induced through the propeller disc. Together these determine the relative airflow past the blade. 
If the blade is to develop any lift to pull the aircraft forward it must have an angle of attack. It will work most efficiently if alpha is at about 4°, for best lift/drag ratio, and give maximum thrust at critical alpha just before the stall. 

Propellor Systems 
Thrust and Torque Forces 

The principal forces on the propeller are as shown here. The total reaction force on the aerofoil acts out of the cambered side of the blade, called the thrust face, and is split into two components. These are thrust, which acts at 90° to the plane of rotation and propeller torque, which is parallel to the rotational plane. Propeller torque normally opposes engine torque. Propeller torque is sometimes referred to as the drag of the propeller. If engine torque is equal to propellor torque (drag), then the propellor speed is constant. 

Propellor Systems 
ft.ii+lo 
Blade Twist 
The chord of the blade is set at an angle to the plane of rotation, this is called the blade angle or pitch angle. If the blade angle is large the propeller is said to have a coarse pitch if the angle is small it has a fine pitch. 
Since the rotational velocity on any part of the blade is a function ofthe radius ofthe propeller at that point it follows that, if all sections of the blade are to have the same alpha, the blade must be twisted along its longitudinal axis. The convention is that the blade angle is measured three quarters ofthe way along the blade. 
Not only does the blade need to be twisted, it also needs to be thickened towards the root. There are two reasons for this, one is that the root carries the heaviest loads and needs to be strongest, the other is that the blade requires a high speed aerofoil section where rotational velocity is greatest but, at the root a fatter, low speed profile, is more efficient. 


Propellor Systems 
ft.Hflo 
The Effect of Forward Speed 
lfwe now look at the effect of changing forward speed, TAS, on a fixed pitch propeller at constant RPM, we see that at low speed alpha will be large and at high speed alpha will be small. There will be a speed where alpha will be optimum, about+ 4°. On a fixed pitch propeller the blade angle is selected to provide this optimum condition at the aircraft's normal cruise speed. 
A fixed pitch propeller is simple to construct and to use and is popular on basic aircraft and trainers. A fixed pitch propeller, however, can have only one optimum combination of forward airspeed and engine RPM. At all other combinations it operates less efficiently. 


Propellor Systems 
ft+H+lo 
Variable Pitch Propellers 
Two Pitch Systems 
The early solution to this problem used a two-pitch system where the propeller blade pitch could be set fine for take-off to allow maximum engine RPM, and then selected to coarse pitch for higher speeds. In preparation for landing the propeller was again selected to fine pitch for low speed flight and to allow maximum engine power in the event of a go-around. 


Propeller efficiency

Propellor Systems 
The Full Range of Pitch 
Propeller pitch can also be varied beyond the normal flight range. At one extreme a feather position, at about +85° of pitch, provides minimum drag on a failed engine. At the other extreme a ground range allows a reverse thrust option, provides a ground fine pitch position for easy starting and allows a limited range of ground fine pitch positions for taxiing. 
The range between Flight Fine Pitch (FFP) and feather is called the Alpha range. It incorporates the constant speed range between FFP at, typically, 17° and the flight coarse pitch stop at about 27°. The range from the flight fine pitch stop to full reverse is called the Beta range. The FFP stop is possibly the most critical stop. Using air ground logic circuits and mechanical control stops it prevents the blade from moving into the Beta range in flight. If it failed, particularly on a twin, the excessive asymmetric drag could cause a loss of control. 
ft.Mflo 
The full range of pitch positions 
Feathering stop +85° 
Ground fine pitch stop Reverse thrust stop -15° -------­
Propellor Systems 
Reverse Thrust 
Reverse thrust is primarily used to slow the aircraft on the landing run. Air-ground (weight-on-wheels) logic switches prevent it being selected in the air. 
When reverse is selected, the prop is driven to a large negative blade angle which changes the direction of the thrust component of the total reaction. Although the total reaction and thrust are reversed, propeller torque is not. It still acts in opposition to engine torque and absorbs engine power. 
In the next lesson we will look at typical controls for selecting reverse thrust. 

Propellor Systems 
ATM and CTM -Windmilling 
When the propeller is windmilling, following an engine failure, the reaction becomes drag, not thrust, and the ATM acts with the CTM to naturally fine the blade angle. This tells us that, if nothing else is done, a variable pitch prop will move to the flight fine pitch position after an engine failure. 
Propellor Systems 
Windmilling 
As we have seen, the tendency after an engine failure is for aerodynamic and centrifugal twisting moments to drive the blade to fine pitch which, because of the forward speed, will produce a negative angle of attack. In this case though, unlike the selection of reverse, the change in the direction of the total reaction force will cause the torque drag to act in the direction of rotation of the engine. 
On a single this can be a positive advantage, as a windmilling prop can be used to restart the engine, but on a twin the high asymmetric drag can be make handling difficult. This is why there must be a feathering mechanism provided for the propellers on multi engine aircraft. 
Propellor Systems 
Propeller Control Systems 

On a variable pitch system the propeller is attached to the hub on a bearing assembly which carries the centrifugal loads whilst allowing the propeller to swivel about its own axis. A Pitch Change Mechanism (PCM) is used to adjust the blade angle. 
There are essentially two types of pitch change mechanism, single acting or double acting. In a single-acting PCM oil pressure alters propeller pitch in one direction only and a second device, either a spring, gas pressure or a counterweight, achieves a pitch change in the opposite direction. A double-acting mechanism uses oil pressure to alter the pitch setting in both directions. The delivery of hydraulic pressure is controlled by a Constant Speed Unit (CSU), the combination of CSU and PCM is known as a Pitch Control Unit (PCU). 
Small piston engine aircraft use engine oil to operate the PCM. Larger engines and turboprops normally use hydraulic fluid. For simplicity, for the rest of this chapter the fluid used will be termed as hydraulic oil. 


Propellor Systems 
Double Acting PCM 
A double-acting PCM comprises a piston inside a cylinder. The piston is linked off-centre to the propeller so that its linear motion is translated into a swivelling movement at the blade root. By selectively directing oil pressure to either side of the piston, the pitch ofthe propeller can be altered. 
This diagram shows the arrangement. Oil pressure applied to the side of the piston coloured blue would move it backwards, pressure applied to the side coloured purple would move it forwards. Ifthere was a loss of hydraulic pressure with this arrangement then the CTM would drive the blade slowly towards fine pitch, a high frontal drag position. 
Propellor Systems 

Single Acting PCM 
Single Acting PCMs have hydraulic fluid driving the blade in one direction only with a force from one or more alternative sources acting in the opposite direction. On both singles and twins counterweights provide one of the forces. The weights tend to be flung outwards and coarsen a rotating propeller but have no effect on a stationary one. Counterweights are not found with double acting PCMs. 
Counterweight 
moves propeller to Coarse Pitch 

Propellor Systems 
Multi-Engine and Single-Engine Aircraft Differences Multi-Engine Aircraft 
In a single-acting system on a twin-engine aircraft oil pressure at the front of the piston drives the propeller to fine pitch against the pressure of a strong spring, as in this diagram, or the coarsening effect of the counterweights. When an increase in pitch is required the oil pressure is released and spring pressure and the force from the counterweights start to coarsen the blade. 
If the oil pressure fails and the prop keeps rotating both the spring and the weights will drive the propeller to the position for minimum drag, fully coarse. If the engine stops the spring will act on its own in the same direction. 
Single-Engine Aircraft 
The variable pitch propeller on a single-engine aircraft is likely to be arranged differently, with a light spring acting with the oil pressure to fine the prop and the counterweights acting to coarsen it. This means that, if the oil pressure fails but the engine is still running, the props will still be driven to the min drag position, coarse, but, if the propeller and engine stop, the spring pressure will drive the prop to fine, the best position to windmill for a re­start. 
Propellor Systems 
Centrifugal Latch (Low Pitch Stop) 
On a twin engine aircraft, when the engine is shut down on the ground, the spring will naturally tend to drive the propeller to the fully coarse or feathered position as oil pressure leaks away. The difficulty with this is that a fully feathered propeller would impose unacceptable loads on the starter motor during the next start. To overcome this a centrifugal latch or low pitch stop is fitted to prevent the pitch control piston from moving to the feathered position on shutdown. 
This diagram shows a detail of the low pitch stop. At normal operating speeds the weights, also visible in the diagram, draw the latch out and allow free movement of the piston. As the engine speed falls below about 1000 RPM on shutdown a spring pushes out a latch to lock the PCM in fine pitch. 
Although this system helps on the ground it does mean that, if you are operating aircraft with a low pitch stop and you have an engine failure, you must select 'feather' before the RPM decays. 
Propellor Systems 
Constant Speed Units 
A PCM is powered by high pressure hydraulic oil from a CSU. The primary function of the CSU is to maintain the propeller RPM, and therefore the engine RPM, at the level demanded by the RPM control lever. It achieves this by sensing propeller RPM and then adjusting the pitch of the propeller to increase or decrease the amount of torque drag that the propeller is producing. 
The CSU has a simple rule: 
¦ 
If propeller RPM falls the CSU will cause the propeller blades to move to a finer pitch. 

¦ 
If propeller RPM rises the CSU will cause the propeller blades to move to a coarser pitch. 



This automatic RPM control means, for instance, that the engine power can be increased, which would naturally cause the RPM to increase, but the tendency to increase RPM causes the PCU to coarsen the blade angle. Thus an increase in power, power lever forward, leads to no overall change in RPM but a coarser blade angle. This also works in reverse a decrease in power leads to a finer blade angle. 
The PCU must also select the blade angle demanded for feather, air start, ground idling or reverse thrust. 

Propellor Systems 
CSU Operation 
The principal control element in the CSU is a spool valve, or landed valve, whose position is normally governed by propeller RPM. The landed valve will direct oil to the fine pitch or to the coarse pitch side of the pitch control piston, or to neither. 
Pressurised oil is supplied to one side of the valve by a gear type booster pump. This, and the governor, is driven by the engine. As the engine is directly connected to the propeller the drive RPM is proportional to propeller RPM. 
Increasing RPM will throw the flyweights out, lifting the valve against the spring, and decreasing RPM will allow the spring to take control and move the valve down. This diagram shows the arrangement. 
During constant speed operation of the propeller the CSU will be in one of three operating conditions: 
¦ 
On Speed 

¦ 
Over Speed 

¦ 
Under Speed 



Propellor Systems 

On Speed 
When the propeller RPM is exactly right the governor sets the valve so fluid cannot go to either side of the PCU piston. The pitch of the propeller is now fixed and the PCM is hydraulically locked. This is the position shown here. 

Propellor Systems 
Over Speed 
If engine RPM started to increase the governor flyweights would move outwards and raise the landed valve against the pressure from the spring. As the valve moves oil is directed to the coarse pitch side ofthe PCM and, simultaneously, the return line from the fine pitch side of the piston to the low-pressure engine oil system is opened. The propeller moves to a coarser pitch, propeller torque is increased and the engine decelerates back to the on­speed condition. As it does, the landed valve moves back to the on-speed position. 
The movement from fine to coarse pitch is more difficult than from coarse to fine because of the having to overcome the dominating CTM acting on the blades. 
Propellor Systems 

Under Speed 
If propeller torque becomes greater than engine torque, RPM will decrease. The governor flyweights exert less centrifugal force and move inwards and the landed valve moves downwards under spring pressure. This directs oil to the fine pitch side of the pitch change piston and opens the return line from the coarse pitch side of the piston to the low-pressure engine oil system. The propeller moves to a finer pitch and the engine accelerates back to on­speed. 


Propellor Systems 
Feathering 
In a modern aircraft with either single or double acting systems feather selection is initiated by pulling the RPM lever fully backwards. The lever is moved through the fully coarse position into a guarded feather position. Note that this selection does not necessarily stop the engine this action may need to be carried out separately as part of the full feathering drill to prevent excessive loads on the engine as the propeller blade moves to this very coarse pitch. Some aircraft engines have a different controlling system called a single lever control. A condition lever is used to select the engine on, off and to feather. The lever is also moved backwards to select feather. 
On a free power turbine turboprop engine feather can be selected with the engine still running as there is no mechanical connection between the engine and propeller. 
In a single acting system feathering can be achieved by opening the PCM oil line to drain, allowing the spring and counterweights to feather the propeller no other operating force is required. Don't forget the centrifugal latches, however, if you have a single acting system with a feathering spring you still have to actually select feather before the RPM falls below the critical value. 
\ 
Propellor Systems 
Feathering (continued) 

In double-acting systems feathering requires that pressure oil be fed to the coarse pitch side of the PCM piston and the fine pitch oil to drain. This can be done in one of two ways. 
¦ The CSU control valve can be physically lifted by a cockpit control when selected to feather to override the governor and put the valve in the coarse pitch position. 
Finer
¦ Feather selection energises solenoid operated valves which 
pitch
direct high-pressure oil to hydraulically position the CSU 
spool valve. 
The two methods of selection can work together to ensure selection. 
Loss of engine oil or loss of oil pressure may be the reason that feathering is required, and to safeguard the system there will be an electrically driven feathering pump which is switched on when feather is selected to boost oil pressure. There may also be a separate reservoir of oil for the feathering system. When the propeller reaches the fully feathered position oil pressure starts to rise. A pressure cut-out switch then activates to switch off the pump. 




Propellor Systems 

Unfeathering 

By unfeathering a propeller in flight, the pilot can cause it to windmill. This selection of windmilling is controlled and expected by the pilot and will rotate the engine fast enough for a re-start Coarser 
pitch 
attempt. This is the usual method of re-starting an engine in flight. 
The problem is that, with the engine stopped, there is no oil pressure that can be used to drive the propeller away from the feathered position. On simple systems an unfeathering accumulator is linked to the CSU. The accumulator is permanently charged while the engine is running and is protected by a non-return valve. When the engine is shut down oil pressure is lost and the NRV shuts, trapping oil under pressure in the accumulator. A solenoid valve operated by the unfeathering switch opens to allow the pressurised oil to the landed valve. Provided this valve has first been set back into the constant speed range, oil will flow to the fine pitch side of the piston as it would for any underspeed condition. In complex systems unfeathering is carried out like feathering by using an independent electrically operated pump to supply oil pressure. 


Finer p" ch 
Propellor Systems 
Automatic Feathering 
To make sure the propeller of a failed engine is promptly feathered in low speed high thrust situations such as take-off and go-around some aircraft are fitted with an automatic feathering system, called auto feather for short. 
Where fitted auto feather is only used during take off and landing. When an engine fails a torque switch senses the loss of power delivered to the propeller. As the torque falls below a pre-set value the switch activates the feathering system. 
The position of the power lever is used to arm the auto feather circuit. 
Propellor Systems

Propeller Control Systems 
Control Levers 
Two Lever Systems 
In a two lever system engine power is controlled by a power lever and propeller RPM by a separate propeller lever. This is the typical set-up for piston engines, where the power lever is the throttle and controls MAP, and the pitch control is the second lever and selects RPM. The two lever system is also used by some turboprops. 
A simple way to understand the functions of the RPM lever and the MAP gauge is to consider the RPM as the gears of a car, and the MAP as the accelerator on a car. To climb a hill in a certain gear requires a certain RPM, achieved through the gearbox, but needs more power, achieved through the accelerator. 
If the propeller has Beta control there is some mixing of the functions in that range. With the propeller control selected to MAX RPM, the power lever is used to select the Beta range. Movement ofthe power lever within this range, overrides the CSU and removes the Alpha range flight fine pitch stop (flight idle), allowing the Beta range blade angles for flight fine, ground fine (ground idle) and reverse to be set. Within this range the power lever is controlling both propeller blade angle and engine power. 
ft+i·N o 

Typical two lever functions for a turboprop are illustrated here. Positions out of the normal flight range are guarded by detents. Note that a selection of the power lever rearwards through flight idle sets the propeller control in the Beta range. Movement forward through flight idle sets the Alpha range this is standard. 
Propeller levers 
Power levers 
Flight idle Min RPM
Ground fine Reverse Feather 

Propeller Control Systems 
Single Lever Systems 
In a single lever system the power lever controls both engine power and propeller RPM. Advancing the power lever schedules more fuel to the engine and selects increased RPM on the propeller governor. Although called a single lever system there is also a second lever called the condition lever. As it name suggests it selects the 'condition' of the engine and propeller. Air Start, Run, Ground Stop and Feather are the conditions in which the engine and propeller can be selected. 
A single lever system is also appropriate for a turboprop engine designed to run at constant RPM. The condition lever is set to 'run' and then power changes can be made by moving the power lever in the flight range. 
If an increase is required the lever is moved forward, the fuel flow to the engine is increased which increases engine torque which tries to increase the RPM. The propeller governor is set to a constant value of RPM. It senses the engine trying to increase speed and coarsens the propeller. This increases propeller thrust and torque and ultimately maintains a constant RPM at a higher power setting. On reducing power the reverse happens and the propeller fines off. Since the engine does not have to accelerate to increase power these constant speed engines are very responsive. 
ft+.H o 

Movement of the power lever into the Beta range operates the engine in the same way as a two lever system.

Condition levers 




Propeller Control Systems 
Synchronising and Synchrophasing 
Synchronising 
On a multi-engine aircraft very slight differences in propeller RPM can result in a beat frequency noise pattern being established. This results in noise and vibration levels rising and falling rhythmically. To avoid this problem a synchronising unit is fitted. One engine is designated as the master. The synchronising unit compares electrical signals from engine driven generators and electrically trims the other engine speeds to exactly match the master engine. 
Synchrophasing 
A synchrophase unit reduces total noise amplitude by adjusting the synchronised slave engine(s) until propeller blade position or phase is best arranged to reduce the noise. 
The diagram gives the phase pattern for a particular aircraft -a C130 Hercules -with the No.2 engine as the master engine. Notice firstly that synchrophasing is not possible until propeller RPM has been synchronised and secondly that synchronised propellers do not have to hold any specific propeller alignment. The diagram only illustrates the set-up for synchronised propellers giving the worst tip interference. 
ftiiNo 
Synchronised: all same RPM but still noisy 
. ...............,..........,.,.,. ~ 
Synchrophased: all same RPM & phased for minimum noise level 
Propeller Control Systems 
Synchrophasing continued 
The synchrophase unit will hold the propellers in a fixed phase relationship by tweaking their RPM up and down. The phase angles for the C130 with No.2 as master, shown above on the previous page, are No.1 -10° lead, No.3 -20° lag, No.4 -22° lead. 
Synchrophasing reduces noise level along the fuselage, but especially in the area in line with the propellers. This diagram shows a plot of noise level for the C130, with and without synchrophasing.

Propeller Control Systems 
Power Measurement 
With a fixed pitch propeller, RPM provides a reasonable indication of power. Clearly, when a constant speed propeller is used, RPM alone means nothing, so a different measurement is required. Piston engine aircraft normally use MAP and turboprops torque. When you consider the formula Power= torque x 2nn where n = revs/sec, it is easy to see that when RPM is constant, torque has a direct relationship with power. 
Torque Measurement 
Torque is an indication ofthe power output of an engine. One system, based on oil pressure, measures the torque in the reduction gearbox. An alternative, electrical, system measures the torque on the shaft between the engine and the reduction gearbox. 
Torque Measurement Using Oil Pressure 
This diagram shows the arrangement of helical reduction gears, called epicycloidal reducers, in the reduction gearbox. The helical teeth on the gears produce an axial thrust proportional to the torque, which is transmitted by oil pressure to an indicating system. Display may be in PSI or in % torque.

Electrical Torque Measurement 
Most modern engines use an electrical torque meter. Torque is detected by two concentric shafts. One, the drive or torque shaft, transmits the torque from the engine to the gearbox while the 

Torque second is only connected at one end, at the engine. meter assembly 
Each shaft has a serrated ring gear fitted which, during operation, rotates past a magnetic pickup and induces an AC voltage. We have already seen these 'phonic wheel' arrangements to read I AC output RPM. Here we have two together. As output torque is increased the torque shaft physically twists as it drives the gearbox. Propeller Control Systems 
Propeller Checks after Engine Start 
The following describes the checking of the constant speed operation of the propeller with a two lever control system on a piston engine aircraft: 
The optimum blade position for engine start is at a fine pitch, so there is minimal drag from the blades on rotation. This could be indicated as 'max RPM' or 'High RPM'. 
The engine should be allowed to idle for a minute to warm the oil before changing the propeller pitch. If Beta control is fitted these can be selected and checked for correct values of RPM and torque during taxi. 
ft+Wo 

The RPM is noted with the throttle at idle and the propeller control lever set to MAX RPM. The throttle is opened with the propeller still selected to MAX RPM. The propeller will be on the fine pitch stop and act just like a fixed pitch propeller. As the throttle is opened RPM will increase and a value of approximately 2000 RPM is set and noted. With the RPM stable the propeller control lever is slowly moved towards the MIN RPM position and the RPM gauge monitored. Movement of the lever will coarsen the propeller and as engine torque has not been altered the RPM will reduce. Control movement is stopped when the RPM is approximately 1500 RPM. The propeller lever is then moved back to the MAX RPM position and the RPM should return to the previously noted value around 2000 RPM as the propeller pitch reduces back to the fine pitch stop. 
The throttle is then closed to the idle position and the RPM should be as initially noted.
